Nutrition

Nutrition

Complete edition · all 14 chapters · by Dr. Vidya Aggrawal · Nightingale College
Nutrition · Chapter 1

Nutrition Basics

Every day you make dozens of decisions about what to eat, and every one of them delivers a specific chemical payload to a body that has very specific chemical needs. Nutrition is the science that connects those two things — the food on the plate and the physiology inside the person. This chapter builds the scaffolding the rest of the book hangs on: what a nutrient actually is, the six classes they sort into, how food energy is measured and where the familiar 4-4-9 numbers come from, the system of reference standards that tells us how much of a nutrient people need, and how all of that gets translated into practical guidance. None of it requires you to label a food good or bad — it requires you to understand what that food does.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Define nutrition, food, and nutrient, and explain what makes a nutrient essential.
  • Name the six classes of nutrients and sort them into macronutrients and micronutrients.
  • State the energy yield of carbohydrate, protein, fat, and alcohol in kilocalories per gram, and use those factors to work out the energy in a food.
  • Distinguish a kilocalorie from a calorie and from a kilojoule.
  • Explain what the DRIs are and define EAR, RDA, AI, and UL, including who each one applies to.
  • Explain why a nutrient recommendation changes with age, sex, pregnancy, and lactation.
  • Contrast nutrient density with energy density, and describe what "empty calories" means and why the phrase is imprecise.
  • Tell the difference between a nutrition claim backed by strong evidence and one that is still preliminary.

Section 1What nutrition is

Food, nutrients, and the difference between them

Nutrition is the science of how the body takes in, breaks down, absorbs, transports, uses, and disposes of the substances in food — and how those processes affect health across a lifetime. Food is what you actually eat: a physical, cultural, sensory, economic thing, bought at a store, cooked in a kitchen, shared at a table. Nutrients are the individual chemical compounds inside that food which the body requires to grow, to repair itself, to run its machinery, and to regulate that machinery.

Holding those two words apart matters more than it looks. Food is never a single nutrient; a chickpea delivers protein, starch, fiber, folate, iron, magnesium, and water simultaneously, and those components arrive bound up in a physical matrix that changes how fast they are digested. This is why nutrition research so often finds that a whole food behaves differently from the isolated nutrient it contains — and why a supplement that delivers one compound in a capsule frequently fails to reproduce the benefit seen with the food.

Essential nutrients: the short list your body cannot make

The body manufactures thousands of compounds for itself. A nutrient is called essential (or indispensable) when the body cannot synthesize it at all, or cannot synthesize enough of it to meet its needs, so it has to come from the diet. There are roughly forty of them, and the definition is strictly operational: remove the nutrient from the diet, a characteristic deficiency develops; put it back, the deficiency resolves. That reversal is the test.

The essential list includes nine essential amino acids (histidine, isoleucine, leucine, lysine, methionine, phenylalanine, threonine, tryptophan, and valine), two essential fatty acids (linoleic acid, an omega-6, and alpha-linolenic acid, an omega-3), thirteen vitamins, a set of minerals, and water. Notice what is not on it: there is no essential carbohydrate. The body can make the glucose it needs from other substrates, which is why very-low-carbohydrate diets are survivable — though that fact says nothing about whether they are optimal, which is a separate question with a much messier evidence base.

TermWhat it meansExample
FoodThe edible material itself, with all its components and its physical structureAn orange
NutrientA specific chemical in food the body needsVitamin C in the orange
Essential nutrientThe body cannot make enough; the diet must supply itVitamin C — humans lost the enzyme to synthesize it
Non-essential nutrientUseful, but the body can synthesize it from other materialAlanine, an amino acid the body builds itself
PhytochemicalPlant compound with biological activity but no established requirementLycopene in tomatoes
Deficiency diseaseThe syndrome that appears when an essential nutrient runs shortScurvy from prolonged lack of vitamin C

Why diet matters to health — and how strongly

Diet influences health through at least three distinct routes, and they carry very different weights of evidence. The first is deficiency: the classic nutrient-deficiency diseases — scurvy, beriberi, pellagra, rickets, iodine-deficiency goiter, iron-deficiency anemia — are causally settled. We know the nutrient, the syndrome, and the cure, and that knowledge is among the most solid in all of medicine. The second is dietary pattern and chronic disease: large cohort studies plus a smaller number of randomized trials link overall eating patterns rich in vegetables, fruits, legumes, whole grains, nuts, and unsaturated fats to lower rates of cardiovascular disease. This evidence is good but is about patterns, not single foods. The third route is food insecurity and access, which shapes what is realistically eatable long before preference enters the picture.

The contrast with single-nutrient supplement claims is instructive, and worth internalizing early. Observational studies once suggested that people with high beta-carotene intakes had less lung cancer. When that was tested directly in large randomized trials of beta-carotene supplements in smokers, lung cancer incidence went up, not down. Similarly, large trials of vitamin D supplementation have not reproduced the broad non-skeletal benefits that observational data implied. The pattern repeats often enough to be a rule of thumb: a nutrient's behavior inside a whole diet is not reliably predicted by its behavior inside a pill.

Nutrition is not a morality system. Foods are not clean or dirty, sinful or virtuous, and eating one does not make a person good or bad. Foods have compositions, and bodies have requirements. Throughout this book we describe what a food contains and what the evidence shows it does — that is the whole job. Framing that is shame-based has not been shown to improve health outcomes, and it makes accurate self-report of intake far less likely, which degrades the data clinicians depend on.
Interactive Tap each of the six nutrient classes to see the job it does in the body — energy, structure, or regulation — then use the sliders to watch grams turn into kilocalories at 4, 4, 9, and 7 per gram. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Nutrition is the science of how the body uses food; food is what is eaten, and nutrients are the chemicals inside it.
  • An essential nutrient is one the body cannot make in adequate amounts — removing it causes a deficiency, restoring it cures the deficiency.
  • There are about forty essential nutrients, including 9 essential amino acids, 2 essential fatty acids, 13 vitamins, several minerals, and water; there is no essential carbohydrate.
  • Evidence for deficiency diseases is very strong; evidence for whole dietary patterns is good; evidence for isolated single-nutrient supplements preventing chronic disease is generally weak or negative.

Section 2The six classes of nutrients

Six classes, two categories

Every nutrient in every food belongs to one of exactly six classes: carbohydrate, lipid (fat), protein, vitamin, mineral, and water. That list has not changed in a century, and memorizing it early pays off, because almost every later topic in nutrition is an elaboration of one of these six.

The classes then split along a second axis. Macronutrients are needed in gram quantities each day: carbohydrate, lipid, protein, and — by most conventions — water. Micronutrients are needed in milligram or microgram quantities: the vitamins and minerals. "Macro" and "micro" refer to the amount required, not the importance. The recommended intake of carbohydrate for adults is 130 g a day — a figure derived from the brain's average minimum glucose use, not from carbohydrate being essential — while the recommendation for iodine is 150 µg — a difference approaching a millionfold, yet running out of iodine damages the thyroid just as surely as running out of energy starves the body.

The energy-yielding three, plus the three that are not

Only three classes supply usable energy: carbohydrate, lipid, and protein. Vitamins, minerals, and water supply none. This is a point students get wrong constantly, usually about vitamins — a "B-complex for energy" supplement does not contain any energy. Several B vitamins act as coenzymes in the pathways that release energy from macronutrients, which is a genuine and important role, but the coenzyme is a tool, not fuel. Giving extra tools to a system that already has enough does not make it run faster.

A quick tour of the six. Carbohydrates — sugars, starches, and fibers — are the body's most readily used fuel — glucose is the brain's preferred substrate and, because mature red blood cells have no mitochondria, their obligatory one; fiber is a carbohydrate that human enzymes cannot digest, so it reaches the colon largely intact and feeds the gut microbiota. Lipids include triglycerides, phospholipids, and sterols; they are the densest fuel store, they build every cell membrane, they carry the fat-soluble vitamins A, D, E, and K, and they supply the two essential fatty acids. Proteins are chains of amino acids that build and repair tissue and serve as enzymes, transporters, antibodies, and some hormones; the body will burn protein for fuel, but that is a fallback, not its job. Vitamins are organic compounds — four fat-soluble (A, D, E, K) and nine water-soluble (vitamin C and the eight B vitamins) — that regulate metabolic processes. Minerals are inorganic elements, split into major minerals needed at more than 100 mg/day (calcium, phosphorus, magnesium, sodium, potassium, chloride, sulfur) and trace minerals needed in smaller amounts (iron, zinc, iodine, selenium, copper, manganese, fluoride, chromium, molybdenum). Water is a nutrient in its own right, makes up roughly 50–70% of adult body weight, and is the one whose absence kills fastest — days, not weeks or months.

ClassMacro or microOrganic?Yields energy?Main jobs
CarbohydrateMacroYesYes — 4 kcal/gPrimary fuel; fiber feeds gut microbiota and adds stool bulk
Lipid (fat)MacroYesYes — 9 kcal/gConcentrated fuel and storage; membranes; carries vitamins A, D, E, K
ProteinMacroYesYes — 4 kcal/gStructure and repair; enzymes, transporters, antibodies
VitaminMicroYesNoRegulate metabolism; many act as coenzymes
MineralMicroNoNoStructure (bone), fluid balance, nerve and muscle signaling, cofactors
WaterMacroNoNoSolvent, transport, temperature regulation, chemical participant

Deficiency and excess both have consequences

Because micronutrients are needed in tiny amounts, it is tempting to treat them as trivial. The deficiency syndromes say otherwise, and they are worth knowing by name. Vitamin C deficiency produces scurvy; thiamin (B1) deficiency produces beriberi, and in the setting of chronic alcohol use, Wernicke–Korsakoff syndrome; niacin (B3) deficiency produces pellagra. Vitamin D deficiency causes rickets in growing children and osteomalacia in adults. Vitamin A deficiency causes night blindness progressing to xerophthalmia and is a leading cause of preventable childhood blindness worldwide. Iodine deficiency causes goiter and, during pregnancy, impaired neurological development in the child. Iron deficiency causes iron-deficiency anemia. Vitamin B12 deficiency causes megaloblastic anemia together with neurological damage that can become permanent.

The mirror image is real too. Fat-soluble vitamins are stored rather than readily excreted, so chronic high intakes of preformed vitamin A can cause liver damage and are teratogenic in pregnancy. Excess iron is toxic, and acute iron ingestion is a classic pediatric poisoning. Water itself can be harmful in extreme excess by diluting blood sodium (hyponatremia). The take-home is that a nutrient's dose–response curve has two ends, which is exactly the problem the upper-limit standard in Section 4 exists to solve.

"Macro" is about quantity, not rank. Students often hear "micronutrient" and mentally file it as minor. Iodine is required at roughly 150 µg per day for adults — about the mass of a few grains of salt — yet insufficient iodine during pregnancy is one of the most common preventable causes of impaired cognitive development in the world. Small requirement, enormous consequence.
Interactive Explore the six classes of nutrients on an interactive wheel that sizes each one by how much the body needs, sort twenty real food components into them, then move sliders to watch grams of carbohydrate, protein, fat, and alcohol turn into calories. Open full ↑
Key points
  • The six classes are carbohydrate, lipid, protein, vitamin, mineral, and water.
  • Macronutrients (carbohydrate, lipid, protein, water) are needed in grams; micronutrients (vitamins, minerals) in milligrams or micrograms.
  • Only carbohydrate, lipid, and protein yield energy; vitamins, minerals, and water yield none.
  • Carbohydrate, lipid, protein, and vitamins are organic (carbon-containing); minerals and water are inorganic — vitamins are compounds, minerals are single elements.
  • Both deficiency and excess cause defined syndromes, which is why standards specify an upper limit as well as a recommended amount.

Section 3Calories and energy

What a Calorie actually is

A calorie is a unit of energy: the heat needed to raise the temperature of one gram of water by one degree Celsius. That unit is inconveniently tiny for food, so nutrition uses the kilocalorie — 1,000 calories, written kcal, the heat needed to raise one kilogram of water by one degree Celsius. Here is the vocabulary trap: the "Calories" printed on a food label are kilocalories. A 200-Calorie granola bar contains 200 kcal, which is 200,000 calories in the strict physics sense. Some countries and most scientific journals use the SI unit instead, the kilojoule (kJ), where 1 kcal = 4.184 kJ. Nothing about the food changes; only the units do.

Food energy was originally measured with a bomb calorimeter: burn a weighed sample of food in oxygen inside a sealed chamber surrounded by water, and measure how much the water warms. That gives gross energy. The human body is not a bomb calorimeter — it does not absorb everything and does not oxidize everything completely — so the values used in nutrition are physiological ones, refined by W. O. Atwater and colleagues and known as the Atwater factors.

The 4-4-9 rule (and 7 for alcohol)

These four numbers are among the very few worth committing to memory exactly:

SubstanceEnergy per gramPer gram in kJNote
Carbohydrate4 kcal/g~17 kJ/gApplies to sugars and starches; digestible carbohydrate
Protein4 kcal/g~17 kJ/gGross energy is higher (~5.65 kcal/g); nitrogen is excreted as urea, so ~4 is available
Fat (lipid)9 kcal/g~37 kJ/gMore than twice carbohydrate or protein — the densest fuel
Alcohol (ethanol)7 kcal/g~29 kJ/gYields energy but is not a nutrient — it is not required for any body function
Fiber~2 kcal/g~8 kJ/gNot digested by human enzymes; some energy is recovered from colonic fermentation
Vitamins, minerals, water0 kcal/g0Essential, but contribute no energy at all

With those factors you can compute a food's energy from its composition. A food supplying 30 g carbohydrate, 10 g protein, and 12 g fat delivers (30 × 4) + (10 × 4) + (12 × 9) = 120 + 40 + 108 = 268 kcal. You can also run it backwards to find the share of energy from each macronutrient: in this example fat supplies 108 of 268 kcal, or about 40% of the energy, even though it is only about 23% of the 52 g of macronutrient weight. That gap — fat being a small share of the mass but a large share of the energy — is the direct consequence of the 9.

Energy in, energy out

Total energy expenditure has three main components. Basal metabolic rate (BMR) is the energy needed to keep a resting body alive — breathing, circulation, ion gradients, cell turnover — and it is by far the largest piece, typically around 60–70% of the total in a sedentary adult. Physical activity is the most variable component, ranging from a few percent to a very large share in athletes and heavy manual workers. The thermic effect of food, the energy spent digesting and processing a meal, accounts for roughly 10% of intake. Individual requirements differ substantially with body size, body composition, age, sex, growth, pregnancy, lactation, and activity, which is precisely why the standards in the next section are stated for defined groups rather than as one number for everybody.

"Empty calories" is shorthand, not chemistry. No calorie is literally empty — a calorie is a unit of energy, and energy always arrives attached to carbohydrate, fat, protein, or alcohol. The phrase is a loose way of saying that a food supplies energy with relatively few vitamins, minerals, fiber, or protein alongside it. Section 5 gives that idea a cleaner name: nutrient density.
Interactive Enter grams of carbohydrate, protein, fat, and alcohol and watch the kilocalorie total assemble itself from the Atwater factors 4, 4, 9, and 7 — with worked food examples, a kilojoule toggle, and the chemistry behind why each number is what it is. Open full ↑
Key points
  • A food-label Calorie is a kilocalorie (kcal); 1 kcal = 4.184 kJ.
  • Energy yields: carbohydrate 4, protein 4, fat 9, alcohol 7 kcal/g; fiber contributes roughly 2 kcal/g.
  • Vitamins, minerals, and water supply no energy, and alcohol supplies energy without being a nutrient.
  • Multiply grams by these factors to get a food's energy; divide by total energy to get the percentage of calories from each macronutrient.
  • Expenditure = BMR (largest share) + physical activity (most variable) + thermic effect of food (~10% of intake).

Section 4Dietary standards

The DRI framework

The Dietary Reference Intakes (DRIs) are a set of reference values for nutrient intake, developed by expert committees under the U.S. National Academies of Sciences, Engineering, and Medicine in partnership with Canadian authorities. They are not a single number per nutrient. They are a family of four related values — EAR, RDA, AI, and UL — each answering a different question, and each set separately for defined life-stage and sex groups: infants, children by age band, adolescent males and females, adult males and females by age band, pregnancy, and lactation.

Two structural points prevent most of the confusion. First, the DRIs describe healthy people; they are not treatment doses for disease. Second, the RDA and AI are goals for usual intake averaged over time, not targets to hit precisely at every meal or even every day. A person who eats no vitamin A on Tuesday and plenty on Wednesday is fine; the body's storage and turnover are what these values are built around.

EAR, RDA, AI, and UL — one at a time

The Estimated Average Requirement (EAR) is the intake estimated to meet the requirement of half the healthy individuals in a life-stage and sex group. Because half the group needs more than the EAR, it is a poor goal for an individual. Its real uses are two: assessing the adequacy of intakes in populations, and serving as the mathematical starting point for the RDA.

The Recommended Dietary Allowance (RDA) is the intake sufficient to meet the requirement of nearly all — about 97–98% — of healthy individuals in that group. It is derived from the EAR, conventionally as the EAR plus two standard deviations of the requirement. The RDA is the appropriate individual goal, and it is deliberately generous: most people need less than the RDA, and an intake below it is not by itself evidence of deficiency.

The Adequate Intake (AI) is used when the scientific evidence is too thin to establish an EAR, and therefore too thin to calculate an RDA. It is an observed or experimentally determined approximation of the intake of apparently healthy people. An AI is used the same way an RDA is used — as an individual goal — but it carries less certainty, and that difference in confidence is the entire reason for the separate label.

The Tolerable Upper Intake Level (UL) is the highest usual daily intake likely to pose no risk of adverse effects for almost all individuals in the group. Above the UL, risk of harm rises. Two cautions: the UL is not a recommended intake, and the absence of a UL for a nutrient means the data were insufficient to set one, not that unlimited intake is safe. For several nutrients the UL applies only to specific forms — the folate UL applies to synthetic folic acid from supplements and fortified foods, not to folate occurring naturally in food.

ValueCoversUse it forDo not use it for
EARMeets the need of 50% of the groupAssessing group/population adequacy; deriving the RDAAn individual's daily goal
RDAMeets the need of 97–98% of the groupAn individual's daily intake goalAssessing a population mean
AIBest estimate when evidence cannot support an EARAn individual goal, with lower confidenceConcluding that a lower intake is deficient
ULHighest usual intake likely to carry no riskA ceiling — a safety check on supplements and fortificationA target or a recommended amount

The related Acceptable Macronutrient Distribution Ranges (AMDRs) work differently, expressing goals as a percentage of total energy for adults: carbohydrate 45–65%, fat 20–35%, and protein 10–35% of calories. There is also an Estimated Energy Requirement (EER) for calories — notably there is no RDA for energy, because setting an energy value that covers 97–98% of a group would systematically overshoot the average person's need.

Reading a real value: population, units, and life stage

A DRI value means nothing without its group and its units. Some worked examples, all for healthy people, all per day:

NutrientType and valueGroupHow it shifts
ProteinRDA 0.8 g/kg body weightAdults 19+, both sexesHigher in pregnancy and lactation; higher per kg in infancy and childhood
Vitamin CRDA 90 mg / 75 mgAdult men / adult women 19+Add 35 mg/day for people who smoke; higher in pregnancy and lactation
IronRDA 8 mg / 18 mgMen 19+; women 19–50Falls to 8 mg for women 51+; rises to 27 mg in pregnancy. UL 45 mg
CalciumRDA 1,000 mgAdults 19–501,200 mg for women 51+ and for everyone 71+. UL 2,500 mg (19–50)
Vitamin DRDA 600 IU (15 µg)Ages 1–70800 IU (20 µg) at 71+. UL 4,000 IU (100 µg) for adults
FolateRDA 400 µg DFEAdults 19+600 µg DFE in pregnancy. UL 1,000 µg/day of folic acid only
FiberAI 38 g / 25 gMen / women 19–50An AI, not an RDA — scaled from about 14 g per 1,000 kcal
Total waterAI 3.7 L / 2.7 LAdult men / adult womenIncludes water from food and all beverages, not just plain water
An intake below the RDA is not a diagnosis. Because the RDA is set to cover 97–98% of a group, most individuals genuinely require less than it. A single day, or even a run of days, under the RDA tells you a person is at somewhat higher probability of inadequacy — nothing more. Deficiency is established with clinical findings and laboratory data, not with a diet record. This chapter is general education, not individualized dietary or medical advice.
Interactive Drag an intake across a population's requirement distribution and watch the EAR, RDA, AI, and UL each answer a different question — with nine real nutrients, live coverage and risk calculations, and honest empty panels where the evidence cannot support a curve. Open full ↑
Key points
  • The DRIs are a family of reference values for healthy people, set separately by life stage and sex.
  • EAR covers 50% of a group — a population tool and the basis of the RDA.
  • RDA covers 97–98% of a group — the appropriate individual goal.
  • AI replaces the RDA when evidence is insufficient; it is used the same way but with lower confidence.
  • UL is a safety ceiling, not a target; no UL means insufficient data, not proven safety.
  • Always state the population and units with any value — iron is 8 mg/day for adult men but 18 mg/day for women 19–50 and 27 mg/day in pregnancy.

Section 5Dietary guidelines and nutrient density

From nutrient numbers to something you can eat

Nobody shops for 18 mg of iron. The DRIs are stated in nutrients, but people buy, cook, and eat foods, so a translation layer is required. That layer is the food-based dietary guideline, and most countries publish one. In the United States the Dietary Guidelines for Americans are revised on a five-year cycle by the Departments of Agriculture and Health and Human Services, accompanied by a consumer graphic — historically a pyramid, more recently the MyPlate plate icon. Other countries use their own imagery, from Canada's plate to Japan's spinning top, but the underlying nutrient science is broadly shared.

Recent editions of the U.S. guidelines converge on a few durable themes. Follow a healthy dietary pattern across every life stage rather than chasing individual nutrients. Make that pattern adaptable to personal preference, culture, tradition, and budget — a guideline that ignores cost or culture will not be followed, and an unfollowed guideline improves nothing. Meet nutrient needs primarily from nutrient-dense foods. And limit added sugars, saturated fat, sodium, and alcohol, with typical quantified targets of under 10% of calories from added sugars, under 10% of calories from saturated fat, and under 2,300 mg of sodium per day for adults. The MyPlate graphic renders this as proportions: roughly half the plate vegetables and fruit, about a quarter grains with at least half of those whole grains, about a quarter protein foods, plus dairy or a fortified alternative.

Nutrient density versus energy density

Nutrient density is the amount of beneficial nutrients a food provides relative to its energy content — nutrients per calorie. Energy density is a separate measure: calories per gram of food. The two vary independently, and confusing them causes real errors. Nuts are high in energy density and also high in nutrient density. A sugar-sweetened soft drink is the opposite pairing — appreciable energy with almost no accompanying nutrients. Lettuce sits at a third corner: very low energy density, and because the calorie denominator is so small it rates well on nutrients per calorie, even though one serving supplies only small absolute amounts. Fortified breakfast cereal can be nutrient-dense for certain micronutrients while also carrying substantial added sugar. The concept is a comparison tool, not a ranking of foods into approved and forbidden.

Which brings us to empty calories, a phrase you will hear constantly. It refers to energy delivered with few accompanying nutrients — classically from added sugars, solid fats, and alcohol. It is a useful shorthand and an imprecise one, and current guidance has largely moved to naming the specific components (calories from added sugars, calories from saturated fat) rather than labeling whole foods "empty." That shift is deliberate, because in practice the label got applied to foods, and then to the people eating them. A more accurate framing: nearly every diet contains some foods eaten mainly for pleasure, celebration, or convenience, and the useful question is what the overall pattern looks like — not whether any single item earns approval.

IdeaDefinitionUnitsWorth remembering
Nutrient densityBeneficial nutrients per calorieNutrients / kcalCompares foods at equal energy; high here means more nutrition per calorie
Energy densityEnergy per unit weight of foodkcal / gDriven largely by fat and water content; independent of nutrient density
Added sugarsSugars added during processing or preparationg, or % of kcalCommon target: under 10% of total calories for adults
"Empty calories"Informal term for energy with few accompanying nutrients—Imprecise; prefer naming added sugars, saturated fat, or alcohol directly
Dietary patternThe overall combination of foods eaten over time—The unit at which the chronic-disease evidence is strongest

How much confidence do the guidelines deserve?

Different pieces of the guidance rest on different foundations, and a careful reader separates them. The strongest support is for the nutrient standards themselves — the essentiality of each nutrient and the intakes that prevent deficiency, established through decades of depletion–repletion work. Also strong: folic acid fortification reducing neural tube defects, and reduced sodium intake lowering blood pressure. Good, mostly from cohort studies plus a limited set of randomized trials: dietary patterns emphasizing vegetables, fruits, legumes, whole grains, nuts, and unsaturated fats associate with lower cardiovascular risk. Weaker or contested: precise optimal percentages for individual macronutrients, and most claims that a single nutrient in supplement form prevents chronic disease.

None of this makes the guidelines arbitrary. It means they are a reasonable synthesis of evidence of mixed strength, aimed at populations, and they should be read that way — as a starting framework, not a prescription for any particular person. Individual recommendations belong with a qualified clinician or registered dietitian who knows the person's history, medications, laboratory values, resources, and preferences.

Judge the pattern, not the plate. The evidence linking diet to chronic disease is overwhelmingly about what people eat over months and years, not about any one meal. A single food cannot be evaluated in isolation, and no single food ruins or rescues a diet. This is also why guidance framed around adding foods — more vegetables, more legumes, more whole grains — tends to be both more accurate and more sustainable than guidance framed around forbidding them.
Interactive Compare 13 foods across six nutrients on three live bases — per portion, per 100 kilocalories, and head-to-head at matched energy — and watch why a calorie count alone cannot answer the question. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Food-based dietary guidelines translate nutrient standards into foods, food groups, and proportions people can act on.
  • Common quantified targets for adults: added sugars under 10% of calories, saturated fat under 10% of calories, sodium under 2,300 mg/day.
  • MyPlate proportions: about half vegetables and fruit, a quarter grains (at least half whole), a quarter protein foods, plus dairy or a fortified alternative.
  • Nutrient density is nutrients per calorie; energy density is calories per gram — they are independent measures.
  • "Empty calories" is informal shorthand for energy with few nutrients; naming added sugars, saturated fat, or alcohol directly is more precise and less prone to misuse.
  • The dietary pattern, not the individual food, is the level at which the chronic-disease evidence is strongest.

The chapter in one breath

  • Nutrition studies how the body uses food; food is what is eaten and nutrients are the chemicals within it, with essential nutrients being those the body cannot make in adequate amounts.
  • There are exactly six classes of nutrients: carbohydrate, lipid, protein, vitamin, mineral, and water.
  • Macronutrients are required in grams (carbohydrate, lipid, protein, water); micronutrients in milligrams or micrograms (vitamins, minerals) — the labels describe quantity, not importance.
  • Energy yields are carbohydrate 4, protein 4, fat 9, and alcohol 7 kcal/g; vitamins, minerals, and water yield none, and a label "Calorie" is a kilocalorie (1 kcal = 4.184 kJ).
  • The DRIs set nutrient reference values for healthy people by life stage and sex: EAR covers 50% of a group, RDA covers 97–98%, AI substitutes when evidence is thin, and UL is a safety ceiling rather than a goal.
  • Any cited value needs its population and units — iron is 8 mg/day for adult men, 18 mg/day for women 19–50, and 27 mg/day in pregnancy.
  • Dietary guidelines convert nutrient standards into food-group proportions such as MyPlate, with typical limits on added sugars, saturated fat, sodium, and alcohol.
  • Nutrient density (nutrients per calorie) is the more precise successor to "empty calories," and the dietary pattern over time — not any single food — is what the chronic-disease evidence actually addresses.

ReferenceWords worth knowing

A quick reference for the terms introduced in this chapter — skim it now, then come back whenever a word slows you down.

Acceptable Macronutrient Distribution Range (AMDR)
A DRI value giving a healthy range of intake for an energy-yielding macronutrient as a percentage of total calories; for adults, carbohydrate 45–65%, fat 20–35%, protein 10–35%.
Adequate Intake (AI)
A DRI used when evidence is insufficient to set an EAR and therefore an RDA; an observed or experimentally derived approximation of the intake of healthy people, used as an individual goal with lower confidence.
Calorie (kilocalorie, kcal)
The unit of food energy. One kilocalorie is the heat required to raise one kilogram of water by one degree Celsius, equals 1,000 small calories, and equals 4.184 kilojoules. The capital-C "Calorie" on a label is a kilocalorie.
Carbohydrate
A macronutrient class comprising sugars, starches, and fibers; digestible forms yield 4 kcal/g and serve as the body's most readily used fuel.
Dietary Reference Intakes (DRIs)
The umbrella set of nutrient reference values (EAR, RDA, AI, UL, and related figures) for healthy people, established by life-stage and sex group.
Empty calories
Informal term for energy supplied with few accompanying nutrients, typically from added sugars, solid fats, or alcohol; imprecise, and increasingly replaced by naming the specific component.
Energy density
The energy a food supplies per unit of weight, in kcal per gram; independent of nutrient density.
Essential nutrient
A nutrient the body cannot synthesize in adequate amounts, so the diet must supply it; its absence produces a characteristic deficiency that restoring the nutrient resolves.
Estimated Average Requirement (EAR)
The intake estimated to meet the requirement of 50% of healthy individuals in a life-stage and sex group; used for population assessment and to derive the RDA.
Kilojoule (kJ)
The SI unit of energy used on food labels in many countries; 1 kcal = 4.184 kJ.
Lipid
The macronutrient class that includes triglycerides, phospholipids, and sterols; yields 9 kcal/g, builds cell membranes, and carries the fat-soluble vitamins A, D, E, and K.
Macronutrient
A nutrient required in gram quantities each day: carbohydrate, lipid, protein, and (by convention) water.
Micronutrient
A nutrient required in milligram or microgram quantities each day: the vitamins and minerals.
Mineral
An inorganic element required in the diet; major minerals are needed at more than 100 mg/day, trace minerals in smaller amounts. Minerals supply no energy.
Nutrient density
The amount of beneficial nutrients a food supplies relative to its energy content — nutrients per calorie.
Nutrition
The science of how the body ingests, digests, absorbs, transports, uses, and excretes the substances in food, and how those processes affect health.
Protein
The macronutrient class made of amino acid chains; yields 4 kcal/g and supplies structure, enzymes, transporters, antibodies, and some hormones. Nine amino acids are essential.
Recommended Dietary Allowance (RDA)
The average daily intake sufficient to meet the requirement of about 97–98% of healthy individuals in a life-stage and sex group; the appropriate individual intake goal.
Tolerable Upper Intake Level (UL)
The highest average daily intake likely to pose no risk of adverse effects for almost all individuals in a group; a ceiling, never a target.
Vitamin
An organic compound required in small amounts to regulate metabolism; four are fat-soluble (A, D, E, K) and nine are water-soluble (vitamin C and eight B vitamins). Vitamins supply no energy.
Water
An essential nutrient making up roughly 50–70% of adult body weight; it serves as solvent, transport medium, temperature regulator, and chemical reactant, and supplies no energy.

Check yourself

1. Distinguish "food" from "nutrient," and explain why a whole food often behaves differently from an isolated nutrient it contains.
Food is the edible material as eaten; a nutrient is a specific chemical inside it that the body requires. A whole food delivers many nutrients at once, embedded in a physical matrix that alters how quickly they are digested and absorbed — which is one reason isolated supplements repeatedly fail to reproduce the benefits observed with whole foods.
2. What makes a nutrient "essential"? What is the experimental test?
The body cannot synthesize it in adequate amounts, so the diet must supply it. The test is depletion and repletion: remove it and a characteristic deficiency appears; restore it and the deficiency resolves. There are roughly forty essential nutrients, including 9 essential amino acids, 2 essential fatty acids, 13 vitamins, several minerals, and water.
3. Name the six classes of nutrients and state which are macronutrients and which are micronutrients.
Carbohydrate, lipid, protein, vitamin, mineral, and water. Macronutrients: carbohydrate, lipid, protein, and (by convention) water. Micronutrients: vitamins and minerals. "Macro" and "micro" describe the quantity required, not the importance — iodine is needed at about 150 µg/day for adults yet its shortage during pregnancy is a major preventable cause of impaired development.
4. Which nutrient classes yield energy, and which yield none? Address the common belief that vitamins provide energy.
Only carbohydrate, lipid, and protein yield energy. Vitamins, minerals, and water yield none. Several B vitamins act as coenzymes in the pathways that release energy from macronutrients, which is why they get marketed as "energy" nutrients — but a coenzyme is a tool, not fuel, and adding more to a system that already has enough does not generate energy.
5. State the energy yield in kcal per gram for carbohydrate, protein, fat, and alcohol.
Carbohydrate 4 kcal/g, protein 4 kcal/g, fat 9 kcal/g, alcohol 7 kcal/g. Fiber contributes roughly 2 kcal/g from colonic fermentation. Alcohol yields energy but is not a nutrient, because no body function requires it.
6. A serving of food contains 40 g carbohydrate, 15 g protein, and 10 g fat. How many kilocalories does it supply, and what percentage comes from fat?
(40 × 4) + (15 × 4) + (10 × 9) = 160 + 60 + 90 = 310 kcal; fat supplies 90/310, or about 29%. Note that fat is only about 15% of the food's weight but nearly 29% of its energy — the direct consequence of 9 kcal/g.
7. A label lists "230 Calories." Explain what that unit actually is, and convert it to kilojoules.
The capital-C Calorie on a label is a kilocalorie (kcal) — 1,000 small calories — so the food supplies 230 kcal. At 4.184 kJ per kcal, that is about 962 kJ. A kilocalorie is the heat needed to raise one kilogram of water by one degree Celsius.
8. Define the EAR and the RDA, state the percentage of the group each covers, and explain which one belongs on an individual's goal sheet.
The EAR meets the requirement of 50% of healthy individuals in a life-stage and sex group; the RDA meets the requirement of about 97–98% of them. The RDA is the individual goal. The EAR is used for assessing population adequacy and as the mathematical basis of the RDA (conventionally EAR plus two standard deviations); because half the group needs more than the EAR, it is unsuitable as a personal target.
9. When is an AI used instead of an RDA, and what does that substitution tell you about the evidence?
An AI is set when the evidence is insufficient to establish an EAR, which means an RDA cannot be calculated. It is an observed or experimentally derived approximation of the intake of apparently healthy people, used as an individual goal but with explicitly lower confidence. Fiber (38 g/day for men and 25 g/day for women aged 19–50) and total water (3.7 L/day for adult men, 2.7 L/day for adult women) are AIs, not RDAs.
10. What is the UL, and what are the two most common ways students misread it?
The Tolerable Upper Intake Level is the highest usual daily intake likely to pose no risk of adverse effects for almost all individuals in a group. First misreading: treating it as a recommended or target intake — it is a ceiling. Second: assuming that a nutrient with no UL is safe in unlimited amounts, when in fact it means the data were insufficient to set one. Some ULs also apply only to a specific form, such as the folate UL of 1,000 µg/day, which covers synthetic folic acid, not naturally occurring food folate.
11. Why must a nutrient recommendation always be reported with its population? Illustrate with iron.
Because requirements differ by age, sex, pregnancy, and lactation, a bare number is meaningless. The iron RDA is 8 mg/day for men 19 and older, 18 mg/day for women aged 19–50, 8 mg/day for women 51 and older, and 27 mg/day during pregnancy, with a UL of 45 mg/day for adults. Quoting "the iron RDA is 18 mg" without the group is wrong more often than it is right.
12. Distinguish nutrient density from energy density, and explain why "empty calories" is being replaced by more specific language.
Nutrient density is beneficial nutrients per calorie; energy density is calories per gram of food. They vary independently — nuts are high in both; a sugar-sweetened drink is fairly energy-dense but low in nutrient density; lettuce is very low in energy density yet rates well on nutrients per calorie. "Empty calories" is informal shorthand for energy arriving with few nutrients, but no calorie is literally empty, and in practice the label migrated from components to whole foods and then to the people eating them. Naming the specific component — calories from added sugars, from saturated fat, or from alcohol — is both more precise and less prone to misuse.
Nutrition · Chapter 2

Digestion, Absorption & Transport

A sandwich is not nutrition. It becomes nutrition only after your body takes it apart. Between the first bite and the moment a glucose molecule crosses into a capillary lies a chemical disassembly line several meters long — muscles that grind, acids that unfold proteins, enzymes that cut specific bonds, and a lining folded so cleverly that a tube the width of your thumb presents a surface the size of a small room. This chapter follows food through that line, organ by organ, and then follows the freed nutrients into blood and lymph, through the liver, and past the trillions of resident bacteria that finish the parts we cannot. Understanding this pathway is what turns a nutrition label from a list of numbers into a story about what your body can actually reach and use.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Trace a bolus of food through every organ of the gastrointestinal tract and state what each contributes.
  • Distinguish mechanical from chemical digestion, and explain hydrolysis as the reaction behind all nutrient breakdown.
  • Describe how carbohydrate, protein, and fat are each broken down to their absorbable units, and state the energy each yields (4, 4, and 9 kcal/g).
  • Name the major digestive enzymes and secretions, where each is made, and what each acts on.
  • Explain how plicae, villi, and microvilli amplify absorptive surface area, and match key nutrients to their absorption sites.
  • Contrast the blood (hepatic portal) and lymphatic routes out of the intestine, and describe the liver's first-pass role.
  • Summarize what the gut microbiota does, and separate well-supported claims from preliminary ones.
  • Explain why a nutrient that is present in food is not automatically a nutrient that is bioavailable.

Section 1The GI tract — mouth to anus

One long tube, plus helpers

The gastrointestinal (GI) tract, or alimentary canal, is a continuous muscular tube running from mouth to anus. Textbooks often quote about nine meters, but that number comes from cadaver measurement, where the loss of muscle tone lets the intestine stretch; in a living person with tone intact the whole tract is closer to five meters. It is worth pausing on a strange fact: the inside of that tube is topologically outside your body. Food sitting in your stomach has not entered you yet. It has entered a chamber that your body controls but does not yet own. Nothing counts as nutrition until it crosses the single layer of cells lining the intestine — and that crossing, called absorption, is the event the entire tract exists to make possible.

Alongside the canal sit the accessory organs — salivary glands, liver, gallbladder, and pancreas. Food never passes through them. Instead they manufacture secretions and deliver them into the tube through ducts. Losing an accessory organ (a gallbladder, say) does not break the pathway, but it changes how well one part of it runs.

Station by station

In the mouth, teeth shear and grind while the tongue mixes food with saliva into a swallowable bolus. The pharynx and esophagus are pure transport: the epiglottis covers the airway, and a wave of peristalsis pushes the bolus down in a few seconds, whether you are upright or lying flat. The lower esophageal sphincter then opens to admit it and closes to keep acidic stomach contents from washing back up.

The stomach is a mixing vat and a holding tank. It churns food with acid and enzymes into a soupy chyme and then releases it through the pyloric sphincter in small, metered squirts — typically over about two to four hours, longer for meals rich in fat and protein. This metering matters more than it sounds: the small intestine can only work on so much at once, so the stomach's job is as much traffic control as digestion.

The small intestine is where the real work happens. About three meters long in a living person (longer at autopsy, when muscle tone is gone), it has three segments: the short duodenum, where pancreatic juice and bile arrive and most chemical digestion occurs; the jejunum, where the bulk of nutrients are absorbed; and the ileum, which finishes the job and handles two specialty items — vitamin B12 and recycled bile salts. Chyme spends roughly three to five hours here.

The large intestine (colon) receives what is left. It absorbs water and electrolytes, hosts the densest microbial community in the body, and compacts residue into stool over anywhere from ten hours to two or three days. The rectum stores stool and the anus, with its internal (involuntary) and external (voluntary) sphincters, controls its exit.

OrganMain contributionKey secretionTypical dwell time
MouthMechanical breakdown; bolus formation; starch digestion beginsSaliva (amylase, lipase, mucus)Seconds to a minute
EsophagusTransport by peristalsisMucus onlyAbout 5–10 seconds
StomachChurning; protein denaturation; metered release of chymeHCl, pepsinogen, gastric lipase, intrinsic factor, mucusAbout 2–4 hours
Small intestineMost digestion and nearly all absorptionBrush-border enzymes, mucus, bicarbonate; receives bile & pancreatic juiceAbout 3–5 hours
Large intestineWater & electrolyte absorption; microbial fermentation; stool formationMucus; microbial productsAbout 10–59 hours
Liver & gallbladderAccessory — emulsify fat; process absorbed nutrientsBile (about 500–600 mL/day)—
PancreasAccessory — supplies most digestive enzymes and bicarbonatePancreatic juice (about 1.5 L/day)—
Transit time is a range, not a number. Whole-gut transit in healthy adults commonly spans about 24 to 72 hours, and it varies with fiber intake, fluid, physical activity, medications, sex, and hormonal cycle. A faster gut is not a "better" gut and a slower one is not a failure — there is a wide healthy range. Persistent, unexplained change from your own normal is the finding worth mentioning to a clinician, not the number itself.
Interactive Tap each organ from mouth to colon to see what happens to food there — then switch to Follow a meal and watch one travel the whole tract on a live transit-time slider that recomputes when you change the meal. Open full ↑
Key points
  • The alimentary canal is one continuous tube; its lumen is functionally outside the body until nutrients are absorbed.
  • Accessory organs (salivary glands, liver, gallbladder, pancreas) contribute secretions through ducts but never contain food.
  • The stomach mixes and meters; the small intestine does most digestion and nearly all absorption; the colon reclaims water and ferments residue.
  • Sphincters — lower esophageal, pyloric, ileocecal, anal — are the valves that make one-way, metered flow possible.

Section 2Mechanical and chemical digestion

Two kinds of breakdown

Mechanical digestion makes pieces smaller without changing their chemistry: chewing (mastication), the stomach's churning, the rhythmic back-and-forth mixing of the small intestine called segmentation, and the emulsifying action of bile on fat droplets. Its purpose is surface area. Enzymes can only work at the outside of a particle, so a single large fat globule broken into thousands of small ones is digested vastly faster even though not one chemical bond has been touched.

Chemical digestion changes the molecules themselves. Almost all of it is a single reaction repeated millions of times: hydrolysis, in which a water molecule is inserted across a bond to split one large molecule into two smaller ones. Every digestive enzyme in the body is a hydrolase. Starch, protein, and fat differ enormously in structure, but the logic of taking them apart is identical.

The two kinds of digestion work together and depend on each other. Chewing a nut properly exposes far more surface for pancreatic enzymes; whole nuts swallowed nearly intact deliver measurably less of their fat, which is one reason whole almonds yield fewer usable calories than almond butter made from the same almonds. This is a physiology observation, not a recommendation — both foods are perfectly good food.

What happens to each nutrient class

Carbohydrates. Digestible starch is a chain of glucose units. Salivary amylase begins clipping it in the mouth, is silenced by stomach acid, and pancreatic amylase finishes the job in the small intestine — but only down to short chains and disaccharides. The final cut is made by enzymes anchored in the intestinal brush border: lactase splits lactose to glucose plus galactose, sucrase splits sucrose to glucose plus fructose, maltase splits maltose to two glucoses. Only monosaccharides can be absorbed. Fiber is the exception that proves the rule: human enzymes cannot hydrolyze its bonds at all, so it reaches the colon intact.

Protein. Stomach acid denatures proteins — unfolding them so their bonds are reachable — and activates pepsin, which cuts long chains into shorter peptides. Pancreatic proteases (trypsin, chymotrypsin, carboxypeptidase) continue in the duodenum, and brush-border peptidases finish. The absorbable products are free amino acids plus di- and tripeptides, which have their own transporter.

Fat. Fat is not water-soluble, which makes it the hardest case. Bile from the liver emulsifies large fat droplets into a fine suspension; pancreatic lipase, helped by colipase, then hydrolyzes triglycerides into monoglycerides and free fatty acids. These, with cholesterol and fat-soluble vitamins, are packaged into tiny bile-salt spheres called micelles that ferry them to the intestinal surface. Healthy adults absorb well over 95 percent of the fat they eat.

Alcohol and water need no digestion. Both are already small enough to cross membranes, which is why alcohol is absorbed partly in the stomach and reaches the bloodstream within minutes.

NutrientBroken down toWhere mostlyEnergy yield
CarbohydrateMonosaccharides (glucose, fructose, galactose)Mouth begins; small intestine finishes4 kcal/g
ProteinAmino acids; di- and tripeptidesStomach begins; small intestine finishes4 kcal/g
Fat (triglyceride)Monoglycerides + free fatty acidsSmall intestine (bile + pancreatic lipase)9 kcal/g
AlcoholNot digested — absorbed as isStomach and small intestine7 kcal/g
FiberNot hydrolyzed by human enzymesFermented by microbes in the colonAbout 2 kcal/g at most, indirectly
Vitamins & mineralsReleased from the food matrix; not broken downThroughout0 kcal/g
WaterNot digestedAbsorbed throughout, mostly small intestine0 kcal/g
Energy yields are averages from real measurement, not arbitrary numbers. The familiar 4-4-9 (and 7 for alcohol) are Atwater factors — the heat released by burning each macronutrient, corrected for what is actually digested, absorbed, and excreted. Protein loses about a kcal per gram to nitrogen excreted as urea, which is why protein and carbohydrate land at the same 4 despite different combustion values. Fat's 9 comes from its highly reduced, hydrogen-rich carbon chains.
Interactive Set the grams of carbohydrate, fibre, protein, fat and alcohol in a mixed meal, then step it from mouth to colon and watch each macronutrient come apart at its own point in the tract — with energy and absorption recalculated live. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Mechanical digestion buys surface area; chemical digestion breaks bonds by hydrolysis.
  • Carbohydrate must reach monosaccharides, protein reaches amino acids and small peptides, fat reaches monoglycerides and fatty acids before absorption.
  • Energy yields: carbohydrate 4, protein 4, fat 9, alcohol 7 kcal per gram.
  • Fiber resists human enzymes entirely and passes to the colon — the basis for its distinct effects.

Section 3Enzymes and secretions

Every enzyme has an address and a target

Digestive enzymes are specific: each recognizes one kind of bond and ignores everything else. Learning them is much easier if you file each one under two questions — which cell made it, and which bond does it cut. Their names help. The suffix -ase marks an enzyme, and the stem names the substrate: amylase attacks amylose (starch), lipase attacks lipids, lactase attacks lactose, protease attacks protein.

Protein-digesting enzymes pose an obvious hazard: they would digest the organs that make them. The body solves this with zymogens — inactive precursors. The stomach's chief cells release pepsinogen, which acid converts to pepsin only inside the lumen. The pancreas releases trypsinogen, which is activated only when it meets enteropeptidase anchored to the duodenal lining; trypsin then activates the rest of the pancreatic proteases. Acute pancreatitis is, in essence, what happens when this safety interlock fails and the enzymes activate early.

The three great secretions

Gastric juice comes from the stomach lining. Parietal cells secrete hydrochloric acid, holding the stomach at roughly pH 1.5–3.5 — acidic enough to denature protein, kill most swallowed microbes, and free iron and vitamin B12 from the food proteins they are bound to. The same cells make intrinsic factor, a protein without which B12 cannot be absorbed downstream. Chief cells make pepsinogen and gastric lipase; mucous cells lay down the alkaline mucus blanket that keeps the stomach from digesting itself.

Pancreatic juice is the workhorse: about 1.5 liters a day carrying amylase, lipase, proteases, and nucleases — plus a large load of bicarbonate that neutralizes arriving acid. That neutralization is not optional; pancreatic enzymes work near neutral pH and are destroyed in acid.

Bile is made continuously by the liver (about 500–600 mL/day), concentrated and stored in the gallbladder, and released into the duodenum when fat arrives. Bile contains no enzymes at all. Its bile salts are detergents: one end water-loving, the other fat-loving, so they break fat into fine droplets and build the micelles that carry fat to the absorptive surface. Most bile salts are then reclaimed in the ileum and returned to the liver — the enterohepatic circulation, which recycles a bile-salt pool of only a few grams roughly twice per meal, six to ten times a day.

All of this is choreographed by gut hormones. Gastrin ramps up acid when food arrives. Secretin, released when acid hits the duodenum, calls for pancreatic bicarbonate. Cholecystokinin (CCK), released by fat and protein, triggers gallbladder contraction and pancreatic enzyme release and contributes to the feeling of fullness. The tract is not a passive pipe; it is a sensing organ that measures what arrives and adjusts.

Secretion / enzymeSourceActs onProduct or effect
Salivary amylaseSalivary glandsStarchShorter chains, maltose

Intrinsic factorParietal cells (stomach)Vitamin B12Required for B12 uptake in the ileum
Pepsin (from pepsinogen)Chief cells (stomach)ProteinLarge polypeptides
Pancreatic amylasePancreasStarchDisaccharides, short chains
Pancreatic lipase (+ colipase)PancreasTriglyceridesMonoglycerides + free fatty acids
Trypsin, chymotrypsin, carboxypeptidasePancreas (as zymogens)PolypeptidesShort peptides, amino acids
BicarbonatePancreatic duct cellsAcidic chymeRaises pH so pancreatic enzymes can work

Lactase, sucrase, maltaseBrush border (small intestine)DisaccharidesMonosaccharides
Peptidases, enteropeptidaseBrush border (small intestine)Peptides; trypsinogenAmino acids; activates trypsin
Lactase non-persistence is the human norm, not a disease. Most mammals switch off lactase after weaning, and roughly two-thirds of the world's adults retain only partial lactase activity. Undigested lactose then draws water into the gut and is fermented by colonic bacteria, producing gas and loose stools in some people. Persistent high lactase activity into adulthood is the derived trait — common in populations with a long dairying history. Tolerance is highly individual, and many people with low lactase comfortably handle modest amounts, especially in yogurt or hard cheese. This is a description of variation, not a verdict on any food.
Interactive Tap any digestive organ to see the enzymes it secretes and watch each reaction drawn out from substrate to products, then test yourself with a 17-question matching drill that scores source organ and substrate together. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Enzymes are named for their substrates and each cuts one specific bond type.
  • Proteases are secreted as inactive zymogens and activated only in the lumen, protecting the organs that make them.
  • The pancreas supplies most digestive enzymes plus the bicarbonate that makes them functional.
  • Bile contains no enzymes — it is a detergent, and its salts are recycled via the enterohepatic circulation.

Section 4Absorption — the small intestine's surface

Three levels of folding

The small intestine's absorptive surface is amplified three times over. First, the wall itself is thrown into circular ridges (plicae circulares), which also spiral the chyme so it stays in contact longer. Second, the entire lining is carpeted with villi — finger-like projections about half a millimeter to a millimeter and a half tall. Third, each individual absorptive cell (enterocyte) wears a dense pile of microvilli on its apical face, the brush border. Each level multiplies the level beneath it, so a plain tube becomes a surface orders of magnitude larger.

How much larger, exactly? This is a good place to watch a textbook number get corrected. Older texts assign generous multipliers — about 3× for the plicae, 10× for villi, and 20× for microvilli, roughly 600-fold in all — and arrive at a total "the size of a tennis court" (about 250–300 m2). Careful morphometric measurement has revised both halves of that claim. The measured multipliers are more modest (closer to 1.6×, 6.5×, and 13×, or about 130-fold combined), and the small intestine's total mucosal surface comes out near 30 m2 — about the floor of a small studio apartment. A number repeated confidently for decades is not automatically correct. And 30 m2 is still an extraordinary amount of surface for an organ that fits inside your abdomen.

The brush border is not just surface. It is a chemical workbench: the final disaccharidases and peptidases are embedded in the microvillar membrane, so the last cut and the absorption happen in the same square micrometer. Damage the brush border and you lose digestion and absorption together — which is exactly what happens in celiac disease, where an immune reaction to gluten in genetically susceptible people flattens the villi and produces malabsorption of iron, folate, calcium, and fat.

Getting across the cell

Crossing the enterocyte is not one process but several. Passive diffusion moves small, fat-soluble items down their gradients — fatty acids, some water, alcohol. Facilitated diffusion uses a carrier protein but no energy; fructose enters this way through the GLUT5 transporter. Active transport spends energy to move nutrients against a gradient; glucose and galactose ride the sodium-glucose cotransporter SGLT1, and most amino acids use similar sodium-linked carriers. Di- and tripeptides get their own proton-driven transporter, which is why peptides are often absorbed faster than an equivalent mix of free amino acids.

Fat takes a different path. Fatty acids and monoglycerides diffuse out of micelles into the enterocyte, are reassembled into triglycerides inside the cell, and are then packaged with protein and cholesterol into chylomicrons — too large for a capillary, so they leave by a different door entirely (Section 5).

Absorption also has geography. Iron and calcium are taken up mainly in the duodenum; most sugars, amino acids, and fats in the jejunum; vitamin B12 and bile salts specifically in the terminal ileum; water, electrolytes, and microbially produced vitamin K and biotin in the colon. Losing a specific segment of intestine therefore causes a specific, predictable pattern of deficiency rather than general malnutrition.

SiteChiefly absorbed hereNotes
Mouth & stomachAlcohol, small amounts of water, a few drugsMinimal — not a real absorptive surface
DuodenumIron, calcium, magnesium, some glucoseIron uptake is up-regulated when stores are low
JejunumMost monosaccharides, amino acids, fats, water-soluble vitaminsThe main absorptive workhorse
IleumVitamin B12 (with intrinsic factor); bile saltsHighly site-specific — no backup elsewhere
ColonWater, sodium, potassium; microbial vitamin K and biotin; short-chain fatty acidsReclaims roughly 1–1.5 L of fluid daily
Present in food is not the same as available to you. Bioavailability is the fraction of a nutrient actually absorbed and usable, and it depends on the whole meal. Vitamin C markedly increases absorption of non-heme (plant) iron, while coffee, tea, and calcium reduce it in single-meal studies — though whether ordinary calcium intake meaningfully changes long-term iron status is far less clear. Heme iron from animal foods is absorbed more efficiently than non-heme iron. Fat-soluble vitamins A, D, E, and K depend on dietary fat and bile for efficient absorption, and their uptake falls sharply without them. None of this makes any food better or worse — it just means nutrients arrive in combinations, and food-label numbers describe the plate, not the bloodstream.

Open full ↑
Key points
  • Plicae, villi, and microvilli amplify absorptive area roughly a hundredfold; the best current measurement puts the total near 30 m2, not the older "tennis court" figure.
  • The brush border performs the final digestive cuts and absorption at the same site.
  • Nutrients cross by passive diffusion, facilitated diffusion, or active transport, depending on size, solubility, and gradient.
  • Absorption is site-specific — B12 and bile salts in the terminal ileum, iron and calcium in the duodenum.

Section 5Transport, the liver, and the microbiota

Two exits: blood and lymph

Once a nutrient is inside an enterocyte it must still get to the rest of the body, and there are exactly two routes. Water-soluble nutrients — monosaccharides, amino acids, water-soluble vitamins, minerals, and short- and medium-chain fatty acids — enter the capillary in the core of each villus and are carried by the hepatic portal vein straight to the liver. Fat-soluble products cannot use that door. Chylomicrons are too large for capillary pores, so they enter the villus's central lacteal, a lymphatic vessel, and travel through the lymphatic system to the thoracic duct, which empties into a large vein near the left collarbone.

The consequence is elegant: fat enters the general circulation before reaching the liver, while everything water-soluble is inspected by the liver first. That inspection is called the first-pass effect, and it explains why the liver is the body's metabolic customs house — and why oral medications are often dosed higher than injected ones, since a fraction is metabolized before it ever reaches the rest of the body.

What the liver does with the delivery

The liver receives the entire nutrient stream and buffers it. It stores excess glucose as glycogen and releases it between meals, keeping blood glucose remarkably steady. It handles amino acids, converting the nitrogen of surplus ones into urea for the kidneys to excrete. It assembles lipoproteins to ship fat back out. It stores vitamins A, D, and B12 and the mineral iron. It manufactures bile. And it chemically modifies drugs, alcohol, and other compounds. This is why a meal does not send your blood chemistry lurching: the liver flattens the peaks before the rest of you sees them.

The gut microbiota

The colon houses the densest microbial community known in nature. Best current estimates put the total at roughly 3.8 × 1013 bacterial cells — about the same order as the number of human cells in the body, revising the widely repeated "ten to one" claim, which was never well supported. Their combined mass is only a couple hundred grams, but their metabolic reach is large.

What they demonstrably do: ferment the fiber and resistant starch our own enzymes cannot touch, producing short-chain fatty acids (acetate, propionate, and butyrate) that acidify the colon — and butyrate in particular is the preferred fuel of the colon's own lining cells; synthesize modest amounts of vitamin K and several B vitamins; occupy niches and consume resources that would otherwise be available to pathogens (competitive exclusion); and help train the intestinal immune system. Fermentation also produces gas — a normal by-product of feeding your microbes, not a malfunction.

Here the evidence needs sorting. Strong evidence supports the benefits of dietary fiber itself: large meta-analyses link higher fiber intake to lower cardiovascular mortality and lower colorectal cancer incidence — those two come from prospective cohorts, so the pattern is consistent and dose-related but still observational — while the effects on laxation and on blood lipids and glucose come from randomized trials. Strong evidence also supports fecal microbiota transplant for recurrent Clostridioides difficile infection, a specific medical therapy delivered under specialist care. Moderate and strain-specific evidence supports certain probiotics for reducing antibiotic-associated diarrhea. Even there the picture is mixed: two large randomized trials of probiotics for acute gastroenteritis in children found no benefit, and results do not transfer between strains, doses, or products. Preliminary evidence covers most of the rest: the many reported associations between microbiome composition and obesity, mood, autoimmunity, and metabolic disease are largely observational or from rodent studies, and causal direction in humans is usually unresolved. Commercial "microbiome tests" that promise a personalized diet are far ahead of the science.

What is reliable and practical: diets consistently higher in fiber-rich plant foods — vegetables, fruit, legumes, whole grains, nuts — are associated with more diverse microbial communities and better long-term health outcomes. The recommended fiber intake is expressed as an Adequate Intake of about 14 g per 1,000 kcal, which works out to roughly 25 g/day for adult women and 38 g/day for adult men aged 19–50, and somewhat lower after age 50 (about 21 and 30 g/day). Most adults consume around half of that. Increasing fiber gradually, with adequate fluid, is generally better tolerated than a sudden jump.

First destinationLiver, via the hepatic portal veinThoracic duct, then a vein near the left collarbone
Liver first pass?Yes — screened and processed before general circulationNo — bypasses the liver initially
SpeedFast, minutesSlower — blood triglycerides peak roughly 2–4 hours after a fatty meal
Judge microbiome claims by what kind of study is behind them. "People with condition X have a different gut flora" is an association, and the difference may be a consequence of the condition (or of the diet that accompanies it) rather than its cause. Ask whether the finding came from humans or mice, whether it was randomized, and whether the specific strain and dose were tested. This is one of the fastest-moving areas in nutrition science and one of the most heavily marketed — a combination that calls for interest and patience in equal measure. Nothing here is individualized medical advice; digestive symptoms that persist deserve a clinician, not a supplement aisle.
Interactive Build a meal and watch each absorbed nutrient pick its road out of the villus — sugars, amino acids, short-chain fats and water-soluble vitamins into the blood capillary and on to the liver, while long-chain fat rebuilt into chylomicrons leaves by the lacteal for the thoracic duct — with the energy on each route recalculated live. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Water-soluble nutrients leave via the hepatic portal vein to the liver; fats leave as chylomicrons through the lymphatic system and bypass the first pass.
  • The liver buffers the nutrient stream — storing glycogen, making urea, building lipoproteins, storing vitamins and iron, and making bile.
  • The microbiota ferments fiber into short-chain fatty acids, makes some vitamin K and B vitamins, and resists pathogen colonization.
  • Fiber's health benefits are strongly supported; most specific microbiome–disease claims remain preliminary.

The chapter in one breath

  • The GI tract is one continuous tube whose lumen is functionally outside the body; accessory organs add secretions without ever holding food.
  • The stomach mixes and meters, the small intestine does most digestion and nearly all absorption, and the colon reclaims water and ferments residue.
  • Mechanical digestion creates surface area; chemical digestion is hydrolysis, and every digestive enzyme is a hydrolase.
  • Carbohydrate must become monosaccharides, protein amino acids and small peptides, fat monoglycerides and fatty acids; yields are 4, 4, and 9 kcal/g, with alcohol at 7.
  • The pancreas supplies most enzymes plus neutralizing bicarbonate; bile holds no enzymes and works as a detergent, recycled through the enterohepatic circulation.
  • Plicae, villi, and microvilli amplify surface roughly a hundredfold to about 30 m2, and absorption is site-specific — B12 and bile salts only in the terminal ileum.
  • Water-soluble nutrients go to the liver by the portal vein for a first pass; fat enters the lymph as chylomicrons and skips it.
  • The microbiota ferments fiber into short-chain fatty acids; fiber's benefits are well established, while most microbiome–disease claims are still preliminary.

ReferenceWords worth knowing

These are the terms this chapter leans on. Skim them now to prime the vocabulary, then come back whenever a word in the text refuses to hold still.

Absorption
The movement of digested nutrients across the intestinal lining into blood or lymph — the moment food actually enters the body.
Bile
A liver secretion, stored in the gallbladder, that emulsifies fat and forms micelles; it contains bile salts, cholesterol, and pigments but no enzymes.
Bioavailability
The proportion of a nutrient in food that is actually absorbed and available for use, which depends on the food matrix and the rest of the meal.
Bolus
The soft, chewed, saliva-moistened mass of food that is swallowed.
Brush border
The dense layer of microvilli on the apical surface of enterocytes, carrying the final digestive enzymes and the absorptive transporters.
Chylomicron
A lipoprotein particle assembled in the enterocyte to carry absorbed fat; too large for capillaries, so it exits via the lymph.
Chyme
The semi-liquid mixture of partly digested food and gastric secretions released from the stomach into the duodenum.
Emulsification
The mechanical breaking of large fat globules into a fine suspension of small droplets, increasing the surface available to lipase.
Enterocyte
An absorptive cell of the small intestinal lining.
Enterohepatic circulation
The recycling loop in which bile salts are reabsorbed in the ileum and returned to the liver for reuse.
First-pass effect
The liver's processing of absorbed substances arriving by the hepatic portal vein before they reach the general circulation.
Hepatic portal vein
The vessel that carries nutrient-rich blood from the intestines directly to the liver.
Hydrolysis
The reaction that splits a bond by adding water; the chemical basis of essentially all nutrient digestion.
Intrinsic factor
A protein from the stomach's parietal cells that is required for vitamin B12 absorption in the terminal ileum.
Lacteal
The lymphatic vessel in the core of each villus that receives chylomicrons.
Micelle
A tiny bile-salt sphere that ferries fatty acids, monoglycerides, cholesterol, and fat-soluble vitamins to the absorptive surface.
Microbiota (gut)
The community of microorganisms living in the digestive tract, most densely in the colon.
Peristalsis
The wave of muscular contraction that propels contents forward along the GI tract.
Segmentation
Rhythmic non-propulsive contractions of the small intestine that mix chyme with secretions and expose it to the absorptive surface.
Short-chain fatty acid (SCFA)
Acetate, propionate, or butyrate — produced when colonic bacteria ferment fiber and resistant starch; butyrate is the colonocyte's preferred fuel.
Villi
Finger-like projections of the small intestinal lining that greatly increase absorptive surface area.
Zymogen
An inactive enzyme precursor, such as pepsinogen or trypsinogen, activated only after it is safely outside the cell that made it.

Check yourself

1. Explain the claim that food in your stomach is "not yet inside your body." Why does this framing matter?
The lumen of the GI tract is continuous with the outside world, so material in it is topologically outside the body. A nutrient only enters the body when it crosses the intestinal lining during absorption. This matters because a nutrient can be present in a food, survive digestion, and still never be absorbed — the difference between what is on the label and what is bioavailable.
2. Distinguish mechanical from chemical digestion and give two examples of each.
Mechanical digestion makes pieces smaller without changing chemistry; chemical digestion breaks covalent bonds. Mechanical: chewing, gastric churning, segmentation, emulsification by bile. Chemical: salivary amylase splitting starch, pepsin cutting protein, pancreatic lipase splitting triglycerides. Mechanical digestion exists to give chemical digestion more surface to work on.
3. State the energy yield of carbohydrate, protein, fat, and alcohol, and explain why protein yields the same as carbohydrate.
Carbohydrate 4 kcal/g, protein 4 kcal/g, fat 9 kcal/g, alcohol 7 kcal/g. These are Atwater factors — heat of combustion adjusted for digestibility and losses. Protein's raw combustion value is higher than carbohydrate's, but roughly a kilocalorie per gram is lost because its nitrogen is excreted as urea rather than oxidized, landing it at the same 4.
4. A person has had part of the terminal ileum surgically removed. Which two absorption problems are most predictable, and why?
Vitamin B12 malabsorption and loss of bile salts. Both are absorbed almost exclusively in the terminal ileum, with no alternative site. B12 depletion can eventually cause megaloblastic anemia and neurological injury; bile-salt loss impairs the enterohepatic circulation and can cause fat malabsorption with reduced uptake of vitamins A, D, E, and K. Absorption is site-specific, so losing a segment causes a specific pattern rather than general malnutrition.
5. Why are pancreatic proteases secreted as zymogens, and what activates the cascade?
Because active proteases would digest the pancreas itself. Trypsinogen is released inactive and converted to trypsin only when it encounters enteropeptidase anchored to the duodenal brush border; trypsin then activates chymotrypsinogen and procarboxypeptidase. Acute pancreatitis is essentially premature activation inside the gland.
6. Bile is essential for fat digestion, yet bile contains no digestive enzymes. Resolve the apparent contradiction.
Bile salts are detergents, not enzymes — they work mechanically. Having both a water-soluble and a fat-soluble end, they break large fat globules into fine droplets (emulsification) and form micelles that ferry lipid products to the absorptive surface. The actual bond-breaking is done by pancreatic lipase, which can only act on the vastly increased surface bile creates.
7. Trace the three levels of folding in the small intestine and state the best current estimate of total absorptive surface area.
Plicae circulares, then villi, then the microvilli of the brush border — each level multiplying the surface of the level beneath it. Older texts give multipliers of roughly 3×, 10×, and 20× (about 600-fold) and a "tennis court" total of 250–300 m2. Careful morphometric measurement finds smaller multipliers — nearer 1.6×, 6.5×, and 13×, about 130-fold combined — and a total near 30 m2. It is a useful example of a confidently repeated number being revised by better measurement.
8. Compare the absorption of glucose and fructose across the enterocyte.
Glucose uses active transport (the sodium-glucose cotransporter SGLT1); fructose uses facilitated diffusion (GLUT5). Active transport spends energy and can move glucose against its concentration gradient, while facilitated diffusion needs a carrier but no energy and cannot move fructose uphill. Both exit the cell into the capillary via GLUT2.
9. Why do absorbed fats take a different exit route than absorbed sugars, and what is the functional consequence?
Fat is repackaged into chylomicrons, which are too large to enter capillary pores, so they leave through the central lacteal into the lymphatic system. Sugars and amino acids enter the capillary and go straight to the liver via the hepatic portal vein. The consequence is that fat reaches the general circulation before any liver first-pass processing, while water-soluble nutrients are screened by the liver first.
10. Name four things the liver does with the nutrient stream arriving from the intestine.
Any four: stores excess glucose as glycogen and releases it between meals; converts surplus amino-acid nitrogen to urea for excretion; assembles lipoproteins to export fat; stores vitamins A, D, and B12 and iron; manufactures bile; metabolizes drugs and alcohol. Collectively these buffer the nutrient surge so the rest of the body sees a steady supply rather than a spike.
11. Human enzymes cannot digest fiber. Explain how fiber still affects the body, and what the colonic bacteria make from it.
Fiber passes intact to the colon, where bacteria ferment much of it into short-chain fatty acids — acetate, propionate, and butyrate.
