Butyrate is the preferred fuel of colonocytes; SCFAs also acidify the colon. Fiber additionally adds bulk and holds water, supporting laxation. Recommended intake is an Adequate Intake of about 14 g per 1,000 kcal — roughly 25 g/day for adult women and 38 g/day for adult men aged 19–50, lower after 50.
12. A supplement is advertised as "rebalancing your microbiome to improve mood and metabolism." How should you evaluate that claim?
Skeptically — most microbiome-to-disease links in humans are associations, not demonstrated causes. Ask whether the evidence is from humans or rodents, whether it was randomized and controlled, and whether the exact strain and dose were tested, since probiotic effects are strain-specific and do not generalize across products. Genuinely strong evidence exists for fiber-rich dietary patterns and for fecal microbiota transplant in recurrent C. difficile infection; moderate strain-specific evidence supports some probiotics for antibiotic-associated diarrhea. Broad mood-and-metabolism claims remain preliminary, and persistent symptoms warrant a clinician rather than a supplement.
Nutrition · Chapter 3

Carbohydrates

Carbohydrates supply most of the world's dietary energy, and they do it at a steady 4 kilocalories per gram — the same as protein, and well under fat's 9. But "carbohydrate" is not one thing: it covers the single sugar molecules in fruit, the starch in a potato, and the fiber in a bean that your own enzymes cannot touch at all. Those forms behave very differently once they reach the gut, and the differences explain why two foods with identical carbohydrate grams can have very different effects on digestion, blood glucose, and long-term health. This chapter follows carbohydrate from its chemical structure through digestion and absorption into the bloodstream, then out to the evidence on how much we need and what the research actually supports. Along the way, resist the urge to sort foods into "good" and "bad" — the physiology is far more interesting than the moralizing.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Classify carbohydrates as monosaccharides, disaccharides, or polysaccharides, and name the common members of each.
  • State the energy yield of carbohydrate and compare it with protein, fat, and alcohol.
  • Distinguish soluble from insoluble fiber and describe what each does in the gut.
  • Trace starch from the mouth to the portal vein, naming the enzymes and transporters at each step.
  • Explain how insulin and glucagon hold blood glucose within a narrow range.
  • Define glycemic index and glycemic load, and state the limits of both.
  • Cite the RDA and AMDR for carbohydrate and the AI for fiber, with the population and units for each.
  • Describe what the evidence does — and does not — show about added sugars and type 2 diabetes risk.

Section 1Types of carbohydrates

One building block, many arrangements

Every dietary carbohydrate is built from small sugar units, and the name is a fossil of nineteenth-century chemistry: carbo (carbon) plus hydrate (water), because these molecules carry carbon, hydrogen, and oxygen in roughly a 1:2:1 ratio. What separates a spoonful of table sugar from a slice of bread is not the atoms but the architecture — how many sugar units are strung together and how the links between them are angled. That architecture decides whether your digestive enzymes can take the molecule apart, and that single fact drives most of this chapter.

Carbohydrates that your small intestine can break down and absorb yield 4 kilocalories per gram. Hold that number next to the others: protein also yields 4 kcal/g, fat yields 9 kcal/g, and alcohol yields 7 kcal/g. Carbohydrate is not an unusually dense fuel; it is simply the most abundant one in most food supplies.

Monosaccharides and disaccharides — the sugars

A monosaccharide is a single sugar unit and cannot be broken down further by digestion. Three matter nutritionally. Glucose is the body's reference fuel, the sugar that circulates in blood and the one the brain relies on most heavily. Fructose, found in fruit and honey, is the sweetest of the common sugars and is handled largely by the liver. Galactose rarely appears free in food; it arrives bound up in milk sugar.

A disaccharide is two monosaccharides joined together. Sucrose (glucose + fructose) is table sugar, and chemically it is identical whether it came from cane, beet, or a fruit. Lactose (glucose + galactose) is the sugar of milk. Maltose (glucose + glucose) appears mostly as an intermediate when starch is being digested or grain is being malted. Notice that all six of these — three monosaccharides and three disaccharides — are what everyday language calls "sugars," and every one of them ends up as monosaccharides before it can cross the intestinal wall.

Polysaccharides — starch, glycogen, and fiber

String hundreds or thousands of glucose units together and you get a polysaccharide. Starch is the plant storage form and comes in two shapes: amylose, a long straight chain, and amylopectin, a branched chain. Branching matters, because enzymes attack chain ends, and a branched molecule offers many more ends at once — which is part of why amylopectin-rich foods digest faster than amylose-rich ones. Glycogen is the animal storage form, even more highly branched, and it is stored in your liver (roughly 100 g) and muscle (roughly 400 g); it is not a meaningful source of carbohydrate in food, because it degrades quickly after slaughter.

Fiber is the third group, and it is defined by what your body cannot do. Cellulose is also a straight chain of glucose — the same monomer as starch — but the bonds joining the units are oriented differently (beta rather than alpha linkages), and human digestive enzymes have no key for that lock. Cellulose therefore passes into the colon intact. That one geometric difference is the entire reason lettuce and bread behave so unlike each other.

ClassMembersBuilt fromCommon food sources
MonosaccharidesGlucose, fructose, galactoseOne sugar unitFruit, honey, some vegetables; glucose in syrups
DisaccharidesSucrose, lactose, maltoseTwo sugar unitsTable sugar, milk and yogurt, malted grains
OligosaccharidesRaffinose, stachyose, fructans3–10 unitsLegumes, onions, wheat — largely fermented, not digested
Digestible polysaccharidesAmylose, amylopectin (starch); glycogenHundreds to thousands of glucose units, alpha bondsGrains, potatoes, legumes, roots
Fiber polysaccharidesCellulose, hemicellulose, pectin, beta-glucan, gumsMany units, beta or otherwise indigestible bondsWhole grains, vegetables, fruit, legumes, seeds
"Simple" and "complex" are blunt instruments. The old split — simple sugars bad, complex carbohydrates good — does not survive contact with the data. Finely milled starch in a cracker is chemically "complex" yet digests very fast, while the sugars in an intact apple arrive wrapped in fiber and water and behave quite differently from the same grams of sugar in a glass. What predicts a food's behavior is its physical structure and the company it keeps, not the number of sugar units in the molecule.
Interactive Climb the carbohydrate ladder from single sugar rings to starch, glycogen and cellulose — see every Haworth structure and glycosidic bond, then build a chain of your own and watch the formula, molar mass, particle count and digestibility recompute. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Digestible carbohydrate yields 4 kcal/g; protein 4, fat 9, alcohol 7.
  • Monosaccharides (glucose, fructose, galactose) are single units; disaccharides (sucrose, lactose, maltose) are pairs.
  • Polysaccharides include digestible starch and glycogen (alpha bonds) and indigestible fiber (beta and other bonds).
  • Every absorbable carbohydrate must be reduced to a monosaccharide before it can cross the intestinal wall.

Section 2Fiber

Two behaviors: soluble and insoluble

Dietary fiber is carbohydrate (plus the non-carbohydrate polymer lignin) that resists digestion in the small intestine. Nutrition scientists sort it by how it behaves in water, and while the categories overlap in real food, they predict real physiological differences.

Soluble fiber dissolves or disperses in water and often forms a gel. Pectin in apples and citrus, beta-glucan in oats and barley, gums, inulin, and psyllium all belong here. The category is not uniform, though, and the split that matters physiologically is viscosity: beta-glucan, pectin, guar gum, and psyllium thicken, while inulin dissolves without gelling. Where a gel does form, it slows gastric emptying and slows the rate at which glucose is absorbed, which blunts the rise in blood glucose after a meal. Viscous soluble fibers also bind bile acids and carry them out in the stool, forcing the liver to pull cholesterol from circulation to make replacements — the mechanism behind the well-replicated finding that oat beta-glucan and psyllium modestly lower LDL cholesterol. That specific effect is strong evidence, reproduced across many controlled trials.

Insoluble fiber does not dissolve. Cellulose, most hemicelluloses, and lignin are the classic examples, and they concentrate in the bran layers of grains, in vegetable stalks, and in seeds and skins. Insoluble fiber works mechanically: it holds water, adds bulk to stool, and speeds transit through the colon. Low total fiber intake is commonly associated with harder, slower-moving stool. Worth noting, though, that the trial evidence for relieving established constipation is actually strongest for viscous soluble fiber such as psyllium; the neat textbook pairing of "insoluble fiber equals regularity" describes the mechanism better than it describes the outcome data.

What the colon does with it

Fiber is not inert once it reaches the large intestine. Gut bacteria ferment much of the soluble and some of the insoluble fraction, producing gas and short-chain fatty acids (SCFAs) — chiefly acetate, propionate, and butyrate. Butyrate is the preferred fuel of the cells lining the colon, and the SCFAs collectively lower colonic pH and feed the resident microbial community. Because some of this fermentation energy is recovered, fiber is not truly calorie-free; regulatory bodies assign fermentable fiber roughly 2 kcal/g, well below the 4 of digestible carbohydrate.

Resistant starch deserves a mention here, because it is starch that behaves like fiber. Starch trapped inside intact grains and legumes, the high-amylose starch of green bananas, and the retrograded starch that forms when cooked potatoes, rice, or pasta are cooled all escape small-intestinal digestion and are fermented in the colon instead.

How much, and who needs how much

Fiber has an Adequate Intake (AI) rather than an RDA, because the evidence was judged sufficient to set a target but not to define a precise requirement. The AI is anchored at 14 g of total fiber per 1,000 kcal, a level associated in observational studies with lower coronary heart disease risk. Because it scales with energy intake, the gram target differs by age and sex, and it rises in pregnancy and lactation.

PopulationTotal fiber AINote
Men 19–50 y38 g/dayHighest energy needs, highest target
Men 51 y and older30 g/dayFalls with declining energy intake
Women 19–50 y25 g/day—
Women 51 y and older21 g/day—
Pregnancy28 g/dayReflects increased energy needs
Lactation29 g/dayReflects increased energy needs
Children 1–3 y19 g/daySame 14 g/1,000 kcal principle

Actual intake falls far short of these figures — adults in the United States average roughly 15 g/day, well under half the target for younger men. There is no Tolerable Upper Intake Level for fiber; very high intakes cause bloating, gas, and discomfort rather than toxicity, though extremely high intakes can modestly reduce absorption of some minerals.

Fiber intake that rises gradually is tolerated better than a sudden jump. A large, abrupt increase outpaces the gut microbiota's ability to adapt and commonly produces gas and cramping — which is often why people conclude fiber "does not agree with them." Studies of tolerance suggest that spreading the increase over several weeks, alongside adequate fluid, avoids much of that. This is a description of population-level dietary patterns, not a treatment plan; specific gastrointestinal conditions need individualized care from a qualified clinician.
Interactive Follow both kinds of fiber through the stomach, small intestine, and colon, plot nine real fibers by viscosity and fermentability to see where the two-bucket model breaks down, then total a day's grams against the Adequate Intake. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Soluble/viscous fiber gels, slows glucose absorption, and — for oat beta-glucan and psyllium — modestly lowers LDL cholesterol (strong evidence).
  • Insoluble fiber adds bulk and speeds transit; for relieving established constipation, however, the trial evidence is strongest for viscous soluble fiber such as psyllium.
  • Colonic fermentation yields short-chain fatty acids, including butyrate, the colonocyte's preferred fuel.
  • The AI is 14 g/1,000 kcal — 38 g/day for men 19–50 y, 25 g/day for women 19–50 y, 28 g/day in pregnancy; there is no UL.

Section 3Digestion and absorption

Mouth and stomach: a short start, then a pause

Digestion begins before you swallow. Chewing breaks food apart mechanically, and saliva delivers salivary amylase, which starts cleaving the alpha bonds of starch into shorter chains called dextrins and into maltose. This is why a plain cracker held in the mouth slowly turns sweet. The work is brief, though: as the bolus reaches the stomach, gastric acid drops the pH far below amylase's working range and the enzyme is inactivated. No meaningful carbohydrate digestion occurs in the stomach, and none is absorbed there.

Small intestine: pancreatic amylase and the brush border

The real work happens in the duodenum. The pancreas releases pancreatic amylase into the small intestine, where bicarbonate has neutralized the acid, and starch is rapidly reduced to disaccharides and short oligosaccharides. These fragments are still too large to absorb, so the final cut is made by enzymes anchored in the brush border — the microvilli covering the intestinal cells. Maltase splits maltose into two glucose molecules, sucrase splits sucrose into glucose and fructose, and lactase splits lactose into glucose and galactose. Only now, as single sugars, can carbohydrate be absorbed.

SiteEnzymeSubstrateProduct
MouthSalivary amylaseStarchDextrins, maltose
StomachNone active—Amylase is inactivated by acid
Small intestine (lumen)Pancreatic amylaseStarch, dextrinsMaltose, short oligosaccharides
Brush borderMaltaseMaltoseGlucose + glucose
Brush borderSucraseSucroseGlucose + fructose
Brush borderLactaseLactoseGlucose + galactose
Large intestineBacterial fermentationFiber, resistant starchShort-chain fatty acids, gases

Crossing the wall and reaching the liver

The three monosaccharides do not all enter the same way. Glucose and galactose ride the sodium-glucose cotransporter SGLT1, a secondary active transport system that uses the sodium gradient to pull sugar in against its concentration gradient — the same physiology that oral rehydration solutions exploit when they pair glucose with sodium. Fructose takes a different route, entering by facilitated diffusion through GLUT5, which needs no energy and moves fructose only down its gradient. All three then exit the far side of the cell through GLUT2 into the capillaries of the villus.

From there, blood carries the sugars through the hepatic portal vein straight to the liver — a deliberate piece of anatomy. The liver sees the entire absorbed load first and converts most fructose and galactose into glucose or into liver glycogen before the rest of the body ever meets them. What leaves the liver and enters general circulation is largely glucose.

Lactose intolerance is an enzyme shortage, not an allergy. Most humans produce less lactase after early childhood — a normal, genetically programmed pattern called lactase nonpersistence that affects a majority of the world's adults. Undigested lactose then reaches the colon, where bacteria ferment it and draw in water, producing gas, cramping, and loose stool. A milk allergy, by contrast, is an immune response to milk protein and is an entirely different mechanism with different risks. Confusing the two leads people to avoid foods they do not need to avoid.
Interactive Follow starch from the mouth to the bloodstream — salivary and pancreatic amylase, the brush-border enzymes, SGLT1 and GLUT2, the liver's first pass — then set a meal's grams and resistant fraction and watch starch become glucose, kilocalories, and short-chain fatty acids. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Salivary amylase starts starch digestion; the stomach contributes nothing, since acid inactivates the enzyme.
  • Pancreatic amylase plus the brush-border enzymes maltase, sucrase, and lactase finish the job in the small intestine.
  • Glucose and galactose enter via SGLT1 (active, sodium-dependent); fructose enters via GLUT5 (facilitated diffusion).
  • Absorbed sugars travel the hepatic portal vein to the liver, which converts most of them to glucose or glycogen.

Section 4Blood glucose regulation

A two-hormone thermostat

Blood glucose is held within a remarkably narrow band. In a person without diabetes, fasting plasma glucose sits below 100 mg/dL (5.6 mmol/L) and rarely exceeds about 140 mg/dL even after a large meal. The control system is a classic negative feedback loop run from the islets of Langerhans in the pancreas.

When glucose rises after a meal, beta cells release insulin. Insulin is the storage signal: it prompts muscle and adipose cells to move GLUT4 transporters to their surfaces so glucose can enter, it drives the liver and muscle to build glycogen, and it switches off the liver's own glucose production. When glucose falls — between meals, overnight, during exercise — alpha cells release glucagon, which does the opposite: it breaks liver glycogen back down (glycogenolysis) and, as that store runs low, drives the liver to manufacture new glucose from amino acids and glycerol (gluconeogenesis). Epinephrine, cortisol, and growth hormone reinforce glucagon's side of the ledger during stress.

 InsulinGlucagon
SourcePancreatic beta cellsPancreatic alpha cells
TriggerRising blood glucose (after eating)Falling blood glucose (fasting, exercise)
Net effectLowers blood glucoseRaises blood glucose
Main actionsGlucose uptake via GLUT4; glycogen synthesis; fat and protein synthesisGlycogenolysis; gluconeogenesis
Chief targetMuscle, adipose, liverLiver

Where the glucose goes, and what runs out first

The body's glucose reserves are small. Liver glycogen (roughly 100 g) is the only store that can be released back into the bloodstream, and it is largely depleted after 12–24 hours without food. Muscle glycogen (roughly 400 g) is far larger but is locked in place: muscle lacks the enzyme needed to release free glucose, so muscle glycogen fuels that muscle only and never rescues blood glucose. Beyond those stores, the brain's substantial daily glucose requirement — on the order of 120 g — is met by gluconeogenesis, which consumes body protein, and increasingly by ketone bodies as fasting continues. Adequate dietary carbohydrate spares that protein, which is the physiological argument behind the carbohydrate RDA you will meet in the next section.

Glycemic index and glycemic load

The glycemic index (GI) ranks a food by how much it raises blood glucose over two hours compared with a reference (pure glucose = 100), using portions matched for 50 g of available carbohydrate. Values of 55 or below are called low, 56–69 medium, and 70 or above high. Because GI ignores portion size — watermelon has a high GI but very little carbohydrate per slice — the glycemic load (GL) was introduced: GI multiplied by the grams of available carbohydrate in an actual serving, divided by 100. A GL of 10 or below is low; 20 or above is high.

Both tools are genuinely useful for understanding why foods differ, and low-GI dietary patterns show modest benefits for post-meal glucose in controlled trials. But treat published values cautiously. GI varies substantially between individuals eating the identical food, and within a food it shifts with ripeness, particle size, cooking time, cooling, and whatever else is on the plate: fat, protein, acid, and fiber all lower the glycemic response of a mixed meal. GI also says nothing about nutritional quality — a food can have a low GI and little else to recommend it.

A number on a chart is not a property of a food. Pasta cooked al dente has a lower GI than the same pasta boiled soft; a cooled potato has a lower GI than a hot one, because some starch has retrograded into resistant starch. Published GI values are population averages measured on foods eaten alone, and almost nobody eats that way. Use GI to understand mechanisms, not to rank your groceries.
Interactive Build a meal from carbohydrate, fat, protein and viscous fiber and watch blood glucose rise and fall in real time as insulin and glucagon respond — then walk the six phases from the fasting steady state to the counter-regulatory return. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Insulin (beta cells) lowers blood glucose by driving uptake and storage; glucagon (alpha cells) raises it via glycogenolysis and gluconeogenesis.
  • Liver glycogen (≈100 g) maintains blood glucose; muscle glycogen (≈400 g) fuels only that muscle.
  • The brain uses on the order of 120 g of glucose per day, met by diet, gluconeogenesis, and eventually ketones.
  • GI ranks a fixed 50 g carbohydrate portion; GL adjusts for real serving size — both vary with cooking, ripeness, and the rest of the meal.

Section 5Carbohydrate and health

The actual numbers

The RDA for carbohydrate is 130 g/day for adults and for children aged 1 year and older, of both sexes. That figure is not about health optimization; it is the amount judged sufficient to supply the brain's glucose needs without forcing reliance on gluconeogenesis. The RDA rises in pregnancy to 175 g/day and in lactation to 210 g/day, reflecting fetal and milk glucose demands. Infants have Adequate Intakes rather than an RDA: 60 g/day at 0–6 months and 95 g/day at 7–12 months.

Separately, the Acceptable Macronutrient Distribution Range (AMDR) for carbohydrate is 45–65% of total energy for adults and children aged 1 year and older. Note what these two numbers are doing: 130 g is a floor for a specific physiological function, while 45–65% describes the range within which populations appear to meet nutrient needs while keeping chronic disease risk low. There is no Tolerable Upper Intake Level for total carbohydrate.

Reference valueAmountPopulation
Carbohydrate RDA130 g/dayAdults and children ≥1 y, both sexes
Carbohydrate RDA, pregnancy175 g/dayPregnant adolescents and adults
Carbohydrate RDA, lactation210 g/dayLactating adolescents and adults
Carbohydrate AMDR45–65% of energyAdults and children ≥1 y
Total fiber AI14 g/1,000 kcalAll ages ≥1 y (gram target varies by age/sex)
Added sugars (US Dietary Guidelines)Less than 10% of energyAge 2 y and older; avoid added sugars under age 2
Free sugars (WHO)Less than 10% of energy (strong recommendation)Adults and children; further reduction below 5% is conditional
Carbohydrate ULNone established—

Added sugars

Added sugars are sugars and syrups put into foods during processing or preparation, plus sugars from syrups and honey and from concentrated fruit or vegetable juices. They are distinguished from the sugars intrinsic to intact fruit, vegetables, and milk. Chemically the molecules are the same; the distinction is about the package they arrive in and about what the food displaces. Regulators find the distinction useful enough that added sugars now appear as their own line on the US Nutrition Facts label.

What is the evidence? Two conclusions are solid. First, added sugars supply energy with essentially no vitamins, minerals, or fiber, so at high intakes they crowd out nutrient-dense foods — a straightforward dilution argument. Second, the frequency and amount of free sugars is strongly and consistently linked to dental caries; this is among the best-established diet-disease relationships in nutrition. Sugar-sweetened beverages are the largest single source of added sugars in many countries, and their consumption is consistently associated with weight gain and with type 2 diabetes risk in cohort studies — a relationship attributed partly to the fact that liquid calories seem to trigger less compensatory reduction in later eating. Claims about specific metabolic harms of fructose at ordinary dietary intakes remain preliminary, extrapolated largely from high-dose feeding studies; whole fruit, which also contains fructose, is consistently associated with lower disease risk.

Carbohydrate and diabetes risk

Type 1 diabetes is an autoimmune destruction of pancreatic beta cells producing absolute insulin deficiency. It is not caused by diet, and no eating pattern in childhood causes or prevents it. Type 2 diabetes is different: insulin resistance in muscle, liver, and fat combined with progressive beta-cell failure. Its risk is shaped by genetics, age, physical activity, sleep, body-fat distribution, and dietary pattern together.

The most robust dietary findings point to carbohydrate quality rather than quantity. Higher intakes of whole grains, legumes, and total fiber are consistently associated with lower type 2 diabetes incidence across large cohorts and long follow-up; higher intakes of refined grains and sugar-sweetened beverages track the other way. Randomized lifestyle-intervention trials show that structured programs combining dietary change with physical activity substantially reduce progression from prediabetes to type 2 diabetes — strong evidence, though those programs change many things at once, which makes it impossible to credit any single nutrient. Very-low-carbohydrate patterns can improve glycemic markers over the short term in trials, but long-term comparative evidence is mixed, and adherence, not macronutrient ratio, tends to predict outcomes.

"Sugar causes diabetes" is not what the evidence says. Eating sugar does not directly damage beta cells or induce insulin resistance the way the slogan implies. What the data support is more careful: sugar-sweetened beverages are associated with higher type 2 diabetes risk, an overall pattern rich in fiber and whole plant foods is associated with lower risk, and much — not all — of the beverage association operates through energy intake and body fatness. Precision here matters, because the slogan version leads people to fear fruit while missing the pattern-level changes that actually track with risk. Nothing in this chapter is individual medical or dietary advice; anyone managing diabetes or prediabetes should work with a qualified clinician or registered dietitian.
Interactive Hunt added sugars through their many aliases — scan eight real-style ingredient lists, decode twenty-two names into the glucose and fructose they actually deliver, and run the teaspoon and percent-of-calories arithmetic on live sliders. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Carbohydrate RDA: 130 g/day for adults and children ≥1 y; 175 g/day in pregnancy, 210 g/day in lactation.
  • AMDR: 45–65% of energy; there is no UL for total carbohydrate.
  • Guidance caps added sugars below 10% of energy (age 2+), with none recommended under age 2; the caries link is strong evidence.
  • For type 2 diabetes, carbohydrate quality — whole grains, legumes, fiber — tracks with lower risk more reliably than total carbohydrate does.

The chapter in one breath

  • Digestible carbohydrate yields 4 kcal/g — the same as protein, less than fat's 9 and alcohol's 7.
  • Carbohydrates are monosaccharides, disaccharides, or polysaccharides; the alpha-versus-beta bond geometry decides whether human enzymes can digest them.
  • Soluble fiber gels and slows glucose absorption; the viscous types — oat beta-glucan, psyllium — also modestly lower LDL cholesterol. Insoluble fiber adds bulk and speeds transit. Colonic bacteria ferment the soluble fraction and part of the insoluble one into short-chain fatty acids.
  • The fiber AI is 14 g/1,000 kcal — 38 g/day for men 19–50 y, 25 g/day for women 19–50 y — and typical intakes are roughly half that.
  • Starch is digested by salivary and pancreatic amylase and finished by brush-border maltase, sucrase, and lactase; monosaccharides enter via SGLT1 (glucose, galactose) and GLUT5 (fructose) and travel the portal vein to the liver.
  • Insulin stores glucose and glucagon releases it; liver glycogen (≈100 g) defends blood glucose while muscle glycogen (≈400 g) serves only muscle.
  • GI and GL describe glycemic behavior but shift with ripeness, cooking, cooling, and the rest of the meal, and vary between people.
  • The RDA is 130 g/day (175 in pregnancy, 210 in lactation), the AMDR is 45–65% of energy, and the strongest health signal is carbohydrate quality — not a verdict on any individual food.

ReferenceWords worth knowing

A quick reference for the terms in this chapter — skim it now, or circle back whenever a word trips you up.

Added sugars
Sugars and syrups added during processing or preparation, plus sugars from honey, syrups, and concentrated juices; distinguished from sugars intrinsic to intact fruit, vegetables, and milk.
Amylase
The enzyme that cleaves the alpha bonds of starch; secreted by the salivary glands and, far more importantly, by the pancreas.
Amylopectin
The branched form of plant starch; its many chain ends allow faster enzymatic digestion than amylose.
Amylose
The straight-chain form of plant starch; digests more slowly and is more likely to retrograde into resistant starch on cooling.
Dietary fiber
Carbohydrate (plus lignin) that resists digestion in the human small intestine and passes into the colon.
Disaccharide
Two monosaccharides joined together: sucrose, lactose, or maltose.
Fructose
A monosaccharide found in fruit and honey and in half of every sucrose molecule; absorbed via GLUT5 and handled largely by the liver.
Glucagon
The pancreatic alpha-cell hormone that raises blood glucose through glycogenolysis and gluconeogenesis.
Gluconeogenesis
The liver's synthesis of new glucose from non-carbohydrate precursors such as amino acids and glycerol.
Glycemic index (GI)
A ranking of how much a 50 g available-carbohydrate portion of a food raises blood glucose over two hours, relative to glucose set at 100.
Glycemic load (GL)
Glycemic index adjusted for the carbohydrate actually present in a realistic serving: GI × available carbohydrate ÷ 100.
Glycogen
The highly branched storage polysaccharide of animals, held in liver (≈100 g) and muscle (≈400 g).
Insulin
The pancreatic beta-cell hormone that lowers blood glucose by promoting cellular uptake via GLUT4 and stimulating storage.
Lactase nonpersistence
The normal, genetically programmed decline in lactase production after early childhood, affecting most of the world's adults; the basis of lactose intolerance.
Lactose
Milk sugar; a disaccharide of glucose and galactose.
Monosaccharide
A single sugar unit — glucose, fructose, or galactose — and the only form of carbohydrate that can be absorbed.
Polysaccharide
A carbohydrate of many sugar units: starch and glycogen (digestible) or fiber such as cellulose (not digestible).
Resistant starch
Starch that escapes small-intestinal digestion — trapped in intact grains, high in amylose, or retrograded on cooling — and is fermented in the colon.
Short-chain fatty acids (SCFAs)
Acetate, propionate, and butyrate, produced when colonic bacteria ferment fiber; butyrate is the preferred fuel of colon cells.
Soluble fiber
Fiber that disperses in water — pectin, beta-glucan, gums, inulin, psyllium. The viscous, gel-forming members slow glucose absorption and modestly lower LDL cholesterol; non-viscous soluble fibers such as inulin do not reliably do either.
Starch
The plant storage polysaccharide, made of amylose and amylopectin and the largest source of dietary carbohydrate worldwide.
Sucrose
Table sugar; a disaccharide of glucose and fructose, chemically identical whatever plant it was refined from.

Check yourself

1. State the energy yield of carbohydrate, protein, fat, and alcohol in kcal per gram, and say which of the four is the most energy-dense.
Carbohydrate 4 kcal/g, protein 4 kcal/g, fat 9 kcal/g, alcohol 7 kcal/g — fat is the most energy-dense. Carbohydrate and protein are identical on this measure; carbohydrate dominates most food supplies because of how much of it is eaten, not because each gram carries more energy.
2. Cellulose and starch are both long chains of glucose. Why can you digest one and not the other?
The bonds joining the glucose units are oriented differently — alpha linkages in starch, beta linkages in cellulose — and human enzymes can only cleave alpha bonds. Cellulose therefore reaches the colon intact, where bacteria may ferment part of it. One difference in bond geometry is the entire basis of dietary fiber.
3. Name the three nutritionally important monosaccharides and the three common disaccharides, giving the components of each disaccharide.
Monosaccharides: glucose, fructose, galactose. Disaccharides: sucrose (glucose + fructose), lactose (glucose + galactose), maltose (glucose + glucose). All six are what everyday language calls "sugars," and all must be reduced to single units before absorption.
4. Contrast soluble and insoluble fiber by physical behavior and by what each does in the gut. Give one food source of each.
Soluble fiber disperses in water, and the viscous members form a gel — slowing gastric emptying and glucose absorption and binding bile acids, which modestly lowers LDL cholesterol (oats, barley, legumes, citrus, psyllium). Insoluble fiber does not dissolve — it holds water, adds stool bulk, and speeds transit (wheat bran, vegetable stalks, seeds and skins). Real foods contain both; the categories describe behavior, not separate ingredients. Note that solubility and viscosity are not the same axis — inulin is soluble but does not gel, and the LDL effect belongs to the viscous fibers.
5. State the Adequate Intake for total fiber for a woman aged 30 years and for a man aged 30 years, and give the underlying rule the values come from.
25 g/day for women 19–50 y and 38 g/day for men 19–50 y, both derived from 14 g of total fiber per 1,000 kcal. Because the rule scales with energy intake, the targets fall after age 50 (21 g and 30 g) and rise in pregnancy (28 g) and lactation (29 g). There is no Tolerable Upper Intake Level for fiber.
6. Why does no meaningful carbohydrate digestion occur in the stomach?
Gastric acid drops the pH below the working range of salivary amylase and inactivates it, and the stomach secretes no carbohydrate-digesting enzyme of its own. Digestion restarts in the duodenum once bicarbonate neutralizes the acid and pancreatic amylase arrives.
7. Name the three brush-border enzymes of carbohydrate digestion, their substrates, and their products.
Maltase splits maltose into two glucose; sucrase splits sucrose into glucose and fructose; lactase splits lactose into glucose and galactose. These enzymes sit on the microvilli of the intestinal cells and perform the final cut, because only monosaccharides can be absorbed.
8. Compare how glucose and fructose cross the intestinal cell, and explain why oral rehydration solutions pair glucose with sodium.
Glucose (with galactose) enters by secondary active transport on SGLT1, using the sodium gradient; fructose enters by facilitated diffusion on GLUT5, without energy. Both leave the cell through GLUT2. Oral rehydration solutions exploit SGLT1: sodium co-transported with glucose pulls water across the intestinal wall with it.
9. A patient reports bloating and cramping after milk. Explain the mechanism of lactose intolerance and how it differs from a milk allergy.
Reduced lactase (lactase nonpersistence) leaves lactose undigested; it reaches the colon, draws in water osmotically, and is fermented by bacteria, producing gas, cramping, and loose stool. A milk allergy is an immune response to milk protein — a different mechanism entirely. Lactase nonpersistence is the normal adult pattern for most of the world's population, not a disease.
10. Contrast insulin and glucagon by source, trigger, and net effect, and explain why muscle glycogen cannot correct a falling blood glucose.
Insulin comes from pancreatic beta cells, is triggered by rising glucose, and lowers blood glucose by driving uptake and storage. Glucagon comes from alpha cells, is triggered by falling glucose, and raises it via glycogenolysis and gluconeogenesis. Muscle lacks the enzyme needed to release free glucose into the blood, so its roughly 400 g of glycogen fuels only that muscle. Only liver glycogen — roughly 100 g — can defend the blood glucose concentration.
11. Define glycemic index and glycemic load, and give two reasons a published GI value may not predict what happens when a person eats that food.
GI ranks the two-hour glucose rise from a portion containing 50 g available carbohydrate, with glucose = 100; GL multiplies GI by the available carbohydrate in a realistic serving and divides by 100. Any two: GI varies substantially between individuals; it shifts with ripeness, particle size, cooking time, and cooling; and fat, protein, acid, and fiber in a mixed meal blunt the response. GI also says nothing about a food's overall nutritional quality.
12. State the carbohydrate RDA and AMDR, including how the RDA changes in pregnancy, then explain what the evidence does and does not support about sugar and type 2 diabetes.
RDA 130 g/day for adults and children ≥1 y, rising to 175 g/day in pregnancy and 210 g/day in lactation; AMDR 45–65% of total energy. Eating sugar does not directly cause type 2 diabetes. What is supported: sugar-sweetened beverage intake is consistently associated with higher type 2 diabetes risk (partly through energy intake and body fatness), and patterns higher in whole grains, legumes, and fiber are associated with lower risk. Type 1 diabetes is autoimmune and is not caused by diet. This is general education, not medical or dietary advice.
Nutrition · Chapter 4

Lipids

Fat has spent decades as the most misunderstood molecule on the plate — first blamed for everything, then declared harmless, and now understood as something more interesting than either story. Lipids are a chemically diverse family united by one shared trait: they do not dissolve in water. That single property explains almost everything about them — why they store more energy per gram than any other nutrient, why your cell membranes are built from them, why four vitamins cannot be absorbed without them, and why your body has to build tiny protein-wrapped delivery vehicles just to move them through your bloodstream. This chapter takes lipids apart structurally, follows them from the fork to the cell, and then looks honestly at what the evidence does and does not say about fat and cardiovascular health.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Describe the structure and dietary role of triglycerides, phospholipids, and sterols.
  • Classify fatty acids by chain length, degree of saturation, and geometry (cis vs trans).
  • Name the two essential fatty acids, give their Adequate Intakes with population and units, and describe deficiency.
  • Trace lipid digestion from emulsification by bile through micelle formation to chylomicron release into lymph.
  • Rank the major lipoprotein classes by density and lipid cargo, and explain what each one delivers.
  • Explain fat's roles in energy storage, insulation and protection, and fat-soluble vitamin absorption.
  • Distinguish what is strongly established about LDL and cardiovascular risk from what is still preliminary.
  • State current population-level intake recommendations for total fat, saturated fat, and trans fat.

Section 1Types of lipids

What makes something a lipid

Carbohydrates are defined by their chemistry and proteins by their building blocks, but lipids are defined by a behavior: they are the biological molecules that dissolve poorly in water and readily in organic solvents. That is a loose definition, and deliberately so — it gathers together molecules that look nothing alike. A stick of butter, the membrane around every one of your cells, the cholesterol in your bloodstream, vitamin D, and the estrogen and testosterone circulating right now are all lipids. What they share is a chemical backbone dominated by carbon and hydrogen with very little oxygen, which is exactly why they are water-repellent (hydrophobic) and, as we will see, why they carry so much energy.

Three families matter most nutritionally. Triglycerides are what people mean when they say "fat" — roughly 95% of the lipid you eat and nearly all of what your body stores. Phospholipids are the structural lipids that build membranes. Sterols are the ring-shaped lipids, of which cholesterol is the famous example. Learn these three shapes and the rest of the chapter falls into place.

Triglycerides: the storage and dietary form

A triglyceride (also called a triacylglycerol) is built from a three-carbon glycerol backbone with three fatty acids attached, one to each carbon, by ester bonds. Think of a capital E: the spine is glycerol, the three arms are fatty acids. The three fatty acids on a single triglyceride are usually not identical, and it is their identity — how long they are, how saturated they are — that determines whether the resulting fat is a firm solid at room temperature or a pourable oil.

This is why terminology in the kitchen and the laboratory differ. Chemically, a fat is a triglyceride that is solid at room temperature and an oil is one that is liquid, but both are triglycerides and both yield 9 kcal per gram — more than double the 4 kcal per gram from carbohydrate or protein. Nothing about being liquid or solid changes the energy content. When triglycerides are broken down for fuel, the glycerol can enter glucose metabolism and the fatty acids are oxidized for energy.

Phospholipids and sterols

A phospholipid is a triglyceride with an edit: one fatty acid is swapped for a phosphate-containing group. The result is a molecule with a split personality — a hydrophilic (water-loving) phosphate head and two hydrophobic fatty acid tails. Drop enough of these into water and they spontaneously arrange themselves tails-inward, heads-outward, forming the lipid bilayer that is the basis of every cell membrane in your body. That same dual nature makes phospholipids excellent emulsifiers, molecules that let fat and water mix; lecithin (phosphatidylcholine), found in egg yolks and soybeans, is the classic food example and is why an egg yolk holds mayonnaise together. Your liver makes all the phospholipid you need, so none of them are dietary essentials.

Sterols look entirely different: four fused carbon rings, flat and rigid, with assorted side groups. Cholesterol is the principal animal sterol. It appears only in animal-source foods — there is no cholesterol in any plant oil, no matter what the label boasts — but you do not need to eat it, because your body synthesizes roughly 800 to 1,000 mg of it daily — much of that in the liver, though nearly every cell can make some — considerably more than most people consume. Cholesterol is structurally indispensable: it stiffens and stabilizes membranes, and it is the raw material for bile acids, vitamin D, cortisol, aldosterone, estrogen, and testosterone. Plants make their own versions, phytosterols, which compete with cholesterol for absorption in the gut.

Lipid classStructureShare of dietary lipidMain roleFood examples
TriglyceridesGlycerol + 3 fatty acids≈95%Energy supply and storageOils, butter, meat fat, nuts, avocado
PhospholipidsGlycerol + 2 fatty acids + phosphate head≈4–5%Cell membranes; emulsificationEgg yolk, soybeans, liver (lecithin)
SterolsFour fused carbon rings<1%Membrane rigidity; precursor to bile acids, vitamin D, steroid hormonesCholesterol: egg yolk, shellfish, organ meats, dairy fat
PhytosterolsPlant sterols/stanolsTraceCompete with cholesterol for absorptionVegetable oils, nuts, seeds, fortified spreads
"Cholesterol-free" on a bottle of vegetable oil is technically true and nutritionally meaningless. No plant oil has ever contained cholesterol — plants do not make it. The claim tells you nothing about the oil that the fatty acid profile on the back of the label would not tell you better. Reading past marketing language to the actual nutrient panel is a skill worth building early.
Interactive Take triglycerides, phospholipids, and sterols apart piece by piece, drop all three into water to watch structure dictate behavior, then total a day's intake and see why one of the three accounts for about 95 percent of the lipid you eat. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Lipids are defined by being hydrophobic, not by a shared structure; the nutritional families are triglycerides, phospholipids, and sterols.
  • A triglyceride is glycerol plus three fatty acids and supplies 9 kcal/g, versus 4 kcal/g for carbohydrate and protein and 7 kcal/g for alcohol.
  • Phospholipids have a water-loving head and water-fearing tails, which builds membranes and makes them natural emulsifiers.
  • Cholesterol occurs only in animal foods, is made in ample quantity by the liver, and is the precursor to bile acids, vitamin D, and steroid hormones.

Section 2Fatty acids

Chain length and saturation

A fatty acid is a chain of carbon atoms with hydrogens attached along its length and an acid (carboxyl) group at one end. Two variables describe any fatty acid you will meet. The first is chain length: short-chain fatty acids have fewer than 6 carbons, medium-chain 6 to 12, and long-chain more than 12. Most fatty acids in food are long-chain, typically 16 or 18 carbons. (Cut-points vary slightly between sources, so expect to see 14 rather than 12 used as the long-chain threshold in some texts.) Chain length is not trivia — it changes the absorption route entirely, as Section 3 shows.

The second variable is saturation, meaning how many hydrogens the chain carries relative to the maximum. A saturated fatty acid has every available carbon bonded to hydrogen and no carbon–carbon double bonds. The chain is straight, so molecules pack together tightly, which is why saturated fats such as butter, coconut oil, and beef fat are solid at room temperature. A monounsaturated fatty acid (MUFA) has exactly one double bond; oleic acid in olive oil is the standard example. A polyunsaturated fatty acid (PUFA) has two or more. Each double bond in the natural cis configuration puts a rigid kink in the chain, so the molecules cannot stack neatly — hence the liquid, pourable character of most plant and fish oils. Double bonds are also chemically reactive, which is why polyunsaturated oils go rancid faster than saturated ones.

The trans problem

Nearly all naturally occurring unsaturated fatty acids are in the cis form, with both hydrogens on the same side of the double bond — the arrangement that creates the kink. In the trans form, the hydrogens sit on opposite sides and the chain straightens out, behaving much more like a saturated fat despite technically being unsaturated.

Small amounts of trans fat occur naturally in dairy and beef from ruminant animals. The large historical source, however, was partial hydrogenation — an industrial process that bubbles hydrogen through liquid oil to firm it up and extend shelf life, converting some cis bonds to trans along the way. This is one of the few places in nutrition where the evidence is genuinely one-directional and strong: industrially produced trans fat raises LDL cholesterol, lowers HDL cholesterol, and is associated with increased coronary heart disease risk in large cohort studies. The regulatory response was decisive. The U.S. Food and Drug Administration withdrew the "generally recognized as safe" status of partially hydrogenated oils, with compliance phased in through 2020–2021, and the World Health Organization has pushed for global elimination of industrial trans fat. Naturally occurring ruminant trans fats occur at far lower intakes and the evidence about them is much less clear — a useful reminder that dose and source matter.

The two essential fatty acids

Your body can synthesize almost every fatty acid it needs, but human enzymes cannot place a double bond past the ninth carbon counting from the acid (carboxyl) end — that is, nowhere in the stretch of chain closest to the methyl end. We can make an omega-9 fatty acid such as oleic acid; we cannot make an omega-6 or an omega-3. That single enzymatic gap makes exactly two fatty acids essential — they must come from food:

  • Linoleic acid (18:2, an omega-6 fatty acid) — abundant in soybean, corn, sunflower, and safflower oils, and in nuts and seeds. It is the parent of arachidonic acid and of many signaling compounds.
  • Alpha-linolenic acid or ALA (18:3, an omega-3 fatty acid) — found in flaxseed, chia, walnuts, canola oil, and soybean oil. The body converts ALA into the longer-chain omega-3s EPA and DHA, but the conversion is inefficient, which is why preformed EPA and DHA from fatty fish and algae are treated separately.

The "omega" number simply counts carbons from the methyl (omega) end of the chain to the first double bond. Because no true RDA could be established, the Food and Nutrition Board set Adequate Intakes (AI) instead, and these differ by sex, age, and reproductive status — always cite the population along with the number.

Essential fatty acidPopulationAdequate IntakeAMDR (% of energy)
Linoleic acid (omega-6)Men 19–50 y17 g/day5–10%
Linoleic acid (omega-6)Men 51+ y14 g/day
Linoleic acid (omega-6)Women 19–50 y12 g/day
Linoleic acid (omega-6)Women 51+ y11 g/day
Linoleic acid (omega-6)Pregnancy13 g/day
Linoleic acid (omega-6)Lactation13 g/day
Alpha-linolenic acid (omega-3)Men 19+ y1.6 g/day0.6–1.2%
Alpha-linolenic acid (omega-3)Women 19+ y1.1 g/day
Alpha-linolenic acid (omega-3)Pregnancy1.4 g/day
Alpha-linolenic acid (omega-3)Lactation1.3 g/day

Essential fatty acid deficiency is genuinely rare in people eating ordinary mixed diets, because linoleic acid is so widespread in the food supply. It appears in specific clinical circumstances: prolonged fat-free intravenous nutrition, severe fat malabsorption, or very-low-fat regimens sustained over months. The classic presentation is a scaly, flaking dermatitis, hair loss, impaired wound healing, and — in infants — growth failure. The biochemical marker is a rising triene-to-tetraene ratio, reflecting the substitution of an unusual fatty acid where arachidonic acid should be.

Fatty acid ratios are less settled than the internet suggests. You will encounter confident claims about an ideal omega-6 to omega-3 ratio. The evidence that total omega-3 and omega-6 intake matters is reasonable; the evidence that a specific ratio is the operative variable is preliminary, and randomized trials have not established a target. Lowering linoleic acid intake to shift the ratio has not been shown to improve outcomes. Treat ratio-based advice as a hypothesis, not a rule.
Interactive Build a fatty acid bond by bond — set the chain length, add cis or trans double bonds, and watch the chain kink, the way five chains stack together loosen, and the melting point fall. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Fatty acids differ by chain length (short, medium, long) and by saturation (saturated, monounsaturated, polyunsaturated).
  • Cis double bonds kink the chain and keep oils liquid; trans double bonds straighten it, so trans fats behave like saturated fats.
  • Industrial trans fat raises LDL, lowers HDL, and is linked to coronary risk — this evidence is strong, and partially hydrogenated oils have been removed from the U.S. food supply.
  • The only essential fatty acids are linoleic acid (omega-6) and alpha-linolenic acid (omega-3); AIs differ by sex, age, pregnancy, and lactation.

Section 3Digestion, absorption, and transport

The problem with digesting fat

Every other macronutrient dissolves in the watery contents of the gut. Fat does not, and that creates a real engineering problem: the enzymes that break down triglycerides are water-soluble proteins that can only work at the surface of a fat droplet. A single large globule of fat has almost no surface area relative to its volume, so digestion would be hopelessly slow. The entire architecture of lipid digestion is a solution to this one problem — increase surface area, then shuttle the water-insoluble products to the absorbing cell.

Digestion begins modestly. Lingual lipase from the mouth and gastric lipase in the stomach start clipping a small fraction of the triglycerides, and the churning of the stomach breaks the fat into coarse droplets. Gastric lipase is relatively more important in infants, whose pancreatic function is still maturing and whose diet is high in milk fat.

Bile, micelles, and the enterocyte

The decisive step happens in the small intestine. Fat entering the duodenum triggers the release of the hormone cholecystokinin (CCK), which does two things at once: it tells the gallbladder to contract and squeeze out stored bile, and it signals the pancreas to release digestive enzymes. Bile is made continuously by the liver from cholesterol and concentrated in the gallbladder between meals. It contains no enzymes at all — bile is a detergent, not a digestant. Its bile salts break the coarse fat droplets into a fine suspension of tiny ones, a process called emulsification, which multiplies the available surface area enormously.

Now pancreatic lipase, working with its helper protein colipase, can do its job efficiently, cleaving triglycerides into monoglycerides and free fatty acids. Pancreatic phospholipase and cholesterol esterase handle the phospholipids and cholesterol esters. These products are still water-insoluble, so bile salts wrap around them to form micelles — microscopic spheres with the lipid cargo tucked inside and the water-friendly surfaces of the bile salts facing out. The micelle ferries its passengers across the watery layer next to the intestinal wall and delivers them to the brush border of the enterocyte, where they diffuse in. The bile salts themselves are not absorbed here; most are reclaimed further downstream in the ileum and recycled back to the liver through the enterohepatic circulation, a thrifty loop that reuses each bile salt many times.

Chylomicrons and the lipoprotein fleet

Inside the enterocyte, the route splits based on chain length. Short- and medium-chain fatty acids are water-soluble enough to pass straight into the capillaries of the portal vein bound to albumin and travel directly to the liver — fast and simple. Long-chain fatty acids, which are the bulk of dietary fat, take the long way. They are reassembled into triglycerides inside the enterocyte and packaged with cholesterol, phospholipids, and proteins into a chylomicron.

A chylomicron is the first of the lipoproteins — spherical transport vehicles with a hydrophobic lipid core and a shell of phospholipids, cholesterol, and apolipoproteins that faces the water. Chylomicrons are too large to enter blood capillaries, so they exit into a lacteal, a lymphatic vessel inside each intestinal villus, and travel through the lymphatic system to the thoracic duct, finally entering the bloodstream near the left subclavian vein. This is why fat, uniquely among nutrients, bypasses the liver's first pass. As chylomicrons circulate, the enzyme lipoprotein lipase anchored to capillary walls in muscle and adipose tissue strips out their triglycerides, and the shrunken remnant is cleared by the liver.

The liver then assembles its own vehicles, and the family is named by density — the more lipid and the less protein, the less dense the particle.

LipoproteinMade byDensityMain cargoJob
ChylomicronSmall intestineLowestDietary triglycerideCarries absorbed dietary fat from gut to tissues via lymph
VLDLLiverVery lowLiver-made triglycerideDelivers triglyceride from liver to muscle and adipose tissue
IDLFrom VLDL in circulationIntermediateTriglyceride + cholesterolTransitional particle; becomes LDL or is cleared
LDLFrom IDL in circulationLowCholesterolDelivers cholesterol to peripheral tissues
HDLLiver and intestineHighest (most protein)Cholesterol + proteinReverse cholesterol transport — returns cholesterol to the liver
Fat malabsorption is a bile-and-enzyme story. Anything that interrupts bile delivery (gallbladder disease, bile duct obstruction) or pancreatic enzyme output (chronic pancreatitis, cystic fibrosis) leaves triglycerides undigested. The result is steatorrhea — pale, bulky, greasy, foul-smelling stools — and, because the fat-soluble vitamins ride along with dietary fat, a predictable risk of vitamin A, D, E, and K deficiency. Recognizing that connection is the payoff for learning this pathway.
Interactive Follow dietary fat through ten stops — bile, emulsification, lipase, micelles, the enterocyte, chylomicrons and the lymph — then compute how far emulsification multiplies surface area and where a meal's fat actually goes. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Fat digestion is a surface-area problem; bile solves it by emulsifying fat, and bile contains no enzymes.
  • CCK triggers gallbladder contraction and pancreatic enzyme release; pancreatic lipase with colipase does most of the work.
  • Micelles ferry monoglycerides and fatty acids to the enterocyte; bile salts are recycled via the enterohepatic circulation.
  • Short- and medium-chain fatty acids go directly to the portal vein; long-chain fatty acids are packaged into chylomicrons and enter the lymph.

Section 4Functions of fat

The densest fuel and the deepest reserve

Fat yields 9 kcal per gram, more than twice the 4 kcal per gram from carbohydrate or protein and well above alcohol's 7 kcal per gram. The reason is chemical: fatty acid chains are almost pure carbon and hydrogen with very little oxygen already attached, so there is far more left to oxidize. That density is a feature, not a flaw. It lets the body bank a great deal of energy in a small mass.

The advantage is even larger than the per-gram number suggests. Glycogen, the carbohydrate store, is hydrophilic and holds roughly three to four grams of water for every gram of glycogen, so its effective energy density is a small fraction of fat's. Adipose tissue stores triglyceride almost anhydrously. A typical adult carries perhaps 1,500 to 2,000 kcal of glycogen but tens of thousands of kilocalories in adipose tissue — the difference between a day's reserve and a season's. Fat is also the dominant fuel at rest and during prolonged low-to-moderate intensity activity, with carbohydrate taking over as intensity climbs.

Two practical notes follow. Because fat is energy-dense, small volumes of fat-rich food carry a lot of energy — useful when appetite is poor, energy needs are high, or a person is trying to gain weight, and simply a fact of composition otherwise. And because fat slows gastric emptying and stimulates CCK, meals containing fat leave the stomach more slowly. Whether that translates into feeling fuller for longer is less settled than it is often stated: per calorie, fat generally tests as no more satiating than carbohydrate and clearly less satiating than protein, and short-term appetite studies disagree with one another. Treat the gastric-emptying mechanism as established and the satiety payoff as preliminary.

Insulation, protection, and structure

Nutritionists distinguish storage fat, the adipose tissue that expands and contracts with energy balance, from essential fat, the lipid built into cell membranes, nerve tissue, bone marrow, and organs, which is not expendable. Both do structural work. Subcutaneous adipose tissue under the skin is a thermal insulator, slowing heat loss to the environment. Visceral adipose tissue packed around the kidneys, heart, and other organs provides mechanical cushioning against impact. Adipose tissue is also an endocrine organ in its own right, secreting hormones including leptin and adiponectin that participate in appetite and metabolic signaling.

Lipids build structures beyond storage as well. Every cell membrane is a phospholipid bilayer stabilized by cholesterol. The myelin sheath insulating nerve fibers is lipid-rich, which is one reason infants and toddlers need proportionally more fat than adults — the AMDR for fat is 30–40% of energy for ages 1–3 years and 25–35% for ages 4–18, compared with 20–35% for adults. And fatty acids are the raw material for eicosanoids, local signaling compounds involved in inflammation, clotting, and blood vessel tone.

Fat-soluble vitamin absorption

Vitamins A, D, E, and K are lipids themselves, which means they dissolve in dietary fat, ride in the same micelles, and are packaged into the same chylomicrons. Eat them without any fat and absorption falls substantially — a salad of carrots and dark greens dressed with an oil-based vinaigrette delivers meaningfully more carotenoid than the same salad dry. Fat also carries the flavor compounds and provides the texture that makes many vegetables palatable in the first place, which is a nutritional argument in its own right.

Because these vitamins are stored in liver and adipose tissue rather than excreted freely in urine, deficiency develops slowly — but excess from concentrated supplements can also accumulate. The values below are for adults; requirements differ for children, during pregnancy, and during lactation.

VitaminAdult reference intakeUpper limit (adults)Deficiency syndromeExcess
ARDA 900 µg RAE/day men; 700 µg RAE/day womenUL 3,000 µg/day preformed retinolNight blindness, xerophthalmiaHypervitaminosis A; preformed retinol is teratogenic in pregnancy
DRDA 15 µg (600 IU)/day ages 1–70; 20 µg (800 IU)/day ages 71+UL 100 µg (4,000 IU)/dayRickets in children; osteomalacia in adultsHypercalcemia
ERDA 15 mg alpha-tocopherol/dayUL 1,000 mg/day from supplementsRare; neuropathy and hemolysis, chiefly with fat malabsorption or in preterm infantsMay interfere with clotting at high supplemental doses
KAI 120 µg/day men; 90 µg/day womenNo UL establishedImpaired clotting, bleedingNo established toxicity from food forms
Very-low-fat eating has a hidden cost. Very-low-fat patterns sustained over time make two things harder: meeting the essential fatty acid Adequate Intakes, and absorbing vitamins A, D, E, and K, which need a fat carrier to ride in. Be careful with the numbers here, though. Frank essential fatty acid deficiency has been documented mainly at near-zero fat intake — fat-free parenteral nutrition or severe malabsorption — not merely below the 20% lower bound of the AMDR, and linoleic acid is common enough in the food supply that most low-fat patterns still clear its AI. The general point is about nutrient delivery, not a verdict on any way of eating: some fat, from whatever sources fit a person's food pattern, keeps the carrier system working.
Interactive Tap ten jobs fat does beyond fuel — membranes, myelin, steroid hormones, the A/D/E/K ferry, insulation and cushioning — then calculate why the body stores energy as fat rather than glycogen, and track a day's essential fatty acids against the AI. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Fat supplies 9 kcal/g and stores energy almost water-free, giving adipose tissue a far larger reserve than glycogen.
  • Subcutaneous fat insulates, visceral fat cushions organs, and adipose tissue also acts as an endocrine organ.
  • Lipids build cell membranes and myelin, and children need proportionally more fat (AMDR 30–40% at ages 1–3) than adults (20–35%).
  • Vitamins A, D, E, and K require dietary fat for absorption, so fat malabsorption predicts their deficiency.

Section 5Lipids and health

LDL, HDL, and what a lipid panel actually measures

A standard lipid panel reports total cholesterol, LDL cholesterol, HDL cholesterol, and triglycerides. It is worth being precise about what those labels mean: LDL cholesterol is not a type of cholesterol but a measure of how much cholesterol is being carried inside low-density lipoprotein particles. Cholesterol is one molecule; the particles carrying it are what differ.

LDL particles deliver cholesterol out to the tissues. When they are present in excess, they can lodge in the arterial wall, become oxidized, and initiate the inflammatory process of atherosclerosis — the plaque that narrows arteries and can rupture to cause a heart attack or ischemic stroke. The evidence that LDL is causally involved in atherosclerotic cardiovascular disease is about as strong as nutrition-adjacent science gets: it converges from prospective cohorts, from genetic studies of people who inherit lifelong high or low LDL, and from randomized trials showing that lowering LDL by several different mechanisms lowers event rates proportionally.

HDL particles run the other direction, collecting cholesterol from tissues and returning it to the liver — reverse cholesterol transport. Low HDL cholesterol reliably predicts higher cardiovascular risk. But here the story diverges in an instructive way, and the distinction is worth holding onto: the observation that HDL is a strong risk marker is well established, while the idea that raising HDL causes lower risk is not supported — multiple large trials of drugs that substantially raised HDL cholesterol failed to reduce cardiovascular events. A marker of risk and a cause of risk are different things, and conflating them is one of the most common errors in nutrition reasoning.

What dietary fat does to blood lipids

The most consistently replicated dietary finding is a replacement effect: what matters is less "how much saturated fat" in isolation than "what replaces it." Controlled feeding trials show consistently that replacing saturated fat with polyunsaturated fat lowers LDL cholesterol, and pooled analyses of randomized clinical trials find a modest reduction in coronary heart disease events alongside it — the LDL effect is the better-established of the two, and the event data come from a relatively small set of older trials. Replacing saturated fat with refined carbohydrate, by contrast, produces little or no benefit. This is why a low-fat product loaded with refined starch is not automatically an improvement, and why the framing "swap toward unsaturated sources" is more useful than "avoid fat."

Several other points deserve calibration. Dietary cholesterol was long assumed to drive blood cholesterol, but for most people the effect is modest, because the liver reduces its own synthesis when intake rises; U.S. dietary guidance dropped the specific 300 mg/day cap and now advises keeping intake as low as practical without compromising a nutritionally adequate diet. There is meaningful individual variation in this response. Omega-3 supplements are a case where enthusiasm has outrun the trial data: eating fish is consistently associated with lower cardiovascular risk in cohort studies, but large randomized trials of omega-3 supplements in general populations have shown little to no reduction in events, and results in specific high-triglyceride populations have been inconsistent between trials. Call that preliminary and contested, not settled. Coconut oil raises LDL cholesterol much as other highly saturated fats do; claims of a unique metabolic advantage are not supported by controlled trials.

Current recommendations, and how to hold them

These are population-level guidelines for general education. They are not individualized medical advice, and they are not treatment for any diagnosed condition — anyone managing a lipid disorder, cardiovascular disease, or a malabsorption syndrome needs care from their own clinician and a registered dietitian.

TargetRecommendationOn a 2,000 kcal dayStrength of evidence
Total fatAMDR 20–35% of energy (adults); 25–35% ages 4–18; 30–40% ages 1–3≈44–78 g/day for adultsConsensus range, not a hard threshold
Saturated fatLess than 10% of calories, ages 2 and older (Dietary Guidelines for Americans)Under ≈22 g/dayStrong for the replacement effect with unsaturated fat
Trans fatAs low as possible; partially hydrogenated oils removed from the U.S. food supplyEffectively near zeroStrong
Linoleic acidAMDR 5–10% of energy≈11–22 g/dayEstablished as essential
Alpha-linolenic acidAMDR 0.6–1.2% of energy≈1.3–2.7 g/dayEstablished as essential
SeafoodAbout 8 oz (two servings) per week for adults—Moderate; based largely on cohort data
Dietary cholesterolNo numeric limit; keep as low as practical within an adequate diet—Effect on blood lipids modest and variable

One last framing note. Fats are not moral categories, and neither are the people who eat them. Butter is not a sin and olive oil is not a virtue; they are different mixtures of fatty acids with different measurable effects on blood lipids, eaten in the context of an entire dietary pattern, a body, a budget, a culture, and a life. The most defensible summary the evidence supports is unglamorous: overall eating patterns that lean toward unsaturated fats from liquid plant oils, nuts, seeds, and fish — and that include plenty of vegetables, fruits, legumes, and whole grains — are associated with lower cardiovascular risk than patterns that do not. No single food, and no single fatty acid, carries that outcome by itself.

Beware the single-nutrient headline. "Study finds X causes heart disease" almost always describes a small effect measured in a specific population, often observational, and always inside a whole diet that changed in more ways than one. Ask three questions before you believe it: What was the comparison? Was it randomized or observational? And has it replicated? Those questions will retire most nutrition headlines on the spot.
Interactive LDL and HDL carry the same cholesterol molecule in opposite directions — follow the five carriers around the outbound and inbound lanes, build a particle and watch its protein share set its density and name, then run the Friedewald math to see a lipid panel split cholesterol by direction of travel. Open full ↑
Key points
  • LDL delivers cholesterol to tissues and is causally linked to atherosclerotic cardiovascular disease — a strongly supported conclusion.
  • HDL performs reverse cholesterol transport and is a good risk marker, but raising it with drugs has not lowered event rates — marker ≠ cause.
  • The key dietary finding is a replacement effect: swapping saturated for polyunsaturated fat lowers LDL and events; swapping for refined carbohydrate does not.
  • Population guidance: total fat 20–35% of energy for adults, saturated fat under 10%, trans fat as low as possible.

The chapter in one breath

  • Lipids are hydrophobic molecules in three nutritional families — triglycerides (about 95% of dietary fat), phospholipids (membranes and emulsification), and sterols (cholesterol and its hormone descendants).
  • Fat supplies 9 kcal/g, against 4 for carbohydrate, 4 for protein, and 7 for alcohol.
  • Fatty acids differ by chain length and saturation; cis double bonds kink chains and keep oils liquid, while industrial trans fat straightens them and clearly raises cardiovascular risk.
  • Only two fatty acids are essential: linoleic acid (omega-6) and alpha-linolenic acid (omega-3), with Adequate Intakes that vary by sex, age, pregnancy, and lactation.
  • Bile emulsifies fat without any enzyme activity, pancreatic lipase cleaves it, micelles ferry the products to the enterocyte, and chylomicrons carry long-chain fat out through the lymph.
  • The lipoprotein fleet runs from chylomicron to VLDL to IDL to LDL to HDL, growing denser and more protein-rich as it goes.
  • Fat is the body's densest energy reserve, insulates and cushions, builds membranes and myelin, and is required for absorbing vitamins A, D, E, and K.
  • LDL is causally tied to atherosclerosis; HDL is a risk marker but not a proven target; the strongest dietary lever is replacing saturated fat with unsaturated fat rather than with refined carbohydrate.

ReferenceWords worth knowing

A quick reference for the terms in this chapter — skim it now, or circle back whenever a word trips you up.

Adequate Intake (AI)
A recommended average daily intake used when the evidence is insufficient to set an RDA; the essential fatty acids have AIs, not RDAs.
Alpha-linolenic acid (ALA)
The essential omega-3 fatty acid (18:3), found in flaxseed, chia, walnuts, canola, and soybean oil; the precursor the body converts, inefficiently, to EPA and DHA.
AMDR
Acceptable Macronutrient Distribution Range — the share of total energy a macronutrient should supply; 20–35% from fat for adults.
Atherosclerosis
The progressive build-up of cholesterol-rich, inflamed plaque within artery walls; the underlying process in most heart attacks and ischemic strokes.
Bile
A liver-made, gallbladder-stored fluid containing bile salts derived from cholesterol; it emulsifies fat and contains no digestive enzymes.
Cholecystokinin (CCK)
The hormone released when fat and protein reach the duodenum; it triggers gallbladder contraction and pancreatic enzyme release.
Cholesterol
The principal animal sterol; a membrane component and the precursor to bile acids, vitamin D, and the steroid hormones. Made by the liver and found only in animal-source foods.
Chylomicron
The lipoprotein assembled in the enterocyte to carry absorbed dietary triglyceride out through the lymphatic system.
Cis fatty acid
An unsaturated fatty acid with both hydrogens on the same side of the double bond, producing a kinked chain; the form found in nature.
Emulsification
Breaking large fat globules into a fine suspension of small droplets, vastly increasing the surface area available to lipase.
Enterohepatic circulation
The recycling loop in which bile salts are reabsorbed in the ileum and returned to the liver for reuse.
Essential fatty acid
A fatty acid the body cannot synthesize and must obtain from food: linoleic acid and alpha-linolenic acid.
HDL (high-density lipoprotein)
The protein-rich lipoprotein that performs reverse cholesterol transport; a strong risk marker whose causal role has not been demonstrated.
Hydrogenation
Adding hydrogen across double bonds to firm up a liquid oil; partial hydrogenation was the main industrial source of trans fat.
LDL (low-density lipoprotein)
The cholesterol-carrying lipoprotein that delivers cholesterol to tissues; causally linked to atherosclerotic cardiovascular disease.
Linoleic acid
The essential omega-6 fatty acid (18:2), abundant in soybean, corn, sunflower, and safflower oils, nuts, and seeds.
Lipoprotein
A spherical particle with a lipid core and a phospholipid-and-protein shell that transports water-insoluble lipids through blood or lymph.
Micelle
A bile-salt sphere that encloses monoglycerides, fatty acids, cholesterol, and fat-soluble vitamins and ferries them to the intestinal brush border.
Phospholipid
A lipid with a phosphate head and two fatty acid tails; the structural basis of cell membranes and a natural emulsifier.
Sterol
A lipid built on four fused carbon rings; cholesterol in animals, phytosterols in plants.
Trans fatty acid
An unsaturated fatty acid whose hydrogens sit on opposite sides of the double bond, straightening the chain so it behaves like a saturated fat.
Triglyceride
Glycerol plus three fatty acids; the dominant dietary and storage lipid, supplying 9 kcal per gram.

Check yourself

1. A serving of food contains 12 g fat, 30 g carbohydrate, 8 g protein, and no alcohol. How many kilocalories does it supply, and how much of that comes from fat?
260 kcal total, of which 108 kcal (about 42%) comes from fat. Fat gives 9 kcal/g (12 × 9 = 108), carbohydrate 4 kcal/g (30 × 4 = 120), protein 4 kcal/g (8 × 4 = 32). Alcohol, if present, would contribute 7 kcal/g.
2. Describe the structural difference between a triglyceride and a phospholipid, and explain how that difference makes phospholipids useful in cell membranes.
A triglyceride has three fatty acids on glycerol; a phospholipid has two fatty acids plus a phosphate-containing head group. That gives the phospholipid a hydrophilic head and hydrophobic tails, so in water the molecules self-assemble tails-inward into a bilayer — the structure of every cell membrane. The same dual nature makes phospholipids good emulsifiers.
3. A student sees "cholesterol-free" on a bottle of sunflower oil and concludes it is a healthier choice than olive oil. Evaluate that reasoning.
The claim is true but uninformative — no plant oil contains cholesterol, because plants do not make it. The label distinguishes nothing between two plant oils. What actually differs is the fatty acid profile, which is on the nutrition panel.
4. Why are most saturated fats solid at room temperature while most polyunsaturated oils are liquid?
Saturated chains are straight and pack tightly together; cis double bonds put kinks in unsaturated chains so they cannot stack neatly. Looser packing means a lower melting point, hence a liquid oil. The same double bonds also make polyunsaturated oils more prone to going rancid.
5. Name the two essential fatty acids, state why they are essential, and give the adult Adequate Intake for alpha-linolenic acid with the population specified.
Linoleic acid (omega-6) and alpha-linolenic acid (omega-3); human enzymes cannot place a double bond past the ninth carbon counting from the carboxyl end, so omega-6 and omega-3 fatty acids cannot be synthesized. ALA AI: 1.6 g/day for men 19+ years, 1.1 g/day for women 19+ years, 1.4 g/day in pregnancy, 1.3 g/day in lactation. These are Adequate Intakes rather than RDAs because the data were insufficient to set an RDA.
6. Under what circumstances does essential fatty acid deficiency actually occur, and what does it look like?
It is rare, appearing with prolonged fat-free parenteral nutrition, severe fat malabsorption, or very-low-fat intake sustained over months. It presents as scaly dermatitis, hair loss, impaired wound healing, and growth failure in infants. The biochemical marker is a rising triene-to-tetraene ratio. Linoleic acid is so widespread in food that ordinary mixed diets easily meet the need.
7. Bile is often called a digestive enzyme. Correct that statement and explain what bile actually does.
Bile contains no enzymes at all — it is a detergent. Bile salts emulsify large fat globules into a fine droplet suspension, multiplying the surface area on which water-soluble pancreatic lipase can work. It is made by the liver from cholesterol, stored in the gallbladder, and released when CCK signals after fat reaches the duodenum.
8. Trace a long-chain fatty acid from the intestinal lumen into the bloodstream, and explain how a medium-chain fatty acid's path differs.
Long-chain: emulsified by bile, cleaved by pancreatic lipase, carried in a micelle to the enterocyte brush border, re-esterified into triglyceride inside the cell, packaged into a chylomicron, released into a lacteal, up the lymphatic system to the thoracic duct, and into blood near the left subclavian vein. Medium-chain: absorbed directly into the portal vein bound to albumin and delivered straight to the liver. Chain length determines the route because shorter chains are water-soluble enough to skip the chylomicron system.
9. Rank chylomicrons, LDL, VLDL, and HDL from least to most dense, and say what each mainly carries.
Chylomicron (least dense, dietary triglyceride) → VLDL (liver-made triglyceride) → LDL (cholesterol) → HDL (most dense, most protein; returns cholesterol to the liver). Density rises as the protein share rises and the lipid core shrinks. IDL sits between VLDL and LDL as a transitional particle.
