10. A person has chronic pancreatitis with reduced pancreatic enzyme output. Predict two nutritional consequences and explain the mechanism.
Steatorrhea (pale, bulky, greasy stools) from undigested triglyceride, and risk of vitamin A, D, E, and K deficiency. Without pancreatic lipase, triglycerides are not cleaved into absorbable monoglycerides and fatty acids, so they pass into the stool — and because the fat-soluble vitamins depend on being carried in micelles with dietary fat, they are lost along with it.
11. Explain the difference between a risk marker and a causal factor, using LDL and HDL as the example.
LDL is causal: cohort data, genetic studies of lifelong high and low LDL, and randomized trials of several different LDL-lowering mechanisms all converge on lower LDL producing fewer cardiovascular events. HDL is a marker: low HDL reliably predicts higher risk, but large trials of drugs that substantially raised HDL failed to reduce events. Predicting an outcome and producing it are different claims, and treating a marker as a target is a common reasoning error.
12. State the current population recommendation for saturated fat, convert it to grams on a 2,000 kcal day, and explain why the food that replaces it matters.
Less than 10% of calories for ages 2 and older — under about 22 g/day on 2,000 kcal (200 kcal ÷ 9 kcal/g). The evidence describes a replacement effect: substituting polyunsaturated fat for saturated fat reliably lowers LDL and, in pooled trial data, modestly lowers coronary events, while substituting refined carbohydrate produces little or no benefit. "Swap toward unsaturated sources" is therefore a more accurate summary than "eat less fat."
Nutrition · Chapter 5

Proteins & Amino Acids

Carbohydrate and fat are made of carbon, hydrogen, and oxygen. Protein adds one more element — nitrogen — and that single addition changes everything it can do. Protein is the only macronutrient that can build a muscle fiber, catalyze a reaction, carry oxygen, neutralize a virus, and hold fluid inside a blood vessel. It also delivers 4 kilocalories per gram, the same as carbohydrate, but the body treats burning protein for fuel as something close to a last resort. This chapter follows protein from a single amino acid to a folded working molecule, through the digestive tract, into the jobs it performs, and finally onto the plate, where the practical questions live: how much, from where, and does quality matter as much as people say?

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Draw and label the parts of an amino acid and explain what the side chain determines.
  • Distinguish indispensable (essential), dispensable, and conditionally indispensable amino acids, and name the nine indispensable ones.
  • Describe the four levels of protein structure and explain how sequence produces shape, and shape produces function.
  • Explain denaturation, list what causes it, and say what it does and does not destroy.
  • Trace protein digestion and absorption from the stomach through the brush border to the portal vein.
  • Give examples of protein's roles in structure, enzymes, hormones, transport, immunity, fluid balance, and acid–base balance.
  • Compare methods of judging protein quality and explain how complementary proteins work.
  • State the adult RDA for protein with its units and population, and describe how needs shift with age, pregnancy, and lactation.

Section 1Amino acids

One backbone, twenty side chains

Every amino acid is built on the same small frame. A central carbon atom — the alpha carbon — holds four things: an amino group (–NH2), a carboxyl group (–COOH), a hydrogen atom, and a fourth attachment called the side chain or R group. Three of those four are identical in every amino acid. Only the side chain varies, and that variation is the whole story: it is what makes one amino acid water-loving and another water-fearing, one acidic and another basic, one small enough to fit into a tight turn and another bulky enough to force the chain to bend around it.

Twenty amino acids are used to build human proteins. The amino group is where the nitrogen lives, and it is the reason protein behaves differently from the other energy-yielding nutrients. Protein is roughly 16 percent nitrogen by weight, which is why laboratories have historically estimated the protein content of a food by measuring its nitrogen and multiplying by 6.25. It is also why the body cannot simply stockpile surplus protein the way it stores glycogen or fat: the nitrogen has to be stripped off and excreted as urea before the carbon skeleton can be burned or stored.

Protein yields 4 kcal per gram. Same as carbohydrate. Fat yields 9, and alcohol yields 7. Protein is not a dense fuel, and the body treats it as a reluctant one — the amino acids in a meal are far more useful as raw material than as calories.

Indispensable, dispensable, conditional

The body can manufacture some amino acids from scratch, rearranging carbon skeletons and moving nitrogen around. Others it cannot make at all, or cannot make fast enough, and those must arrive in food. Nine amino acids are indispensable (the older term is essential): histidine, isoleucine, leucine, lysine, methionine, phenylalanine, threonine, tryptophan, and valine. The remaining amino acids are dispensable — not unimportant, simply synthesizable.

A third category sits in between. Conditionally indispensable amino acids are ones the body normally makes for itself but cannot keep up with under particular circumstances — rapid growth, serious illness, major injury, or an inherited enzyme defect. Tyrosine is the clearest example: it is normally made from phenylalanine, but a person with phenylketonuria (PKU) lacks the enzyme for that conversion, so tyrosine becomes indispensable for them and phenylalanine must be tightly restricted under medical supervision.

CategoryAmino acidsWhat it means
Indispensable (essential)Histidine, isoleucine, leucine, lysine, methionine, phenylalanine, threonine, tryptophan, valineThe body cannot synthesize them; they must come from food
Dispensable (nonessential)Alanine, asparagine, aspartic acid, glutamic acid, serineReadily made from other compounds; no dietary requirement
Conditionally indispensableArginine, cysteine, glutamine, glycine, proline, tyrosineNormally made in adequate amounts, but not during illness, injury, prematurity, or certain inherited disorders

Notice that the branched-chain amino acids — leucine, isoleucine, and valine — are all indispensable. Leucine in particular has drawn research attention as a signal that helps switch on muscle protein synthesis. That signaling role is well described in laboratory and short-term human studies; whether adding isolated leucine or branched-chain supplements on top of an already adequate protein intake produces meaningful long-term gains is not settled, and the current evidence is best called preliminary.

Interactive Sort all twenty amino acids into essential, conditionally essential, and nonessential — then slide a body weight to compute what the nine indispensable amino acids actually amount to in milligrams per day. Open full ↑
Key points
  • An amino acid is an alpha carbon carrying an amino group, a carboxyl group, a hydrogen, and a variable side chain.
  • The side chain alone determines an amino acid's chemical personality, and therefore how a finished protein folds.
  • Nine amino acids are indispensable and must come from food; some become conditionally indispensable during illness, growth, or inherited disorders.
  • Protein is about 16% nitrogen and yields 4 kcal/g — the same as carbohydrate.

Section 2Protein structure

From sequence to shape

Two amino acids join when the carboxyl group of one reacts with the amino group of the other, releasing a molecule of water and leaving behind a peptide bond. Repeat that thousands of times and you have a polypeptide — a long chain whose order of amino acids was dictated, letter by letter, by a gene. That order is the protein's primary structure, and it is not a detail. Change one amino acid and you can change the whole molecule: sickle cell disease arises from a single substitution, valine in place of glutamic acid, at position six of the beta-globin chain of hemoglobin.

A polypeptide does not stay stretched out. Hydrogen bonds form along the backbone and pull local stretches into repeating patterns — the coiled alpha helix and the pleated beta sheet — which together make up secondary structure. Then the whole chain collapses on itself. Water-fearing side chains bury themselves in the interior, water-loving ones face outward, oppositely charged side chains attract, and sulfur-containing cysteines can lock the fold in place with disulfide bridges. The resulting three-dimensional shape is the tertiary structure. Some proteins go one step further and assemble from several finished chains: hemoglobin is four polypeptide subunits clicked together, an arrangement called quaternary structure.

LevelWhat it isHeld together byExample
PrimaryThe linear order of amino acidsPeptide bonds (strong, covalent)The gene-specified sequence of insulin
SecondaryLocal coils and pleats — alpha helix, beta sheetHydrogen bonds along the backboneThe helical rods of keratin in hair
TertiaryThe overall 3-D fold of one chainSide-chain attractions, water-avoidance, disulfide bridgesThe pocket in an enzyme's active site
QuaternaryTwo or more folded chains assembledThe same side-chain forces, acting between subunitsHemoglobin's four subunits

The reason to learn this hierarchy is that shape is function. An enzyme works because its active site is a pocket contoured to fit one particular molecule. An antibody works because its tips are shaped to grip one particular antigen. A membrane transporter works because it has a channel of the right size and charge. Lose the shape and you lose the job, even though every peptide bond is still intact.

Denaturation

Anything that disrupts the weak forces holding a fold together will unravel a protein. This is denaturation, and the usual culprits are heat, acid or alkali, mechanical agitation, alcohol, certain salts and heavy metals, and ultraviolet light. Crucially, denaturation breaks the hydrogen bonds and side-chain attractions that create secondary, tertiary, and quaternary structure — it does not break the peptide bonds of the primary structure. The sequence survives; the shape does not.

You have watched this happen. Egg white is a clear solution of folded proteins, chiefly ovalbumin; heat it and those proteins unfold, tangle with one another, and set into an opaque solid that will not go back. Whipping egg whites denatures them mechanically. Adding acid to milk denatures casein and produces curds. Marinating fish in citrus juice, "cooking" it without heat, is acid denaturation. Because denatured protein is more open and more accessible, cooking generally makes protein easier to digest, not harder.

The body does this deliberately, too. Stomach acid denatures dietary proteins on arrival, unfolding them so digestive enzymes can reach the peptide bonds inside. And it explains part of why certain extremes are dangerous: a very high fever, a large shift in blood pH, or certain poisons can begin to unfold the body's own proteins, which is one reason those states are treated urgently.

Denaturation is not digestion. A fried egg is denatured, not digested — every peptide bond is still there. Digestion is the separate, enzyme-driven work of actually cutting those bonds. Confusing the two is the most common error students make on this topic.
Interactive Turn up heat or turn down pH and watch a folded protein unravel bond by bond — while its peptide backbone, and its calories, stay intact. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Peptide bonds link amino acids into polypeptides; the sequence is the primary structure.
  • Hydrogen bonding produces secondary structure (alpha helix, beta sheet); side-chain forces produce tertiary; multiple chains produce quaternary.
  • A single amino acid substitution can change function — sickle cell disease is the classic example.
  • Denaturation (heat, acid, agitation, alcohol) destroys shape but leaves peptide bonds intact, and usually improves digestibility.

Section 3Digestion and absorption

The stomach: acid first, enzyme second

Nothing happens to protein in the mouth beyond chewing. Saliva contains enzymes for starch and fat, but none for protein, so chewing contributes only mechanical breakdown and surface area. The chemistry begins in the stomach, and it begins with acid.

Parietal cells in the gastric lining secrete hydrochloric acid, dropping stomach contents to roughly pH 1.5–3.5. That acid does two jobs at once. It denatures the incoming proteins, unfolding them so their interior bonds become reachable. And it converts pepsinogen — an inactive precursor released by chief cells — into the active enzyme pepsin. Pepsin then clips the long chains at specific interior points, producing shorter polypeptides. Pepsin is unusual among enzymes in preferring a strongly acidic environment; it is the only major digestive protease that works well at gastric pH.

The small intestine finishes the job

Acidic chyme entering the duodenum triggers two hormones. Secretin prompts the pancreas to release bicarbonate, neutralizing the acid so intestinal enzymes can work; cholecystokinin (CCK) prompts the pancreas to release its digestive enzymes. Those enzymes arrive as inactive zymogens — trypsinogen, chymotrypsinogen, procarboxypeptidase — because a pancreas that secreted active proteases would digest itself.

Activation happens on the intestinal wall. Enteropeptidase, an enzyme anchored in the brush border of the small intestine, converts trypsinogen into trypsin; trypsin then activates the remaining zymogens in a cascade. Trypsin, chymotrypsin, and elastase cut chains at interior bonds; carboxypeptidase nibbles amino acids off the carboxyl end. Finally, brush-border peptidases — aminopeptidases and dipeptidases embedded in the microvilli of the intestinal cells — reduce what is left to free amino acids and short di- and tripeptides.

SiteSecretionActive enzymeWhat it does to protein
MouthSaliva— none for proteinChewing only; increases surface area
StomachHydrochloric acid—Denatures protein; activates pepsinogen
StomachPepsinogen (chief cells)PepsinCuts interior bonds → shorter polypeptides
PancreasTrypsinogenTrypsin (via enteropeptidase)Cuts interior bonds; activates the other zymogens
PancreasChymotrypsinogen, proelastaseChymotrypsin, elastaseFurther interior cuts → small peptides
PancreasProcarboxypeptidaseCarboxypeptidaseRemoves amino acids from the carboxyl end
Small intestineBrush-border enzymesAminopeptidase, dipeptidaseFinal cuts → free amino acids, di- and tripeptides

Absorption and the amino acid pool

Absorption is efficient. Free amino acids cross into the intestinal cell on sodium-dependent carriers, and di- and tripeptides cross on a separate hydrogen-ion-driven transporter, then are broken down to single amino acids inside the cell. Because several carriers exist and each handles a family of chemically similar amino acids, very large doses of a single amino acid can compete with its chemical relatives for transport — one of several reasons that isolated single-amino-acid supplements are approached cautiously. From the intestinal cell, amino acids enter the portal vein and travel to the liver, which decides what to keep, what to convert, and what to release into general circulation. Healthy adults absorb the great majority of the protein they eat; digestibility is typically above 90 percent for animal foods and refined soy products and somewhat lower for whole legumes and grains, where fiber and other plant compounds interfere.

What arrives is added to the amino acid pool, the body's circulating and intracellular supply. That pool is fed from two directions: from food, and from the continuous dismantling of the body's own proteins. This protein turnover is enormous — adults recycle on the order of 250–300 grams of protein daily, far more than anyone eats — and it means dietary protein tops up a system that is already vigorously reusing its own material.

The pancreas ships its knives sheathed. Trypsin, chymotrypsin, and carboxypeptidase are all secreted as inactive zymogens and switched on only after they reach the intestine. When that safety system fails and the enzymes activate early, the pancreas begins to digest itself — the mechanism behind acute pancreatitis.
Interactive Step through the digestive tract and watch one protein chain get denatured and cut into absorbable amino acids — acid and pepsin, pancreatic enzymes, brush-border peptidases, and transport into the blood. Open full ↑
Key points
  • No protein digestion occurs in the mouth; stomach acid denatures protein and activates pepsin.
  • Pancreatic proteases are secreted as zymogens; brush-border enteropeptidase activates trypsin, which activates the rest.
  • Brush-border peptidases finish digestion; amino acids and small peptides are absorbed and carried by the portal vein to the liver.
  • The amino acid pool is refilled by both diet and the constant turnover of the body's own proteins.

Section 4Functions of protein

Building, and rebuilding

The most familiar role of protein is structural. Collagen is the single most abundant protein in the human body: it forms the scaffolding of skin, bone matrix, tendon, ligament, cartilage, and the walls of blood vessels, and it is the framework on which a wound heals. Keratin makes hair and nails and toughens the outer skin. Elastin lets tissue recoil. Actin and myosin are the sliding filaments that produce every muscle contraction. None of this is built once and left alone — skin, gut lining, and blood cells are replaced continuously, and every replacement draws on the amino acid pool.

Growth raises the demand sharply. Infancy, childhood, adolescence, pregnancy, lactation, and recovery from surgery, burns, or serious infection all add new tissue or repair damaged tissue, and all require that more protein be retained than is lost. That balance is described by nitrogen balance: nitrogen in versus nitrogen out. Healthy adults sit at equilibrium. Growing children, pregnant women, and people recovering from injury are in positive balance. Prolonged inadequate intake, severe illness, burns, or immobility push a person into negative balance, where body protein is being lost faster than it is replaced.

Enzymes, hormones, and transport

Almost every chemical reaction in the body is run by a protein enzyme. Enzymes are catalysts: they speed reactions enormously without being consumed, and each one is shaped for its specific substrate. Pepsin, trypsin, lactase, and the enzymes of every metabolic pathway are proteins.

Many hormones are proteins or short peptides — insulin, glucagon, growth hormone, parathyroid hormone, and the appetite signals leptin and ghrelin among them. (Not all hormones are protein; the steroid hormones are built from cholesterol, and thyroid hormones from tyrosine plus iodine.)

Transport proteins move things that could not otherwise travel. Hemoglobin carries oxygen in red blood cells. Albumin ferries fatty acids, calcium, bilirubin, and many medications through the bloodstream. Transferrin carries iron, retinol-binding protein carries vitamin A, and lipoproteins carry fat and cholesterol through watery plasma. Embedded in every cell membrane are protein pumps and channels that move sodium, potassium, glucose, and amino acids across a barrier that would otherwise block them.

FunctionExample proteinsWhat it accomplishes
StructureCollagen, keratin, elastin, actin, myosinSkin, bone matrix, tendon, hair, muscle contraction, wound healing
EnzymesPepsin, trypsin, lactase, metabolic enzymesCatalyze essentially every reaction in the body
HormonesInsulin, glucagon, growth hormone, leptinChemical messengers that coordinate organs
TransportHemoglobin, albumin, transferrin, lipoproteins, membrane pumpsMove oxygen, iron, fats, vitamins, minerals, and drugs
ImmunityAntibodies (immunoglobulins), complement proteinsRecognize and neutralize specific foreign invaders
Fluid balanceAlbumin and other plasma proteinsHold water inside blood vessels by osmotic pull
Acid–base balanceHemoglobin, plasma proteinsBuffer excess acid or base to hold blood pH near 7.4
ClottingFibrinogen → fibrinForm the mesh that seals a damaged vessel
EnergyAny protein, when needed4 kcal/g — used when intake is inadequate or demand is extreme

Immunity, fluid balance, and the acid–base buffer

Antibodies are proteins, and each is shaped to bind one specific antigen. Producing them costs amino acids, which is part of why severe protein–energy undernutrition weakens immune defense and slows recovery from infection.

Fluid balance is a less obvious but clinically important job. Plasma proteins, especially albumin, are too large to slip out of capillaries. Staying inside, they exert an osmotic pull that keeps water in the bloodstream. When plasma protein falls very low — in severe undernutrition, advanced liver disease, or protein-losing kidney disease — that pull weakens, fluid can leak into surrounding tissue, and visible swelling called edema appears. It is worth being careful about how far to push this explanation. It accounts well for the edema of advanced liver disease and protein-losing kidney disease. In severe childhood undernutrition it is only part of the story — the swollen belly and puffy limbs of a profoundly malnourished child were long explained by low albumin alone, and that account is now considered incomplete (see Section 5). What is not in doubt is the observation itself: the swelling is fluid, not tissue, so a child can look filled out and be severely malnourished at the same time.

Proteins also act as buffers. Their side chains can accept or release hydrogen ions, soaking up excess acid or base and helping hold arterial blood pH in its narrow survivable range near 7.4. And when carbohydrate and fat intake is insufficient, protein can be sacrificed for energy — deaminated, with the nitrogen excreted as urea and the carbon skeleton burned or converted to glucose. Adequate carbohydrate intake is described as protein-sparing precisely because it prevents that sacrifice.

Protein is the body's most over-qualified fuel. Burning an amino acid for 4 kcal means dismantling a molecule that could have been an enzyme, an antibody, or a strand of collagen — and it costs the liver and kidneys the work of packaging and excreting the leftover nitrogen. The body burns protein when it must, not when it prefers to.
Interactive Tap through protein's six functional roles — from structure and enzymes to fluid and acid–base balance — then use live sliders to calculate colloid osmotic pressure and a daily amino-acid budget. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Protein builds and repairs tissue: collagen is the body's most abundant protein.
  • Nitrogen balance is positive in growth, pregnancy, and recovery; negative in severe illness, injury, or inadequate intake.
  • Proteins serve as enzymes, many hormones, transporters, antibodies, buffers, and clotting factors.
  • Albumin holds fluid inside blood vessels; when plasma protein falls very low, edema can result — though in childhood undernutrition it is not the whole explanation.

Section 5Protein in the diet

Judging protein quality

Protein quality describes how well a food's amino acid pattern matches human needs, combined with how well that protein is digested. A protein supplying all nine indispensable amino acids in adequate proportions is called complete, or high quality: eggs, dairy, meat, poultry, fish, and among plants, soy and quinoa. Most single plant proteins are incomplete — a term that needs care, because it almost never means an amino acid is absent. It means one or more indispensable amino acids are present in too small a proportion relative to human need. The one in shortest supply is called the limiting amino acid, because protein synthesis can proceed only as far as the scarcest part allows. Grains tend to be limited in lysine; legumes tend to be limited in methionine.

Scoring systems formalize this. The PDCAAS (Protein Digestibility-Corrected Amino Acid Score) compares a food's indispensable amino acid pattern to a reference pattern and corrects for digestibility, capped at 1.00 — egg, milk, whey, casein, and soy protein isolate all reach the cap. The FAO has recommended replacing it with DIAAS (Digestible Indispensable Amino Acid Score), which measures digestibility at the end of the small intestine rather than in the stool and is not capped, so it distinguishes among the high scorers more finely. These are useful research and formulation tools; for a person eating a varied diet with enough total protein, the scores rarely change what they should do.

Complementary proteins are two incomplete proteins whose gaps cover each other — classically legumes with grains. Beans and rice, lentils and flatbread, hummus and pita, peanut butter on bread: each pairing supplies what the other lacks. This is the culinary logic of a great many traditional cuisines, arrived at long before anyone measured an amino acid.

The "same meal" rule was retracted. Early popular writing insisted complementary proteins had to be eaten together at one sitting. The author of that claim later withdrew it, and it is not what the evidence supports. The body maintains a circulating amino acid pool, so proteins eaten across the course of a day complement one another perfectly well. Beans at lunch and rice at dinner is not a problem.

How much is enough — and what about more?

The RDA for protein in healthy adults age 19 and older is 0.8 grams per kilogram of body weight per day, and it is the same figure for men and women. In the DRI reference tables that works out to about 56 g/day for a 70-kg man and 46 g/day for a 57-kg woman, but the per-kilogram figure is the one to remember, since it adapts to the actual person. This RDA is set to meet the needs of nearly all healthy adults; the median requirement (the EAR) is lower, at 0.66 g/kg/day. Needs are expressed differently across life stages, and pregnancy and lactation raise them substantially.

Life stageProtein DRIApproximate g/day
Infants 0–6 moAdequate Intake (no RDA set)~9.1 g/day
Infants 7–12 moRDA 1.2 g/kg/day~11 g/day
Children 1–3 yRDA 1.05 g/kg/day~13 g/day
Children 4–8 yRDA 0.95 g/kg/day~19 g/day
Ages 9–13 yRDA 0.95 g/kg/day~34 g/day
Ages 14–18 yRDA 0.85 g/kg/day~52 g (male), ~46 g (female)
Adults 19+ y, both sexesRDA 0.8 g/kg/day~56 g (male), ~46 g (female) at reference weights
Pregnancy & lactationRDA 1.1 g/kg/day~71 g/day

Two more numbers matter. The AMDR — the Acceptable Macronutrient Distribution Range — places protein at 10–35 percent of total calories for adults, 10–30 percent for children and adolescents 4–18 years, and 5–20 percent for children 1–3 years. And there is no Tolerable Upper Intake Level for protein, not because any amount is known to be safe but because the data were judged insufficient to set one.

Certain groups are studied as needing more than the RDA. Joint position statements from sports nutrition and dietetics organizations describe intakes of roughly 1.2–2.0 g/kg/day for athletes in heavy training, and several expert panels have proposed roughly 1.0–1.2 g/kg/day for adults over about 65 to help offset age-related loss of muscle mass. Both figures come from expert consensus and mostly short-term trials rather than from the DRI process, so treat them as reasonable professional guidance rather than as established requirements. In the United States and most high-income countries, average protein intake already comfortably exceeds the RDA, and outright protein deficiency in an otherwise well-fed adult is uncommon.

What about excess? Several old concerns have not held up well. The idea that high protein intake damages healthy kidneys is not supported by the available evidence in people with normal kidney function — though that evidence is mostly short- and medium-term trials plus observational cohorts, so it is better read as an absence of demonstrated harm than as proof of long-term safety at any intake. Protein restriction in established kidney disease is a separate, clinician-managed matter and not a general recommendation. The concern that high protein leaches calcium from bone has likewise weakened; current evidence suggests protein is neutral or modestly favorable for bone when calcium intake is adequate. What remains reasonable to say is more modest: extremely high protein intakes displace other foods and their nutrients, they are not known to confer added benefit once needs are met, and the International Agency for Research on Cancer has classified processed meat as carcinogenic to humans and red meat as probably carcinogenic, based mainly on colorectal cancer. Those two labels are not the same strength of claim: the processed-meat classification rests on evidence the agency judged sufficient, while the red-meat one rests on evidence it judged limited. Both come from observational studies, the absolute risk attached to a serving is small, and neither says anything about protein itself — the suspicion falls on processing, preservatives, and cooking chemistry, not on the amino acids. Protein deficiency at the severe end is a different matter entirely: marasmus is severe wasting from inadequate energy and protein together, while kwashiorkor presents with edema, an enlarged fatty liver, and skin and hair changes. Kwashiorkor was long attributed to protein deficiency with adequate energy, but that explanation is now considered incomplete — oxidative stress, aflatoxin exposure, and gut microbiome differences are all under investigation, and the mechanism should be described as unresolved.

Vegetarian and vegan patterns

Well-planned vegetarian and vegan diets are recognized by major dietetics organizations as nutritionally adequate and appropriate at all stages of life, including pregnancy, lactation, infancy, childhood, and athletic training. Meeting protein needs without animal foods is straightforward when total energy intake is adequate and the diet includes legumes, soy foods, whole grains, nuts, and seeds — and soy in particular is a complete, highly digestible protein. Because plant proteins are somewhat less digestible and some are limited in lysine or methionine, some authorities suggest vegans aim modestly above 0.8 g/kg/day; the size of that adjustment is debated and the evidence is not strong enough to specify a single number.

The nutrients that actually require planning on a plant-based pattern are mostly not protein. Vitamin B12 is the firm one: it is not reliably available from unfortified plant foods, and vegans need fortified foods or a supplement — this is well established, not preliminary. Iron from plants is non-heme and less well absorbed, though vitamin C eaten at the same meal improves uptake; zinc, calcium, iodine, vitamin D, and the long-chain omega-3 fats EPA and DHA also deserve attention. These are planning items, not arguments against the pattern.

FoodTypical portionProtein (approx.)
Cooked chicken, beef, or fish3 oz (85 g)21–26 g
Greek yogurt, plain6 oz (170 g)15–18 g
Cooked lentils1 cup~18 g
Cooked black beans or chickpeas1 cup14–15 g
Firm tofu1/2 cup~10 g
Milk or fortified soy milk1 cup~8 g
Cooked quinoa1 cup~8 g
Peanut butter2 tbsp~7 g
Large egg1~6 g
Cooked rice or pasta1 cup4–8 g

A last practical note on supplements. Protein powders — whey, casein, soy, pea — are food, not medicine, and they are neither necessary nor harmful for most people. They are convenient. They are not superior to the same protein from a meal, and buying them is a budget decision more than a physiological one. As with all of this chapter, the material here is general education about how protein works, not individualized dietary or medical advice; questions about a specific person's needs, a kidney or liver condition, PKU, food allergy, or celiac disease belong with that person's own clinician or registered dietitian.

Interactive Blend two plant foods and watch the limiting amino acid rise to meet the reference pattern in a live amino-acid scoring chart. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Complete proteins supply all nine indispensable amino acids; the scarcest one in a food is the limiting amino acid.
  • Complementary proteins (legumes plus grains) cover each other's gaps — and need not be eaten in the same meal.
  • Adult RDA is 0.8 g/kg/day (age 19+, both sexes); pregnancy and lactation raise it to 1.1 g/kg/day; the AMDR is 10–35% of calories with no UL.
  • Well-planned vegetarian and vegan diets meet protein needs; vitamin B12 supplementation is the firm requirement for vegans.

The chapter in one breath

  • An amino acid is an alpha carbon plus an amino group, a carboxyl group, a hydrogen, and a variable side chain — and the side chain determines everything downstream.
  • Nine of the twenty amino acids are indispensable; several more become conditionally indispensable during illness, growth, or inherited disorders.
  • Sequence (primary structure) dictates fold, and fold dictates function; denaturation destroys the fold but leaves peptide bonds intact.
  • Digestion starts with stomach acid and pepsin, is completed by pancreatic zymogens activated via enteropeptidase and by brush-border peptidases, and delivers amino acids to the liver by the portal vein.
  • Protein serves as structure, enzymes, hormones, transport, antibodies, buffers, clotting factors, and — reluctantly — fuel at 4 kcal/g.
  • Albumin holds fluid in the bloodstream; when plasma protein falls very low, edema can appear.
  • Protein quality reflects amino acid pattern plus digestibility; complementary plant proteins work across the whole day, not just within one meal.
  • The adult RDA is 0.8 g/kg/day (19+, both sexes), rising to 1.1 g/kg/day in pregnancy and lactation, with an AMDR of 10–35% of calories and no upper limit set.

ReferenceWords worth knowing

A quick reference for the terms in this chapter — skim it now, or circle back whenever a word trips you up.

Amino acid
The building block of protein: a central carbon bonded to an amino group, a carboxyl group, a hydrogen, and a variable side chain.
Amino acid pool
The body's circulating and intracellular supply of free amino acids, refilled from both food and the breakdown of body proteins.
Complementary proteins
Two incomplete proteins whose limiting amino acids differ, so that together they supply the full pattern — classically legumes with grains.
Complete (high-quality) protein
A protein supplying all nine indispensable amino acids in adequate proportions; includes eggs, dairy, meat, fish, soy, and quinoa.
Conditionally indispensable amino acid
An amino acid the body normally synthesizes but cannot make in sufficient quantity during illness, injury, prematurity, or certain inherited disorders.
Denaturation
Loss of a protein's three-dimensional shape from heat, acid, alkali, agitation, or alcohol; peptide bonds and sequence remain intact.
Digestible Indispensable Amino Acid Score (DIAAS)
A protein quality measure using digestibility at the end of the small intestine; recommended by the FAO to succeed PDCAAS and not capped at 1.00.
Dispensable (nonessential) amino acid
An amino acid the body can synthesize in adequate amounts, so it carries no dietary requirement.
Edema
Visible swelling from fluid accumulating in tissue; one cause is a fall in plasma proteins such as albumin.
Enteropeptidase
A brush-border enzyme of the small intestine that converts trypsinogen to trypsin, triggering the pancreatic protease cascade.
Indispensable (essential) amino acid
One of the nine amino acids the body cannot synthesize and must obtain from food.
Kwashiorkor
A form of severe acute malnutrition marked by edema, fatty liver, and skin and hair changes; historically attributed to protein deficiency, though the mechanism is now considered unresolved.
Limiting amino acid
The indispensable amino acid present in shortest supply relative to need, which caps how much protein synthesis can proceed.
Marasmus
Severe acute malnutrition from prolonged deficiency of both energy and protein, marked by extreme wasting.
Nitrogen balance
The comparison of nitrogen consumed with nitrogen excreted; positive in growth and recovery, negative in illness or inadequate intake.
Pepsin
The stomach's protein-digesting enzyme, formed from pepsinogen by hydrochloric acid and active only at low pH.
Peptide bond
The covalent bond joining the carboxyl group of one amino acid to the amino group of the next, formed with the loss of water.
Primary structure
The linear sequence of amino acids in a polypeptide, specified by a gene.
Protein Digestibility-Corrected Amino Acid Score (PDCAAS)
A protein quality measure comparing a food's amino acid pattern to a reference and correcting for digestibility, capped at 1.00.
Protein turnover
The continuous breakdown and resynthesis of body proteins, amounting to roughly 250–300 grams per day in adults.
Quaternary structure
The assembly of two or more folded polypeptide chains into one functional protein, as in hemoglobin's four subunits.
Zymogen (proenzyme)
An inactive enzyme precursor, such as trypsinogen, activated only after it reaches its site of action.

Check yourself

1. What single element distinguishes protein from carbohydrate and fat, and what two practical consequences follow from it?
Nitrogen, carried in the amino group. Two consequences: protein content of a food can be estimated from its nitrogen content (protein is about 16% nitrogen, hence the 6.25 conversion factor), and surplus amino acids cannot simply be stored — the nitrogen must be stripped off and excreted as urea before the carbon skeleton is used.
2. Name the four groups attached to the alpha carbon of an amino acid, and say which one varies.
An amino group, a carboxyl group, a hydrogen atom, and a side chain (R group) — only the side chain varies. The side chain determines whether the amino acid is polar or nonpolar, acidic or basic, large or small, and therefore how the finished protein folds.
3. Distinguish indispensable, dispensable, and conditionally indispensable amino acids, and give an example of the third category.
Indispensable (nine of them) cannot be synthesized and must come from food; dispensable can be made in adequate amounts; conditionally indispensable are normally synthesized but not in sufficient quantity under certain conditions. Tyrosine is the classic conditional example — normally made from phenylalanine, but indispensable for someone with phenylketonuria, who lacks the converting enzyme.
4. How many kilocalories per gram does protein supply, and how does that compare with carbohydrate, fat, and alcohol?
Protein supplies 4 kcal/g — the same as carbohydrate. Fat supplies 9 kcal/g and alcohol 7 kcal/g. Despite yielding usable energy, protein is a metabolically expensive fuel, since burning it costs the body the amino acids themselves plus the work of excreting the nitrogen.
5. Explain how a change in primary structure can disable a protein, using a real example.
Primary structure determines how the chain folds, and the fold determines function — so one substituted amino acid can change the whole molecule. In sickle cell disease, valine replaces glutamic acid at position six of hemoglobin's beta-globin chain, and the altered protein stacks abnormally, deforming the red blood cell.
6. A student says that frying an egg "digests" the protein. Correct the statement and explain the difference.
Frying denatures the protein — it unfolds the shape by breaking hydrogen bonds and side-chain attractions — but every peptide bond is still intact, so nothing has been digested. Digestion is the separate, enzyme-driven cutting of peptide bonds. Because denatured protein is more open, cooking generally makes protein easier to digest.
7. Describe the two jobs hydrochloric acid performs in protein digestion.
It denatures incoming dietary proteins so their interior bonds are reachable, and it converts inactive pepsinogen into active pepsin. Pepsin then cuts interior peptide bonds, producing shorter polypeptides; it is unusual in working best at strongly acidic pH.
8. Why does the pancreas secrete proteases as zymogens, and what switches the cascade on?
Active proteases inside the pancreas would digest the organ itself, so they are shipped inactive; enteropeptidase in the intestinal brush border converts trypsinogen to trypsin, and trypsin then activates the remaining zymogens. Failure of this safeguard — early activation within the pancreas — is the mechanism underlying acute pancreatitis.
9. A person with severe protein–energy undernutrition develops swelling of the legs and abdomen. Explain the physiology.
Plasma proteins, especially albumin, are too large to leave capillaries and exert an osmotic pull that keeps water in the bloodstream. When plasma protein falls very low, that pull weakens and fluid can leak into surrounding tissue, producing edema. The swelling is fluid, not tissue — a person can be visibly swollen and profoundly malnourished at the same time. One caution: low albumin is a good explanation for the edema of liver and kidney disease, but in severe childhood undernutrition it is only part of the picture, and the full mechanism of kwashiorkor remains unresolved.
10. Define limiting amino acid and explain how complementary proteins solve the problem. Must they be eaten in the same meal?
The limiting amino acid is the indispensable amino acid in shortest supply relative to need, which caps how far protein synthesis can proceed. Complementary proteins are two incomplete proteins with different limiting amino acids — grains (limited in lysine) with legumes (limited in methionine) — so each covers the other's gap. No, they need not be eaten together. The body maintains a circulating amino acid pool, so proteins eaten across the day complement one another; the older "same meal" claim was withdrawn by its own author.
11. State the protein RDA for healthy adults with its units and population, and say how it changes in pregnancy and lactation.
0.8 grams per kilogram of body weight per day for healthy adults aged 19 and older, the same for men and women — roughly 56 g/day and 46 g/day at the DRI reference weights. In pregnancy and lactation the RDA rises to 1.1 g/kg/day, about 71 g/day. Infants and children are set per kilogram as well, from 1.2 g/kg/day at 7–12 months down to 0.85 g/kg/day at 14–18 years. The AMDR is 10–35% of calories for adults, and no Tolerable Upper Intake Level has been established.
12. Someone tells you that a high-protein diet damages the kidneys and weakens bone. How should you characterize the evidence?
Both claims are weaker than their reputations. There is no good evidence that high protein intake damages kidneys in people with normal kidney function — though that literature is largely short- to medium-term, so it shows an absence of demonstrated harm rather than proven long-term safety — and current evidence suggests protein is neutral to modestly favorable for bone when calcium intake is adequate. Protein restriction in established kidney disease is a separate, clinician-managed decision, not general advice. The defensible statements about very high intakes are that they can displace other foods and nutrients and are not known to add benefit once needs are met.
Nutrition · Chapter 6

Vitamins

Vitamins are the smallest thing on your plate that can change everything about how you feel. They supply no energy at all — zero kilocalories per gram, against carbohydrate's 4, protein's 4, fat's 9, and alcohol's 7 — yet without milligram and microgram quantities of thirteen specific organic compounds, blood will not clot, collagen will not hold, nerves will not conduct, and red cells will not mature. That is the paradox worth sitting with: a nutrient can be needed in vanishing amounts and still be non-negotiable. This chapter walks through the two families of vitamins, what each one actually does inside a cell, the classic deficiency syndromes that taught us they existed, and where the evidence for supplementation is strong versus where it is thin. Along the way you will learn to read a Recommended Dietary Allowance properly — which population, which units, and when the number shifts with age, sex, or pregnancy.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Define a vitamin as an organic micronutrient and explain why vitamins yield no energy.
  • Separate the fat-soluble (A, D, E, K) from the water-soluble (B complex and C) vitamins and predict how solubility affects absorption, storage, and toxicity risk.
  • State the principal function, food sources, deficiency syndrome, and toxicity picture for vitamins A, D, E, and K.
  • Describe the coenzyme role of the B vitamins and match each to its classic deficiency disease.
  • Explain vitamin C's role in collagen hydroxylation and iron absorption, and recognize scurvy.
  • Read an RDA, AI, or UL correctly, naming the population, units, and life-stage adjustments.
  • Describe factors that change bioavailability, including food matrix, dietary fat, and cooking method.
  • Distinguish strong from preliminary evidence when evaluating a single-nutrient supplement claim.

Section 1What vitamins are

Organic, essential, and needed in tiny amounts

A vitamin is an organic compound — carbon-containing, built by a living thing — that the human body requires in small amounts, cannot make at all (or cannot make enough of), and must therefore obtain from the diet. Each of those clauses does work. Organic separates vitamins from minerals such as calcium, iron, and zinc, which are single elements and cannot be destroyed by heat or light. Small amounts puts them in the micronutrient category: intakes are measured in milligrams (mg) or micrograms (µg) per day, not the tens or hundreds of grams we use for the macronutrients. Essential means that if intake falls low enough for long enough, a specific and reproducible deficiency disease appears — and resolving the deficiency resolves the disease. That last criterion is how each vitamin was discovered.

A common point of confusion: vitamins do not provide energy. Only three macronutrients and one non-nutrient do. Carbohydrate yields about 4 kcal per gram, protein about 4 kcal per gram, fat about 9 kcal per gram, and alcohol about 7 kcal per gram. Vitamins yield 0 kcal per gram. What they do instead is make energy release possible: several B vitamins are built into the coenzymes that run glycolysis, the citric acid cycle, and the electron transport chain. A vitamin does not add fuel to the fire; it is part of the machinery that burns the fuel. That is why "vitamins for energy" is a marketing phrase rather than a physiological one — correcting a genuine deficiency can restore energy dramatically, while adding more vitamin on top of adequacy does not.

Two nuances keep the definition honest. First, humans can make some vitamin D in skin exposed to ultraviolet B radiation, and gut bacteria contribute some vitamin K and biotin — so "cannot be synthesized" is a near-rule rather than an absolute one. Second, a handful of compounds sit at the edge of the category: choline, for example, has an Adequate Intake (550 mg/day for adult men, 425 mg/day for adult women) and is essential, but is usually classed separately because the body synthesizes a meaningful fraction of it.

The two families, and why solubility decides everything

Thirteen compounds are recognized as vitamins for humans, and they divide cleanly by what they dissolve in. The four fat-soluble vitamins — A, D, E, and K — travel with dietary fat, need bile and pancreatic lipase for efficient absorption, get packaged into chylomicrons for transport in lymph, and are stored in the liver and adipose tissue. The nine water-soluble vitamins — the eight B vitamins plus vitamin C — dissolve in the watery interior of foods and of the body, are generally absorbed directly into portal blood, are stored only in small amounts, and are excreted in urine when intake exceeds need.

From that one physical property, a whole set of clinical predictions follows. Fat-soluble vitamins accumulate, so deficiency takes months to years to appear but toxicity (hypervitaminosis) is a real hazard from concentrated supplements. Water-soluble vitamins turn over quickly, so deficiency can develop in weeks to months and intake needs to be regular — but excess is mostly excreted, which makes toxicity less common. "Less common" is not "impossible," and Section 5 covers the important exceptions. One more consequence: anything that impairs fat digestion — cystic fibrosis, cholestatic liver disease, bariatric surgery, untreated celiac disease, some fat-blocking medications — puts the fat-soluble vitamins at risk long before it touches the water-soluble ones.

PropertyFat-soluble (A, D, E, K)Water-soluble (B complex, C)
AbsorptionRequires dietary fat, bile, micelle formationGenerally direct; some need carriers (B12 needs intrinsic factor)
TransportChylomicrons and lipoproteins, via lymphFree or protein-bound in portal blood
StorageSubstantial — liver and adipose tissueMinimal, except vitamin B12 (liver stores last years)
ExcretionPoor; excess accumulatesReadily excreted in urine
Deficiency onsetMonths to yearsWeeks to months
Toxicity riskHigher — A and D especiallyLower, but real for B6, niacin, folic acid, vitamin C
Cooking lossesRelatively heat-stable; lost with discarded fatLeached into cooking water; C and thiamin heat-labile

Reading the reference values

Vitamin recommendations in North America come from the Dietary Reference Intakes (DRIs), and the labels matter. An RDA (Recommended Dietary Allowance) is the intake estimated to meet the needs of about 97–98% of healthy people in a specified life-stage and sex group — it is deliberately generous, and it is a target for individuals, not a minimum below which harm is certain. An AI (Adequate Intake) is used when the evidence is too thin to set an RDA; vitamins K, B5, and B7 all have AIs rather than RDAs. A UL (Tolerable Upper Intake Level) is the highest chronic daily intake unlikely to cause harm — not a goal, a ceiling. Every one of these numbers is meaningless without its population and units, which is why this chapter always states both.

A number without a population is not a number. "The RDA for vitamin C is 90 mg" is only true for adult men. For adult women it is 75 mg/day; pregnancy raises it to 85 mg/day and lactation to 120 mg/day; people who smoke are advised to add 35 mg/day. Whenever you see a vitamin value quoted — on a label, in a headline, in an article — ask whose number it is before you use it.
Interactive Sort every vitamin into fat- or water-soluble, then drag a timeline to watch how solubility drives where it is stored, how fast reserves run down, and its supplement-toxicity picture. Open full ↑
Key points
  • A vitamin is an organic micronutrient the body needs in mg or µg amounts and largely cannot synthesize.
  • Vitamins supply 0 kcal/g; energy comes from carbohydrate (4), protein (4), fat (9), and alcohol (7) kcal/g.
  • Fat-soluble vitamins A, D, E, K are stored and can accumulate to toxicity; water-soluble B vitamins and C turn over fast and need regular intake.
  • RDA covers ~97–98% of a specified group, AI is used when evidence is limited, and UL is a ceiling — all three are population-specific.

Section 2Fat-soluble vitamins

Vitamin A: vision, epithelium, and the one with a teratogenic ceiling

Vitamin A arrives in two chemically different forms. Preformed vitamin A (retinol and retinyl esters) comes from animal-source foods — liver above all, plus dairy fat, egg yolk, and fortified foods. Provitamin A carotenoids — principally beta-carotene, plus alpha-carotene and beta-cryptoxanthin — come from deeply colored plants such as sweet potato, carrot, pumpkin, mango, and dark leafy greens, and are converted to retinol in the intestinal wall with modest and variable efficiency. Because the two forms are not interchangeable gram for gram, intakes are expressed in retinol activity equivalents (RAE): 1 µg RAE equals 1 µg of retinol, 12 µg of dietary beta-carotene, or 24 µg of the other provitamin A carotenoids.

Functionally, vitamin A does three big jobs. As 11-cis-retinal bound to opsin it forms rhodopsin, the pigment that lets rod cells detect dim light. As retinoic acid it acts as a hormone-like regulator of gene transcription, directing the differentiation of epithelial tissue — skin, cornea, respiratory and gut lining. And it supports immune competence, partly by keeping those epithelial barriers intact. Deficiency therefore starts with night blindness (nyctalopia), progresses to xerophthalmia with drying of the conjunctiva and cornea, Bitot's spots, and eventually keratomalacia and irreversible blindness; it also raises susceptibility to infection. Globally, vitamin A deficiency remains a leading preventable cause of childhood blindness, and randomized trials of supplementation in deficient populations show reduced child mortality — strong trial-based evidence, though the size of the effect varies across trials and appears smallest where deficiency is least common.

Toxicity is the other half of the story and it is genuinely important. The adult UL is 3,000 µg/day of preformed vitamin A. Chronic excess causes liver injury, bone and joint pain, hair loss, dry cracking skin, and raised intracranial pressure. Most consequentially, preformed vitamin A is a teratogen: high supplemental intakes in early pregnancy are associated with birth defects, which is also why the retinoid medications used for severe acne carry pregnancy-prevention requirements. Carotenoids from food behave differently — they are not converted fast enough to cause hypervitaminosis, and very high intakes produce only carotenodermia, a harmless yellow-orange tint to the skin that fades. The RDA for adults is 900 µg RAE/day for men and 700 µg RAE/day for women, rising to 770 µg RAE in pregnancy and 1,300 µg RAE in lactation.

Vitamin D: a hormone that we happen to call a vitamin

Vitamin D is unusual twice over. It can be synthesized in skin, where UVB radiation converts 7-dehydrocholesterol to cholecalciferol (vitamin D3); and it functions as a steroid hormone rather than a coenzyme. The dietary form from fungi, notably UV-exposed mushrooms, is ergocalciferol (vitamin D2); food sources of D3 include oily fish, cod liver oil, egg yolk, and — the largest contributor in many countries — fortified milk and plant milks. Whichever the source, activation takes two steps: the liver adds a hydroxyl group to make 25-hydroxyvitamin D, the circulating storage form measured on a lab report, and the kidney adds another to make 1,25-dihydroxyvitamin D (calcitriol), the active hormone.

Calcitriol's main job is calcium and phosphate economy: it increases intestinal absorption of both, works with parathyroid hormone to maintain serum calcium, and thereby permits normal bone mineralization. Deficiency produces rickets in children — soft, deforming bone with bowed legs, widened wrists, and a beaded costochondral "rachitic rosary" — and osteomalacia in adults, with bone pain and proximal muscle weakness. Risk rises with limited sun exposure, higher skin melanin (which lengthens the UVB exposure needed), high latitude, clothing coverage, older age, higher body fat (which sequesters vitamin D in adipose tissue), and fat malabsorption; exclusively breastfed infants are routinely given a supplement because human milk is low in vitamin D. The RDA is 15 µg (600 IU)/day for ages 1–70, including pregnancy and lactation, and 20 µg (800 IU)/day for adults over 70; infants have an AI of 10 µg (400 IU)/day. Note the unit conversion, which trips everyone: 1 µg = 40 IU. The adult UL is 100 µg (4,000 IU)/day, and toxicity — hypercalcemia, hypercalciuria, and calcium deposition in kidneys and soft tissue — comes from supplements, never from sunlight, because skin synthesis is self-limiting.

Vitamin D is also a case study in evidence quality. That it prevents and treats rickets and osteomalacia is beyond dispute. The much broader claims — that supplementation in already-replete people prevents cancer, cardiovascular disease, diabetes, or respiratory infection — looked promising in observational studies and then largely failed to hold up in large randomized trials. Treat the skeletal evidence as strong and the extraskeletal evidence as preliminary and mostly negative so far.

Vitamin E and vitamin K

Vitamin E is a family of eight related compounds, but only alpha-tocopherol is retained by the body well enough to meet human requirements, because a hepatic transfer protein selectively loads that form back into circulating lipoproteins. Its role is as the principal lipid-phase antioxidant: it intercepts free radicals inside cell membranes and lipoproteins, protecting polyunsaturated fatty acids from chain-reaction oxidation, and is then regenerated with help from vitamin C. Sources are concentrated in plant fats — sunflower and safflower oil, wheat germ oil, almonds, sunflower seeds, peanuts, avocado, and leafy greens. The adult RDA is 15 mg/day of alpha-tocopherol for both men and women, rising to 19 mg/day in lactation. Dietary deficiency is rare in healthy people and shows up mainly with severe fat malabsorption or specific genetic disorders, producing peripheral neuropathy, ataxia, myopathy, retinopathy, and hemolytic anemia in premature infants. The UL for supplemental alpha-tocopherol is 1,000 mg/day; high-dose supplements can interfere with vitamin K–dependent clotting, and large trials of high-dose vitamin E have found no cardiovascular benefit and some signals of harm.

Vitamin K exists as phylloquinone (K1), abundant in green leafy vegetables such as kale, spinach, collards, and broccoli and in some plant oils, and as the menaquinones (K2) from fermented foods, some animal foods, and colonic bacteria. Its function is precise: it is the cofactor for gamma-glutamyl carboxylase, the enzyme that carboxylates specific glutamate residues so those proteins can bind calcium. The substrates include clotting factors II, VII, IX, and X plus proteins C and S, and also bone proteins such as osteocalcin and the vessel-wall matrix Gla protein. So the deficiency picture is bleeding — easy bruising, mucosal bleeding, prolonged prothrombin time. There is an AI rather than an RDA: 120 µg/day for adult men and 90 µg/day for adult women, and no UL has been set because toxicity from the natural forms has not been demonstrated. Newborns are the classic at-risk group: placental transfer is limited, human milk is low in vitamin K, and the newborn gut is not yet colonized, which is why a single dose at birth is standard practice to prevent vitamin K deficiency bleeding.

VitaminCore functionFood sourcesDeficiencyToxicity / UL (adults)
A (retinol, carotenoids)Rhodopsin for night vision; epithelial differentiation; immunityLiver, dairy fat, egg yolk; sweet potato, carrot, greens (carotenoids)Night blindness → xerophthalmia; infection riskYes — liver injury, bone pain, teratogenic; UL 3,000 µg preformed
D (calciferol)Calcium and phosphate absorption; bone mineralizationOily fish, egg yolk, fortified milk; skin synthesis from UVBRickets (children), osteomalacia (adults)Yes — hypercalcemia; UL 100 µg (4,000 IU)
E (alpha-tocopherol)Lipid-phase antioxidant in membranesVegetable oils, nuts, seeds, avocado, greensRare; neuropathy, ataxia, hemolysis with malabsorptionBleeding risk at high dose; UL 1,000 mg supplemental

Fat-soluble vitamins travel with fat. Absorption of A, D, E, and K depends on bile and on a fatty meal to form the micelles that carry them across the intestinal lining. A vitamin D capsule swallowed with black coffee is absorbed considerably less well than the same capsule taken with a meal containing some fat — and the same principle explains why a drizzle of oil on a salad meaningfully increases carotenoid uptake from the greens.
Interactive Tap vitamins A, D, E, and K for function, sources, deficiency, and toxicity, then use a dietary-fat slider to see why fat-soluble vitamins are absorbed and stored with fat. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Vitamin A: night blindness and xerophthalmia when deficient; preformed retinol is teratogenic in excess (UL 3,000 µg/day); food carotenoids are not.
  • Vitamin D: activated in liver then kidney; RDA 15 µg (600 IU)/day ages 1–70, 20 µg (800 IU)/day over 70; deficiency causes rickets and osteomalacia.
  • Vitamin E: alpha-tocopherol is the active form; RDA 15 mg/day for adults; deficiency is a malabsorption phenomenon, not a dietary one.
  • Vitamin K: carboxylates clotting factors II, VII, IX, X; AI 120 µg/day men, 90 µg/day women; newborns are uniquely at risk.

Section 3The B vitamins

One job description, eight employees

The eight B vitamins are chemically unrelated to one another; they were grouped together historically because they were all water-soluble factors isolated from the same yeast and liver extracts. What unites them functionally is that nearly every one becomes a coenzyme — a small non-protein molecule an enzyme must have bound in order to work. Thiamin becomes thiamin pyrophosphate; riboflavin becomes FAD and FMN; niacin becomes NAD+ and NADP+; pantothenic acid becomes coenzyme A; B6 becomes pyridoxal 5'-phosphate; biotin rides on carboxylase enzymes; folate becomes tetrahydrofolate; and B12 becomes methylcobalamin and adenosylcobalamin. Between them they are required for glycolysis, the citric acid cycle, the electron transport chain, fatty acid synthesis and breakdown, amino acid metabolism, heme synthesis, neurotransmitter synthesis, and the one-carbon transfers that build DNA.

Two practical consequences follow. First, because these coenzymes sit in energy pathways, early B-vitamin deficiency tends to present with fatigue and neurological complaints — but so does almost everything else, which is why the specific syndromes below matter for recognition. Second, because they are water-soluble and stored only briefly, intake must be reasonably regular. Vitamin B12 is the great exception: liver stores can supply several years of need, so deficiency develops insidiously and is often well advanced by the time it is noticed.

The classic deficiency diseases

Thiamin (B1) deficiency causes beriberi. The "wet" form is cardiovascular — high-output heart failure with peripheral edema; the "dry" form is a symmetrical peripheral neuropathy with muscle wasting. In the context of chronic heavy alcohol use, which impairs both intake and absorption, thiamin deficiency produces Wernicke encephalopathy — the triad of confusion, eye-movement abnormalities, and ataxia — which can progress to the largely irreversible memory disorder called Korsakoff syndrome. Adult RDA: 1.2 mg/day for men, 1.1 mg/day for women, rising to 1.4 mg/day in pregnancy and lactation. Historically, beriberi followed the milling of white rice, which strips the thiamin-rich bran.

Riboflavin (B2) deficiency (ariboflavinosis) shows up in the mouth and skin: cracking at the corners of the lips (angular cheilitis), a sore magenta-colored tongue, and seborrheic dermatitis. Adult RDA: 1.3 mg/day for men, 1.1 mg/day for women. Riboflavin is notably light-sensitive, which is why milk is sold in opaque containers.

Niacin (B3) deficiency causes pellagra, remembered as the four Ds: dermatitis (a symmetrical, sun-exposed rash, including the collar-like Casal's necklace), diarrhea, dementia, and, untreated, death. Niacin is partly spared by dietary tryptophan — roughly 60 mg of tryptophan yields 1 mg of niacin — so intakes are expressed as niacin equivalents (NE). Adult RDA: 16 mg NE/day for men, 14 mg NE/day for women. Pellagra devastated the American South in the early twentieth century among people eating maize-based diets, until Joseph Goldberger's field studies showed it was a dietary deficiency rather than an infection; traditional nixtamalization of maize with alkali, long practiced in Mesoamerica, releases the bound niacin and prevents it. The UL of 35 mg/day applies to niacin from supplements and fortified foods, where excess produces cutaneous flushing.

Vitamin B6 deficiency causes a microcytic anemia (heme synthesis stalls), dermatitis, and in severe cases seizures and peripheral neuropathy. RDA: 1.3 mg/day for adults aged 19–50, rising to 1.7 mg/day for men and 1.5 mg/day for women over 50; 1.9 mg/day in pregnancy. B6 is also the one water-soluble vitamin with a well-documented toxicity: sustained intakes above the UL of 100 mg/day can cause a sensory peripheral neuropathy.

Folate (B9) and vitamin B12 are best learned as a pair, because both are required for DNA synthesis in rapidly dividing cells and both produce a megaloblastic (macrocytic) anemia when deficient. Folate's RDA is 400 µg DFE/day for adults, 600 µg DFE/day in pregnancy, and 500 µg DFE/day in lactation — DFE meaning dietary folate equivalents, which adjust for the fact that synthetic folic acid is absorbed better than the folate naturally present in leafy greens, legumes, and liver. The single strongest piece of evidence in this whole chapter is that 400 µg/day of folic acid taken before conception and in early pregnancy substantially reduces neural tube defects; randomized trial evidence and the fall in defect rates after national flour fortification both support it, and the recommendation applies to anyone who could become pregnant because the neural tube closes before most pregnancies are recognized.

Vitamin B12 (RDA 2.4 µg/day for adults, 2.6 µg in pregnancy, 2.8 µg in lactation) is found naturally only in animal-source foods and in fortified foods and supplements — a fact of real importance for anyone eating a vegan diet, who needs a reliable fortified or supplemental source. Its absorption is elaborate: stomach acid and pepsin free it from food protein, intrinsic factor from gastric parietal cells binds it, and the complex is taken up in the terminal ileum. Any break in that chain causes deficiency — autoimmune loss of intrinsic factor (pernicious anemia), atrophic gastritis with low acid (common with age), gastric or ileal surgery, or ileal disease. Beyond anemia, B12 deficiency damages the spinal cord and peripheral nerves — subacute combined degeneration — producing numbness, gait unsteadiness, and cognitive change that may not fully reverse. Two smaller members complete the set: pantothenic acid (B5), AI 5 mg/day for adults, and biotin (B7), AI 30 µg/day for adults; deficiency of either is rare outside unusual circumstances.

B vitaminCoenzyme formAdult reference intakeDeficiency syndrome
B1 ThiaminThiamin pyrophosphate (TPP)RDA 1.2 mg/day men, 1.1 mg/day womenBeriberi (wet, dry); Wernicke–Korsakoff
B2 RiboflavinFAD, FMNRDA 1.3 mg/day men, 1.1 mg/day womenAngular cheilitis, glossitis, dermatitis
B3 NiacinNAD+, NADP+RDA 16 mg NE/day men, 14 mg NE/day womenPellagra — dermatitis, diarrhea, dementia, death
B5 Pantothenic acidCoenzyme AAI 5 mg/dayVery rare; paresthesias
B6 PyridoxinePyridoxal 5'-phosphate (PLP)RDA 1.3 mg/day (19–50); 1.7 men / 1.5 women (51+)Microcytic anemia, dermatitis, neuropathy, seizures
B7 BiotinBound to carboxylasesAI 30 µg/dayRare; rash, hair loss, neurologic signs
B9 FolateTetrahydrofolate (THF)RDA 400 µg DFE/day; 600 µg in pregnancyMegaloblastic anemia; neural tube defects
B12 CobalaminMethyl- and adenosylcobalaminRDA 2.4 µg/day; 2.6 µg in pregnancyMegaloblastic anemia plus neurologic damage
Folic acid can hide a B12 problem. Both deficiencies enlarge red blood cells, so generous folic acid intake can correct the anemia of B12 deficiency while the neurological damage quietly continues. That is the reason the UL for folic acid from supplements and fortified foods is set at 1,000 µg/day for adults, and the reason a macrocytic anemia should never be attributed to folate without considering B12 — a question for a clinician, not for a supplement aisle.
Interactive A tap-to-match game that wires each B vitamin to its coenzyme and its deficiency disease, plus a live niacin-equivalents calculator. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Nearly every B vitamin functions as a coenzyme in energy, amino acid, or DNA metabolism — they enable energy release but supply none.
  • Classic pairings: thiamin–beriberi and Wernicke–Korsakoff; niacin–pellagra (4 Ds); folate and B12–megaloblastic anemia.
  • 400 µg/day of folic acid periconceptionally prevents neural tube defects — among the strongest single-nutrient findings in nutrition.
  • B12 occurs naturally only in animal foods and requires intrinsic factor; deficiency causes anemia plus potentially irreversible neurologic injury.

Section 4Vitamin C

What ascorbate actually does

Vitamin C (ascorbic acid) is a water-soluble vitamin that humans, other primates, guinea pigs, and a few other species must eat because we all carry a broken copy of the gene for L-gulonolactone oxidase, the final enzyme in the synthetic pathway that most mammals still use. Its chemistry is simple — it is a reducing agent, an electron donor — but the consequences are wide.

The best-defined role is in collagen synthesis. The enzymes prolyl hydroxylase and lysyl hydroxylase modify proline and lysine residues in procollagen, and those hydroxyl groups are what allow the triple helix to cross-link and hold tension. Both enzymes need iron in its reduced state, and vitamin C is what keeps it there. Without ascorbate, collagen is made but is structurally weak — which explains almost every sign of scurvy, since collagen is the scaffolding of skin, blood vessel walls, gums, tendon, and bone matrix. Vitamin C is also required for the synthesis of carnitine (which shuttles fatty acids into mitochondria) and of norepinephrine, it acts as a water-phase antioxidant and regenerates oxidized vitamin E, and it markedly enhances absorption of non-heme iron from plant foods by reducing ferric iron to the absorbable ferrous form — a genuinely useful pairing for people relying on beans, lentils, tofu, and fortified grains for iron.

Sources are broader than the citrus stereotype. Per typical serving, sweet peppers, guava, kiwifruit, broccoli, Brussels sprouts, strawberries, papaya, cantaloupe, tomatoes, and potatoes all contribute meaningfully alongside oranges and grapefruit. The adult RDA is 90 mg/day for men and 75 mg/day for women; pregnancy raises it to 85 mg/day and lactation to 120 mg/day, and an additional 35 mg/day is recommended for people who smoke because of higher oxidative turnover. The UL is 2,000 mg/day, above which osmotic diarrhea and gastrointestinal discomfort are common and urinary oxalate rises, which matters for people prone to calcium oxalate kidney stones.

Scurvy, and the first controlled trial

Scurvy is what happens when body ascorbate is depleted, typically after roughly one to three months of near-zero intake. The presentation follows the collagen story exactly: fatigue and irritability first, then perifollicular hemorrhages and distinctive corkscrew-shaped body hairs, swollen bleeding gums with loosening teeth, easy bruising, joint pain and swelling from bleeding into joints, poor wound healing, and the eerie sign of old, long-healed wounds reopening. Anemia is common. In children the growing bone is affected, with painful subperiosteal bleeding. Untreated scurvy was fatal, and repletion reverses it dramatically.

Scurvy is also where clinical nutrition became a science. In 1747, aboard HMS Salisbury, the naval surgeon James Lind took twelve sailors with scurvy, kept their diets otherwise identical, and assigned them in pairs to six different treatments. Only the pair given oranges and lemons recovered. It is one of the first recorded controlled clinical trials, and it worked more than 175 years before the compound responsible was isolated — a reminder that a well-designed comparison can identify a cause long before anyone can explain the mechanism. Scurvy is not merely historical: it still appears today in the context of very restricted eating patterns, severe food insecurity, alcohol use disorder, some psychiatric and neurodevelopmental conditions that narrow food variety, and isolated older adults.

Feature of scurvyUnderlying mechanism
Bleeding, swollen gums; loose teethWeak collagen in gingival connective tissue and periodontal ligament
Perifollicular hemorrhage; corkscrew hairsFragile capillary walls; abnormal keratin and follicle structure
Easy bruising, petechiaeDefective collagen in blood vessel basement membrane
Poor wound healing; old wounds reopenNew collagen cannot be hydroxylated or cross-linked
Joint pain and swellingBleeding into joint spaces and periosteum
Fatigue; anemiaImpaired carnitine synthesis; blood loss; reduced iron absorption
The common cold claim, honestly stated. Systematic reviews of randomized trials show that routine vitamin C supplementation does not reduce how often adults in the general population catch colds, and that it shortens duration only modestly — on the order of 8% in adults and 14% in children — when taken continuously beforehand. Starting a high dose after symptoms begin has not shown consistent benefit. That is a real but small effect, and it is a good template for how to talk about any single-nutrient claim: state the size, not just the direction.

Key points
  • Humans cannot synthesize vitamin C; it is required to hydroxylate proline and lysine in collagen, and for carnitine and norepinephrine synthesis.
  • Vitamin C strongly enhances non-heme iron absorption by reducing ferric to ferrous iron.
  • RDA 90 mg/day for adult men, 75 mg/day for adult women; +35 mg/day for smokers; UL 2,000 mg/day.
  • Scurvy — bleeding gums, perifollicular hemorrhage, poor wound healing — is a collagen failure and still occurs today.

Section 5Vitamins in practice

Bioavailability: what is in the food is not what reaches the cell

Bioavailability is the fraction of an ingested nutrient that is actually absorbed and available for use. A food composition table reports what is in the food; bioavailability determines what you get. Several factors move it. The food matrix matters: carotenoids locked inside intact plant cell walls are poorly released, so chopping, pureeing, and gentle cooking measurably increase carotenoid absorption from carrots and tomatoes — one of the few cases where cooking increases a vitamin's availability rather than decreasing it. Dietary fat matters, as Section 2 covered: A, D, E, and K need it. Chemical form matters: synthetic folic acid is absorbed more completely than natural food folate, which is exactly why intakes are expressed in dietary folate equivalents, and crystalline B12 in fortified foods and supplements does not require stomach acid to release it, which is why fortified sources are more reliable for older adults with reduced acid production. Nutrient interactions matter in both directions: vitamin C boosts non-heme iron uptake, while very high doses of one fat-soluble vitamin can interfere with another.

Losses in storage, prep, and cooking

Vitamins are chemicals, and chemicals degrade. Water-soluble vitamins are lost by two routes — leaching into cooking water and destruction by heat, light, oxygen, or alkaline pH. Vitamin C is the most fragile of all: it is water-soluble, heat-labile, and readily oxidized, so it is often used as the indicator nutrient in cooking studies. Boiling vegetables in a large volume of water and discarding the water causes the largest losses; steaming, microwaving, stir-frying, and roasting retain far more, and any liquid kept and served (as in a soup, stew, or pan sauce) recovers what leached out. Thiamin is destroyed by prolonged heat, by alkaline conditions such as adding baking soda to vegetables or beans, and by sulfite preservatives. Riboflavin is degraded by light. Folate is both heat-sensitive and water-soluble. The fat-soluble vitamins are generally more heat-stable, but they follow the fat — drain the fat and you drain some of the A, D, E, and K with it, and vitamin E is degraded by repeated high-heat frying.

Two practical corrections to common assumptions. First, frozen produce is not nutritionally inferior to fresh. It is typically harvested at peak ripeness and frozen within hours, whereas "fresh" produce may have spent many days in transit and storage losing vitamin C the whole way — frozen often measures equal or higher. Canning involves more heat and more leaching into the packing liquid, though using that liquid recovers some. Second, none of this is a reason to fear cooked food. A cooked vegetable eaten regularly delivers vastly more nutrition than a raw vegetable nobody eats, and cooking improves the availability of carotenoids and the digestibility of many foods. The goal is variety and regularity, not perfection — and no food needs to be described as clean, dirty, or earned.

Supplements: where the evidence is strong, and where it is not

In the United States, dietary supplements are regulated as a category of food rather than as drugs. Manufacturers are responsible for safety and for the truthfulness of their claims, but products are not reviewed or approved for safety or effectiveness before they reach the shelf, and independent testing has repeatedly found products whose contents differ from the label. Voluntary third-party verification programs — USP Verified, NSF Certified for Sport, and similar — test for identity, potency, and contaminants, and a verification mark is a reasonable thing to look for. Note that supplements can interact with medications: high-dose vitamin E with anticoagulants, vitamin K intake variability for someone on warfarin, and high-dose biotin, which interferes with several common laboratory immunoassays (including some troponin and thyroid tests) and can produce misleading results unless the lab is told.

The evidence base is genuinely uneven, and separating its tiers is the most useful thing this section can teach. There are situations where supplementation is strongly supported: folic acid before and during early pregnancy to prevent neural tube defects; vitamin K at birth to prevent hemorrhagic disease of the newborn; vitamin D for exclusively breastfed infants; vitamin B12 from fortified foods or supplements for people eating vegan diets and, commonly, for older adults with reduced gastric acid; vitamin A supplementation for children in populations where deficiency is endemic; and repletion of any diagnosed deficiency. There are also situations where the evidence is preliminary, mixed, or negative: routine multivitamins have not been shown to reduce cardiovascular disease, cancer, or mortality in generally well-nourished adults; large trials of high-dose vitamin E found no cardiovascular benefit; and beta-carotene supplements increased lung cancer incidence in people who smoked or had heavy asbestos exposure, a result replicated in two separate large trials. That last finding is the cautionary tale of the field: an association observed for a food (carotenoid-rich vegetables) did not transfer to an isolated high-dose nutrient, and the isolated nutrient caused harm.

A reasonable summary is that supplements fill gaps; they do not upgrade an already-adequate diet. Whole foods deliver vitamins in the company of fiber, minerals, phytochemicals, and a food matrix that shapes how they are absorbed, and hypervitaminosis from food alone is rare — the notable exception being preformed vitamin A, since liver is concentrated enough that frequent large portions can push intake past the UL.
