Decisions about whether a specific person needs a specific supplement — and at what amount — belong with a clinician or registered dietitian who knows that person's labs, medications, and circumstances. This chapter describes physiology and evidence; it is general education, not individualized medical or dietary advice.

PracticeEffect on vitaminsWhy
Boiling and discarding waterLargest losses of vitamin C, thiamin, folateWater-soluble vitamins leach out and are thrown away
Steaming / microwavingMuch better retentionLess contact with water; shorter heating time
Adding baking soda to vegetablesDestroys thiamin and vitamin CAlkaline pH degrades both
Chopping and gently cooking carrots, tomatoesIncreases carotenoid availabilityBreaks plant cell walls, releasing carotenoids
Adding a little oil to a vegetable dishImproves uptake of A, D, E, K and carotenoidsFat is required to form absorptive micelles
Storing milk in clear glass; produce in bright lightDegrades riboflavin, folateBoth are light-sensitive
Freezing at harvestRetention equal to or better than "fresh"Vitamin C declines during transport and storage
Reusing cooking liquid in soup or sauceRecovers leached vitaminsThe nutrients are in the liquid, not destroyed
Water-soluble does not mean risk-free. The old shortcut that "you just pee out the excess" fails for at least three vitamins: sustained B6 above 100 mg/day can cause a sensory peripheral neuropathy, supplemental niacin above 35 mg/day causes flushing and at higher doses can affect the liver, and folic acid above 1,000 µg/day can mask the anemia of B12 deficiency while nerve damage advances. Upper limits exist for water-soluble vitamins for a reason.
Interactive Change the cooking method, time, cut size, and whether you keep the liquid, and watch water-soluble vitamins leach and degrade while a fat-soluble control holds steady. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Bioavailability depends on food matrix, dietary fat, chemical form, and nutrient interactions — not just on what the food table says.
  • Water-soluble vitamins are lost by leaching and heat; steaming, microwaving, and using the cooking liquid retain far more than boiling and draining.
  • Supplements in the US are not pre-approved for safety or efficacy; third-party verification marks and disclosure to clinicians both matter.
  • Strong evidence supports targeted supplementation (periconceptional folic acid, newborn vitamin K, B12 for vegan diets and many older adults); routine multivitamins in well-nourished adults have not shown disease-prevention benefit, and high-dose beta-carotene caused harm in smokers.

The chapter in one breath

  • Vitamins are organic micronutrients required in mg or µg amounts; they supply 0 kcal/g, while carbohydrate and protein give 4, fat 9, and alcohol 7 kcal/g.
  • Solubility drives everything: fat-soluble A, D, E, K need dietary fat, are stored, and can reach toxicity; water-soluble B and C turn over fast and need regular intake.
  • Vitamin A runs night vision and epithelial differentiation — deficiency causes xerophthalmia, and preformed retinol above the 3,000 µg/day UL is teratogenic.
  • Vitamin D (RDA 15 µg / 600 IU/day ages 1–70; 20 µg / 800 IU/day over 70) governs calcium absorption; deficiency causes rickets and osteomalacia, and its non-skeletal claims remain unproven.
  • Vitamin E is the membrane antioxidant (RDA 15 mg/day); vitamin K carboxylates clotting factors (AI 120 µg/day men, 90 µg/day women) and newborns need a dose at birth.
  • The B vitamins are coenzymes: thiamin→beriberi and Wernicke–Korsakoff, niacin→pellagra's four Ds, folate and B12→megaloblastic anemia, with B12 adding irreversible neurologic injury.
  • Vitamin C hydroxylates collagen and boosts non-heme iron absorption (RDA 90 mg men, 75 mg women); its absence is scurvy, the subject of Lind's 1747 controlled trial.
  • In practice, bioavailability and cooking method change what you actually absorb, and supplement evidence is strong for a few targeted uses and weak or negative for routine high-dose use.

ReferenceWords worth knowing

A quick reference for the terms in this chapter — skim it now, or circle back whenever a word trips you up.

Adequate Intake (AI)
A reference intake used when evidence is insufficient to set an RDA; vitamins K, B5, and B7 have AIs.
Ascorbic acid
Vitamin C; a water-soluble reducing agent required for collagen hydroxylation, carnitine and norepinephrine synthesis, and non-heme iron absorption.
Beriberi
Thiamin (B1) deficiency disease; the "wet" form is cardiac with edema, the "dry" form a peripheral neuropathy.
Bioavailability
The proportion of an ingested nutrient that is absorbed and available for the body to use.
Carotenoid
A plant pigment; the provitamin A carotenoids (beta-carotene, alpha-carotene, beta-cryptoxanthin) are converted to retinol in the body.
Coenzyme
A small non-protein molecule an enzyme requires in order to catalyze its reaction; most B vitamins become coenzymes.
Dietary folate equivalent (DFE)
The unit for folate intake, adjusting for the higher absorption of synthetic folic acid relative to naturally occurring food folate.
Fat-soluble vitamin
Vitamins A, D, E, and K, which require dietary fat and bile for absorption and are stored in liver and adipose tissue.
Hypervitaminosis
Vitamin toxicity from excessive intake, most often involving preformed vitamin A or vitamin D from supplements.
Intrinsic factor
A protein secreted by gastric parietal cells that is required for vitamin B12 absorption in the terminal ileum.
Megaloblastic anemia
Anemia with abnormally large, immature red cells caused by impaired DNA synthesis, classically from folate or vitamin B12 deficiency.
Micronutrient
A vitamin or mineral required in milligram or microgram quantities, supplying no energy.
Niacin equivalent (NE)
The unit for niacin intake, accounting for niacin synthesized from tryptophan (about 60 mg tryptophan yields 1 mg niacin).
Osteomalacia
Softening of adult bone from vitamin D deficiency, with bone pain and proximal muscle weakness; the adult counterpart of rickets.
Pellagra
Niacin (B3) deficiency disease, remembered as dermatitis, diarrhea, dementia, and death.
Recommended Dietary Allowance (RDA)
The daily intake estimated to meet the needs of about 97–98% of healthy people in a stated age and sex group.
Retinol activity equivalent (RAE)
The unit for vitamin A intake: 1 µg RAE equals 1 µg retinol, 12 µg dietary beta-carotene, or 24 µg other provitamin A carotenoids.
Rickets
Vitamin D deficiency in children, causing soft, deforming bone with bowed legs and widened growth plates.
Scurvy
Vitamin C deficiency disease: bleeding gums, perifollicular hemorrhage, corkscrew hairs, poor wound healing, and joint pain.
Tolerable Upper Intake Level (UL)
The highest chronic daily intake unlikely to cause adverse effects in almost all people; a ceiling, not a target.
Water-soluble vitamin
The eight B vitamins and vitamin C; minimally stored, readily excreted, and easily lost into cooking water.
Xerophthalmia
Progressive drying and damage of the conjunctiva and cornea from vitamin A deficiency; a leading preventable cause of childhood blindness worldwide.

Check yourself

1. A supplement label promises "vitamins for energy." Explain, using the energy yields of the macronutrients, why that claim is physiologically misleading.
Vitamins supply 0 kcal/g; only carbohydrate (4 kcal/g), protein (4 kcal/g), fat (9 kcal/g), and alcohol (7 kcal/g) yield energy. B vitamins act as coenzymes that let the body release energy from those fuels, so correcting a real deficiency can restore energy — but adding vitamins on top of adequacy adds no calories and no extra capacity.
2. Name the four fat-soluble vitamins and give three consequences of their solubility that differ from the water-soluble vitamins.
A, D, E, and K. They require dietary fat and bile for absorption; they are stored in liver and adipose tissue rather than excreted, so deficiency takes months to years to appear; and because they accumulate, toxicity from concentrated supplements is a real hazard. A fourth: fat malabsorption threatens them long before it affects water-soluble vitamins.
3. Distinguish preformed vitamin A from provitamin A carotenoids, and explain why the RAE unit exists.
Preformed vitamin A (retinol, retinyl esters) comes from animal-source foods; provitamin A carotenoids such as beta-carotene come from plants and must be converted. Conversion is partial and variable, so retinol activity equivalents standardize them: 1 µg RAE = 1 µg retinol = 12 µg dietary beta-carotene = 24 µg other provitamin A carotenoids.
4. Why is preformed vitamin A treated with particular caution in pregnancy, and why are food carotenoids not treated the same way?
Preformed vitamin A is teratogenic in excess — high supplemental intakes in early pregnancy are associated with birth defects — and the adult UL is 3,000 µg/day. Carotenoids from food are converted too slowly to produce hypervitaminosis; very high intakes cause only carotenodermia, a harmless reversible yellow-orange skin tint.
5. Trace vitamin D from skin or food to its active form, and state the adult RDA including how it changes with age.
UVB converts 7-dehydrocholesterol in skin to cholecalciferol (D3); the liver hydroxylates it to 25-hydroxyvitamin D (the form measured in blood), and the kidney converts that to 1,25-dihydroxyvitamin D, the active hormone. RDA is 15 µg (600 IU)/day for ages 1–70, including pregnancy and lactation, and 20 µg (800 IU)/day for adults over 70. Remember 1 µg = 40 IU; the adult UL is 100 µg (4,000 IU)/day.
6. Contrast the strength of evidence for vitamin D's skeletal effects versus its claimed non-skeletal effects.
Strong: vitamin D prevents and treats rickets and osteomalacia, and is essential for calcium absorption and bone mineralization. Preliminary and largely unsupported: claims that supplementing already-replete people prevents cancer, cardiovascular disease, diabetes, or respiratory infection looked promising in observational data but did not hold up in large randomized trials.
7. Vitamin K deficiency bleeding is a newborn concern specifically. Give three reasons why, and name the enzyme vitamin K serves.
Placental transfer of vitamin K is limited, human milk is low in it, and the newborn gut is not yet colonized with menaquinone-producing bacteria. Vitamin K is the cofactor for gamma-glutamyl carboxylase, which carboxylates clotting factors II, VII, IX, and X, proteins C and S, and bone proteins such as osteocalcin.
8. Match each classic deficiency disease to its vitamin and its cardinal features: beriberi, pellagra, and Wernicke encephalopathy.
Beriberi = thiamin (B1): wet form with high-output heart failure and edema, dry form with peripheral neuropathy. Pellagra = niacin (B3): dermatitis, diarrhea, dementia, death. Wernicke encephalopathy = thiamin: confusion, eye-movement abnormalities, ataxia. Wernicke is classically associated with chronic heavy alcohol use and can progress to Korsakoff syndrome, a largely irreversible memory disorder.
9. Folate and vitamin B12 deficiency both cause megaloblastic anemia. Explain why generous folic acid intake is a problem in undiagnosed B12 deficiency.
Folic acid can correct the anemia of B12 deficiency while the neurological damage — subacute combined degeneration, with numbness, gait unsteadiness, and cognitive change — continues and may become irreversible. This masking effect is why the UL for folic acid from supplements and fortified foods is 1,000 µg/day for adults, and why macrocytic anemia should be evaluated for B12 by a clinician rather than self-treated.
10. State the periconceptional folic acid recommendation, who it applies to, and why the timing is so specific.
400 µg/day of folic acid from fortified foods or a supplement, in addition to food folate, for anyone who could become pregnant — started before conception. The neural tube closes in the first weeks of gestation, usually before a pregnancy is recognized, so starting after a positive test is too late. The folate RDA itself rises to 600 µg DFE/day in pregnancy. This is among the strongest single-nutrient findings in nutrition.
11. Describe two features of scurvy and explain each in terms of vitamin C's biochemical role. Then state the adult RDA by sex.
Any two: bleeding swollen gums and easy bruising reflect weak collagen in gingiva and vessel walls; poor wound healing and reopening of old wounds reflect the inability to hydroxylate and cross-link new collagen; perifollicular hemorrhage and corkscrew hairs reflect fragile capillaries and abnormal follicles. Vitamin C keeps the iron in prolyl and lysyl hydroxylase reduced so proline and lysine residues can be hydroxylated, stabilizing the collagen triple helix. RDA: 90 mg/day for adult men, 75 mg/day for adult women, +35 mg/day for people who smoke.
12. You want to retain as much vitamin C and thiamin as possible when cooking vegetables, and you also want good carotenoid absorption. What do you change, and why is "water-soluble means harmless" a flawed shortcut?
Steam, microwave, or roast rather than boiling and draining; keep and serve any cooking liquid; skip baking soda, which destroys thiamin and vitamin C at alkaline pH; and add a little oil plus chop or gently cook carotenoid-rich vegetables, since breaking cell walls and providing fat both increase carotenoid uptake. The shortcut fails because several water-soluble vitamins have real upper limits: B6 above 100 mg/day can cause sensory peripheral neuropathy, supplemental niacin above 35 mg/day causes flushing, and folic acid above 1,000 µg/day can mask B12 deficiency.
Nutrition · Chapter 7

Minerals & Water

Everything you have studied so far — carbohydrate and protein at 4 kilocalories per gram, fat at 9, alcohol at 7 — burns. Minerals and water do not. They yield zero kilocalories per gram, and yet nothing in the body works without them: no heartbeat, no nerve signal, no bone, no thyroid hormone, no oxygen delivery. Minerals are simple inorganic elements the body cannot make and cannot destroy, so they must arrive in food and leave in urine, sweat, and stool. Water is the medium all of it happens in, and it is the nutrient a person runs out of fastest. This chapter walks through the major minerals, the trace minerals, the special case of iron, and the fluid-and-electrolyte system that keeps blood pressure and cell volume where they belong.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Distinguish major minerals from trace minerals and explain why neither yields energy.
  • State the main function, a reference intake with its population and units, and the deficiency or toxicity picture for calcium, phosphorus, magnesium, sodium, potassium, and chloride.
  • Do the same for the trace minerals iron, zinc, iodine, selenium, and fluoride.
  • Explain bioavailability and name specific dietary enhancers and inhibitors of mineral absorption.
  • Contrast heme and nonheme iron, describe how absorption is regulated, and sequence the stages from depleted stores to iron-deficiency anemia.
  • Describe the functions of body water, account for daily fluid balance, and cite the Adequate Intake for total water by sex.
  • Explain how the sodium–potassium pump, osmosis, and hormones distribute fluid between compartments.
  • Summarize what the evidence does and does not support about sodium, potassium, and blood pressure, separating strong findings from preliminary ones.

Section 1Major minerals

What a mineral is, and what it is not

A mineral is a single inorganic element — calcium, iron, zinc — that the body needs but cannot synthesize. This makes minerals fundamentally different from the vitamins in the previous chapter. A vitamin is a complex organic molecule that heat, light, oxygen, or long storage can destroy. A mineral is an element: you cannot break calcium down into something that is not calcium. Minerals survive cooking, survive the oven, and survive being burned to ash. What they can do is dissolve, which is why boiling vegetables and pouring off the water leaches potassium and magnesium down the drain.

Because minerals are not oxidized for fuel, they supply 0 kcal per gram. Their value is structural and regulatory: they build the crystal lattice of bone, carry electrical charge across membranes, sit at the active site of enzymes, and hold the osmotic pressure that decides where water goes.

Nutritionists split minerals by how much the body needs, not by how important they are. A major mineral (or macromineral) is one required in amounts greater than 100 mg per day, or present in the body in amounts above about 5 grams. A trace mineral is required in smaller amounts — often milligrams or micrograms. A trace mineral is not a minor mineral: iodine deficiency is measured in micrograms and causes goiter and impaired brain development.

The structural three: calcium, phosphorus, magnesium

Calcium is the most abundant mineral in the body, and roughly 99 percent of it is locked into bone and teeth as hydroxyapatite. The remaining one percent, circulating in blood and inside cells, does the exciting work: muscle contraction, nerve transmission, hormone secretion, and blood clotting. The RDA is 1,000 mg per day for adults 19–50, rising to 1,200 mg per day for women 51 and older and men 71 and older, and it is highest of all in adolescence — 1,300 mg per day for ages 9–18, when the skeleton is being built. Pregnancy in adults does not raise the requirement (still 1,000 mg for ages 19–50), because absorption efficiency increases instead; pregnant and lactating teenagers stay at 1,300 mg. The Tolerable Upper Intake Level is 2,500 mg per day for adults 19–50 and 2,000 mg for adults 51 and older.

Phosphorus partners with calcium in bone, but it is also in every ATP molecule, every phospholipid membrane, and the backbone of DNA and RNA. The RDA is 700 mg per day for adults and 1,250 mg per day for ages 9–18. Dietary deficiency is rare because phosphorus is abundant in protein-containing foods and in phosphate additives; the clinical problem is more often the opposite, when failing kidneys cannot clear phosphate.

Magnesium is a cofactor for more than 300 enzymes, including every reaction that uses ATP — ATP is biologically active as an Mg-ATP complex. About half of body magnesium is in bone. The RDA differs by sex and shifts at age 31: 400 mg per day for men 19–30 and 420 mg for men 31 and older; 310 mg for women 19–30 and 320 mg for women 31 and older, with roughly 40 mg more in pregnancy. Note a quirk: the UL of 350 mg per day applies only to supplemental magnesium, not to magnesium from food, because it is set by the laxative effect of magnesium salts rather than by any harm from food.

The salts of body fluid: sodium, potassium, chloride

These three are the body's principal electrolytes — minerals that dissociate into charged ions in solution and thereby conduct electricity, hold water in place, and maintain acid–base balance. Sodium and chloride dominate the fluid outside cells; potassium dominates the fluid inside them. Because these ions determine osmotic pressure, they determine blood volume, and therefore blood pressure. Section 5 takes that story further.

Their reference values are set as Adequate Intakes rather than RDAs, because the evidence was not strong enough to define an average requirement. For adults, sodium AI is 1,500 mg per day, chloride AI is 2,300 mg per day for ages 19–50 (falling to 2,000 mg at 51–70 and 1,800 mg at 71 and older), and potassium AI is 3,400 mg per day for men 19 and older and 2,600 mg per day for women 19 and older, with 2,900 mg in pregnancy. In its 2019 review, the National Academies replaced the sodium UL with a Chronic Disease Risk Reduction Intake of 2,300 mg per day — a threshold above which reducing intake is expected to reduce chronic disease risk. Average intake in the United States runs near 3,400 mg per day.

MineralAdult reference intakeMain jobsFood contributorsDeficiency / excess
CalciumRDA 1,000 mg/d (19–50); 1,200 mg/d (women 51+, men 71+); 1,300 mg/d (9–18)Bone mineral; muscle contraction; nerve signaling; clottingDairy, fortified plant beverages, tofu set with calcium, kale, bok choy, canned fish with bonesLow intake over years → reduced peak bone mass, osteoporosis risk; very high supplemental intake → hypercalcemia, kidney stones in susceptible people
PhosphorusRDA 700 mg/dBone; ATP; phospholipids; DNA/RNA; bufferProtein foods, dairy, legumes, whole grains, phosphate additivesDietary deficiency rare; excess is a problem chiefly in kidney disease
MagnesiumRDA 400/420 mg/d men (19–30 / 31+); 310/320 mg/d womenCofactor for 300+ enzymes; Mg-ATP; nerve and muscle functionNuts, seeds, legumes, whole grains, leafy greens, some mineral watersSevere deficiency → neuromuscular irritability, tremor, arrhythmia, resistant hypokalemia and hypocalcemia; supplemental excess → diarrhea
SodiumAI 1,500 mg/d; CDRR 2,300 mg/dMain extracellular cation; fluid volume; nerve impulse; nutrient co-transportPackaged and restaurant foods, breads, cured meats, cheese, table saltDietary deficiency essentially unknown; hyponatremia arises from losses or excess water; high intake raises blood pressure in salt-sensitive people
PotassiumAI 3,400 mg/d men; 2,600 mg/d womenMain intracellular cation; membrane potential; cardiac rhythmPotatoes, beans, lentils, bananas, oranges, tomatoes, yogurt, leafy greens, fishHypokalemia usually from vomiting, diarrhea, or diuretics → weakness, arrhythmia; hyperkalemia is a risk in kidney disease
ChlorideAI 2,300 mg/d (19–50)Extracellular anion; gastric hydrochloric acid; acid–base balanceTable salt and salted foods (accompanies sodium)Heavy vomiting → hypochloremic metabolic alkalosis
A normal blood calcium says nothing about calcium intake. Serum calcium is defended within a narrow range by parathyroid hormone, calcitriol, and calcitonin — and the reserve they draw on is bone. A person can eat far too little calcium for years with a perfectly normal blood level the entire time, because the skeleton is quietly paying the bill. This is why bone health is judged by intake history and bone density, not by a chemistry panel.
Interactive Tap each of the seven major minerals to explore its role, food sources, deficiency signs, and reference intake, with a live bar chart comparing daily needs. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Minerals are inorganic elements that supply 0 kcal/g; they are indestructible by heat but can leach into cooking water.
  • Major minerals are needed in amounts above 100 mg/day: calcium, phosphorus, magnesium, sodium, potassium, chloride (plus sulfur).
  • Calcium RDA is 1,000 mg/d for adults 19–50, 1,200 mg/d for women 51+ and men 71+, and 1,300 mg/d for ages 9–18.
  • Sodium, potassium, and chloride carry Adequate Intakes, not RDAs; sodium also carries a CDRR of 2,300 mg/d.

Section 2Trace minerals

Small amounts, large consequences

Trace minerals are needed in milligram or microgram quantities, and the total amount of some of them in an adult body would not cover a fingernail. Their smallness is deceptive. Most trace minerals work as the functional core of a protein: iron sits in the heme group of hemoglobin, zinc holds the shape of hundreds of transcription factors, iodine is built directly into thyroid hormone, and selenium is incorporated as the amino acid selenocysteine into glutathione peroxidase and the enzymes that activate thyroid hormone. Remove a few micrograms and an entire hormone axis stalls.

Because the working range is narrow, trace minerals are also the group where the gap between "not enough" and "too much" is smallest. Selenium is the classic example: the RDA is 55 mcg per day for adults, and the UL is 400 mcg per day — barely seven-fold. Some vitamins have a far wider margin (vitamin E, RDA 15 mg against a UL of 1,000 mg), though others are tight as well (niacin, folate), so the rule is not absolute — the point is that for most trace minerals there is very little room above the requirement.

Five worth knowing well

Iron carries oxygen in hemoglobin and myoglobin and drives electron transport. Its RDA is strongly sex- and life-stage-dependent: 8 mg per day for men 19 and older, 18 mg per day for women 19–50, 8 mg per day for women 51 and older, and 27 mg per day in pregnancy. Section 3 is devoted to it.

Zinc participates in more than 300 enzymes and in the zinc-finger motifs that let transcription factors grip DNA. The RDA is 11 mg per day for men and 8 mg per day for women, rising to 11 mg in pregnancy and 12 mg during lactation; the UL is 40 mg per day. Deficiency shows up as growth faltering in children, delayed wound healing, impaired immune response, loss of taste sensitivity, hair loss, and a characteristic rash. Chronic high-dose supplementation causes a copper deficiency, because zinc induces the intestinal protein that traps copper.

Iodine exists for one purpose: thyroid hormone. The RDA is 150 mcg per day for adults, 220 mcg per day in pregnancy, and 290 mcg per day during lactation, with a UL of 1,100 mcg per day for adults. Deficiency enlarges the thyroid into a goiter and causes hypothyroidism; deficiency during pregnancy impairs fetal brain development and has historically been the leading preventable cause of intellectual disability worldwide. Excess iodine can also disturb thyroid function, so the dose–response curve is U-shaped.

Selenium defends cells against oxidative damage through glutathione peroxidase and activates thyroid hormone through the deiodinases. RDA is 55 mcg per day for adults (60 mcg in pregnancy, 70 mcg during lactation), UL 400 mcg per day. Deficiency is regional and tied to selenium-poor soils; in parts of China it produced Keshan disease, a cardiomyopathy. Toxicity, called selenosis, produces brittle nails, hair loss, garlic-smelling breath, and gastrointestinal upset.

Fluoride is the outlier: it is not required for life, but it measurably strengthens tooth enamel against acid demineralization. The AI is 4 mg per day for men 19 and older and 3 mg per day for women, with a UL of 10 mg per day for adults. The evidence that fluoride reduces dental caries is strong and long-standing. Too much during the years when permanent teeth are forming produces dental fluorosis — white flecking to brown mottling of enamel — which is why fluoride intake in young children is watched closely.

Bioavailability: the label number is not the absorbed number

Bioavailability is the fraction of an ingested nutrient that is actually absorbed and usable. For minerals it can swing enormously depending on what else is on the plate. Phytate (phytic acid) in whole grains, legumes, nuts, and seeds binds zinc, iron, calcium, and magnesium; oxalate in spinach, rhubarb, and beet greens binds calcium so tightly that spinach is a poor calcium source despite a high calcium number on paper. Working the other way, vitamin C sharply increases nonheme iron absorption, and soaking, sprouting, fermenting, and leavening with yeast all reduce phytate and raise mineral availability — which is a large part of why traditional food preparation methods around the world involve exactly those steps.

Minerals also compete. Large supplemental doses of one divalent mineral can crowd out another at shared intestinal transporters — zinc against copper, calcium against iron, iron against zinc. This competition is a supplement-dose phenomenon; it is not something that ordinary mixed meals produce.

Trace mineralAdult reference intakeAdult ULKey roleDeficiencyExcess
IronRDA 8 mg/d men; 18 mg/d women 19–50; 8 mg/d women 51+; 27 mg/d pregnancy45 mg/dHemoglobin, myoglobin, electron transportDepleted stores → iron-deficiency anemia (microcytic, hypochromic)Hereditary hemochromatosis; acute overdose is a leading poisoning of young children
ZincRDA 11 mg/d men; 8 mg/d women; 11 mg/d pregnancy40 mg/d300+ enzymes; zinc-finger transcription factors; immune functionGrowth faltering, poor wound healing, impaired immunity, taste loss, rashNausea; induced copper deficiency with chronic high doses
IodineRDA 150 mcg/d adults; 220 mcg/d pregnancy; 290 mcg/d lactation1,100 mcg/dThyroid hormone synthesisGoiter, hypothyroidism; impaired fetal neurodevelopmentThyroid dysfunction — the curve is U-shaped
SeleniumRDA 55 mcg/d adults; 60 mcg/d pregnancy; 70 mcg/d lactation400 mcg/dGlutathione peroxidase; thyroid hormone activationKeshan cardiomyopathy in selenium-poor regionsSelenosis: brittle nails, hair loss, garlic breath
FluorideAI 4 mg/d men; 3 mg/d women10 mg/dStrengthens enamel against demineralizationMore dental cariesDental fluorosis during tooth formation; skeletal fluorosis at very high chronic intakes
Two Brazil nuts can exceed a day's selenium several times over. Selenium content in Brazil nuts depends entirely on the soil where the tree grew. A single nut commonly supplies somewhere around 70 to 90 mcg — already above the 55 mcg adult RDA — but measured values span a very wide range, from a few micrograms to several hundred, so no fixed per-nut number is trustworthy. That single fact teaches the whole trace-mineral lesson: these nutrients are needed in micrograms, the food supply is not uniform, and "more" is not a safety margin. It is also a reminder that concentration in a food is not a moral property of that food — it is soil chemistry.
Interactive Add servings of everyday foods and watch iron, zinc, iodine, selenium, and fluoride recompute live against their reference intakes and upper limits — then tap any mineral to see what carried it. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Trace minerals are needed in mg or mcg amounts and usually act inside a specific protein — heme, a zinc finger, thyroid hormone, selenocysteine.
  • The gap between deficiency and toxicity is narrow: selenium RDA 55 mcg/d versus UL 400 mcg/d.
  • Iodine deficiency causes goiter and, in pregnancy, impaired fetal brain development; needs rise to 220 mcg/d in pregnancy.
  • Bioavailability is modified by phytate, oxalate, vitamin C, food preparation, and competition between minerals at shared transporters.

Section 3Iron and anemia

Two forms of dietary iron

Iron arrives in food in two chemically distinct forms, and they behave so differently that they are absorbed by separate pathways. Heme iron is iron already held inside a porphyrin ring, released from the hemoglobin and myoglobin of meat, poultry, and fish. It is taken up intact by the intestinal cell and absorbed at roughly 15 to 35 percent — efficiently, and largely indifferent to whatever else is in the meal. Nonheme iron is free ionic iron, and it is what you get from plants, eggs, dairy, and enriched or fortified grains. Its absorption ranges from about 2 to 20 percent and is exquisitely sensitive to the rest of the meal.

Most dietary iron in most diets is nonheme, even in people who eat meat. That is why the modifiers matter so much. Vitamin C reduces ferric iron to the absorbable ferrous form and holds it in solution; a citrus fruit, tomatoes, or peppers eaten with a bean dish can multiply the iron absorbed from it. A separate, still incompletely characterized enhancer in meat, poultry, and fish — historically called the MFP factor — increases nonheme absorption from the same meal. Pulling the other direction are phytates in whole grains and legumes, polyphenols and tannins in tea and coffee, calcium in large supplemental doses, and oxalates. Because the DRI committee accounted for this, the iron requirement for people eating no meat, poultry, or fish is estimated at roughly 1.8 times the standard RDA.

Absorption is the control valve

Here is the fact that organizes all of iron physiology: the body has no regulated route for excreting iron. Small amounts are lost in shed skin and gut cells, in menstrual blood, and in any bleeding, but there is no hormone that tells the kidney to dump excess iron. Iron balance is therefore controlled almost entirely at the point of absorption.

The gatekeeper is a liver hormone called hepcidin. When body iron is plentiful — or when inflammation is present — hepcidin rises, binds the export protein ferroportin on intestinal and macrophage membranes, and degrades it, so absorbed iron stays trapped in the mucosal cell and is shed when that cell sloughs off. When iron is scarce, hepcidin falls, ferroportin persists, and more iron enters the blood, where transferrin carries it to the marrow. Storage is as ferritin, mostly in liver, spleen, and marrow.

The same logic explains two clinical realities. First, iron supplements taken in large doses are poorly and increasingly inefficiently absorbed, because a big dose transiently drives hepcidin up. Second, in chronic inflammatory disease, hepcidin stays high and iron is locked away in storage even though the marrow needs it — the anemia of inflammation, which is a different problem from dietary iron deficiency and is not fixed the same way.

From depleted stores to anemia

Iron deficiency is not an on/off state. It develops in stages, and anemia is the last one, which means a person can be meaningfully iron-depleted with a completely normal hemoglobin.

StageWhat is happeningWhat changes in the labsSymptoms
1. Depleted storesFerritin stores are being drawn downSerum ferritin falls; hemoglobin still normalUsually none
2. Early functional deficiencyTransport iron drops; the marrow starts to run shortTransferrin saturation falls, total iron-binding capacity rises, erythrocyte protoporphyrin risesFatigue may begin
3. Iron-deficiency anemiaRed cells are made small and pale for lack of ironHemoglobin and hematocrit fall; MCV below about 80 fL (microcytic), hypochromicFatigue, pallor, breathlessness on exertion, cold intolerance, sometimes pica

The groups at greatest risk follow directly from the physiology: menstruating people, pregnancy (RDA 27 mg per day), infants and toddlers, adolescents in a growth spurt, frequent blood donors, people with gastrointestinal blood loss, and people eating no meat, poultry, or fish. Iron deficiency remains the most common single nutrient deficiency worldwide.

Enhances nonheme iron absorptionInhibits nonheme iron absorption
Vitamin C in the same mealPhytates in whole grains, legumes, nuts, seeds
MFP factor from meat, poultry, fishPolyphenols and tannins in tea, coffee, some herbs
Low body iron stores (low hepcidin)High-dose calcium supplements taken with the meal
Acid gastric environmentOxalates; high-dose zinc supplements; high hepcidin from inflammation
Not all anemia is iron deficiency, and not all iron deficiency is dietary. Anemia from vitamin B12 or folate deficiency is macrocytic — the cells are too large, not too small. And in adults, newly discovered iron deficiency is a finding to be explained, not simply fed, because blood loss from the gastrointestinal tract is a common cause. Ferritin is also an acute-phase reactant, so inflammation can push it into the normal range while stores are genuinely empty. Interpreting any of this belongs to a clinician; the point here is that "tired plus low iron" is a starting question, not a conclusion.
Interactive Set heme and non-heme iron with sliders, flip enhancers and inhibitors on and off, and watch the milligrams that cross the gut wall recalculate live on a diagram of the enterocyte. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Heme iron (meat, poultry, fish) absorbs at about 15–35 percent; nonheme iron at about 2–20 percent and is heavily modified by the meal.
  • The body cannot regulate iron excretion, so absorption is the control point, governed by hepcidin and ferroportin.
  • Deficiency progresses: depleted stores (low ferritin) → early functional deficiency → microcytic, hypochromic anemia.
  • Iron RDA is 18 mg/d for women 19–50, 8 mg/d for men and for women 51+, and 27 mg/d in pregnancy; the adult UL is 45 mg/d.

Section 4Water

What water does

Water is the nutrient the body needs in the largest quantity and can survive without for the shortest time — days, against weeks without food. It makes up roughly 50 to 70 percent of body weight, and the variation is mostly a matter of tissue composition rather than anything else: lean tissue is about 73 percent water while adipose tissue holds much less, so people with more lean mass have a higher percentage. Infants are the wettest, near 70–75 percent, and the proportion declines gradually across the lifespan, which is one reason dehydration is a more serious threat at the two ends of life.

Its jobs are so basic they are easy to overlook. Water is the universal solvent in which nearly every metabolic reaction occurs, and it is a direct participant: every hydrolysis reaction in digestion consumes a water molecule. It is the transport medium — blood plasma, lymph, urine. It regulates temperature, because water has an unusually high heat capacity and heat of vaporization, so evaporating a modest volume of sweat sheds a large amount of heat. It lubricates and cushions as synovial fluid, cerebrospinal fluid, mucus, tears, and amniotic fluid. And because it carries the electrolytes, it maintains blood volume and therefore blood pressure.

Body water is divided into two compartments. Roughly two-thirds is intracellular fluid (ICF), inside cells, and one-third is extracellular fluid (ECF) — the interstitial fluid between cells plus the plasma inside vessels. Water moves freely between them by osmosis, following whichever side has the higher concentration of dissolved particles. Nothing pumps water; water always follows solute.

Where it comes in, where it goes out

Intake has three sources. Beverages of all kinds supply roughly 80 percent of total water intake, and this includes coffee and tea — the mild diuretic effect of caffeine at ordinary intakes does not outweigh the fluid the drink itself provides. Food supplies most of the remainder, since fruits and vegetables can be 85–95 percent water and even bread is about a third water. A small final contribution is metabolic water, roughly 250–350 mL per day, produced as a byproduct when carbohydrate, fat, and protein are oxidized.

Output has four routes. Urine is the only one that is finely regulated, and the kidney must produce a minimum of roughly 500 mL per day — the obligatory water loss — simply to carry out the day's urea, sodium, and other solutes. Insensible losses through skin and exhaled air run near 700–1,000 mL per day and continue whether you notice them or not. Sweat is small at rest and enormous during heat and exertion, sometimes exceeding a liter per hour. Feces account for only 100–200 mL in health, but that figure rises dramatically in diarrheal illness, which is why diarrhea is a leading cause of dehydration deaths in young children worldwide.

Two hormone systems keep the ledger balanced. Osmoreceptors in the hypothalamus sense rising blood concentration and trigger thirst plus release of antidiuretic hormone (ADH, vasopressin), which tells the kidney to reabsorb water. Falling blood volume or pressure activates the renin–angiotensin–aldosterone system, and aldosterone makes the kidney retain sodium — water follows the sodium osmotically.

How much? The honest answer

The Adequate Intake for total water — from beverages plus food combined — is 3.7 L per day for men 19 and older and 2.7 L per day for women 19 and older. About 3.0 L and 2.2 L of those respective totals come from beverages, with food supplying the balance. In pregnancy the total-water AI is 3.0 L per day, and during lactation it is 3.8 L per day.

Two cautions about those numbers. First, they are population Adequate Intakes drawn from what generally healthy people consume; they are not a target every individual must hit. Real requirements swing widely with air temperature, humidity, altitude, physical activity, fever, illness, and body size. Second, the familiar "eight glasses a day" rule is not a DRI and has no clear origin in the evidence; eight 8-ounce glasses is about 1.9 L, which is in the right neighborhood for beverages for some women and low for many men, and it ignores food entirely. For most healthy adults, thirst plus drinking with meals tracks needs well. The important exception is older adults, in whom the thirst response is genuinely blunted, so thirst becomes a less reliable signal.

Water in (typical adult, mL/day)AmountWater outAmount
Beverages~1,500–2,500Urine (regulated; minimum ~500)~1,000–2,000
Food~700–1,000Insensible: skin and lungs~700–1,000
Metabolic water from oxidation~250–350Sweat (at rest)~100–500; heat or exertion can add >1,000 per hour
——Feces (much higher in diarrhea)~100–200
Too much plain water is also a real problem. Exercise-associated hyponatremia occurs when endurance athletes drink large volumes of plain water over hours, diluting blood sodium faster than the kidney can excrete the excess. Symptoms — headache, nausea, confusion — look deceptively like dehydration, and it can be fatal. The physiology is worth understanding: hydration is a balance problem, not a maximization problem, and "drink as much as you possibly can" is not better advice than "drink to thirst."
Interactive A live 24-hour fluid-balance calculator that weighs water in (beverages, food moisture, metabolic water from burned macros) against water out (urine, breath, skin, sweat, stool) and shows the kidney adjusting urine to keep the day even. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Water is 50–70 percent of body weight, about two-thirds intracellular and one-third extracellular; water moves by osmosis, following solute.
  • Functions: solvent and reactant, transport, temperature regulation, lubrication and cushioning, blood volume.
  • The total-water AI is 3.7 L/day for men and 2.7 L/day for women (3.0 L in pregnancy, 3.8 L in lactation), including water from food.
  • Thirst and ADH defend concentration; aldosterone defends volume. Thirst is blunted in older adults.

Section 5Electrolytes and fluid balance

Ions, compartments, and the pump that keeps them apart

An electrolyte is a mineral salt that dissociates into ions in water. Sodium (Na+) and chloride (Cl−) are the dominant ions of extracellular fluid; potassium (K+), magnesium, and phosphate dominate inside the cell. Plasma sodium is normally about 135–145 mEq/L and plasma potassium about 3.5–5.0 mEq/L — and notice how narrow that potassium window is, which is exactly why potassium disturbances threaten the heart.

That separation is not passive; it is bought with energy. The sodium–potassium pump (Na+/K+-ATPase) in every cell membrane exports three sodium ions and imports two potassium ions per molecule of ATP. Running this pump consumes a substantial share of resting energy expenditure — estimates vary by tissue and by method, but it is a continuous, non-negotiable cost. The payoff is the membrane potential that makes nerve impulses and muscle contraction possible, and the sodium gradient that powers the intestinal co-transporters for glucose and amino acids — which, incidentally, is the physiological principle behind oral rehydration solution containing both salt and sugar.

Because water follows solute by osmosis, the distribution of these ions decides the distribution of fluid. Retain sodium and the extracellular compartment expands; lose sodium and it shrinks. This is the mechanical link from a mineral to a vital sign.

Sodium, potassium, and blood pressure

This is one of the most heavily studied questions in nutrition, so it is worth separating what is well established from what is still being worked out.

Strong evidence. Reducing sodium intake lowers blood pressure on average. This comes from many randomized controlled feeding trials, pooled in multiple meta-analyses, and the effect is consistent. The magnitude is modest at the individual level and larger in specific groups: people who already have hypertension, older adults, and Black adults tend to show bigger reductions — a trait called salt sensitivity. The DASH–Sodium trial demonstrated that lowering sodium and following an eating pattern rich in fruits, vegetables, and low-fat dairy each lowered blood pressure, and that the two effects added together.

Moderate evidence. Higher potassium intake also lowers blood pressure in randomized trials, but the base here is thinner than for sodium and is usually graded moderate rather than strong: the effect is clearest in adults who already have hypertension and whose sodium intake is high, and is small or inconsistent in adults with normal blood pressure. Tellingly, when the National Academies reviewed potassium in 2019 they judged the chronic-disease evidence insufficient to set a CDRR — unlike sodium, which did receive one. The direction of effect is nonetheless consistent with the idea that the two ions act on the same system from opposite directions.

Preliminary evidence. Whether lowering sodium reduces heart attacks and strokes — not just blood pressure — rests on a thinner base, because the trials required are long and expensive. Long-term follow-up of the Trials of Hypertension Prevention found fewer cardiovascular events in the sodium-reduction groups, and a large trial of potassium-enriched salt substitute in rural China reported fewer strokes and deaths. Those are encouraging, but they are a small number of trials in specific populations, and generalizing them to everyone would be overstating the case. Observational work suggesting that the sodium-to-potassium ratio predicts risk better than either ion alone is similarly plausible but not proven, since observational data cannot separate the ratio from everything else that accompanies a potassium-rich diet.

What is genuinely uncertain. Whether very low sodium intakes carry risk of their own is still argued, and the studies on both sides have measurement problems — estimating a person's habitual sodium intake accurately is notoriously hard. Honest teaching says so.

Where the sodium actually comes from

Roughly 70 percent of sodium in the United States food supply is already in packaged, processed, and restaurant foods before anyone reaches for a salt shaker — in bread, cured meats, cheese, soups, sauces, and savory snacks. Table salt added at home and at the table is a minority contributor. One teaspoon of table salt contains about 2,300 mg of sodium, since salt is roughly 40 percent sodium by weight, which is why food labels list sodium and not salt.

That distribution matters for how the topic should be framed. Sodium intake is largely a property of the food supply rather than a personal failing, and reformulation by manufacturers moves population intake far more than individual vigilance does. Salt is also not a villain: it is the vehicle for iodine in iodized salt, it makes food palatable, and food that people do not want to eat has no nutritional value at all. The useful skill is reading a Nutrition Facts label and knowing roughly where a day's total lands — not anxiety about any single meal.

IonWhere it dominatesTypical plasma rangeReference intake (adults)Relationship to blood pressure
SodiumExtracellular fluid135–145 mEq/LAI 1,500 mg/d; CDRR 2,300 mg/dHigher intake raises blood pressure on average; effect largest in salt-sensitive groups (strong evidence)
PotassiumIntracellular fluid3.5–5.0 mEq/LAI 3,400 mg/d men; 2,600 mg/d womenHigher intake lowers blood pressure, most in those with hypertension and high sodium intake (moderate evidence — smaller trial base than sodium)
ChlorideExtracellular fluid98–107 mEq/LAI 2,300 mg/d (19–50)Accompanies sodium; sodium chloride raises pressure more than sodium with other anions
Na/K ratio——No DRI setLower ratio associated with lower risk (observational — preliminary)
Potassium-based salt substitutes are not universally safe. Replacing some sodium chloride with potassium chloride lowers sodium intake and raises potassium intake at once, and in one large trial it reduced strokes. But potassium is cleared by the kidney, and people with reduced kidney function, or taking potassium-sparing diuretics, ACE inhibitors, or angiotensin receptor blockers, can accumulate dangerous levels. This is the clearest illustration of why nutrition education stops where individual clinical care begins: the same intervention that helps one population can harm another, and only a clinician who knows a specific person can tell which is which. Nothing in this chapter is medical or dietary advice.
Interactive Set sodium and potassium directly and watch a tilting balance scale and an illustrative blood-pressure gauge redraw live — showing how the Na:K ratio, not either mineral alone, tracks with blood pressure. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Sodium and chloride rule the extracellular fluid; potassium rules the intracellular fluid, a separation maintained by the Na+/K+-ATPase (3 Na out, 2 K in, per ATP).
  • Water follows solute osmotically, so sodium balance sets extracellular volume and therefore blood pressure.
  • Strong evidence: lowering sodium lowers blood pressure. Moderate evidence: raising potassium lowers it too, mainly in people with hypertension. Weaker evidence: the size of the resulting reduction in cardiovascular events.
  • About 70 percent of dietary sodium comes from packaged and restaurant foods; 1 teaspoon of salt is about 2,300 mg sodium.

The chapter in one breath

  • Minerals are inorganic elements that supply 0 kcal/g (versus carbohydrate and protein at 4, fat at 9, alcohol at 7) yet are required for structure, signaling, and enzyme function.
  • Major minerals — calcium, phosphorus, magnesium, sodium, potassium, chloride — are needed above 100 mg/day; calcium RDA is 1,000 mg/d for adults 19–50, 1,200 mg/d for women 51+ and men 71+, and 1,300 mg/d for ages 9–18.
  • Trace minerals work in mcg-to-mg amounts inside specific proteins, and their deficiency-to-toxicity window is narrow — selenium RDA 55 mcg/d against a UL of 400 mcg/d.
  • Bioavailability is the real variable: phytate, oxalate, tannins, and mineral–mineral competition reduce absorption; vitamin C, MFP factor, and soaking, sprouting, or fermenting increase it.
  • Iron comes as well-absorbed heme (15–35 percent) and meal-sensitive nonheme (2–20 percent); because iron excretion is unregulated, hepcidin controls balance at absorption.
  • Iron deficiency runs depleted stores → early functional deficiency → microcytic, hypochromic anemia, so a normal hemoglobin does not rule out depletion — and not every anemia is iron.
  • Water is 50–70 percent of body weight and serves as solvent, transport, coolant, cushion, and reactant; total-water AI is 3.7 L/day for men and 2.7 L/day for women, including food.
  • Electrolytes set fluid distribution: lowering sodium lowers blood pressure (strong evidence), raising potassium does too but on a smaller trial base and mainly in people with hypertension (moderate evidence), while the effect of either on cardiovascular events rests on fewer trials still (weaker evidence).

ReferenceWords worth knowing

A quick reference for the terms in this chapter — skim it now, or circle back whenever a word trips you up.

Adequate Intake (AI)
A reference intake used when evidence is insufficient to set an RDA; based on observed intakes of apparently healthy groups. Sodium, potassium, chloride, fluoride, and water all use AIs.
Bioavailability
The proportion of an ingested nutrient that is actually absorbed and available for use — for minerals, heavily modified by the rest of the meal.
Chronic Disease Risk Reduction Intake (CDRR)
A reference value introduced in 2019 for sodium (2,300 mg/day for adults), marking an intake above which reduction is expected to lower chronic disease risk.
Dental fluorosis
White flecking to brown mottling of tooth enamel caused by excess fluoride during the years permanent teeth are forming.
Electrolyte
A mineral salt that dissociates into charged ions in solution, conducting electricity and governing fluid distribution; chiefly sodium, potassium, and chloride.
Extracellular fluid (ECF)
Body water outside cells — interstitial fluid plus blood plasma; about one-third of total body water, dominated by sodium and chloride.
Ferritin
The storage form of iron and the usual laboratory marker of iron stores; also an acute-phase reactant, so inflammation can falsely raise it.
Goiter
Enlargement of the thyroid gland, classically from iodine deficiency as the gland strains to make hormone without its raw material.
Heme iron
Iron bound within a porphyrin ring, found in meat, poultry, and fish; absorbed at roughly 15–35 percent and largely unaffected by other meal components.
Hepcidin
A liver hormone that controls iron absorption by degrading ferroportin; it rises with high iron stores and with inflammation, shutting the gate.
Hyponatremia
Blood sodium below the normal range, from sodium loss or from excess water; symptoms range from headache and nausea to confusion and seizures.
Insensible water loss
Unnoticed daily water loss through the skin and exhaled air, roughly 700–1,000 mL per day in an adult at rest.
Intracellular fluid (ICF)
Body water inside cells; about two-thirds of total body water, dominated by potassium, magnesium, and phosphate.
Iron-deficiency anemia
The final stage of iron depletion, in which hemoglobin falls and red cells are microcytic (MCV below about 80 fL) and hypochromic.
Major mineral (macromineral)
A mineral required in amounts greater than 100 mg per day: calcium, phosphorus, magnesium, sodium, potassium, chloride, sulfur.
Metabolic water
Water generated as a byproduct of oxidizing carbohydrate, fat, and protein — roughly 250–350 mL per day.
Nonheme iron
Free ionic iron from plants, eggs, dairy, and fortified grains; absorbed at roughly 2–20 percent and strongly modified by enhancers and inhibitors.
Phytate (phytic acid)
A phosphorus storage compound in whole grains, legumes, nuts, and seeds that binds zinc, iron, calcium, and magnesium and lowers their absorption.
Salt sensitivity
The tendency for blood pressure to change appreciably with sodium intake; more common with existing hypertension, older age, and among Black adults.
Sodium–potassium pump
The Na+/K+-ATPase, which moves three sodium ions out and two potassium ions into the cell per ATP, maintaining the membrane potential.
Tolerable Upper Intake Level (UL)
The highest daily intake unlikely to cause harm in almost all people; exceeding it raises risk of toxicity, as with selenium above 400 mcg/day.
Trace mineral
A mineral required in amounts below 100 mg per day, including iron, zinc, iodine, selenium, copper, manganese, fluoride, chromium, and molybdenum.

Check yourself

1. A student says minerals must supply some energy because they are called nutrients. Correct the statement and give the energy yields that do apply to the energy nutrients.
Minerals and water supply 0 kcal per gram. Only carbohydrate (4 kcal/g), protein (4 kcal/g), fat (9 kcal/g), and alcohol (7 kcal/g) yield energy. Minerals are inorganic elements used for structure, signaling, and enzyme function, not as fuel.
2. Define the cutoff that separates major minerals from trace minerals, and explain why the label "trace" does not mean "less important."
Major minerals are needed in amounts greater than 100 mg per day; trace minerals in smaller amounts. The division is about quantity, not importance — iodine is needed in micrograms, but deficiency causes goiter, hypothyroidism, and impaired fetal brain development.
3. State the calcium RDA for a 30-year-old adult, a 60-year-old woman, and a 15-year-old, and explain why the teenager's is highest.
1,000 mg/day at age 30; 1,200 mg/day for a woman of 60; 1,300 mg/day at age 15. Adolescence is when the skeleton is being built and peak bone mass accrued, so the requirement is highest then. Adult pregnancy does not raise the requirement, because absorption efficiency rises instead.
4. Why can someone have a chronically low calcium intake and still show a normal serum calcium?
Serum calcium is tightly defended by parathyroid hormone, calcitriol, and calcitonin, which draw on the skeleton to hold blood levels steady. The cost is paid in bone mineral over years, so a normal chemistry panel cannot confirm adequate intake — intake history and bone density can.
5. The UL for magnesium is 350 mg/day, yet the RDA for an adult man over 31 is 420 mg/day. Explain why this is not a contradiction.
The magnesium UL applies only to magnesium from supplements and medications, not from food. It is set by the osmotic laxative effect of concentrated magnesium salts, a problem that food-borne magnesium does not produce.
6. Give the RDA for iodine in a nonpregnant adult and in pregnancy, and name the consequences of deficiency at each stage.
150 mcg/day for adults, rising to 220 mcg/day in pregnancy and 290 mcg/day in lactation. Deficiency causes goiter and hypothyroidism in the adult; in pregnancy it impairs fetal brain development and has been the leading preventable cause of intellectual disability worldwide. The adult UL is 1,100 mcg/day, and excess iodine can also disturb thyroid function.
7. Selenium's RDA is 55 mcg/day and its UL is 400 mcg/day. Use that pair of numbers to explain a general principle about trace minerals.
The window between requirement and toxicity is narrow — only about seven-fold — so "more" is not a safety margin. Toxicity (selenosis) presents as brittle nails, hair loss, garlic-smelling breath, and gastrointestinal upset, while deficiency in selenium-poor regions produced Keshan cardiomyopathy.
8. Compare heme and nonheme iron by source, typical absorption, and sensitivity to the rest of the meal.
Heme iron comes from meat, poultry, and fish, absorbs at about 15–35 percent, and is largely unaffected by other foods; nonheme iron comes from plants, eggs, dairy, and fortified grains, absorbs at about 2–20 percent, and is highly sensitive to the meal. Enhancers include vitamin C and the MFP factor; inhibitors include phytates, tannins in tea and coffee, oxalates, and high-dose calcium supplements.
9. Explain why iron balance is regulated at absorption rather than at excretion, and name the hormone responsible.
The body has no regulated route for excreting iron — losses are passive, through shed cells and bleeding — so the only control point is how much enters. The liver hormone hepcidin sets that gate by degrading ferroportin; hepcidin rises with high iron stores and with inflammation, which is why chronic inflammation can lock iron away in storage.
10. Sequence the three stages of iron deficiency with the laboratory change that marks each, and explain why a normal hemoglobin does not rule out a problem.
Stage 1, depleted stores — serum ferritin falls; stage 2, early functional deficiency — transferrin saturation falls and total iron-binding capacity rises; stage 3, iron-deficiency anemia — hemoglobin and hematocrit fall with a microcytic, hypochromic picture. Anemia is the last stage, so stores can be substantially depleted while hemoglobin is still normal. Note too that macrocytic anemia points instead toward vitamin B12 or folate.
11. State the Adequate Intake for total water by sex, say what "total" includes, and explain what is wrong with treating "eight glasses a day" as the requirement.
3.7 L/day for men 19 and older and 2.7 L/day for women 19 and older, counting beverages plus the water in food (3.0 L and 2.2 L respectively from beverages). "Eight glasses" is roughly 1.9 L, is not a DRI, has no clear evidentiary origin, and ignores food entirely. Needs vary widely with heat, activity, illness, and body size, and AIs describe populations rather than individual targets.
12. Describe what the sodium–potassium pump does, and use it to explain how sodium intake connects to blood pressure. Then state which part of the sodium evidence is strong and which is weaker.
The Na+/K+-ATPase moves three sodium ions out of the cell and two potassium ions in per ATP, keeping sodium extracellular and potassium intracellular; because water follows solute by osmosis, sodium balance sets extracellular fluid volume and therefore blood pressure. Strong evidence: randomized trials consistently show that lowering sodium lowers blood pressure, with larger effects in salt-sensitive groups. Moderate evidence: raising potassium lowers blood pressure as well, but on a smaller trial base and most clearly in people who already have hypertension. Weaker evidence: how much any of this reduces heart attacks and strokes rests on a small number of long trials in specific populations, and the risk of very low sodium intakes remains disputed.
Nutrition · Chapter 8

Energy Balance & Weight

Every day your body runs an account. Food and drink deposit energy; breathing, thinking, digesting, fidgeting, and moving withdraw it. Over years, the running balance of that account shows up as body weight — but the ledger is nothing like a bank statement, because the two sides talk to each other constantly, and the body actively defends the balance it has settled on. This chapter walks through where food energy comes from, where it goes, how body composition is measured and mismeasured, why hunger and satiety are hormonally orchestrated rather than willed, and what the evidence actually shows about managing weight. Along the way, one rule holds: physiology is described, never moralized. No food is a sin, and no body is a verdict.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • State the energy yield of carbohydrate, protein, fat, and alcohol and use it to estimate the energy in a food.
  • Define energy balance and explain why the two sides of the equation are not independent.
  • Break total energy expenditure into basal metabolism, the thermic effect of food, and activity, and rank them by size and by variability.
  • Calculate and interpret BMI and waist circumference, and name at least four situations in which BMI misleads.
  • Distinguish body weight from body composition and compare the common assessment methods.
  • Describe how leptin, ghrelin, and gut satiety peptides signal to the hypothalamus, and explain why the body defends weight loss more strongly than weight gain.
  • Summarize the behaviors with the strongest evidence for weight management, and distinguish them from preliminary or single-study claims.
  • Explain the physiological and behavioral reasons fad diets reliably fail, without blaming the person following them.

Section 1Energy balance — intake versus expenditure

What a calorie actually is

A calorie in nutrition is really a kilocalorie (kcal) — the energy needed to raise one kilogram of water by one degree Celsius. When a food label in the United States says "Calories 230," it means 230 kilocalories. Much of the world uses kilojoules instead; the conversion is fixed: 1 kcal = 4.184 kJ. So a 230 kcal snack is about 962 kJ, and the same food is described two different ways on two different labels without any disagreement about the physics.

Only four things in the diet supply energy, and their yields are worth memorizing cold, because nearly every calculation in this chapter rests on them. These are the Atwater factors — average physiological fuel values, already discounted for the fraction of each nutrient the body fails to digest or absorb.

Energy sourceYieldIn kilojoulesNotes
Carbohydrate4 kcal/g17 kJ/gIncludes sugars and starch; the body's default fuel
Protein4 kcal/g17 kJ/gUsable for energy, but that is not its primary job
Fat9 kcal/g37 kJ/gMost energy-dense — more than twice carbohydrate or protein
Alcohol7 kcal/g29 kJ/gSupplies energy but essentially no nutrients
Dietary fiber~2 kcal/g~8 kJ/gPartly fermented by colonic bacteria into absorbable short-chain fatty acids
Water, vitamins, minerals00Essential, but they yield no energy themselves

Put those numbers to work. A food with 20 g carbohydrate, 5 g protein, and 10 g fat supplies (20 × 4) + (5 × 4) + (10 × 9) = 80 + 20 + 90 = 190 kcal, and 47 percent of that energy comes from fat. This is exactly the arithmetic behind every "percent of calories from…" statement you will ever read.

The balance equation, and why it is not a bank account

Energy balance is the relationship between energy consumed and energy expended over time. Three states are possible. In energy equilibrium, intake equals expenditure and body energy stores are stable. In positive energy balance, intake exceeds expenditure and the surplus is stored — a small amount as glycogen with its accompanying water, the rest as triglyceride in adipose tissue, and, when training or growth demands it, some as lean tissue. In negative energy balance, expenditure exceeds intake and the body mobilizes stored fuel, along with some lean tissue.

The first law of thermodynamics genuinely applies here: energy is not created or destroyed inside a person. But the equation is a poor predictive model, because intake and expenditure are not independent variables. Eat less and resting metabolism falls, spontaneous movement drops, and hunger rises. Eat more and the thermic cost of processing that food rises, fidgeting increases, and satiety signals push back. The body behaves like a thermostat with an opinion, not like a spreadsheet.

The old rule of thumb that a 3,500 kcal deficit removes one pound of fat comes from the energy content of adipose tissue, and as a one-week estimate it is roughly fine. Extended over months it is demonstrably wrong, and predictably so: it assumes expenditure never adapts. Dynamic models — the kind behind the NIH Body Weight Planner — account for the falling expenditure that accompanies a smaller body and consistently match real trials better. The static rule overpredicts long-term loss by a wide margin, and telling someone otherwise sets them up to feel like a failure at physics they never violated.

StateRelationshipWhat happens to storesTypical context
EquilibriumIntake = expenditureStableWeight-stable adult
Positive balanceIntake > expenditureEnergy storedGrowth, pregnancy, recovery, muscle gain, weight gain
Negative balanceIntake < expenditureEnergy mobilizedWeight loss, acute illness, prolonged undereating

Note that positive balance is not a problem to be solved — it is required during childhood growth, pregnancy, lactation, wound healing, and recovery from illness. The Dietary Reference Intakes reflect this: pregnancy adds roughly 340 kcal/day in the second trimester and about 452 kcal/day in the third above pre-pregnancy needs, and lactation adds roughly 330 kcal/day above pre-pregnancy needs in the first six months and about 400 kcal/day in the second six months. Context decides whether a given balance state is healthy.

There is no RDA for energy. Every other nutrient gets a Recommended Dietary Allowance set high enough to cover 97–98 percent of the population — but doing that for calories would systematically overfeed almost everyone. Instead the DRIs give an Estimated Energy Requirement (EER): the average intake predicted to maintain energy balance in a healthy person of a specified age, sex, weight, height, and activity level. It is a midpoint, not a target with a safety margin, and it is an estimate for a group, not a prescription for a person.
Interactive Move intake, resting metabolism, NEAT, and exercise with live sliders and watch a drawn balance beam tip — with a 12-month projection showing why expenditure tracks body mass instead of following the static 3,500-kcal rule. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Energy yields: carbohydrate 4, protein 4, fat 9, alcohol 7 kcal/g; fiber about 2; water, vitamins, and minerals supply none. 1 kcal = 4.184 kJ.
  • Energy balance is intake versus expenditure over time; positive, negative, and equilibrium states all have legitimate contexts.
  • The two sides of the equation influence each other, which is why simple arithmetic underpredicts how hard long-term change is.
  • Energy has an EER, not an RDA, because an allowance with a safety margin would overfeed nearly everyone.

Section 2Components of energy expenditure

The three components

Total energy expenditure (TEE) is the sum of three parts, plus a small fourth in specific conditions. Learning their relative sizes tells you where the leverage is — and, just as importantly, where it is not.

ComponentShare of TEEWhat it isHow changeable
Basal / resting metabolism~60–75%Keeping cells alive at rest: ion pumps, protein turnover, heart, brain, liver, kidneyLargely fixed; driven by body size and composition
Thermic effect of food~10%Digesting, absorbing, transporting, and storing nutrientsSmall and mostly proportional to intake
Physical activity~15–30% (up to 50%+ in athletes)Deliberate exercise plus all other movementMost variable component, by far
Adaptive thermogenesisSmall, variableHeat production responding to cold, overfeeding, or underfeedingInvoluntary; poorly captured by prediction equations

Basal metabolism — the largest and least dramatic piece

Basal metabolic rate (BMR) is the energy cost of staying alive at complete rest, measured under strict conditions: after a 12-hour fast, lying down, awake, in a thermoneutral room, undisturbed. Because those conditions are hard to arrange, laboratories usually report resting metabolic rate (RMR), measured under slightly relaxed conditions, which runs roughly 5–10 percent higher. The two terms are often used interchangeably in practice; the numbers are close enough that the distinction rarely changes a conclusion.

What actually determines BMR? Fat-free mass is the dominant predictor, explaining most of the between-person variation. Organs are metabolically expensive per gram — liver, brain, heart, and kidney together account for roughly 60–70 percent of resting expenditure while making up only about 5–6 percent of body weight. Resting skeletal muscle, by contrast, is surprisingly cheap per kilogram, which is why the popular claim that adding a pound of muscle burns hundreds of extra calories a day is a large exaggeration; realistic estimates are on the order of 6 kcal per pound per day at rest (about 13 kcal per kilogram). Muscle is worth building for many excellent reasons — strength, function, glucose handling, bone loading, independence with age — but a dramatic metabolic bonus is not one of them.

Other influences: males typically have higher BMR than females of the same weight, mostly because they carry more fat-free mass. Thyroid hormone, fever (roughly a 13 percent rise per degree Celsius, or about 7 percent per degree Fahrenheit), pregnancy, growth, healing, and some medications all shift it. Regarding age, a large pooled analysis of doubly labeled water data across the lifespan reported that size-adjusted total expenditure peaks in infancy at roughly 50 percent above adult levels, stays remarkably stable from about age 20 to 60, and then declines slowly — on the order of 0.7 percent per year. That finding, based on thousands of measurements, has reframed the old story of metabolism "crashing" in your thirties, though it is one analysis of pooled observational data and deserves the usual caution.

The thermic effect of food, and the enormous variability of activity

The thermic effect of food (TEF) — also called diet-induced thermogenesis — is the energy spent processing what you eat. On a mixed diet it runs about 10 percent of energy intake, and it differs by macronutrient: protein is the most expensive at roughly 20–30 percent of its own energy, carbohydrate about 5–10 percent, and fat only about 0–3 percent because dietary fat is stored with minimal chemical rearrangement. This is a real reason higher-protein meals feel more "costly," but the absolute numbers are modest — a 100 kcal protein serving nets you perhaps 20–30 kcal of processing cost, not a free pass.

Activity is the component that actually swings. It splits into exercise activity thermogenesis — deliberate, structured movement — and non-exercise activity thermogenesis (NEAT), which covers everything else: walking to the bus, standing, cooking, pacing, fidgeting, occupational movement. NEAT differences between two similarly sized adults can reach hundreds of kilocalories per day, and studies of deliberate overfeeding found that people who spontaneously increased NEAT the most gained the least fat. Crucially, NEAT is largely involuntary and it falls during energy restriction, which is one of several reasons that measured weight loss lags the arithmetic prediction.

MethodWhat it measuresStrengthsLimits
Direct calorimetryHeat released in a sealed chamberReference standard for heat productionExpensive; unnatural setting
Indirect calorimetryO₂ consumed and CO₂ producedPractical clinical RMR measurementRequires standardized conditions
Doubly labeled waterFree-living TEE over 1–2 weeksGold standard for real-world expenditureCostly; gives a total, not components
Prediction equationsEstimated RMR from size, age, sexFree and instant (e.g., Mifflin–St Jeor)Typically ±10% or worse for an individual
Wearables / trackersEstimated activity energyUseful for tracking trendsIndividual calorie estimates are often substantially off

To convert RMR into TEE, multiply by a physical activity level (PAL) factor: roughly 1.0–1.39 for sedentary, 1.4–1.59 for low active, 1.6–1.89 for active, and 1.9–2.5 for very active. Treat the result as a starting hypothesis to be revised against real-world observation, not a fact.

You cannot reliably out-exercise the estimate error. A moderate 45-minute workout might cost 300–400 kcal — real and worth doing — but that is within the margin of error of a day's intake tracking and a tracker's own estimate. This is not an argument against exercise, which improves cardiorespiratory fitness, blood pressure, insulin sensitivity, mood, sleep, and mortality risk whether or not the scale moves. It is an argument against treating exercise as a calorie-cancelling transaction.
Interactive Move sliders for basal metabolism, the thermic effect of food, and activity to watch each slice and your total daily energy expenditure recompute on a live-drawn donut and share bar. Open full ↑
Key points
  • TEE = basal metabolism (~60–75%) + thermic effect of food (~10%) + activity (~15–30%), plus adaptive thermogenesis.
  • Fat-free mass is the strongest predictor of BMR; high-turnover organs, not resting muscle, dominate resting energy use.
  • TEF is about 10% of intake overall — roughly 20–30% for protein, 5–10% for carbohydrate, 0–3% for fat.
  • NEAT is the most variable and most underestimated piece, and it falls during energy restriction.

Section 3Body composition and assessment

Weight is a number; composition is the question

Body weight lumps together things with completely different meanings: skeletal muscle, bone, organs, body water, glycogen and its bound water, gut contents, and adipose tissue. Body composition separates them, most simply into fat mass and fat-free mass. This distinction matters because health outcomes track composition and fat distribution far better than they track total weight.

Some body fat is not optional. Essential fat — in cell membranes, nerve sheaths, bone marrow, and reproductive tissue — is roughly 3–5 percent of body mass in men and 8–12 percent in women, the higher female value reflecting sex-specific reproductive tissue. Sustained levels below that range are associated with impaired hormone production, menstrual disruption, reduced bone density, impaired immune function, and poor thermoregulation. Typical healthy ranges are often quoted as roughly 10–20 percent for men and 18–28 percent for women, rising modestly with age, but these ranges are approximate, source-dependent, and not diagnostic thresholds.

BMI: useful population tool, blunt individual instrument

Body mass index is weight in kilograms divided by height in meters squared: BMI = kg ÷ m². In pounds and inches, BMI = (lb ÷ in²) × 703. A person who is 70 kg and 1.75 m has a BMI of 70 ÷ (1.75)² = 70 ÷ 3.06 = 22.9 kg/m².

Adult category (WHO)BMI (kg/m²)Asian-population cut points (WHO)Comment
Underweight< 18.5< 18.5Warrants evaluation for cause, not assumption
Healthy range18.5–24.918.5–22.9Statistical range, not a personal ideal
Overweight25.0–29.923.0–27.4Risk category, not a diagnosis
Obesity, class I30.0–34.9≥ 27.5Lower thresholds reflect higher risk at lower BMI
Obesity, class II35.0–39.9——
Obesity, class III≥ 40.0——

Children and adolescents aged 2–19 are not classified with adult cut points. BMI is plotted as BMI-for-age percentiles on growth charts: below the 5th percentile is underweight, 5th to below 85th is the healthy range, 85th to below 95th is overweight, and at or above the 95th percentile is obesity. The same BMI number means entirely different things at age 6, age 16, and age 46.

Now the limitations, which are substantial. BMI does not measure fat at all — it measures mass relative to height. It cannot distinguish muscle from adipose tissue, so a muscular athlete may be classified as "overweight" while carrying very little fat. It says nothing about where fat sits, which is arguably the more important variable. It performs differently across ancestry groups, which is precisely why the WHO publishes lower cut points for many Asian populations, where cardiometabolic risk rises at lower BMI values. It misleads in older adults, who lose lean mass and height. It is not valid in pregnancy. And it was never designed for individuals in the first place: it descends from nineteenth-century work by Adolphe Quetelet describing populations, not diagnosing patients.

Fat distribution, and better questions than "what do you weigh?"

Where fat is stored carries independent risk information. Visceral adipose tissue, packed around the abdominal organs, is metabolically active and associated with insulin resistance, dyslipidemia, hypertension, and cardiovascular disease more strongly than subcutaneous fat on the hips and thighs. Ectopic fat — fat deposited in the liver, skeletal muscle, or pancreas — carries risk that total weight does not reveal.

Waist circumference is the cheapest way to get at this. Measure with a flexible tape at the level of the top of the iliac crest, snug but not compressing, at the end of a normal exhalation. Commonly used risk thresholds in adults of European descent are greater than 102 cm (40 in) for men and greater than 88 cm (35 in) for women; lower thresholds — often around 90 cm for men and 80 cm for women — are recommended for South Asian, Chinese, and several other populations. Waist-to-height ratio is a simple alternative with growing support: keeping waist below half of height (ratio under 0.5) is an easy rule that performs at least as well as BMI for cardiometabolic risk in many datasets, though it is not yet universally adopted.

MethodWhat it estimatesAccuracyPractical notes
BMIWeight relative to heightPopulation screening onlyFree; misclassifies muscular, older, and pregnant people
Waist circumferenceCentral adiposityGood risk indicatorNeeds consistent landmark and technique
Skinfold calipersSubcutaneous fatHighly technique-dependentCheap; needs a trained, consistent measurer
Bioelectrical impedance (BIA)Fat-free mass via conductivityModerate; sensitive to hydrationCommon in scales; standardize timing and fluid status
Air displacement (Bod Pod)Body density → fat percentGoodLaboratory equipment
DXAFat, lean, and bone by regionVery good; a common referenceSmall radiation dose; not for routine screening
MRI / CTVisceral versus subcutaneous fatReference standardExpensive; mostly research
An assessment is a measurement, not a moral report card. Clinical thinking has moved firmly toward using BMI as one screening input among several — alongside waist circumference, laboratory values, symptoms, function, and history — rather than as a standalone label. Recent expert consensus even distinguishes adiposity that is currently impairing organ function from adiposity that is not, precisely because the number alone predicts an individual's health poorly.
