When you take a measurement, you are collecting information about a body, not issuing a judgment about a person.
Interactive Compute BMI live from height and weight, then split it into fat-free and fat mass indices to see exactly where a single ratio misclassifies muscle and body composition. Open full ↑
Key points
  • BMI = kg/m²; adult ranges are <18.5, 18.5–24.9, 25.0–29.9, and ≥30, with lower cut points recommended for many Asian populations.
  • Children and teens use BMI-for-age percentiles (5th, 85th, 95th), never adult categories.
  • BMI measures neither fat nor its location; it misleads in athletes, older adults, pregnancy, and across ancestry groups.
  • Waist circumference and waist-to-height ratio capture central adiposity, which carries risk information BMI misses.

Section 4Regulation of body weight

Hunger, appetite, satiation, satiety

Four words that everyday speech blurs together mean four different things. Hunger is the physiological drive to eat, generated largely by signals from the gut, adipose tissue, and hypothalamus. Appetite is the psychological desire to eat, shaped by sight, smell, memory, habit, emotion, social setting, and availability — which is why you can be full and still want dessert. Satiation is the process that brings a meal to an end. Satiety is the feeling of fullness that persists afterward and determines how long until the next meal. A food can be highly satiating but poorly satisfying, or the reverse; both matter.

The hormonal control loop

The hypothalamus, particularly the arcuate nucleus, integrates the signals. Two opposing neuron populations sit there: NPY/AgRP neurons drive eating, and POMC/CART neurons suppress it, acting through what is called the melanocortin pathway and its MC4R receptor. Mutations in MC4R are the most common single-gene cause of severe obesity, though they still explain only a small percentage of cases.

SignalSourceEffect on eatingTimescale
GhrelinStomachIncreases hunger; rises before meals, falls afterMeal to meal
Cholecystokinin (CCK)DuodenumPromotes satiation; slows gastric emptyingWithin a meal
GLP-1Intestinal L cellsPromotes satiety; slows gastric emptyingWithin and after a meal
Peptide YY (PYY)Ileum and colonPromotes satiety after eatingHours
InsulinPancreatic beta cellsCentral satiety signal (distinct from its metabolic role)Hours
LeptinAdipose tissueReduces hunger; tracks total fat storesDays to weeks

Leptin deserves special attention because it is so often misdescribed. Adipocytes secrete it roughly in proportion to fat mass, so it functions as a long-term status report on energy stores. Rare congenital leptin deficiency causes extreme hyperphagia from infancy and responds dramatically to leptin replacement — one of the most striking results in the field. But common obesity is not a leptin deficiency; leptin levels are typically high, and the brain responds to them poorly, a state described as leptin resistance. Giving leptin to people with common obesity does not produce meaningful weight loss. Anyone selling a "leptin booster" is selling you a signal your body already has plenty of.

Why the body defends its weight

Here is the finding that reorganizes how this topic should be taught: the defense is asymmetric. The body resists weight loss far more vigorously than it resists weight gain — sensible for a species whose ancestors faced famine more often than a food court.

After weight loss, several things happen at once and they do not quickly reverse. Leptin falls, in a drop disproportionate to the fat lost. Ghrelin rises. Subjective hunger increases. Total energy expenditure falls by more than the smaller body size alone predicts — a phenomenon called adaptive thermogenesis or metabolic adaptation, on the order of a few hundred kilocalories per day in some studies. A well-known trial that followed people for a year after a weight-loss program found that appetite hormones and hunger ratings were still shifted toward eating a full twelve months later. Follow-up of participants from a televised extreme weight-loss competition found suppressed resting metabolic rate persisting for years, although that was a very small and unusual sample and the magnitude of long-term adaptation remains actively debated.

Weight is also substantially heritable. Twin and adoption studies place the heritability of BMI in the range of roughly 40–70 percent, and genome-wide studies have identified more than a thousand contributing variants, each with a small effect. The strongest common variant, in the FTO gene, is associated with only about a kilogram or so of body weight per risk allele. Genetics strongly influences how a person responds to an environment; it does not lock in an outcome. And the environment changed fast: the human genome did not shift meaningfully in fifty years, but portion sizes, food availability, energy density, and the amount of movement built into daily life all did.

Willpower is not the hidden variable. A tightly controlled inpatient crossover trial in which participants ate freely from either ultra-processed or minimally processed menus — matched for calories offered, sugar, fat, fiber, and sodium — found that people spontaneously ate roughly 500 kcal per day more on the ultra-processed menu and gained weight, then lost it on the other menu. The sample was small (about 20 people), so treat the exact number as provisional, but the design was rigorous and the direction is consistent with other work. Food environment shapes intake below the level of conscious choice.
Interactive Tap the gut, pancreas, and fat-tissue hormones that drive hunger and fullness, then model why energy use and appetite signals shift to defend the body's weight after a loss. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Hunger (physiological), appetite (psychological), satiation (ends a meal), and satiety (delays the next) are distinct.
  • Ghrelin from the stomach drives eating; CCK, GLP-1, PYY, insulin, and leptin restrain it, via the hypothalamic melanocortin pathway.
  • Leptin reports fat stores; common obesity involves leptin resistance, and leptin treatment does not help it.
  • Weight is actively defended, and asymmetrically — loss triggers rising hunger and falling expenditure that persist for a year or more.

Section 5Weight management

What the strong evidence supports

Start with a frame that the evidence actually supports: health behaviors improve health whether or not weight changes. Cardiorespiratory fitness, blood pressure, insulin sensitivity, lipids, sleep, and mood all respond to regular activity and to eating patterns rich in vegetables, fruit, legumes, whole grains, nuts, and fish, independent of the number on the scale. That is not a consolation prize; it is a large and consistent body of evidence, and it means "no weight change" is not the same as "no benefit."

When weight change is a clinically appropriate goal, modest is meaningful. Losses in the range of 5–10 percent of body weight improve blood pressure, blood glucose, triglycerides, and hepatic fat. The clearest demonstration remains the Diabetes Prevention Program, in which an intensive lifestyle program targeting about 7 percent weight loss plus 150 minutes per week of moderate activity reduced progression from prediabetes to type 2 diabetes by 58 percent over roughly three years — substantially better than the medication comparison arm at 31 percent. That is strong evidence from a large randomized trial with long follow-up. Honesty requires the other side too: a later large trial of intensive lifestyle intervention in adults who already had type 2 diabetes produced more weight loss and better fitness but did not reduce cardiovascular events over about a decade. Behaviors help; they are not guaranteed to change every hard outcome.

What about the diet wars? Here the evidence is remarkably settled and remarkably boring. Head-to-head randomized trials comparing macronutrient patterns — low-fat versus low-carbohydrate, and four different fat/protein/carbohydrate combinations — have repeatedly found no meaningful difference in average weight change at one to two years. Within every arm of those trials, the spread between individuals was enormous while the difference between diets was trivial. The conclusion drawn by the investigators themselves: adherence, not composition, is the active ingredient. The best eating pattern for a given person is the nutritionally adequate one they can actually live with.

ApproachStrength of evidenceWhat it does
Comprehensive behavioral programs (14+ sessions in 6 months)Strong — systematic reviews, national screening recommendationsModest sustained loss and improved cardiometabolic markers
Self-monitoring of intake, activity, and weightStrong — consistent across trialsMost reliable single behavioral predictor of success
Regular physical activity (150–300 min/wk moderate plus 2 d/wk resistance)Strong for health; strong for maintenance, modest for initial lossPreserves lean mass; best predictor of keeping weight off
Higher protein and higher fiber intakes for satietyModerateImproves fullness per calorie; helps adherence
Lower energy density (more water- and fiber-rich foods)ModerateAllows satisfying volume at lower energy intake
Specific macronutrient ratios (low-carb, low-fat, etc.)Weak for superiority — repeatedly null head-to-headWorks only insofar as a person can sustain it
Meal timing, "metabolism-boosting" foods, detoxes, cleansesWeak to noneEffects, where present, are small and largely explained by reduced intake

Why fad diets fail — five reasons, none of them "you"

First, the early results are borrowed. Any sharp restriction depletes glycogen, and each gram of stored glycogen holds roughly three grams of water. Several pounds can disappear in the first week that are water, not fat — and they return just as fast. Diets that cut carbohydrate hardest produce the most dramatic first week and thereby earn undeserved credibility.

Second, restriction is a resource with a budget. Rules that eliminate whole food groups, forbid eating after a certain hour, or require unusual foods demand ongoing vigilance. Adherence declines predictably in every long trial regardless of which diet is assigned. A plan followed for six weeks and abandoned does not outperform a modest plan followed for six years.

Third, biology pushes back — the mechanisms from Section 4. Hunger hormones shift, expenditure falls more than predicted, and both effects persist. This is a physiological response, not a character flaw, and it is the single most important thing to understand about why maintenance is harder than loss.

Fourth, most fads have no maintenance phase. They describe an ending but not an after. Since the body's defense of lost weight persists for a year or more, "what happens next" is arguably the more important half of the plan, and it is usually the missing half.

Fifth, the failure is engineered to look personal. When the regain comes — and in most long-term follow-up studies the average participant regains the majority of lost weight within three to five years — the framing is that the person lacked discipline. The evidence says otherwise. Long-term maintainers do exist, roughly one in five people who lose a meaningful amount keeps at least ten percent off for a year or more, and registry data on them consistently show high physical activity (often close to an hour a day), regular self-weighing, a consistent eating pattern across weekdays and weekends, and eating breakfast. Those are observational associations, so they describe what maintainers do rather than proving what causes maintenance — but the pattern is stable across studies.

Two cautions to carry forward. Repeated cycles of loss and regain, and restrictive dieting in adolescence, are associated with disordered eating patterns and, paradoxically, with greater long-term weight gain — another reason extreme approaches are a poor bet. And medical treatments for obesity, including pharmacotherapy and metabolic surgery, are real, effective, and appropriate for some people; they belong in a conversation with a qualified clinician who knows the person's full history. Nothing in this chapter is individualized medical or dietary advice, and no doses or drug choices appear here for exactly that reason.

Change the scoreboard. Weight is a slow, noisy, only partly controllable outcome. Behaviors are immediate, measurable, and genuinely within reach: a vegetable at most meals, a protein source at breakfast, a consistent bedtime, a daily walk, cooking twice more per week, a sleep schedule that does not sabotage appetite hormones. Track those. Health improvements follow behaviors more reliably than they follow the scale — and unlike the scale, behaviors never tell you that you are a failure at nine in the morning.
Interactive Put six popular weight-loss claims on a numeric test bench and watch live sliders compare what each claim implies against what controlled evidence actually measures. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Adherence beats composition — randomized head-to-head trials find no meaningful difference between macronutrient patterns at 1–2 years.
  • 5–10% weight loss meaningfully improves cardiometabolic markers; the DPP showed a 58% reduction in progression to type 2 diabetes.
  • Strongest behavioral evidence: comprehensive multi-session programs, self-monitoring, and regular physical activity, which best predicts maintenance.
  • Fad diets fail because early loss is water, restriction is unsustainable, biology defends lost weight, and there is no maintenance plan — not because of personal weakness.

The chapter in one breath

  • Food energy comes from four sources only: carbohydrate 4, protein 4, fat 9, alcohol 7 kcal/g (fiber ~2); 1 kcal = 4.184 kJ.
  • Energy balance compares intake with expenditure over time, and the two sides adjust to each other — which is why simple arithmetic overpredicts long-term weight loss.
  • Energy has an EER, not an RDA, and requirements shift with age, sex, body size, activity, pregnancy, and lactation.
  • Expenditure = basal metabolism (~60–75%) + thermic effect of food (~10%) + activity (~15–30%); fat-free mass drives the first, and NEAT makes the third the most variable.
  • BMI (kg/m²) screens populations but misclassifies individuals; waist circumference and waist-to-height ratio add the fat-distribution information BMI cannot see.
  • Eating is governed by a hormonal loop — ghrelin up, leptin, GLP-1, PYY, CCK, insulin down — integrated in the hypothalamic melanocortin pathway.
  • The body defends weight asymmetrically: after loss, hunger rises and expenditure falls beyond prediction, and both persist for a year or more.
  • What works is sustained, supported behavior change, not a particular macronutrient ratio; fad diets fail for physiological and structural reasons, and describing a body or a food as good or bad has never once been part of the evidence.

ReferenceWords worth knowing

A quick reference for the terms in this chapter — skim it now, or circle back whenever a word trips you up.

Adaptive thermogenesis
The change in energy expenditure beyond what body-size change predicts, seen after weight loss or overfeeding; also called metabolic adaptation.
Atwater factors
The average physiological energy yields of the energy-supplying nutrients — 4, 4, 9, and 7 kcal/g for carbohydrate, protein, fat, and alcohol.
Basal metabolic rate (BMR)
Energy expended at complete rest under strict standardized conditions; the largest single component of total energy expenditure.
Body composition
The proportions of fat mass and fat-free mass (muscle, bone, organs, water) that make up body weight.
Body mass index (BMI)
Weight in kilograms divided by height in meters squared; a population screening index, not a measure of body fat.
Doubly labeled water
A stable-isotope method that measures total energy expenditure in free-living people over one to two weeks; the field's gold standard.
Energy balance
The relationship over time between energy taken in from food and drink and energy expended; may be positive, negative, or in equilibrium.
Energy density
Energy per gram of food; lowering it (more water and fiber) permits larger, more satisfying portions at the same energy intake.
Estimated Energy Requirement (EER)
The DRI value for energy: the average intake predicted to maintain balance in a healthy person of a given age, sex, size, and activity level.
Fat-free mass
All body mass other than stored fat; the strongest single predictor of basal metabolic rate.
Ghrelin
A stomach-derived hormone that stimulates hunger; it rises before meals and falls afterward.
Indirect calorimetry
Estimating energy expenditure from oxygen consumed and carbon dioxide produced; the practical clinical method for measuring resting metabolic rate.
Kilocalorie (kcal)
The energy to raise 1 kg of water by 1 °C; what food labels call a "Calorie." 1 kcal = 4.184 kJ.
Leptin
A hormone released by adipose tissue in proportion to fat mass that signals long-term energy stores to the brain and reduces hunger.
Melanocortin pathway
The hypothalamic circuit, involving POMC neurons and the MC4R receptor, through which satiety signals suppress food intake.
Non-exercise activity thermogenesis (NEAT)
Energy spent on all movement that is not deliberate exercise — posture, fidgeting, walking, occupational activity.
Resting metabolic rate (RMR)
Energy expended at rest under slightly less strict conditions than BMR; typically 5–10 percent higher than BMR.
Satiation
The set of signals that bring an episode of eating to an end and determine meal size.
Satiety
The feeling of fullness persisting after a meal that suppresses further eating and determines the interval to the next meal.
Thermic effect of food (TEF)
The energy cost of digesting, absorbing, and processing nutrients; roughly 10 percent of intake on a mixed diet.
Visceral adipose tissue
Fat stored around the abdominal organs; more strongly associated with cardiometabolic risk than subcutaneous fat.
Waist circumference
A tape measurement at the level of the top of the iliac crest used to estimate central adiposity and its associated risk.

Check yourself

1. A serving of food contains 30 g carbohydrate, 12 g protein, 14 g fat, and 4 g fiber (already counted within the carbohydrate). Estimate its energy using standard Atwater factors, and state what percentage comes from fat.
About 294 kcal, with roughly 43 percent from fat. (30 × 4) + (12 × 4) + (14 × 9) = 120 + 48 + 126 = 294 kcal; fat contributes 126 ÷ 294 = 43 percent. Carbohydrate and protein yield 4 kcal/g, fat 9 kcal/g, and alcohol 7 kcal/g.
2. Why do the Dietary Reference Intakes give an Estimated Energy Requirement instead of an RDA for energy?
Because an RDA is set high enough to cover 97–98 percent of a group, which for energy would systematically overfeed nearly everyone. The EER is instead the average intake predicted to maintain balance in a healthy person of a stated age, sex, weight, height, and activity level — a group midpoint, not an individual prescription.
3. A person calculates that a 500 kcal/day deficit should produce one pound of loss per week and 52 pounds in a year. Explain, physiologically, why that will not happen.
The static 3,500 kcal-per-pound rule assumes expenditure stays constant, which it does not. As body mass falls, basal metabolism falls; adaptive thermogenesis reduces expenditure beyond what the smaller size predicts; NEAT declines; and hunger signals rise, nudging intake up. Weight-loss curves therefore flatten, and dynamic models match real trials far better.
4. Rank the three main components of total energy expenditure by size, and separately by how much they vary between people.
By size: basal metabolism (~60–75 percent) > activity (~15–30 percent) > thermic effect of food (~10 percent). By variability the order reverses at the top: activity varies most. Basal metabolism is large but relatively fixed by body size and composition, while activity — especially NEAT — can differ by hundreds of kilocalories a day between similarly sized people.
5. Two adults weigh the same. One has considerably more fat-free mass. Who has the higher BMR, and why?
The one with more fat-free mass. Fat-free mass is the strongest predictor of BMR because it includes metabolically expensive organs — liver, brain, heart, and kidney account for roughly 60–70 percent of resting expenditure despite being about 5–6 percent of body weight — whereas adipose tissue is comparatively inexpensive at rest.
6. Give the approximate thermic effect of protein, carbohydrate, and fat, and explain why the practical impact is smaller than it sounds.
Protein roughly 20–30 percent of its own energy, carbohydrate about 5–10 percent, fat about 0–3 percent; a mixed diet averages about 10 percent of intake. The percentages apply to a modest base, so a 100 kcal protein serving costs only about 20–30 kcal to process. Higher-protein meals do help with satiety and lean-mass preservation, but TEF alone is not a weight-management strategy.
7. Calculate the BMI of an adult who is 82 kg and 1.68 m tall, state the WHO category, and then give two reasons this number might misrepresent that individual.
82 ÷ (1.68)² = 82 ÷ 2.82 = 29.1 kg/m², which falls in the WHO "overweight" category (25.0–29.9). Reasons it may mislead: BMI cannot distinguish muscle from fat, so a well-trained person may be misclassified; it gives no information on fat distribution; risk thresholds are lower for many Asian populations; and it performs poorly in older adults and is invalid in pregnancy.
8. A 15-year-old has a BMI of 26. Should adult categories be applied? What should be used instead?
No. For ages 2–19, BMI is interpreted using BMI-for-age percentiles on growth charts. Below the 5th percentile is underweight, 5th to below 85th is the healthy range, 85th to below 95th is overweight, and at or above the 95th is obesity. Because body composition changes markedly through growth and puberty, the same BMI value carries a different meaning at different ages.
9. Why does waist circumference add information that BMI cannot provide, and how should it be measured?
It estimates central (visceral) adiposity, which is more strongly linked to insulin resistance, dyslipidemia, hypertension, and cardiovascular risk than total weight is. Measure with a flexible tape at the level of the top of the iliac crest, snug without compressing, at the end of a normal exhalation. Common adult thresholds are >102 cm (40 in) for men and >88 cm (35 in) for women, with lower values recommended for South Asian, Chinese, and several other populations.
10. Distinguish satiation from satiety, and name one hormone associated with each direction of appetite control.
Satiation ends a meal and determines its size; satiety is the fullness that persists afterward and determines the interval to the next meal. Ghrelin (from the stomach) increases hunger, while CCK, GLP-1, PYY, insulin, and leptin promote satiation or satiety. All are integrated by the hypothalamus through the melanocortin pathway.
11. A supplement claims to "boost leptin so your body stops storing fat." Evaluate that claim against the evidence.
It misrepresents the physiology. In common obesity leptin levels are already high; the problem is leptin resistance, not deficiency, and leptin administration does not produce meaningful weight loss in these individuals. Leptin replacement is dramatically effective only in rare congenital leptin deficiency. Raising a signal the brain is already ignoring is not a plausible mechanism.
12. A person loses 9 kg on a strict eight-week plan, regains most of it over the following year, and concludes they simply lack willpower. Give three evidence-based reasons this outcome was predictable, and one thing better supported by the evidence.
Predictable because: (1) part of the early loss was glycogen and its bound water, which returns quickly; (2) the body defends lost weight — leptin falls, ghrelin and hunger rise, and expenditure drops below what the smaller body predicts, with these changes persisting a year or more; and (3) short restrictive plans have no maintenance phase, so adherence falls once the plan ends. Better supported: a sustained, comprehensive behavioral approach — multiple sessions over months, self-monitoring, an eating pattern the person can genuinely live with, and regular physical activity, which is the strongest predictor of maintenance. Randomized head-to-head trials show macronutrient ratio matters far less than adherence.
Nutrition · Chapter 9

Nutrition & Physical Activity

Movement costs energy, and the body is remarkably good at paying that bill from several accounts at once. Which account it draws on — the tiny cash reserve of stored ATP, the checking account of glycogen, or the vast savings of body fat — depends almost entirely on how hard and how long you are working. This chapter walks through the fuel systems that power activity, then translates them into the practical questions people actually ask: how much carbohydrate, how much protein, how much fluid, and whether any of the products on the supplement shelf are worth the money. Along the way you will learn to separate claims backed by strong, replicated evidence from claims that rest on a single small study and a good marketing budget. Nothing here is individualized advice; it is the physiology and the evidence that sit underneath such advice.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Describe the three energy systems and predict which one dominates for a given intensity and duration.
  • State the energy yields of carbohydrate, protein, fat, and alcohol and estimate the size of the body's fuel stores.
  • Explain what glycogen is, why it runs out, and what carbohydrate loading does and does not accomplish.
  • Apply evidence-based carbohydrate intake ranges by training load and by exercise duration.
  • Compare the protein RDA with the higher intakes studied in active people, and specify the population and units for each value.
  • Distinguish dehydration from exercise-associated hyponatremia and explain why the fix for one worsens the other.
  • Sort common ergogenic aids into strong evidence, preliminary evidence, and no credible evidence.
  • Recognize low energy availability and explain why underfueling is a performance and health problem.

Section 1Fueling activity

One currency, three ways to mint it

Every muscle contraction is paid for in ATP, and the amount of ATP sitting inside a muscle fiber at any moment is tiny — enough for roughly one to two seconds of maximal work. The body therefore does not store much energy as ATP; it stores energy as other molecules and regenerates ATP continuously from them. Three systems do that regenerating, and they are not sequential stages so much as three taps that are always open to different degrees.

The phosphagen system (also called the ATP–PCr system) uses phosphocreatine stored in the muscle to rephosphorylate ADP back to ATP almost instantly. It needs no oxygen and produces no acid, but the tank is small: maximal effort empties it in roughly ten seconds. Anaerobic glycolysis breaks glucose or muscle glycogen down to pyruvate without oxygen, yielding ATP quickly but in small amounts; when it outruns the ability of the mitochondria to accept pyruvate, lactate accumulates. This system carries efforts lasting roughly ten seconds to two minutes. The oxidative system — the mitochondria — then takes over for anything longer, completely combusting carbohydrate and fat with oxygen. It is slow to spin up but essentially unlimited in capacity.

Energy systemFuel usedNeeds oxygen?Dominates roughlyEveryday example
Phosphagen (ATP–PCr)Stored ATP, phosphocreatineNo0–10 seconds, all-outA single jump, a heavy lift, a short sprint
Anaerobic glycolysisMuscle glycogen, blood glucoseNo~10 seconds to ~2 minutesRunning up several flights of stairs
Oxidative — carbohydrateGlycogen, blood glucoseYesMinutes to a few hours, moderate-to-hardA tempo run or a hard group ride
Oxidative — fatAdipose and intramuscular triglycerideYesRest through easy–moderate workSitting, walking, an easy hike

Which fuel dominates, and why intensity is the switch

Once oxygen-dependent metabolism is in charge, the question becomes carbohydrate or fat. Both are always being burned; what changes is the mix. At rest and during easy work, fat supplies the majority of energy. As intensity climbs, the share from carbohydrate rises steadily and the share from fat falls — a pattern physiologists call the crossover. Around moderate intensity the two contribute roughly equally; at hard intensities carbohydrate supplies the large majority. The reason is mechanical: fat oxidation has more steps and depends on transport into the mitochondria, so it simply cannot deliver ATP fast enough for hard work, whereas carbohydrate can be broken down rapidly and is also the only fuel that can be used without oxygen.

The energy yields themselves are fixed and worth memorizing, because every calculation in sports nutrition rests on them. Carbohydrate yields 4 kcal per gram, protein 4 kcal per gram, fat 9 kcal per gram, and alcohol 7 kcal per gram. Notice what those numbers do to the size of the body's reserves. Carbohydrate is stored as glycogen, which is bound with roughly three grams of water per gram of glycogen, making it heavy and bulky — so the body keeps only a modest supply. Fat is stored anhydrously and yields more than twice as much energy per gram, so even a very lean person carries a reserve measured in tens of thousands of kilocalories.

Fuel storeTypical amount in an adultApproximate energyPractical meaning
Circulating glucose~4–5 g~20 kcalMinutes at most; defended tightly by the liver
Liver glycogen~80–110 g~320–440 kcalKeeps blood glucose steady, including overnight
Muscle glycogen~300–500 g~1,200–2,000 kcalThe limiting fuel for prolonged hard exercise
Adipose triglycerideSeveral kilogramsTens of thousands of kcalEffectively unlimited; never the limiting factor

Protein is the fourth fuel, and it is a minor one. During most exercise, amino acid oxidation supplies only about five percent of the energy used, rising somewhat when muscle glycogen is low. That is a physiological argument for eating enough carbohydrate rather than an argument for eating more protein: adequate carbohydrate spares protein for the structural and enzymatic jobs it is better suited to.

Underfueling is not a training strategy. When habitual energy intake does not cover both training and basic physiological function, the body downregulates what it can — bone turnover, menstrual and reproductive hormones, immune function, thyroid activity, mood, and eventually performance itself. This state is described as low energy availability, and the broader syndrome is called Relative Energy Deficiency in Sport (RED-S). It occurs in people of every body size and in all sexes, it is frequently missed because the person may look and even perform well for a while, and it is a medical issue rather than a discipline issue. Eating enough is part of training, not a failure of it.

Interactive Move intensity and duration and watch the phosphagen, glycolytic, and oxidative systems trade places while the fuel tanks drain — a live-calculating model of how the body picks its energy source. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Three systems regenerate ATP: phosphagen (seconds), anaerobic glycolysis (seconds to ~2 minutes), and oxidative (minutes to hours).
  • Energy yields are carbohydrate 4, protein 4, fat 9, alcohol 7 kcal per gram.
  • Intensity is the switch: fat dominates at rest and easy effort; carbohydrate dominates as intensity rises.
  • Glycogen stores are small (~1,500–2,400 kcal) and limit long hard efforts; fat stores never do.
  • Low energy availability harms bone, hormones, immunity, and performance — in every body and every sex.

Section 2Carbohydrate for performance

Glycogen: the tank that actually runs out

Glycogen is a large, highly branched polymer of glucose — the body's storage form of carbohydrate. Its branching matters: the many free ends let enzymes cleave glucose units off from dozens of points at once, which is exactly what a working muscle needs. Muscle glycogen is a private reserve; the muscle that stores it is the muscle that uses it, because skeletal muscle lacks the enzyme required to release free glucose back into the bloodstream. Liver glycogen is the public reserve, released as glucose to keep blood levels stable for the brain, the red blood cells, and any muscle that needs it.

When muscle glycogen falls low during prolonged hard exercise — typically somewhere past the ninety-minute mark at a demanding intensity — power output drops steeply. Endurance athletes call this hitting the wall, and it feels less like ordinary fatigue than like the legs having been swapped for someone else's. The body still has enormous fat reserves at that moment, but fat cannot be oxidized fast enough to sustain the pace. This is among the clearest and best-replicated findings in sports nutrition, established in controlled laboratory trials rather than inferred from observation: for exercise lasting more than roughly ninety minutes at intensity, carbohydrate availability limits performance.

Training loadTypical daily carbohydrateComment
Light activity~3–5 g per kg body mass per dayOrdinary daily movement, low-intensity training
Moderate (about 1 h per day)~5–7 g per kg per dayMost recreationally active adults
Endurance (1–3 h per day, moderate to high)~6–10 g per kg per daySerious endurance training
Very high (more than ~4–5 h per day)~8–12 g per kg per dayStage racing, multi-session days

These ranges come from joint sports-nutrition position statements and are expressed per kilogram of body mass so they scale to the individual. They are population guidance for generally healthy people, not prescriptions, and someone training an hour a day does not need the intake of someone training five.

Loading: what it does, and who it helps

Carbohydrate loading is the deliberate supercompensation of muscle glycogen before a long event. The original Scandinavian protocol from the 1960s required a brutal depletion phase — hard exhaustive exercise plus several low-carbohydrate days — before the loading days. That misery turned out to be unnecessary. Modern protocols simply combine a training taper with a high carbohydrate intake, on the order of 10–12 g per kg body mass per day, for the 36 to 48 hours before the event, and achieve the same elevated glycogen.

Two caveats keep loading honest. First, it only helps events that would otherwise deplete glycogen — roughly ninety minutes or longer of continuous demanding effort. For a 5 km race, a soccer match, or a gym session, there is nothing to gain, because glycogen was never the limiter. Second, because glycogen is stored with water, loading typically adds one to two kilograms of body mass. In a marathon that water is useful; in a weight-sensitive or short event it is dead freight.

Timing: before, during, and after

Timing is where carbohydrate advice gets oddly specific, and the specificity is earned — these numbers come from decades of controlled trials. Before prolonged exercise, a carbohydrate-containing meal one to four hours beforehand tops off liver glycogen, with roughly 1–4 g per kg body mass depending on how much time there is to digest. During exercise, the guidance depends almost entirely on duration. Below about 45 minutes, no carbohydrate is needed, and between roughly 45 and 75 minutes only small amounts — even a carbohydrate mouth rinse — appear to help. Between about one and two and a half hours, roughly 30–60 g per hour improves endurance performance. Beyond about two and a half to three hours, intakes up to about 90 g per hour have been shown to help — but only if the carbohydrate comes from multiple transportable carbohydrates, typically a glucose-plus-fructose blend. The reason is elegant: the intestinal glucose transporter SGLT1 saturates near 60 g per hour, while fructose enters by a separate transporter, GLUT5, so combining them raises the total absorbable rate. Higher hourly intakes are under active study and should be treated as preliminary.

Afterward, the urgency depends on when the next session is. If a second hard session is less than about eight hours away, aggressive refueling of roughly 1.0–1.2 g per kg body mass per hour for the first four hours restores glycogen fastest. If the next session is a day away, ordinary meals meeting the daily target restore glycogen fully within 24 hours, and no special window applies. Adding protein to a recovery meal is sensible for other reasons, but it does not speed glycogen resynthesis when carbohydrate intake is already adequate.

Train the gut, not just the legs. Absorbing 60–90 g of carbohydrate per hour while running hard is a skill the intestine has to practice. Athletes who attempt race-day intakes for the first time on race day frequently meet gastrointestinal distress instead of a personal best. Tolerance is trainable, which is why rehearsing fueling during training sessions is standard practice — and why no fueling plan should debut at the start line.

Interactive Build a training week with sliders and watch muscle and liver glycogen deplete, reload, and supercompensate on live-drawn charts — showing why "the wall" is a rate limit, not an energy shortage. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Muscle glycogen is a local reserve; liver glycogen maintains blood glucose for the whole body.
  • Carbohydrate availability limits performance in efforts beyond roughly 90 minutes at intensity.
  • Daily carbohydrate scales with training load, roughly 3–12 g per kg body mass per day.
  • Loading (about 10–12 g per kg per day for 36–48 h plus a taper) helps only long events, and adds 1–2 kg of water weight.
  • During exercise: little or nothing needed under ~45–75 min; 30–60 g per hour for 1–2.5 h; up to ~90 g per hour beyond that using glucose plus fructose.

Section 3Protein and activity

What the numbers actually say

The RDA for protein is 0.8 g per kg body mass per day for healthy adults aged 19 and older, of both sexes. That figure is often misread. An RDA is set to meet the requirement of nearly all healthy individuals in a group — it is a floor for adequacy in a general population, derived largely from nitrogen balance studies in people who are not training hard. It was never intended to describe the intake that best supports adaptation to training.

Several other values differ by life stage, and quoting an RDA without its population is one of the most common errors in this field. For adolescents aged 14–18 the RDA is 0.85 g per kg per day. For pregnancy and lactation it rises to 1.1 g per kg per day (about 71 g per day for many pregnant adults). Older adults are frequently discussed as needing more than 0.8 g per kg because of anabolic resistance — a blunted muscle response to a given dose of protein — and expert groups commonly suggest 1.0–1.2 g per kg per day for this group, though this is guidance rather than a revised RDA.

For people training regularly, joint position statements from sports-nutrition and dietetics organizations describe a range of roughly 1.2–2.0 g per kg body mass per day, with the lower end suiting endurance training and the upper end suiting heavy resistance training, periods of rapid muscle gain, or phases of restricted energy intake where preserving lean mass matters. Evidence for benefit above roughly 2.0 g per kg per day in most people is weak; the dose–response curve flattens. This is a range, not a target to maximize.

PopulationValueUnitsBasis
Adults 19+, both sexes0.8g per kg body mass per dayRDA — adequacy for nearly all healthy adults
Adolescents 14–180.85g per kg per dayRDA — includes growth needs
Pregnancy and lactation1.1g per kg per dayRDA — supports tissue accretion and milk synthesis
Endurance training~1.2–1.6g per kg per dayPosition-statement guidance, not an RDA
Resistance training / muscle gain~1.6–2.0g per kg per dayPosition-statement guidance, not an RDA
Per eating occasion (adults)~0.25–0.3g per kg, or roughly 20–40 gDose that maximally stimulates muscle protein synthesis

Timing, distribution, and the myth of the window

Muscle protein synthesis rises after a meal containing protein and after resistance exercise, and the two effects are additive. The rise is driven partly by total amino acids and partly by leucine, which acts as a signal; roughly 2–3 g of leucine per meal appears to be the threshold that triggers a full response in younger adults, with older adults often requiring more. Because the response to a single dose plateaus, spreading protein across three or four eating occasions produces a somewhat better daily result than concentrating it in one large evening meal.

The famous "anabolic window" — the claim that protein must be consumed within 30 to 60 minutes of training or the session is wasted — has not held up. Meta-analyses that controlled for total daily protein found that the apparent timing effect largely disappeared: studies showing a timing benefit had usually also given the timed group more protein overall. Post-exercise sensitivity to protein persists for many hours, so eating within a few hours is reasonable and eating within thirty minutes is not obligatory. Total daily intake and consistency dominate; timing is a fine adjustment.

Common claimWhat the evidence shows
"You must eat protein within 30 minutes."Not supported. The window is hours wide; total daily protein matters far more.
"The body can only use 20–30 g at a time; the rest is wasted."Oversimplified. The synthesis response plateaus, but larger doses are still digested and used for other purposes, and whole-food meals raise amino acids for longer.
"High protein damages the kidneys."Not supported in people with healthy kidneys. Advice differs when kidney disease is present — a clinical matter, not a general one.
"Plant protein cannot build muscle."Not supported. Plant sources are generally lower in leucine and slightly less digestible, so a modestly higher total and a variety of sources compensate.
"Athletes need protein powder."Not supported as a requirement. Powders are a convenience food; typical mixed diets at normal energy intakes usually reach 1.2–2.0 g per kg.
"More protein always means more muscle."Not supported. Beyond roughly 2.0 g per kg per day the curve flattens; the training stimulus becomes the limiter.

Protein is a building material, not a magic one. Amino acids build muscle only in the presence of a training stimulus and adequate total energy. In an energy deficit large enough that the body is short on fuel, dietary protein gets diverted toward oxidation and gluconeogenesis instead of tissue building — which is why "eat more protein" cannot compensate for eating too little overall. Foods carrying protein are also carrying iron, zinc, B12, calcium, and fiber depending on the source, and none of those foods are good or bad in a moral sense.

Interactive Drag body mass to recompute the actual protein reference values (EAR, RDA, and the active-adult range) on a live inline-SVG chart, then tap nine common protein claims to see each weighed as strong, mixed, or myth against the evidence. Open full ↑
Key points
  • The protein RDA is 0.8 g per kg per day for adults 19+; 0.85 for ages 14–18; 1.1 for pregnancy and lactation.
  • Active people are commonly guided toward 1.2–2.0 g per kg per day, with little added benefit above that.
  • Roughly 0.25–0.3 g per kg (about 20–40 g) per meal maximizes the muscle protein synthesis response.
  • The narrow "anabolic window" is not supported; total daily protein and distribution matter more.
  • Most people meet elevated needs from food; supplements are convenience, not necessity.

Section 4Fluid and electrolytes

How much water, and how much is lost

Water is not a nutrient in the sense of supplying energy, but it is the medium in which everything else happens: blood volume, temperature regulation through sweating, nutrient transport, and waste removal all depend on it. The Adequate Intake (AI) for total water — from beverages and food, since roughly 20 percent arrives in food — is 3.7 L per day for men aged 19 and older and 2.7 L per day for women aged 19 and older. It rises to about 3.0 L per day in pregnancy and 3.8 L per day during lactation. An AI is a best estimate of typical adequate intake rather than a requirement each person must hit, and actual needs swing widely with body size, climate, and activity.

Sweat is where activity changes the arithmetic. Sweat rates commonly range from about 0.5 to 2.0 L per hour and can exceed that in hot, humid conditions or in large athletes working hard. Sweat is hypotonic — more dilute than plasma — but it is not pure water: sodium concentration typically falls somewhere between about 20 and 80 mmol per liter, varying several-fold between individuals, with smaller amounts of potassium, chloride, and magnesium. That variability is why blanket electrolyte prescriptions make little sense and why individual sweat testing exists.

Dehydration, and its mirror image

Losing fluid without replacing it reduces plasma volume, raises heart rate and core temperature at a given workload, and impairs endurance performance and cognition — effects that become reliably measurable once losses exceed about 2 percent of body mass, and that are amplified in the heat. Losses below that threshold are generally well tolerated. Practical monitoring is unglamorous and effective: body mass before and after a session estimates net fluid loss, and pale straw-colored urine generally indicates adequate hydration while persistently dark, concentrated urine suggests otherwise. Thirst is a real and reasonably good signal in most circumstances.

The mirror-image problem is exercise-associated hyponatremia (EAH), defined as a blood sodium concentration below 135 mmol per liter arising during or shortly after prolonged exercise. Its principal cause is drinking hypotonic fluid — plain water or sports drink — in excess of sweat losses, usually compounded by non-osmotic release of antidiuretic hormone, which prevents the kidneys from excreting the surplus. The result is dilutional: total body sodium may be normal while its concentration falls, water shifts into cells, and the brain swells inside a fixed skull. Early symptoms — nausea, headache, bloating, puffiness, confusion — overlap almost perfectly with dehydration, which is the danger, because the instinctive response of drinking more makes hyponatremia rapidly worse. Severe EAH causes seizures, cerebral and pulmonary edema, and death, and it is a medical emergency requiring immediate professional care.

FeatureDehydrationExercise-associated hyponatremia
Underlying problemFluid loss exceeds intakeHypotonic fluid intake exceeds losses; blood sodium is diluted
Blood sodiumNormal or highBelow 135 mmol/L
Body mass during eventDecreasedUnchanged or increased
Typical settingHot conditions, limited fluid accessEvents longer than ~4 h, slower finishers, abundant fluid stations
Overlapping symptomsHeadache, nausea, fatigue, confusionHeadache, nausea, bloating, confusion
Distinguishing clueWeight loss, dark concentrated urine, thirstWeight gain, puffy hands or face, drinking far beyond thirst
Effect of drinking more waterHelpsMakes it worse

Because the two conditions look alike and the treatments diverge, the prevailing guidance from expert consensus panels has shifted away from rigid drinking schedules and toward drinking to thirst, with the added rule that gaining weight during an endurance event is a warning sign rather than an accomplishment. Sodium-containing drinks help replace sweat sodium and encourage drinking, but they do not prevent EAH, because they too are dilute relative to plasma. After exercise, when full rehydration is the goal, consuming roughly 1.25–1.5 L of fluid per kilogram of body mass lost, along with sodium from drinks or food, accounts for ongoing urine losses.

You cannot tell them apart by symptoms alone. A collapsed endurance athlete with headache, nausea, and confusion could be dehydrated or hyponatremic, and the correct response differs entirely. This is precisely why on-site sodium measurement is standard at long endurance events, and why a general-education chapter can teach you to recognize the pattern but cannot substitute for medical evaluation of an actual person.

Interactive Set your sweat volume and its saltiness, then replace the loss with nothing, water, a sports drink, or an electrolyte mix — and watch plasma sodium and body-mass change plot live toward the two opposite failures, hypernatremia and hyponatremia. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Total water AI: 3.7 L per day for men 19+, 2.7 L per day for women 19+; about 20 percent comes from food.
  • Sweat rates run roughly 0.5–2.0 L per hour; sweat sodium varies widely (~20–80 mmol/L).
  • Performance and cognition decline reliably once fluid loss exceeds about 2 percent of body mass.
  • EAH is dilutional hyponatremia (sodium below 135 mmol/L) from overdrinking; it mimics dehydration and is a medical emergency.
  • Drinking to thirst and avoiding weight gain during long events is the current consensus approach.

Section 5Supplements and ergogenic aids

A very short list has strong evidence

An ergogenic aid is anything taken with the intention of enhancing performance. The category is enormous, the marketing is relentless, and the number of substances with genuinely strong evidence is remarkably small. Expert consensus reviews converge on roughly five: caffeine, creatine monohydrate, dietary nitrate, beta-alanine, and sodium bicarbonate — plus sports foods such as drinks, gels, and bars, which are useful chiefly because they deliver carbohydrate and fluid conveniently rather than because of any special property.

Creatine monohydrate is the most studied dietary supplement in sport. It raises muscle phosphocreatine, which enlarges the phosphagen tank described in Section 1, and consistently improves performance in repeated short, high-intensity efforts and in gains of lean mass and strength when combined with resistance training. It has been studied extensively in healthy adults over years of use. Two honest caveats: a portion of users are non-responders, typically because their muscle creatine is already near saturation, and an early gain of one to two kilograms is largely intracellular water. Caffeine has robust evidence for endurance and some high-intensity performance, studied mainly in the range of about 3–6 mg per kg body mass taken roughly an hour before exercise — that is the range trials have used, not a recommendation — with no further benefit and more side effects at higher intakes; sensitivity varies genetically, and sleep disruption can easily erase the gain. Beta-alanine raises muscle carnosine, an intracellular buffer, and helps efforts lasting roughly 30 seconds to 10 minutes; harmless skin tingling is common. Sodium bicarbonate buffers acid outside the cell and helps similar durations, at a real cost in gastrointestinal distress. Dietary nitrate, usually from beetroot juice, improves exercise economy, though the effect is smaller and less consistent in highly trained athletes.

SubstanceEvidencePlausible effectCaveats
Creatine monohydrateStrongRepeated high-intensity efforts; lean mass and strength with trainingNon-responders exist; early water-weight gain of 1–2 kg
CaffeineStrongEndurance and some high-intensity performanceWide individual sensitivity; can impair sleep and recovery
Beta-alanineStrongEfforts of ~30 s to ~10 minSkin tingling; requires weeks of loading to raise carnosine
Sodium bicarbonateStrongHigh-intensity efforts of ~1–7 minFrequent gastrointestinal distress
Dietary nitrate (beetroot)Strong, but smallerImproved exercise economyEffect diminished in highly trained athletes
Iron, vitamin DOnly when deficient — strong for iron, weaker for vitamin DCorrecting documented iron deficiency restores endurance capacity; correcting vitamin D deficiency supports bone and muscle health, with performance effects less certainNo ergogenic effect in people who are already replete; deficiency is diagnosed by testing, not guessed at
HMB, ketone esters, tart cherry, probioticsPreliminary or mixedPossible small effects in specific contextsSmall studies, inconsistent replication
BCAAs, glutamine, "testosterone boosters," most fat burnersNo credible evidence—BCAAs are inferior to complete protein as an amino acid source; the others rest largely on marketing

Reading claims, and the contamination problem

Two habits protect you from most supplement nonsense. The first is asking what the evidence actually consists of: how many participants, were they trained or untrained, was there a placebo group, was the outcome a real performance measure or a surrogate such as a blood marker, and has the finding replicated in independent laboratories? A single small trial in untrained volunteers is a reason to be curious, not a reason to be convinced. The second habit is distrusting single-nutrient causal claims. Nutrients act within diets and within physiological systems; the confident sentence "compound X boosts metabolism" almost always compresses a small, context-dependent laboratory observation into a promise it cannot keep.

There is also a regulatory reality worth understanding. In the United States, dietary supplements are regulated more like foods than like drugs: manufacturers are responsible for safety and labeling, and products are not reviewed or approved for efficacy before they reach the shelf. Enforcement is largely after the fact. Independent analyses have repeatedly found products containing substances not on the label, including stimulants and anabolic agents, and mismatches between labeled and actual doses. For competitive athletes subject to anti-doping rules, this is not a theoretical risk, since responsibility for a positive test rests with the athlete regardless of intent. Third-party certification programs that test individual production lots are the standard mitigation, and they reduce risk rather than eliminating it.

Supplements sit on top of a diet, not in place of one. The performance return on adequate energy, sufficient carbohydrate around training, enough total protein, decent sleep, and consistent training dwarfs anything on the supplement shelf — and none of the well-supported aids work well in someone who is underfueled. When the fundamentals are genuinely in place, a small number of aids can add a small, real margin. That is the honest size of the effect.

Interactive Map 22 common ergogenic aids by strength of evidence versus size of effect, translate each effect into real seconds or kilograms, and grade them yourself against the Australian Institute of Sport A–D framework. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Only a short list has strong evidence: creatine, caffeine, beta-alanine, sodium bicarbonate, and dietary nitrate.
  • Iron and vitamin D improve performance only by correcting a documented deficiency, not as boosters.
  • Most other products have preliminary, mixed, or no credible evidence; BCAAs are inferior to complete protein.
  • Supplements are regulated as foods, not drugs, and contamination with undeclared substances is documented; third-party lot testing reduces risk.
  • Judge claims by replication, placebo control, and real performance outcomes — not by mechanism stories.

The chapter in one breath

  • ATP is regenerated by three systems — phosphagen (seconds), anaerobic glycolysis (seconds to ~2 min), and oxidative (minutes to hours) — and intensity decides which dominates.
  • Energy yields are fixed: carbohydrate 4, protein 4, fat 9, alcohol 7 kcal per gram; fat stores are effectively unlimited while glycogen stores are only ~1,500–2,400 kcal.
  • Glycogen availability limits hard efforts beyond roughly 90 minutes; daily carbohydrate scales with training load at about 3–12 g per kg per day.
  • Carbohydrate loading helps only long events, and during exercise the targets are ~30–60 g per hour, rising toward ~90 g per hour with glucose-plus-fructose blends.
  • The protein RDA is 0.8 g per kg per day for adults 19+ (0.85 for ages 14–18; 1.1 in pregnancy and lactation), while active people are guided toward 1.2–2.0 g per kg per day.
  • The narrow anabolic window is a myth; total daily protein, sensible distribution, and adequate energy do the work.
  • Fluid losses past about 2 percent of body mass impair performance, while overdrinking causes exercise-associated hyponatremia — a mimic of dehydration that drinking makes worse.
  • Only a handful of ergogenic aids have strong evidence, supplements are regulated as foods rather than drugs, and none of them substitute for adequate fueling.

ReferenceWords worth knowing

A quick reference for the terms in this chapter — skim it now, or circle back whenever a word trips you up.

Anabolic window
The popular claim that protein must be eaten within roughly 30–60 minutes after training; controlled trials show the sensitive period lasts hours and that total daily protein matters far more.
Anaerobic glycolysis
Breakdown of glucose or glycogen to pyruvate without oxygen, producing ATP rapidly but briefly; supports efforts of about 10 seconds to 2 minutes.
Beta-alanine
An amino acid that raises muscle carnosine, an intracellular buffer; strong evidence for efforts lasting roughly 30 seconds to 10 minutes.
Carbohydrate loading
Deliberately supercompensating muscle glycogen before a long event by combining a training taper with a high carbohydrate intake for 36–48 hours.
Creatine monohydrate
The most studied sport supplement; raises muscle phosphocreatine and improves repeated high-intensity efforts and training-driven gains in lean mass and strength.
Dehydration
A body-water deficit from losses exceeding intake; performance and cognition decline reliably once losses exceed about 2 percent of body mass.
Energy availability
Dietary energy remaining to support basic physiological function after the energy cost of exercise is subtracted, expressed per kilogram of fat-free mass.
Ergogenic aid
Any substance or practice taken with the intention of improving performance; only a small number have strong supporting evidence.
Euhydration
A normal, balanced state of body water — neither depleted nor overloaded.
Exercise-associated hyponatremia (EAH)
Blood sodium below 135 mmol per liter arising during or after prolonged exercise, usually from drinking hypotonic fluid in excess of losses; a medical emergency that mimics dehydration.
Gluconeogenesis
Synthesis of glucose from non-carbohydrate precursors such as amino acids, glycerol, and lactate, chiefly in the liver.
Glycogen
The branched storage polymer of glucose held in liver and muscle; muscle glycogen fuels the muscle that stores it, while liver glycogen maintains blood glucose.
Leucine threshold
The approximate amount of the amino acid leucine per meal (about 2–3 g in younger adults, often more in older adults) that triggers a full muscle protein synthesis response.
Multiple transportable carbohydrates
A glucose-plus-fructose blend that uses two intestinal transporters (SGLT1 and GLUT5), raising the absorbable carbohydrate rate above the roughly 60 g per hour ceiling for glucose alone.
Muscle protein synthesis
The building of new muscle protein, stimulated additively by dietary protein and by resistance exercise.
Osmolality
The concentration of dissolved particles in a fluid; sweat is hypotonic to plasma, which is why heavy sweating plus plain water can dilute blood sodium.
Oxidative (aerobic) system
Mitochondrial energy production using oxygen to fully combust carbohydrate and fat; slow to engage but effectively unlimited in capacity.
Phosphagen system
The immediate ATP–phosphocreatine system that powers all-out efforts lasting roughly 10 seconds, requiring no oxygen and producing no acid.
Phosphocreatine
A high-energy compound stored in muscle that donates a phosphate to regenerate ATP from ADP almost instantly.
Relative Energy Deficiency in Sport (RED-S)
A syndrome of impaired bone, endocrine, immune, metabolic, and psychological function caused by sustained low energy availability; occurs in all sexes and body sizes.
Sweat rate
Fluid lost through sweating per unit time, commonly 0.5–2.0 L per hour and highly variable with heat, humidity, intensity, and body size.
Third-party certification
Independent testing of individual supplement production lots for undeclared substances; reduces but does not eliminate contamination risk.

Check yourself

1. A sprinter runs an all-out 8-second effort. Which energy system supplies most of the ATP, and why can it not be sustained?
The phosphagen (ATP–PCr) system. Phosphocreatine regenerates ATP almost instantly and needs no oxygen, but the muscle stores very little of it — maximal effort empties the store in roughly 10 seconds, after which glycolysis and then oxidative metabolism must take over.
2. State the energy yield, in kcal per gram, of carbohydrate, protein, fat, and alcohol.
Carbohydrate 4, protein 4, fat 9, alcohol 7 kcal per gram. Fat's higher yield, plus the fact that it is stored without water while glycogen is stored with roughly 3 g of water per gram, is why fat reserves are enormous and glycogen reserves are small.
3. Why does the proportion of energy from fat fall as exercise intensity rises, even though plenty of fat is available?
Because fat oxidation is comparatively slow — it requires more steps and depends on transport into the mitochondria — so it cannot supply ATP fast enough for hard work. Carbohydrate can be broken down rapidly and is the only fuel usable without oxygen, so its share rises with intensity.
4. Explain why muscle glycogen cannot raise a person's blood glucose, while liver glycogen can.
Skeletal muscle lacks the enzyme needed to release free glucose into the bloodstream, so muscle glycogen is a private reserve used only by the muscle storing it. The liver has that enzyme, so liver glycogen serves the whole body — brain, red blood cells, and working muscle alike.
5. Who benefits from carbohydrate loading, and name two reasons it is a poor idea for a 5 km race.
It benefits people doing continuous demanding events longer than roughly 90 minutes, where glycogen would otherwise become limiting. For a 5 km race, glycogen was never the limiter, so there is nothing to gain — and loading adds roughly 1–2 kg of stored water, which is simply extra mass to carry.
6. During a 4-hour endurance event, why is a glucose-plus-fructose blend able to deliver more carbohydrate per hour than glucose alone?
Glucose is absorbed via the SGLT1 transporter, which saturates near 60 g per hour, while fructose is absorbed by a separate transporter, GLUT5. Using both routes raises the total absorbable rate toward roughly 90 g per hour. Tolerating those intakes is a trainable skill and should be rehearsed in training, not attempted first on event day.
7. Give the protein RDA with its population and units, and explain why quoting "0.8" for a competitive athlete is misleading.
0.8 g per kg body mass per day for healthy adults aged 19 and older, both sexes. An RDA describes adequacy for nearly all healthy people in a general population, largely from nitrogen balance work in untrained individuals. It differs by life stage (0.85 g per kg for ages 14–18; 1.1 g per kg in pregnancy and lactation), and position statements guide active people toward roughly 1.2–2.0 g per kg per day for training adaptation.
8. A friend insists a workout is "wasted" without a shake within 30 minutes. What does the evidence show?
The narrow anabolic window is not supported. Meta-analyses that controlled for total daily protein found the apparent timing effect largely disappeared — studies favoring timing had usually given that group more protein overall. Post-exercise sensitivity lasts hours, so total daily intake and reasonable distribution across three or four meals matter far more than a 30-minute deadline.
9. Roughly how much protein per eating occasion maximizes the muscle protein synthesis response in adults, and why is spreading intake useful?
About 0.25–0.3 g per kg body mass, or roughly 20–40 g of high-quality protein. Because the synthesis response to a single dose plateaus, distributing protein across three or four occasions produces a somewhat better daily result than concentrating it in one very large meal. Older adults often need more per meal because of anabolic resistance.
10. Two runners finish a long hot race with headache, nausea, and confusion. One has lost 3 percent of body mass; the other has gained weight. Explain the likely difference and why it matters urgently.
The first is likely dehydrated; the second is likely experiencing exercise-associated hyponatremia from drinking hypotonic fluid in excess of losses. The symptoms overlap almost perfectly, but the treatments diverge: giving more fluid helps the first and worsens the second, where diluted blood sodium (below 135 mmol/L) is already shifting water into cells and swelling the brain. EAH is a medical emergency requiring immediate professional evaluation, which is why sodium is measured on site at long events.
11. Why do sports drinks fail to prevent exercise-associated hyponatremia, and what approach do current consensus panels favor instead?
Sports drinks are still hypotonic relative to plasma, so drinking large volumes of them also dilutes blood sodium. Consensus guidance has moved away from fixed drinking schedules toward drinking to thirst, with the rule that gaining body mass during an endurance event is a warning sign rather than a sign of good hydration.
12. Name three ergogenic aids with strong evidence and one common product without credible evidence, then state two reasons to be cautious about supplement quality.
Strong evidence: any three of creatine monohydrate, caffeine, beta-alanine, sodium bicarbonate, and dietary nitrate. Without credible evidence: BCAAs (inferior to complete protein), glutamine, or "testosterone boosters." Caution is warranted because supplements are regulated as foods rather than drugs, so products are not reviewed for efficacy before sale, and independent analyses have repeatedly found undeclared substances and dose mismatches — a real problem for anyone subject to anti-doping rules. Third-party lot testing reduces, but does not eliminate, that risk.
Nutrition · Chapter 10

Nutrition Through the Lifespan

Nutrient needs are not a fixed target you hit once and coast on — they move underneath you your whole life. A body that is building a second body, a body doubling its birth weight in six months, a body adding half its adult skeleton in four years, and a body defending muscle at eighty are all solving different problems with the same set of nutrients. This chapter walks that arc: pregnancy, lactation and infancy, childhood, adolescence, and older adulthood. In each stage the physiology tells you which nutrients get tight, and the tight nutrients tell you what a reasonable eating pattern looks like. Throughout, the emphasis is on what the evidence actually supports — and on describing bodies and foods without moralizing about either.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Explain why gestational weight gain recommendations differ by pre-pregnancy BMI, and what the added weight is physically made of.
  • State the folate and iron recommendations in pregnancy with units and population, and explain the timing problem that makes preconception folate matter.
  • List the food-safety and substance exposures advised against in pregnancy and give the reason behind each.
  • Describe the composition of human milk, the nutrients that need attention in infancy, and how commercial infant formula is designed and safely prepared.
  • Identify developmental readiness for complementary foods and summarize current evidence on introducing common allergens.
  • Explain why appetite falls and fluctuates in toddlerhood, and apply a division of responsibility approach to feeding.
  • Relate the adolescent growth spurt to peak calcium and iron needs, and describe weight-neutral ways to talk with teens about food and bodies.
  • Explain why nutrient density becomes the central issue for older adults, and name the nutrients most often short.

Section 1Pregnancy

Energy, weight gain, and what the gain is made of

Pregnancy is among the most nutritionally demanding physiological states a human body undertakes, and yet the energy cost is smaller than most people expect. In the United States, the estimated additional energy need is essentially zero in the first trimester, roughly +340 kcal/day in the second, and roughly +452 kcal/day in the third. That is a snack and a half, not a second dinner. The old phrase "eating for two" overstates the calories badly while understating the part that actually changes: the demand for specific nutrients climbs much faster than the demand for energy, which is exactly why nutrient density matters more here than volume.

Weight gain in pregnancy is not incidental — it is the physical substrate of the pregnancy. Only a minority of it is the fetus. The rest is placenta, amniotic fluid, an enlarged uterus and breast tissue, an expanded blood volume, extracellular fluid, and maternal fat stores laid down to support lactation. Because the healthiest range of gain differs by where a person started, recommendations are indexed to pre-pregnancy BMI. These ranges come from observational data linking gain to outcomes such as preterm birth, birth weight, and cesarean delivery; they are population-level guidance for a clinician to individualize, not a moral scorecard, and both under-gain and over-gain carry risk.

Pre-pregnancy BMIRecommended total gain (singleton)Approximate weekly gain, 2nd & 3rd trimester
Under 18.528–40 lb (12.5–18 kg)~1 lb (0.51 kg)
18.5–24.925–35 lb (11.5–16 kg)~1 lb (0.42 kg)
25.0–29.915–25 lb (7–11.5 kg)~0.6 lb (0.28 kg)
30.0 and above11–20 lb (5–9 kg)~0.5 lb (0.22 kg)

Protein needs rise from the general adult RDA of 0.8 g/kg/day to about 1.1 g/kg/day in pregnancy — an increase of roughly 25 g/day, which is comfortably met by most ordinary eating patterns, including well-planned vegetarian and vegan ones.

Folate and iron: the two that get tight

Two nutrients dominate prenatal counseling because both have large demand increases and real consequences when short. Folate is required for DNA synthesis and cell division, so it is under maximum strain wherever cells are dividing fastest. Its most famous role is closure of the neural tube, which happens by about 28 days after conception — frequently before a person knows they are pregnant. That timing is the whole argument for preconception intake. The evidence here is unusually strong: randomized controlled trials in the late 1980s and early 1990s showed that periconceptional folic acid substantially reduces the recurrence and occurrence of neural tube defects such as spina bifida and anencephaly, and mandatory folic acid fortification of enriched grains followed in many countries.

Note the two forms. Folate is the naturally occurring form in leafy greens, legumes, citrus, and liver; folic acid is the synthetic form used in supplements and fortification, and it is more efficiently absorbed. Recommendations are stated in dietary folate equivalents (DFE) to reconcile the two. The RDA is 400 µg DFE/day for non-pregnant adults, 600 µg DFE/day in pregnancy, and 500 µg DFE/day during lactation; on top of that, public health guidance is that anyone who could become pregnant get 400 µg/day of folic acid from fortified food or a supplement, in addition to food folate. The Tolerable Upper Intake Level of 1,000 µg/day applies to synthetic folic acid only, not to folate from food — the concern is that high folic acid can correct the anemia of vitamin B12 deficiency while the neurological damage quietly continues.

Iron is the second pressure point. Plasma volume expands by roughly 50% during pregnancy while red cell mass rises by a smaller proportion, which mathematically drops hemoglobin concentration — the normal, expected physiologic hemodilution of pregnancy. This is a dilution effect, not by itself a deficiency, though true iron deficiency anemia is also common and is associated with preterm birth and low birth weight. The iron RDA rises from 18 mg/day for non-pregnant women aged 19–50 (and 8 mg/day for adult men and postmenopausal women) to 27 mg/day in pregnancy — a jump few eating patterns close without a supplement, which is why prenatal vitamins routinely carry iron. Heme iron from meat, poultry, and fish is absorbed severalfold better than non-heme iron from plants and supplements; vitamin C eaten in the same meal meaningfully improves non-heme absorption, while the tannins in tea and coffee and the phytates in whole grains and legumes reduce it.

NutrientNon-pregnant adult female (19–50)PregnancyLactationWhy it changes
Folate400 µg DFE600 µg DFE500 µg DFERapid cell division; neural tube closure
Iron18 mg27 mg9 mgExpanded red cell mass, placenta, fetal stores
Iodine150 µg220 µg290 µgFetal thyroid hormone and brain development
Choline (AI)425 mg450 mg550 mgMembrane phospholipids, neurodevelopment
Calcium1,000 mg1,000 mg — unchanged1,000 mgAbsorption efficiency roughly doubles instead

The calcium row surprises people. Pregnancy does not raise the calcium RDA for adults, because intestinal calcium absorption up-regulates sharply on its own. (For pregnant adolescents aged 14–18, calcium stays at the higher adolescent value of 1,300 mg/day because they are still building their own skeleton.)

What to avoid, and why

Avoidance lists circulate widely and are often longer than the evidence justifies, so it helps to know the actual mechanism behind each item. Three categories account for nearly all of it: teratogens, foodborne pathogens, and bioaccumulated toxicants.

Avoid or limitMechanismStrength of evidence
AlcoholTeratogen crossing the placenta freely; causes fetal alcohol spectrum disordersStrong — no threshold established as safe
High-mercury fish (shark, swordfish, king mackerel, tilefish, bigeye tuna)Methylmercury bioaccumulates and is neurotoxic to the developing brainStrong
Unpasteurized milk, soft cheeses from raw milk, deli meats, smoked seafood, raw sproutsListeria monocytogenes; pregnancy raises susceptibility many-fold and it crosses the placentaStrong
Raw or undercooked eggs, meat, shellfishSalmonella, Toxoplasma, CampylobacterStrong
High-dose preformed vitamin A (retinol supplements, retinoid medications, very frequent liver)Teratogenic above the UL of 3,000 µg RAE/day preformed vitamin A; beta-carotene is not implicatedStrong
Caffeine above ~200 mg/day (about one 12 oz coffee)Crosses the placenta; slower fetal clearanceModerate — observational, confounded, guidance is precautionary

Notice the flip side of the fish advice: most fish are encouraged in pregnancy, not discouraged. Low-mercury choices such as salmon, sardines, tilapia, cod, and canned light tuna supply omega-3 fatty acids including DHA, and the balance of evidence favors eating them. Guidance in the United States suggests roughly 8–12 oz per week of low-mercury seafood. Evidence that supplemental DHA improves child neurodevelopmental outcomes is mixed and should be described as preliminary; evidence that it modestly reduces early preterm birth is somewhat stronger but still not settled.

The neural tube closes before the pregnancy test turns positive. This single fact explains why folate guidance targets everyone who could become pregnant rather than everyone who is pregnant. Roughly half of pregnancies are unplanned, so a strategy that waits for a confirmed pregnancy is already late for the one outcome folate protects best. Population-wide grain fortification exists precisely because individual timing is unreliable.
Interactive Tap a nutrient and slide the pre-pregnancy energy need to watch each reference value's before-and-during-pregnancy amounts recompute — seeing why folate and iron climb far more than energy while calcium and vitamin D stay flat. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Added energy is modest (0 / +340 / +452 kcal/day by trimester); added nutrient needs are proportionally much larger.
  • Gestational weight gain ranges are indexed to pre-pregnancy BMI; the gain is mostly placenta, fluid, blood volume, and tissue — not just fetus.
  • Folate 600 µg DFE/day in pregnancy plus 400 µg/day folic acid preconception; iron 27 mg/day versus 18 mg/day non-pregnant.
  • Avoidance lists reduce to three mechanisms: teratogens, foodborne pathogens, and bioaccumulated toxicants — and most seafood is encouraged, not avoided.

Section 2Lactation and infancy

Lactation: an energy cost on the order of late pregnancy

Milk production is energetically expensive — broadly comparable to the third trimester rather than dramatically above it. A person establishing full milk supply makes roughly 750–800 mL/day in the first six months, exporting on the order of 500 kcal/day as milk. The estimated additional energy need is about +330 kcal/day in the first six months — that gross cost minus roughly 170 kcal/day expected from mobilizing fat stored during pregnancy — and about +400 kcal/day in the second six months, when no such offset is assumed. Set those beside the +452 kcal/day of the third trimester and the honest summary is that late pregnancy and lactation sit in the same range. Several micronutrient needs, however, genuinely do peak here rather than in pregnancy: vitamin A rises to 1,300 µg RAE/day and iodine to 290 µg/day. One goes down sharply — iron falls to 9 mg/day for lactating adults, lower than the 18 mg/day baseline, because menstruation is typically suppressed. Fluid needs rise with milk output; thirst is a reasonable guide.

Human milk runs about 65–70 kcal per 100 mL, with roughly 7 g lactose, 3.5–4.5 g fat, and about 1 g protein per 100 mL. Because fat yields 9 kcal/g while carbohydrate and protein each yield 4 kcal/g (and alcohol yields 7 kcal/g), that small fat fraction supplies close to half the total energy — infancy is the one life stage where a high-fat diet is unambiguously the physiologically correct one. The first milk, colostrum, is low in volume but concentrated in protein and secretory IgA. Human milk also contains human milk oligosaccharides (HMOs), which the infant cannot digest at all; they exist to feed Bifidobacterium in the infant gut, an elegant case of a food component whose target is not the eater.

Breastfeeding is recommended exclusively for about the first six months, with continued breastfeeding alongside complementary foods through at least twelve months and beyond as long as mutually desired. The observational evidence for reduced infant gastrointestinal and respiratory infection is consistent and strong; claims about long-term IQ and chronic disease are weaker and heavily confounded by socioeconomic factors, and should be stated cautiously. Two supplements are standard practice in infancy: vitamin K given at birth to prevent vitamin K deficiency bleeding, and vitamin D 400 IU/day for breastfed and partially breastfed infants, because human milk is genuinely low in vitamin D regardless of how well the lactating parent eats.

Formula, and feeding without judgment

Commercial infant formula is a regulated food designed to approximate human milk's nutrient profile. In the United States its composition is governed by the Infant Formula Act, and standard iron-fortified cow-milk-based formula is the appropriate default for infants who are not exclusively breastfed.
