Specialty formulas — soy-based, extensively hydrolyzed, amino-acid-based — exist for specific medical indications and are not upgrades for typical infants. Formula feeding is a safe, complete way to feed a baby, and framing it otherwise adds guilt without adding nutrition.

The safety issues in formula feeding are almost entirely about preparation, and they are serious ones. Formula must be mixed exactly to the label ratio. Over-diluting to stretch a can causes water intoxication and hyponatremia — an infant's kidneys cannot excrete a large free-water load — along with inadequate energy intake. Homemade formula recipes have caused hospitalizations for electrolyte disturbance and nutrient deficiency and should never be used. Powdered formula is not sterile, so preparation guidance for very young or medically fragile infants may call for hot water or ready-to-feed liquid.

FeatureHuman milkStandard iron-fortified formula
Energy~65–70 kcal/100 mL~67 kcal/100 mL (20 kcal/oz)
Protein typeWhey-predominant; changes over lactationCow-milk protein, whey-adjusted
IronLow concentration but highly bioavailableFortified; lower bioavailability, higher amount
Vitamin DLow — supplementation advisedFortified to meet needs
Immune factorsSecretory IgA, lactoferrin, live cells, HMOsNot replicated
CompositionVaries by feed, time of day, and infant ageConstant and predictable

Introducing solids

Infants grow faster in the first year than they ever will again — birth weight roughly doubles by 4–6 months and triples by 12 months — and their energy needs per kilogram show it, starting near 100 kcal/kg/day and easing toward 80 kcal/kg/day by the first birthday, against roughly 30–35 kcal/kg/day for a typical adult. Milk alone stops covering this around six months, and the nutrient that runs out first is iron: a term infant is born with stores that last about 4–6 months. That is the physiological reason complementary foods begin when they do, and the reason iron-rich first foods are emphasized.

Readiness is developmental, not calendar-driven. Look for sitting with support and steady head control, the fading of the extrusion reflex (the automatic tongue-thrust that pushes a spoon back out), and active interest in reaching for food. Offer single new foods a few days apart, with textures that progress as oral motor skill does. Several hard limits belong in every infant-feeding discussion: no honey before 12 months (spores of Clostridium botulinum can germinate in the immature gut and cause infant botulism), no cow's milk as a main drink before 12 months (too little iron, too high a renal solute load, and occult gastrointestinal blood loss), no juice in the first year, and vigilance about choking hazards such as whole grapes, nuts, popcorn, and coin-shaped hot dog slices.

The allergen guidance has genuinely reversed, and this is worth knowing precisely. For years, delaying peanut, egg, and other allergens was standard advice. The LEAP randomized trial, published in 2015, found that early introduction of peanut between roughly 4 and 11 months substantially reduced peanut allergy in high-risk infants. This is strong, randomized evidence, and guidelines now recommend introducing common allergens in the first year rather than delaying them, with medical guidance first for infants with severe eczema or an existing food allergy.

Diluting formula is a nutrition emergency, not a thrift measure. Stretching a can by adding extra water is one of the few feeding mistakes that can cause seizures within days: infant kidneys cannot clear the free-water load, serum sodium falls, and energy intake collapses at the same time. If cost is the barrier, the answer is food assistance programs and clinician help — never a thinner bottle.
Interactive Slide through the first twelve months to watch where an infant's daily energy comes from and tap any food to see when it is typically introduced — and why. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Lactation adds about +330 kcal/day (0–6 mo) and +400 kcal/day (7–12 mo) — comparable to the third trimester's +452, not far above it; iron needs drop to 9 mg/day while vitamin A and iodine reach their lifespan peaks.
  • Fat supplies roughly half of human milk's energy at 9 kcal/g; HMOs feed the infant's gut bacteria, not the infant.
  • Standard supplements in infancy: vitamin K at birth and vitamin D 400 IU/day for breastfed infants; formula is a safe, complete alternative when prepared exactly as labeled.
  • Complementary foods start around 6 months when iron stores run down; introduce common allergens early rather than delaying, and never give honey before 12 months.

Section 3Childhood

Growth slows — and so does appetite

After the first birthday, growth decelerates dramatically. A toddler who gained roughly 7 kg in year one gains only about 2–3 kg per year and grows roughly 7–8 cm per year in the preschool years, easing to about 5–6 cm per year through the school years. Appetite tracks growth, so it falls too. This entirely normal drop is sometimes called the physiologic anorexia of toddlerhood, and it is the source of an enormous amount of unnecessary worry: a child who ate everything at eleven months and picks at dinner at eighteen months is usually not sick, not stubborn, and not underfed — just growing more slowly.

Intake in early childhood is also wildly erratic day to day while being remarkably stable across a week. A toddler may eat almost nothing at dinner and then eat enormously at breakfast. Judge intake over five to seven days, not one meal. The more reliable objective signal is the growth chart: WHO charts are used from birth to two years and CDC charts from two to twenty years, and what matters is whether a child is tracking along their own established curve, not which percentile that curve happens to be. A child steady at the 15th percentile is doing fine; a child crossing downward from the 75th to the 25th warrants a look.

NutrientAges 1–3Ages 4–8Ages 9–13Note
Protein (RDA)1.05 g/kg0.95 g/kg0.95 g/kgHigher per kg than the adult 0.8 g/kg
Calcium (RDA)700 mg1,000 mg1,300 mgRises steeply as bone accrual accelerates
Iron (RDA)7 mg10 mg8 mgToddlers are a peak-risk group for deficiency
Vitamin D (RDA)600 IU (15 µg)600 IU (15 µg)600 IU (15 µg)Same from age 1 through 70
Fiber (AI)19 g25 g26–31 gRoughly 14 g per 1,000 kcal at every age

Iron deficiency deserves a specific warning in this age group. When cow's milk becomes a child's dominant beverage, it displaces iron-rich foods, contributes essentially no iron itself, and can cause small-volume gastrointestinal blood loss — a combination that produces microcytic hypochromic anemia in an otherwise well-fed-looking toddler. Iron deficiency in early childhood is associated with impaired cognitive and motor development, and some of that appears not to fully reverse with later repletion, which is why it is screened for rather than waited on.

Building habits without turning meals into a battle

Food neophobia — reluctance to try unfamiliar foods — emerges around age two and peaks between roughly two and six. It is developmentally normal and probably protective in a species whose toddlers become mobile before they become sensible. It also fades with plain repeated exposure: acceptance often takes eight to fifteen or more neutral encounters with a food, which means "she doesn't like broccoli" after three tries is a premature conclusion.

What consistently backfires is pressure. Experimental and longitudinal research on child feeding consistently finds that pressuring a child to eat a food reduces liking for it, and that using a disliked food to earn a preferred one ("two more bites of peas and you get dessert") lowers the value of the peas while raising the value of dessert — the precise opposite of the intent. Heavy restriction of specific foods tends to increase a child's interest in and consumption of those foods when they become available. The practical alternative, widely taught as the division of responsibility, splits the job: caregivers decide what food is offered, when, and where; the child decides whether to eat and how much. This preserves the child's own hunger and fullness signals, which are the regulatory system you actually want to keep intact for the next eighty years.

"Clean your plate" trains a child to ignore the one signal you want them to keep. Internal hunger and satiety cues are reasonably reliable in young children and are steadily overridden by external rules about finishing food. Serving small portions and allowing seconds preserves the signal; mandating a fixed amount teaches the child to consult the plate instead of the body.
Interactive Move an age slider from birth to 18 and watch growth velocity, kilograms-per-year, and estimated daily energy recompute together — revealing why childhood appetite tracks how fast a body is growing, not how big it is. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Growth and appetite both slow sharply after age one — the drop is normal, and intake should be judged over a week, not a meal.
  • Track a child's own growth curve over time rather than a single percentile.
  • Excess cow's milk displacing iron-rich foods is a leading cause of iron deficiency anemia in toddlers.
  • Neophobia fades with repeated neutral exposure; pressure and food-as-reward reduce liking, while the division of responsibility preserves self-regulation.

Section 4Adolescence

The growth spurt is a construction project

Adolescence delivers the second-fastest growth of the lifespan. Teenagers add roughly 15–20% of their adult height and, more consequentially for nutrition, close to half of their adult skeletal mass during these years. Peak height velocity arrives around ages 11–12 in girls and 13–14 in boys, with growth of roughly 8–10 cm in the peak year. Lean mass, blood volume, and organ size all scale up alongside height. Energy needs during this window are highly individual — driven by growth stage and activity far more than by age — which makes fixed calorie targets unhelpful and appetite a more reasonable guide than arithmetic.

The bone story is the one worth being emphatic about, because it has a deadline. Roughly 90% of peak bone mass is accrued by about age 18–20, with the peak itself reached in the mid-to-late twenties; after that, the skeleton is essentially in maintenance and slow decline. Calcium intake and weight-bearing physical activity during adolescence therefore influence fracture risk half a century later, and the window does not reopen. This is why the calcium RDA is 1,300 mg/day for ages 9–18 in both sexes — the highest of any life stage, higher than in pregnancy — paired with 600 IU (15 µg)/day of vitamin D. Surveys consistently find adolescent calcium intake falling short of that number, particularly in girls.

Iron needs rise in both sexes for different reasons, which is a useful teaching contrast. In males, expanding lean body mass and blood volume drive the increase and it recedes once growth completes; in females, menstrual losses begin and the elevated need persists through the reproductive years. Hence the split RDA at ages 14–18: 11 mg/day for males and 15 mg/day for females. Zinc, needed for growth and sexual maturation, similarly splits at 11 mg/day and 9 mg/day.

NutrientAges 9–13 (both)Ages 14–18 maleAges 14–18 femaleDriver
Calcium1,300 mg1,300 mg1,300 mgPeak bone mineral accrual
Vitamin D600 IU (15 µg)600 IU (15 µg)600 IU (15 µg)Calcium absorption, bone mineralization
Iron8 mg11 mg15 mgLean mass and blood volume; menstrual loss
Zinc8 mg11 mg9 mgGrowth and sexual maturation
Protein0.95 g/kg0.85 g/kg0.85 g/kgRapid accrual of lean tissue

Body image, and how not to make it worse

Adolescence is also when body dissatisfaction and disordered eating most often begin, which places anyone teaching nutrition to teens in a position of real influence — in either direction. The evidence on this is more consistent than people expect. Longitudinal research finds that weight-focused conversations, weight teasing, and weight-control encouragement from adults are associated with more disordered eating and no improvement in health behaviors. Conversations that focus on behaviors rather than bodies — eating regularly, eating enough, moving in ways you enjoy, sleeping — do not carry the same risk.

Two things follow practically. First, a weight-neutral vocabulary is not merely polite; it is the option better supported by the available outcome data, which is largely longitudinal and observational but unusually consistent in direction. Describe what nutrients do and what foods contain, and drop the language of "good" and "bad" foods, "guilt-free," "earning" dessert, and "cheat meals" — the moral framing is what carries the harm, and it does not improve intake. Second, know that this age group is the one where underfueling is a common and under-recognized problem. In physically active teens, low energy availability — not eating enough to cover both training and growth — is linked to menstrual disruption, impaired bone accrual, stress fractures, and poor recovery. The energy-availability link is well supported; the broader syndrome frameworks describing it are still evolving. Persistent restriction, rigid food rules, distress after eating, or loss of menstrual periods are signals to involve a qualified clinician, not to teach around.

Adolescence is the only chance at peak bone mass. Nearly all skeletal mineral is banked before the early twenties, and no adult intervention recovers a deposit that was never made. Calcium, vitamin D, adequate total energy, and weight-bearing activity in the teen years are one of the few places where nutrition genuinely functions as long-term insurance — and where underfueling costs the most.
Interactive Slide through the ages to watch calcium and iron requirements peak during the growth spurt, then build meals and calculate how much of each mineral is actually absorbed. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Adolescents add 15–20% of adult height and roughly half their adult skeletal mass; energy needs are individual, not formula-driven.
  • Calcium 1,300 mg/day (ages 9–18) is the highest of any life stage; about 90% of peak bone mass is set by age 18–20.
  • Iron RDA splits at 14–18: 11 mg/day male, 15 mg/day female — different physiology, same nutrient.
  • Weight-focused talk and weight teasing predict disordered eating; behavior-focused, weight-neutral language is both kinder and better supported.

Section 5Older adults

Fewer calories, the same or more of everything else

The defining nutritional feature of later life is a divergence. Energy needs fall — lean mass declines, resting metabolic rate follows it down, and activity usually decreases — while requirements for most vitamins and minerals stay flat and several actually rise. Less food coming in, the same or more nutrients required from it. That squeeze is why nutrient density, the amount of nutrient delivered per calorie, becomes the organizing idea of this life stage rather than one concept among many.

Several DRIs change at 51 and again at 71, and the pattern is worth memorizing as a group rather than one at a time.

NutrientAdults 19–50Ages 51–70Over 70Reason
Vitamin D600 IU (15 µg)600 IU (15 µg)800 IU (20 µg)Reduced skin synthesis and sun exposure
Calcium1,000 mg1,000 mg men / 1,200 mg women1,200 mg bothAccelerated bone loss after menopause
Vitamin B61.3 mg1.7 mg men / 1.5 mg womenSameReduced efficiency of utilization
Vitamin B122.4 µg2.4 µg from fortified food or supplementSameAtrophic gastritis impairs food-bound B12
Fiber (AI)38 g men / 25 g women30 g men / 21 g womenSameScales with lower energy intake
Water (AI, total)3.7 L men / 2.7 L womenUnchangedUnchangedNeed is the same; thirst sensation is not

The B12 row is the one that trips students, so read it carefully: the amount does not change, but the recommended source does. Roughly 10–30% of older adults develop atrophic gastritis, producing too little stomach acid and pepsin to free B12 from the protein it is bound to in food. Crystalline B12 in fortified cereals and supplements is not protein-bound and is absorbed normally, so the DRI explicitly advises adults over 50 to get most of their B12 from those sources. Untreated deficiency causes megaloblastic (macrocytic) anemia plus a neurological syndrome — paresthesias, gait disturbance, cognitive change — and the neurological damage can become permanent. This is also where the folic acid caution from Section 1 lands: high folic acid intake can normalize the blood count while the nerve damage advances unseen.

Muscle is the other headline. Sarcopenia, the age-related loss of muscle mass and function, begins subtly around age thirty and accelerates after sixty, and it is one of the strongest nutrition-related contributors to falls, fractures, and loss of independence. The protein RDA remains 0.8 g/kg/day at all adult ages, but multiple expert groups have argued this is too low for older adults and suggest roughly 1.0–1.2 g/kg/day. Be precise about the status of that number: it is expert consensus and mechanistic reasoning, not a revised DRI, and the trials are not uniformly positive. What is well established is that protein works far better in combination with resistance exercise than alone, and that spreading protein across meals rather than concentrating it at dinner is likely more effective.

Appetite, access, and the practical work

Appetite commonly declines with age — sometimes called the anorexia of aging — and the causes stack. Gastric emptying slows and satiety hormone signaling shifts, so fullness arrives sooner and lasts longer. Taste and smell acuity decline, flattening the reward of eating. Dentition problems, dry mouth, and dysphagia make eating effortful. Many common medications alter taste or cause nausea. And the non-physiological causes matter at least as much: eating alone after a spouse dies, depression, limited mobility for shopping and cooking, and food insecurity on a fixed income.

One clinical asymmetry is worth flagging plainly. In older adults, unintentional weight loss is a warning sign, not an achievement. It typically reflects loss of muscle along with fat and is associated with worse outcomes — falls, delayed healing, infection, mortality. Unexplained weight loss in an older adult belongs in front of a clinician rather than being congratulated.

The interventions that follow are unglamorous and effective: put protein in every meal rather than only dinner; use flavor generously — herbs, acid, aromatics — to compensate for dulled taste without leaning on salt; choose textures that match chewing and swallowing ability rather than abandoning whole food categories; offer smaller, more frequent meals when early satiety is the barrier; keep fluids visible and offered on a schedule since thirst is unreliable; pair fiber increases with fluid; and treat congregate meal programs, home-delivered meals, and grocery assistance as legitimate nutrition interventions. Eating with other people reliably increases intake, which makes company itself a nutritional variable.

Thirst is the least reliable signal in an older body. Water requirements do not fall with age, but the thirst response blunts, so dehydration can develop before any sensation of being thirsty — presenting as confusion, weakness, or a fall rather than as a dry mouth. Scheduled, offered fluids beat waiting to feel thirsty, and the same principle explains why relying on appetite alone is riskier at eighty than at eighteen.
Interactive A live engine: slide age and activity to watch estimated calorie needs fall while required nutrient density rises, showing why older adults need more nutrients from fewer calories. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Energy needs fall while micronutrient needs hold or rise — making nutrient density the central concern of later life.
  • Needs that increase: vitamin D 800 IU/day after 70, calcium 1,200 mg/day, vitamin B6; B12 stays at 2.4 µg but should come from fortified food or a supplement.
  • Sarcopenia drives falls and lost independence; expert groups suggest 1.0–1.2 g/kg/day protein with resistance exercise, above the RDA of 0.8 g/kg/day — consensus, not a revised DRI.
  • Appetite decline is multifactorial, thirst is unreliable, and unintentional weight loss is a red flag in older adults.

The chapter in one breath

  • Pregnancy adds modest energy (0 / +340 / +452 kcal/day by trimester) but sharply higher nutrient needs; weight gain ranges are indexed to pre-pregnancy BMI and describe outcomes, not virtue.
  • Folate (600 µg DFE/day in pregnancy, plus 400 µg/day folic acid preconception) protects neural tube closure, which happens by day 28 — often before a pregnancy is known.
  • Iron rises to 27 mg/day in pregnancy against a plasma-volume expansion that also causes normal physiologic hemodilution; avoidance advice reduces to teratogens, pathogens, and bioaccumulated toxicants.
  • Lactation carries an energy cost on the order of late pregnancy (+330 to +400 kcal/day, against roughly 500 kcal/day exported as milk); human milk gets about half its energy from fat at 9 kcal/g, and its HMOs feed the infant's gut bacteria rather than the infant.
  • Infancy needs vitamin D supplementation while breastfeeding, iron-rich complementary foods from about six months, no honey before twelve months, and early rather than delayed allergen introduction.
  • Childhood growth and appetite both slow; judge intake across a week and track the child's own growth curve, and remember that pressure and food-as-reward reduce liking while repeated neutral exposure builds it.
  • Adolescence banks nearly all peak bone mass, making calcium 1,300 mg/day the highest of any life stage, and it is the point where weight-focused talk does measurable harm.
  • Older adults need fewer calories but as many or more nutrients, so nutrient density rules — watch B12 source, vitamin D, calcium, protein against sarcopenia, and unreliable thirst and appetite.

ReferenceWords worth knowing

A quick reference for the terms in this chapter — skim it now, or circle back whenever a word trips you up.

Anorexia of aging
The multifactorial decline in appetite common in later life, driven by slower gastric emptying, altered satiety signaling, reduced taste and smell, dental and swallowing difficulty, medications, and social factors.
Colostrum
The first milk produced after birth — small in volume, high in protein and secretory IgA, and lower in fat and lactose than mature milk.
Complementary foods
Foods other than human milk or formula introduced around six months of age to complement, not replace, milk feeding.
Dietary folate equivalents (DFE)
The unit used to state folate recommendations, adjusting for the higher absorption of synthetic folic acid relative to naturally occurring food folate.
Division of responsibility
A feeding framework in which the caregiver decides what food is offered, when, and where, while the child decides whether to eat and how much.
Exclusive breastfeeding
Feeding an infant only human milk, with no other liquids or foods except prescribed supplements such as vitamin D; recommended for roughly the first six months.
Extrusion reflex
The infant tongue-thrust that automatically pushes solid material out of the mouth; its fading is one sign of readiness for complementary foods.
Food neophobia
Reluctance to try unfamiliar foods, emerging around age two and peaking between two and six; it typically fades with repeated neutral exposure.
Gestational weight gain
Total weight gained during pregnancy — comprising fetus, placenta, amniotic fluid, uterine and breast tissue, expanded blood and extracellular fluid, and maternal fat stores — with recommended ranges indexed to pre-pregnancy BMI.
Heme iron
Iron bound within hemoglobin and myoglobin in meat, poultry, and fish; absorbed severalfold more efficiently than non-heme iron and less affected by dietary inhibitors.
Human milk oligosaccharides (HMOs)
Complex carbohydrates in human milk that the infant cannot digest; they act as prebiotics selectively feeding Bifidobacterium in the infant gut.
Neural tube defect
A malformation such as spina bifida or anencephaly arising from incomplete closure of the neural tube by about day 28 after conception; risk is substantially reduced by periconceptional folic acid.
Non-heme iron
Iron from plant foods, eggs, fortified foods, and supplements; absorption is lower and is enhanced by vitamin C and inhibited by phytates, polyphenols, and calcium taken at the same meal.
Nutrient density
The amount of essential nutrients a food supplies relative to its energy content; the organizing principle when energy needs fall but nutrient needs do not.
Peak bone mass
The maximum bone mineral content an individual attains, roughly 90% of it accrued by age 18–20 and the peak reached in the mid-to-late twenties.
Peak height velocity
The point of fastest linear growth during the adolescent spurt, typically around ages 11–12 in girls and 13–14 in boys.
Physiologic hemodilution of pregnancy
The expected fall in hemoglobin concentration when plasma volume expands by roughly 50% while red cell mass rises proportionally less; a dilution effect distinct from true iron deficiency anemia.
Sarcopenia
Age-related loss of skeletal muscle mass and function, beginning in the fourth decade and accelerating after sixty; a major contributor to falls, fractures, and loss of independence.
Teratogen
An agent that causes structural or functional abnormalities in a developing embryo or fetus — alcohol, methylmercury, and high-dose preformed vitamin A are dietary-relevant examples.
Weaning
The gradual process of transitioning an infant from exclusive milk feeding to the family diet; a process spanning months, not a single event.

Check yourself

1. A person in the first trimester says she has been told to "eat for two." What is the actual estimated additional energy need by trimester, and what should the counseling emphasize instead?
Roughly zero additional kcal in the first trimester, about +340 kcal/day in the second, and about +452 kcal/day in the third. The larger change is in specific nutrients — folate, iron, iodine, choline — which rise proportionally far more than energy does. So the emphasis belongs on nutrient density rather than on eating substantially more food.
2. Why is folic acid guidance aimed at everyone who could become pregnant rather than at people who already know they are pregnant?
The neural tube closes by about day 28 after conception, frequently before a pregnancy is recognized. Since roughly half of pregnancies are unplanned, waiting for a positive test is already too late for this specific outcome — which is also the rationale for population-wide folic acid fortification of enriched grains.
3. State the iron RDA for a non-pregnant woman aged 19–50, for pregnancy, and for lactation. Explain why the lactation value is the lowest of the three.
18 mg/day non-pregnant, 27 mg/day in pregnancy, and 9 mg/day during lactation. Lactation is lowest because menstruation is typically suppressed, removing the largest routine iron loss — the milk itself contains relatively little iron.
4. A pregnant patient's hemoglobin is lower than her pre-pregnancy value. Name the normal physiological process that can explain this, and say why it is not automatically a deficiency.
Physiologic hemodilution of pregnancy: plasma volume expands by roughly 50% while red cell mass rises proportionally less, diluting the hemoglobin concentration. It is a concentration effect rather than a shortage of iron — though true iron deficiency anemia is also common in pregnancy, so the two have to be distinguished rather than assumed.
5. Sort these pregnancy avoidance items by mechanism: swordfish, unpasteurized soft cheese, alcohol, high-dose retinol supplements.
Bioaccumulated toxicant: swordfish (methylmercury). Foodborne pathogen: unpasteurized soft cheese (Listeria monocytogenes). Teratogens: alcohol and high-dose preformed vitamin A. Note that beta-carotene is not implicated in the vitamin A concern, and that most low-mercury fish are actively encouraged in pregnancy rather than avoided.
6. Human milk contains roughly 4 g of fat, 7 g of lactose, and 1 g of protein per 100 mL. Using the standard energy yields, explain why fat supplies close to half the energy.
Fat yields 9 kcal/g while carbohydrate and protein each yield 4 kcal/g (alcohol yields 7 kcal/g). So 4 g of fat contributes about 36 kcal, lactose about 28 kcal, and protein about 4 kcal — roughly 68 kcal total, with fat supplying over half despite being the smaller quantity by weight.
7. Which two supplements are standard in early infancy, and why is each needed even when the infant is exclusively breastfed?
Vitamin K at birth to prevent vitamin K deficiency bleeding, and vitamin D 400 IU/day for breastfed and partially breastfed infants. Human milk is genuinely low in vitamin D regardless of how well the lactating parent eats, so this is not a marker of inadequate maternal nutrition.
8. A caregiver mentions adding extra water to formula to make a can last longer. Explain the danger and the appropriate response.
Over-dilution causes water intoxication and hyponatremia — an infant's kidneys cannot excrete the free-water load — alongside inadequate energy and nutrient intake, and it can cause seizures. Formula must be mixed exactly to label ratio; if cost is the barrier, the answer is food assistance programs and clinician support, never a thinner bottle. Homemade formula recipes carry similar risks and should not be used.
9. Which nutrient runs short first and helps set the timing of complementary foods at around six months, and how has allergen guidance changed?
Iron — a term infant's stores last roughly 4–6 months, which is why iron-rich first foods are emphasized. On allergens, guidance reversed: the LEAP randomized trial (2015) showed early peanut introduction at roughly 4–11 months substantially reduced peanut allergy in high-risk infants, so common allergens are now introduced in the first year rather than delayed. That reversal rests on randomized, not merely observational, evidence.
10. A parent is worried that their eighteen-month-old "barely eats anymore." What is the physiological explanation, and what two things should be assessed instead of a single meal?
Growth decelerates sharply after the first year — from about 7 kg gained in year one to 2–3 kg per year — and appetite falls with it; this is the physiologic anorexia of toddlerhood. Assess intake across five to seven days rather than one meal, and look at whether the child is tracking along their own established growth curve rather than at which percentile they sit on.
11. Why does the calcium RDA for ages 9–18 (1,300 mg/day) exceed the value for pregnancy (1,000 mg/day for adults)?
Adolescents are laying down close to half of their adult skeletal mass, with about 90% of peak bone mass accrued by age 18–20 — a one-time window that does not reopen. Pregnancy does not raise the adult calcium RDA because intestinal calcium absorption up-regulates sharply instead. Pregnant adolescents stay at 1,300 mg/day because they are still building their own skeleton.
12. An older adult eats plenty of meat and eggs but has a low serum vitamin B12. Explain the likely mechanism, why the recommended source differs from the recommended amount, and why high folic acid intake complicates the picture.
Atrophic gastritis, present in roughly 10–30% of older adults, reduces stomach acid and pepsin so food-bound B12 cannot be released from dietary protein. The RDA stays at 2.4 µg/day, but adults over 50 are advised to get most of it from fortified foods or a supplement, because crystalline B12 is not protein-bound and absorbs normally. High folic acid intake can correct the resulting megaloblastic anemia while the neurological damage — paresthesias, gait disturbance, cognitive change — continues and may become permanent.
Nutrition · Chapter 11

Food Safety

Nutrition assumes a working food supply, and most of the time we have one — yet an estimated 48 million people in the United States get sick from something they ate each year, about 128,000 are hospitalized, and roughly 3,000 die. Almost none of that is caused by exotic contamination; it is caused by ordinary time, ordinary temperature, and ordinary hands. This chapter walks through the pathogens that actually cause foodborne illness and how they announce themselves, the temperature rules that stop bacteria from multiplying, the handling habits that keep raw and ready-to-eat foods apart, and who carries the most risk and why. It closes with the harder question students ask constantly — what about additives, pesticide residues, and processing? — and gives you a method for weighing that evidence instead of a list of foods to fear.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Name the major foodborne pathogens and match each to its typical onset time, hallmark symptoms, and classic food sources.
  • Explain why onset time distinguishes a preformed toxin from an infection, and why that distinction changes what you expect clinically.
  • State the boundaries of the temperature danger zone and apply the two-hour rule to holding, cooling, and storage.
  • Recall the safe minimum internal cooking temperatures for poultry, ground meat, whole cuts, fish, and reheated leftovers.
  • Describe the four core safe-handling practices — clean, separate, cook, chill — and justify each mechanistically.
  • Identify the four higher-risk populations and explain the physiology behind each one’s vulnerability.
  • List the foods that carry disproportionate risk during pregnancy, infancy, later life, and immunosuppression.
  • Distinguish hazard from risk, and weigh evidence about additives, residues, and processing without overstating single findings.

Section 1Foodborne illness

What “food poisoning” actually is

The phrase covers two mechanically different events. In an infection, you swallow a living microbe, it survives the stomach, it multiplies in the gut or invades beyond it, and you become ill only after that population has grown — which takes hours to days. In an intoxication, the microbe did its work in the food before you ever picked up the fork, leaving behind a preformed toxin; you are poisoned by a chemical, not colonized by an organism, and symptoms arrive fast. A third category, toxicoinfection, sits between them: the organism is swallowed alive and produces toxin once inside you.

That mechanical split is why onset time is the most useful single clue in a suspected foodborne illness. Vomiting that begins two hours after a meal cannot be an infection — no organism multiplies that fast — so it points to a preformed toxin, classically Staphylococcus aureus or the emetic form of Bacillus cereus. Bloody diarrhea three days after a cookout points to an invasive organism that needed time to establish. And a meal eaten four weeks ago is still a plausible source for hepatitis A.

The organisms worth knowing

Norovirus is the leader by a wide margin, responsible for roughly 58 percent of domestically acquired foodborne illness from known agents. It is extraordinarily transmissible — as few as roughly eighteen viral particles can infect — it is shed in enormous numbers in vomit and stool, it survives on surfaces, and it resists alcohol-based hand sanitizer. It produces one to three days of vomiting and watery diarrhea and then relents. Infected food handlers, raw shellfish, and contaminated leafy greens are the usual routes.

Among bacteria, nontyphoidal Salmonella causes the most hospitalizations and deaths, arriving twelve to seventy-two hours after poultry, eggs, produce, or contact with reptiles and backyard poultry. Campylobacter jejuni is the most common bacterial cause of diarrheal illness, incubating two to five days, most often traced to undercooked poultry or unpasteurized milk, and occasionally followed weeks later by Guillain–Barré syndrome. Shiga toxin–producing E. coli (STEC, especially O157:H7) causes bloody diarrhea with severe cramps and often no fever, and in roughly five to ten percent of cases — concentrated in children under five — progresses to hemolytic uremic syndrome, a triad of hemolytic anemia, thrombocytopenia, and acute kidney injury. Because of that risk, clinicians are cautious about antibiotics and antimotility drugs in suspected STEC.

Listeria monocytogenes deserves its own paragraph because it breaks the usual rules: it grows at refrigerator temperature, it tolerates salt, and its invasive form can incubate for weeks. In healthy adults it may cause only a brief febrile gastroenteritis; in pregnancy, later life, and immunosuppression it crosses into blood and central nervous system, and invasive listeriosis carries a case-fatality around one in five. Clostridium botulinum is rarer still but unmistakable: a heat-labile neurotoxin from improperly home-canned low-acid foods producing symmetric descending flaccid paralysis with cranial nerve signs — double vision, difficulty swallowing, slurred speech — in an alert, afebrile person. Infant botulism is a separate route, which is why honey should not be given to infants under twelve months.

AgentTypical onsetHallmarkClassic sources
Staphylococcus aureus30 min – 8 hAbrupt vomiting; preformed, heat-stable toxin — reheating does not helpHand-handled foods held warm: salads, pastries, sliced meats
Bacillus cereus (emetic)30 min – 6 hVomiting; cereulide toxinCooked rice and pasta held at room temperature
Bacillus cereus (diarrheal)6 – 15 hWatery diarrhea, crampsMeats, stews, sauces, vegetable dishes
Clostridium perfringens6 – 24 hCramps and diarrhea, little vomiting or fever; resolves in a dayLarge batches of meat, gravy, beans cooled or held too slowly
Norovirus12 – 48 hVomiting and diarrhea, 1–3 days; alcohol gel unreliableInfected food handlers, raw shellfish, leafy greens
Salmonella (nontyphoidal)12 – 72 hDiarrhea, fever, cramps, 4–7 daysPoultry, eggs, produce, raw flour, reptiles
Campylobacter jejuni2 – 5 daysDiarrhea (often bloody), fever; rare later Guillain–BarréUndercooked poultry, unpasteurized milk, untreated water
STEC / E. coli O157:H73 – 4 daysBloody diarrhea, often afebrile; risk of HUS in young childrenUndercooked ground beef, raw leafy greens, unpasteurized juice
Vibrio species2 – 48 hDiarrhea; V. vulnificus can cause sepsis in liver diseaseRaw oysters and shellfish, warm coastal waters
Listeria monocytogenes1 day – 10 weeksFebrile gastroenteritis, or invasive sepsis/meningitis; grows at 4°CDeli meats, soft cheeses from raw milk, smoked seafood, melon, sprouts
Clostridium botulinum12 – 72 hDescending flaccid paralysis, cranial nerve palsies, no feverImproperly home-canned low-acid foods; honey in infants
Hepatitis A15 – 50 daysFatigue, nausea, jaundice; vaccine-preventableInfected food handlers, shellfish, frozen berries
Scombroid (histamine)Minutes – 1 hFlushing, headache, rash; chemical, not infectiousTemperature-abused tuna, mahi-mahi, mackerel

What the body loses, and what it needs back

Most foodborne illness in an otherwise healthy adult is self-limiting, and the real hazard is not the organism but the fluid and electrolyte loss. Diarrheal stool carries sodium, potassium, chloride, and bicarbonate. Oral rehydration works because glucose and sodium are absorbed together by a shared intestinal transporter, dragging water along with them — which is why the standard reduced-osmolarity oral rehydration solution defined by the World Health Organization pairs sodium at 75 mmol/L with glucose at 75 mmol/L for a total osmolarity near 245 mOsm/L, rather than simply offering water. Plain water does not restore sodium, and high-sugar soft drinks can worsen osmotic diarrhea.

This is general education, not clinical instruction: signs such as bloody stools, high fever, neurologic symptoms, signs of dehydration, or illness in an infant, an older adult, a pregnant person, or someone immunosuppressed warrant prompt medical evaluation rather than home management.

Onset time is your first diagnostic tool. Under six hours with vomiting dominating means a preformed toxin was already in the food — and because staphylococcal enterotoxin is heat-stable, reheating that dish would not have saved anyone. Days later with fever and diarrhea means a living organism had time to multiply. Weeks later with jaundice means hepatitis A. The clock narrows the list before any test does.
Interactive Set the time from meal to first symptom and the holding temperature, then tap any of fourteen foodborne pathogens to explore its source, onset window, symptoms, and whether it can multiply in the food. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Infection (organism multiplies in you, slower onset) differs from intoxication (preformed toxin, rapid onset).
  • Norovirus causes the most illness; Salmonella causes the most hospitalizations and deaths among bacteria.
  • STEC risks hemolytic uremic syndrome, especially in children under five; Listeria uniquely grows at refrigerator temperature.
  • The immediate danger in most cases is fluid and electrolyte loss, which is why glucose-plus-sodium rehydration outperforms plain water.

Section 2Temperature control

The danger zone and the two-hour rule

Bacteria are not exotic; they are arithmetic. Under favorable conditions a single bacterial cell divides roughly every twenty minutes, so one organism becomes more than a million in about seven hours. The temperature danger zone is the range in which that arithmetic runs fast: the U.S. Department of Agriculture defines it as 40°F to 140°F (4°C to 60°C), while the FDA Food Code used by retail and food-service operations tightens it slightly to 41°F to 135°F (5°C to 57°C). The two numbers differ because they were set for different settings, not because anyone disagrees about the microbiology.

The practical rule that follows is the two-hour rule: perishable food should not sit in the danger zone for more than two hours cumulatively, counting time on the counter, on the buffet, and in the car. Above 90°F (32°C) — a summer picnic, a hot car — that budget drops to one hour. Cumulative is the word people miss: an hour thawing on the counter plus ninety minutes on a serving table has already spent the budget, even though neither step felt long.

Cooking: the kill step

Cooking is the one moment in the chain that reliably reduces the pathogen load rather than merely holding it steady. But it only works if the coldest interior point reaches temperature, which colour and texture cannot tell you — browning is a Maillard reaction driven by surface chemistry, not a thermometer. A ground beef patty can look grey and still be under temperature, and a well-cooked steak can look pink from myoglobin chemistry. Use a food thermometer in the thickest part, away from bone and fat, and calibrate it occasionally in an ice-water slurry, which should read 32°F (0°C).

FoodSafe minimum internal temperatureNote
Poultry — all forms165°F (74°C)Whole birds, pieces, ground poultry, and stuffing alike
Ground meats (beef, pork, lamb, veal)160°F (71°C)Grinding moves surface bacteria throughout the interior
Whole cuts — steaks, chops, roasts145°F (63°C)Plus a 3-minute rest before carving or eating
Fish and shellfish145°F (63°C)Or until flesh is opaque and separates easily
Egg dishes; eggs cooked for holding160°F (71°C)Cook plain eggs until yolk and white are firm
Leftovers, casseroles, reheated dishes165°F (74°C)Reheat to steaming throughout, not just warm at the edge
Hot holding / cold holding≥135°F (57°C) / ≤41°F (5°C)Holding maintains safety; it does not create it

Cooling, thawing, and storage

Cooling is where home and food-service kitchens fail most often, because a large stockpot cools from the outside in and can sit for hours in the middle of the danger zone while the surface feels cold. The Food Code therefore specifies two-stage cooling: from 135°F down to 70°F within two hours, then from 70°F down to 41°F within four more hours — six hours total, with the first stage the strict one. You buy that speed by increasing surface area: divide into shallow pans no more than about two inches deep, use an ice bath, stir with an ice paddle, and leave the container uncovered or loosely covered until it is cold.

Thawing has exactly four safe methods — in the refrigerator, submerged in cold running or frequently changed water, in the microwave with cooking to follow immediately, or as part of the cooking process itself. The counter is not on the list, because the outer layer reaches danger-zone temperature long before the centre thaws. Keep the refrigerator at or below 40°F (4°C) and the freezer at 0°F (−18°C), verified with an appliance thermometer rather than the dial. Refrigerated leftovers are generally best used within three to four days; freezing extends that indefinitely from a safety standpoint, though quality declines.

Two limits are worth stating plainly. Freezing does not kill most bacteria — it suspends them, and they resume growing on thawing (it does reliably kill some parasites, which is why certain fish for raw consumption is required to be frozen). And refrigeration is not a stop, only a brake: Listeria monocytogenes multiplies happily at 4°C, which is the whole reason long-stored, ready-to-eat refrigerated foods behave differently from everything else in this chapter.

Cook, cool, and hold are three separate jobs. Cooking reduces pathogens, chilling stops survivors from multiplying, and holding simply preserves whichever state you achieved. Holding food at 145°F never makes an undercooked dish safe, and reheating never removes a heat-stable staphylococcal toxin. If food spent more than two hours in the danger zone, no later step repairs it — when in doubt, throw it out, and never taste to check.
Interactive Drag a thermometer between the freezer and the stove and watch bacterial growth crawl or explode across the 40–140°F danger zone, with live doubling-time, population, and growth-curve calculations. Open full ↑
Key points
  • The danger zone is 40–140°F (4–60°C) by USDA convention, 41–135°F (5–57°C) in the FDA Food Code.
  • The two-hour rule is cumulative, and drops to one hour above 90°F (32°C).
  • Key temperatures: poultry 165°F, ground meat 160°F, whole cuts 145°F plus a 3-minute rest, leftovers reheated to 165°F.
  • Two-stage cooling: 135→70°F in 2 hours, then 70→41°F in 4 more.
  • Freezing suspends bacteria rather than killing them, and Listeria grows at refrigerator temperature.

Section 3Cross-contamination and hygiene

Clean, separate, cook, chill

Public-health agencies compress the entire field into four verbs, and each one has a mechanism behind it. Clean removes organisms from hands and surfaces before they can be moved. Separate keeps raw animal foods away from anything that will not receive a kill step. Cook applies that kill step to a verified internal temperature. Chill denies survivors the time and temperature they need to multiply. Sections 1 and 2 covered cook and chill; this section is about the other two, which is where most household transmission actually happens.

Hands, and why twenty seconds

Handwashing works mechanically, not chemically. Soap is a surfactant: its molecules have a water-loving head and a fat-loving tail, so they lift oils, soil, and microbes off the skin and suspend them in water, where running water carries them down the drain. Twenty seconds of friction is the interval that reliably reaches the places people skip — thumbs, fingertips, nail beds, and the backs of the hands — and then a clean towel finishes the job, because damp hands transfer organisms far more readily than dry ones.

Alcohol-based hand sanitizer at 60 percent or above is a genuine tool, but a limited one. It denatures proteins and disrupts lipid envelopes, so it handles many bacteria and enveloped viruses well. It is unreliable against norovirus, which has a tough non-enveloped capsid, against Clostridioides difficile spores, and against any hand that is visibly soiled or greasy, because it does not physically remove anything. In a kitchen, sanitizer supplements soap and water; it does not replace it.

Wash before starting to prepare food, after handling raw meat, poultry, seafood, eggs, or flour, after using the bathroom, after handling garbage or pets, after diaper changes, and after coughing, sneezing, or blowing your nose. The same logic governs who should be in a kitchen at all: anyone with vomiting, diarrhea, jaundice, a sore throat with fever, or an open infected lesion on the hands should not be preparing food for others. Gloves do not change this — they are a barrier for ready-to-eat food, not a substitute for clean hands, and they must be changed between tasks exactly as hands would be washed between tasks.

Keeping raw and ready-to-eat apart

Cross-contamination is the transfer of pathogens from one food, surface, or hand to another. The classic path runs from raw poultry to a cutting board to a salad, and the classic error is thinking a rinse in between was enough. Use separate boards for raw animal foods and for produce and other ready-to-eat items; wash boards, knives, and counters with hot soapy water and then sanitize; never return cooked food to the plate that held it raw. In the refrigerator, store raw meat, poultry, and seafood on the bottom shelf in sealed containers so nothing can drip onto foods below.

Two counterintuitive rules are worth internalizing. First, do not wash raw poultry or meat. Rinsing does not remove pathogens meaningfully, and controlled kitchen studies using tracer organisms have repeatedly shown that it splashes and spreads bacteria around the sink and onto nearby surfaces and foods; cooking to 165°F is the step that works. Second, do wash produce — but only with running water. Rub firm produce under the tap or scrub it with a clean brush, wash melon rinds before cutting so the knife does not carry surface organisms into the flesh, and skip soap, detergent, bleach, and commercial produce washes, which are not approved for that use and can leave residues. Bagged greens labelled “washed” or “ready to eat” do not need rewashing, and rewashing them in a home sink can add contamination rather than remove it.

PracticeWhat to doWhy it works
Clean — handsSoap and running water, 20 seconds of friction, dry with a clean towelSurfactant lifts microbes; friction and rinsing physically remove them
Clean — surfacesWash with hot soapy water, then sanitize; launder cloths hot; replace sponges oftenWashing removes soil so the sanitizer can actually contact the surface
Separate — boardsDedicated boards for raw animal foods vs. ready-to-eat foodsReady-to-eat foods get no later kill step
Separate — fridgeRaw meat, poultry, seafood sealed on the bottom shelfGravity: drips can only fall onto things below
Separate — platesNever reuse the raw-meat platter or marinade for cooked foodThe plate carries the same load the raw food did
Do not doWashing raw chicken; washing produce with soap or bleachRinsing splashes pathogens; detergents are not approved for produce
CookVerify internal temperature with a thermometerAppearance is Maillard chemistry, not a measure of pathogen kill
ChillRefrigerate within 2 hours (1 hour above 90°F); shallow containersRemoves the time and temperature bacteria need to multiply
The sink is a contaminated zone, not a cleaning zone. If you rinse raw chicken, you have just seeded the sink, the tap handle, and roughly a three-foot radius of counter — and the salad you rinse next goes through that same basin. Handle raw animal foods on a dedicated board, wash your hands before you touch anything else, and let the cooking temperature do the killing.
Interactive Inspect an interactive kitchen scene and tap every cross-contamination hazard — seven ways bacteria jump from raw to ready-to-eat foods, each with the fix that breaks the chain. Open full ↑
Key points
  • The four core practices are clean, separate, cook, chill, and each has a mechanism.
  • Handwashing is mechanical removal: soap plus friction plus running water plus a dry towel, 20 seconds.
  • Alcohol sanitizer (≥60%) supplements soap but fails against norovirus, spores, and soiled hands.
  • Do not wash raw poultry; do wash produce under running water only, including melon rinds before cutting.
  • Store raw animal foods sealed on the bottom shelf, and keep boards and plates for raw and ready-to-eat foods separate.

Section 4High-risk foods and populations

Who is more vulnerable, and why

Four groups carry substantially more risk from the same contaminated meal: pregnant people, children under five, adults over sixty-five, and people who are immunocompromised. The reasons are physiological, not behavioural, and it is worth knowing them rather than memorizing the list.

Pregnancy shifts cell-mediated immunity in ways that protect the fetus from maternal rejection but leave the pregnant person less able to contain intracellular pathogens. The result is a risk of listeriosis roughly ten times that of the general population. Listeria can cross the placenta even when the pregnant person feels only mildly unwell, causing miscarriage, stillbirth, preterm birth, or neonatal sepsis and meningitis. Toxoplasma gondii, acquired from undercooked meat or contaminated soil and cat litter, poses a parallel congenital risk.

Children under five have immature adaptive immunity, a less-developed gastric acid barrier in infancy, and a small body mass, so both the infectious dose required and the fluid reserve available work against them. This is the age band in which STEC infection most often progresses to hemolytic uremic syndrome, and in which dehydration from any gastroenteritis escalates fastest. Infants under twelve months are additionally susceptible to infant botulism, because their gut flora is not yet established enough to suppress germinating C. botulinum spores — hence the honey rule.

Adults over sixty-five face immunosenescence, reduced gastric acid secretion, slower intestinal motility that prolongs contact with pathogens, more comorbid disease, and more medications. Immunocompromising conditions and treatments — cancer chemotherapy, transplant immunosuppression, advanced HIV, chronic corticosteroids, diabetes, liver disease, and kidney disease — blunt the same defences. One underappreciated group cuts across all of these: people taking acid-suppressing medication such as proton pump inhibitors lose much of the gastric acid barrier, and observational studies consistently associate their use with higher rates of Salmonella, Campylobacter, and Vibrio infection — a pattern consistent with a lowered infectious dose, though the size of the effect is debated. And people with liver disease or iron overload are at particular risk from Vibrio vulnificus in raw oysters, because the organism thrives on available iron.

GroupPhysiological reasonChief concerns
PregnancyShift in cell-mediated immunity; placental transferListeria (about 10× risk), Toxoplasma, methylmercury, preformed vitamin A excess
Under 5 yearsImmature immunity, low gastric acid, small fluid reserveSTEC/HUS, Salmonella, rapid dehydration; botulism under 12 months
65 and olderImmunosenescence, less gastric acid, slower motility, comorbidityListeria, Salmonella, higher hospitalization and death rates
ImmunocompromisedImpaired cell-mediated immunity and phagocytosisInvasive Listeria and Salmonella, bacteremia, meningitis
On acid suppressionGastric acid barrier bluntedHigher observed rates of Salmonella, Campylobacter, Vibrio
Liver disease / iron overloadAvailable iron favours the organismVibrio vulnificus sepsis from raw shellfish

The foods that carry disproportionate risk

A food earns a place on this list for one of two reasons: it is eaten without a kill step, or its structure protects organisms from one. Raw and undercooked animal foods are obvious. Less obvious are raw sprouts, whose warm, humid germination conditions are effectively an incubator for any pathogen on the seed; precut melon and leafy greens, where surface contamination is redistributed by cutting and never heated; raw flour, which is an unprocessed agricultural product and has caused both Salmonella and STEC outbreaks in raw dough; and refrigerated ready-to-eat foods such as deli meats, patés, and smoked seafood, which sit for weeks at exactly the temperature Listeria tolerates.

Unpasteurized milk deserves a direct answer, because the claims made for it are nutritional. Pasteurization does not meaningfully reduce the nutritional value of milk. Protein, fat, and calcium are unaffected — a cup of milk supplies roughly 300 mg of calcium either way, against an RDA of 1,000 mg/day for adults aged 19–50 and men to 70, rising to 1,200 mg/day for women 51 and older and for everyone over 70. Losses are limited to a small fraction of a few heat-sensitive vitamins, and milk is not a meaningful source of those. There is no good evidence that raw milk relieves lactose intolerance or prevents asthma, and there is abundant evidence that it transmits Campylobacter, Salmonella, STEC, and Listeria. Standard high-temperature short-time pasteurization — 161°F (72°C) for 15 seconds — is one of the highest-return public-health interventions ever adopted.

Two nutrient-specific cautions belong here as well. In pregnancy, fish is beneficial: the long-chain omega-3 fatty acid DHA supports fetal brain and retinal development, and joint FDA and EPA advice is to eat two to three servings a week from lower-mercury “best choices” such as salmon, sardines, canned light tuna, shrimp, tilapia, and cod, while avoiding the highest-methylmercury species — shark, swordfish, king mackerel, Gulf tilefish, bigeye tuna, marlin, and orange roughy. The message is which fish, not whether fish. Separately, preformed vitamin A (retinol) is teratogenic in excess, with an adult tolerable upper intake level of 3,000 μg RAE/day; liver is extraordinarily concentrated in it, which is why liver is limited in pregnancy. Beta-carotene from plant foods does not carry this risk, because conversion to retinol is regulated.

These are situational precautions, not moral categories. Soft cheese, deli meat, raw oysters, and rare steak are not “bad foods” — they are foods whose risk profile is unfavourable during specific windows of vulnerability. The same deli meat that a pregnant person heats to steaming is unremarkable for a healthy non-pregnant adult. Risk management is about matching handling to physiology, and it should never slide into shaming a person for what they eat.
Interactive Match higher-risk foods to the populations more vulnerable to foodborne illness, and see the physiology behind each pairing. Open full ↑
Key points
  • The four higher-risk groups are pregnancy, under five, sixty-five and older, and immunocompromised, for identifiable physiological reasons.
  • Pregnancy carries roughly ten times the listeriosis risk; children under five carry the highest HUS risk from STEC.
  • High-risk foods lack a kill step: raw animal foods, raw sprouts, precut melon, raw flour, and refrigerated ready-to-eat foods.
  • Pasteurization (161°F/72°C for 15 s) removes pathogens without meaningfully reducing milk’s nutritional value.
  • In pregnancy, choose lower-mercury fish rather than avoiding fish, and limit preformed vitamin A (UL 3,000 μg RAE/day).

Section 5Additives, residues, and processing

Who regulates what

In the United States the responsibility is split. The Food and Drug Administration oversees most of the food supply, including additives, labelling, and produce safety. The USDA Food Safety and Inspection Service handles meat, poultry, catfish, and processed egg products, with continuous inspection at slaughter and processing plants. The Environmental Protection Agency sets the legal maximum residue level — the tolerance — for each pesticide on each crop, and the FDA and USDA enforce those tolerances in monitoring programs. The Food Safety Modernization Act of 2011 shifted the FDA’s posture from reacting to outbreaks toward requiring preventive controls, and most commercial food processors now operate a HACCP plan — Hazard Analysis and Critical Control Points — that identifies where in the process a hazard can be controlled and monitors those points continuously.

Additives are approved under the Federal Food, Drug, and Cosmetic Act as amended in 1958. A substance either goes through formal food-additive approval or qualifies as GRAS — Generally Recognized As Safe — based on scientific consensus or long-standing use. The Delaney Clause in that law bars approval of any food additive shown to induce cancer in humans or animals, which is why the FDA revoked authorization for FD&C Red No. 3 in food in January 2025 on the basis of rat thyroid tumours, even though the mechanism involved is not thought to operate in humans. It is fair to note the system’s most-criticized feature honestly: companies may self-affirm GRAS status without notifying the FDA, and that loophole is a legitimate subject of scientific and policy debate.

How safety limits are actually set

The core concept is the Acceptable Daily Intake (ADI): the amount, in milligrams per kilogram of body weight per day, that can be consumed over a lifetime without appreciable risk. It is derived from the highest dose producing no observed adverse effect in animal studies (the NOAEL), divided by a safety factor of 100 — a factor of ten for possible differences between animals and humans, and another ten for variation among humans. Real-world intakes typically fall far below the ADI. Aspartame is the standard teaching case: the FDA sets its ADI at 50 mg/kg body weight per day and the WHO/FAO Joint Expert Committee on Food Additives (JECFA) at 40 mg/kg body weight per day. In 2023 the International Agency for Research on Cancer classified aspartame as “possibly carcinogenic to humans” (Group 2B) on the basis of limited evidence, while JECFA — reviewing the same literature for risk rather than hazard — reaffirmed its 40 mg/kg ADI unchanged.

That apparent contradiction is the single most important idea in this section. Hazard asks whether a substance can cause harm under some conditions. Risk asks whether it will, at the doses people actually encounter. IARC classifies hazards, not risks — which is why processed meat sits in Group 1 (sufficient evidence) alongside tobacco while conferring a vastly smaller absolute risk. The processed-meat finding itself is a good example of reading a number properly: roughly an 18 percent higher relative risk of colorectal cancer per 50 g/day consumed. Applied to a baseline lifetime risk of about four to five percent, that is a change of well under one percentage point — real, worth knowing, and not equivalent to smoking. It is also an association from observational cohorts, so the effect size should be held loosely. Note also that nitrite in cured meat is there partly to prevent botulism, and that “uncured” products preserved with celery powder deliver nitrite from a natural source rather than none at all.

ConcernWhat is knownStrength of evidence
Pesticide residuesEPA sets crop-specific tolerances; USDA monitoring consistently finds over 99 percent of samples at or below tolerance, and washing and peeling reduce residues furtherStrong that exposures are low; limited that they cause harm at those levels
Organic produceReliably lowers measured pesticide-residue exposureStrong for residue reduction; preliminary for health outcomes
Processed meatIARC Group 1; about 18% higher relative colorectal cancer risk per 50 g/dayStrong association; modest absolute effect
AspartameIARC Group 2B (2023); JECFA reaffirmed its ADI of 40 mg/kg body weight/day (FDA uses 50)Limited and contested; label warning is for phenylketonuria
Synthetic food dyesRed No. 3 authorization revoked (2025) under the Delaney Clause; some children may show behavioural sensitivityPreliminary for behaviour; regulatory action is statutory, not risk-based
AcrylamideForms above about 120°C in starchy foods (asparagine plus reducing sugars); IARC Group 2AStrong in animals; inconsistent in human studies
Heavy metalsArsenic in rice, lead and cadmium traces in some foods; FDA action levels tighteningStrong that exposure is measurable; guidance is precautionary, focused on infants and young children
Ultra-processed foodsCohort studies link higher intake to cardiometabolic outcomes; one small inpatient randomized crossover trial found about 500 kcal/day greater spontaneous intakeConsistent observationally; mechanism unresolved

Processing: neither villain nor neutral

“Processed” is not one thing. Pasteurization, canning, freezing, refrigeration, fortification, and milling are all processing, and several of them are among the most effective public-health measures in the history of food — iodized salt largely ended endemic goiter, folic acid fortification of enriched grains was followed by a substantial drop in neural tube defects, and pasteurization removed a whole category of dairy-borne disease. Frozen and canned vegetables are nutritionally comparable to fresh and are often cheaper and more accessible.

The current research interest is narrower, focused on ultra-processed foods as defined by the NOVA classification: industrial formulations built largely from refined substances and additives rather than from recognizable whole foods. Observational cohorts consistently associate higher intake with cardiometabolic outcomes, and a small inpatient randomized crossover trial in twenty adults found participants spontaneously ate roughly 500 kcal/day more on an ultra-processed diet matched for presented macronutrients. That is a genuine finding, but a single trial in twenty people, and the mechanism is unsettled — candidates include energy density, softer texture and faster eating rate, hyperpalatability, and lower satiety per calorie. Energy density itself is arithmetic: fat supplies 9 kcal/g, while carbohydrate and protein each supply 4 kcal/g and alcohol supplies 7 kcal/g, so a food’s fat and water content largely determine how many calories arrive per bite. Treat the additive hypothesis as preliminary, and do not convert an association into a claim about any one ingredient.

When you meet the next headline, run five questions. What was the study design — test tube, animal, observational cohort, or randomized trial? Was the dose realistic, or hundreds of times typical intake? Is the number a relative or an absolute risk? Is this a hazard classification or a risk estimate? And does one study overturn a body of prior evidence, or add to it? That method is more durable than any list of ingredients to avoid, and it keeps a small, uncertain risk from crowding out a benefit that rests on far better evidence — diets richer in fruits and vegetables are among the most consistently supported patterns in nutrition, backed by large cohorts and by trials of dietary patterns, so residue anxiety that reduces produce intake trades a well-supported benefit for a speculative one.

Hazard is not risk, and relative is not absolute. A substance can be a genuine hazard at some dose and pose negligible risk at the dose you encounter; a 20 percent increase in a rare outcome is still a rare outcome. Nearly every alarming food headline collapses when you ask which of those two things it is actually reporting.
Interactive Weigh common claims about additives and processing on an interactive balance scale that tips as how strong a claim sounds is compared to how strong the evidence actually is. Open full ↑
Key points
  • FDA covers most foods, USDA-FSIS covers meat, poultry, and egg products, and EPA sets pesticide tolerances; HACCP and FSMA made prevention the operating model.
  • The ADI comes from the animal NOAEL divided by a safety factor of 100, and typical intakes fall far below it.
  • Hazard ≠ risk: IARC classifies hazards, so Group 1 processed meat and Group 1 tobacco differ enormously in absolute risk.
  • Processing includes protective steps — pasteurization, fortification, freezing — and the ultra-processed literature is consistent observationally but mechanistically unresolved.
  • Energy density follows the yields: fat 9 kcal/g, carbohydrate and protein 4 kcal/g each, alcohol 7 kcal/g.

The chapter in one breath

  • Foodborne illness is either an infection (organism multiplies in you, slower onset) or an intoxication from a preformed toxin (rapid onset), and onset time is the fastest way to tell them apart.
  • Norovirus causes the most illness; Salmonella the most bacterial hospitalizations and deaths; STEC risks HUS in young children; Listeria uniquely grows at refrigerator temperature.
  • The danger zone is 40–140°F (4–60°C), and the two-hour rule is cumulative — one hour above 90°F.
  • Cook poultry to 165°F, ground meat to 160°F, whole cuts to 145°F with a 3-minute rest, and reheat leftovers to 165°F; cool 135→70°F in 2 hours, then 70→41°F in 4 more.
  • Clean, separate, cook, chill: wash hands 20 seconds with soap and running water, keep raw animal foods on the bottom shelf and on their own board, wash produce under running water only, and never rinse raw poultry.
  • Higher risk falls on pregnancy, children under five, adults 65 and older, and the immunocompromised for reasons of immunity, gastric acid, and reserve — and on anyone taking acid-suppressing medication.
  • Pasteurization removes pathogens without meaningfully costing nutrition, and in pregnancy the advice on fish is which fish, not whether fish.
  • For additives, residues, and processing, separate hazard from risk and relative from absolute, weight evidence by study design, and let strong evidence outrank preliminary evidence.

ReferenceWords worth knowing

A quick reference for the terms in this chapter — skim it now, or circle back whenever a word trips you up.

Acceptable Daily Intake (ADI)
The amount of a substance, in mg per kg body weight per day, judged safe to consume daily over a lifetime; derived from the animal NOAEL divided by a safety factor, conventionally 100.
Acrylamide
A compound formed from asparagine and reducing sugars when starchy foods are cooked above roughly 120°C; classified by IARC as probably carcinogenic to humans based mainly on animal data.
Cross-contamination
Transfer of pathogens from one food, surface, utensil, or hand to another — classically from raw animal foods to ready-to-eat foods.
Danger zone
The temperature range in which bacteria multiply rapidly: 40–140°F (4–60°C) by USDA convention, 41–135°F (5–57°C) in the FDA Food Code.
Delaney Clause
The provision of U.S. food law barring approval of any food additive shown to induce cancer in humans or animals, regardless of dose.
GRAS
Generally Recognized As Safe — a regulatory status allowing use of a substance without formal food-additive approval, based on scientific consensus or long-standing safe use.
HACCP
Hazard Analysis and Critical Control Points — a preventive food-safety system that identifies where hazards can be controlled in a process and monitors those points continuously.
Hazard
The capacity of a substance or agent to cause harm under some conditions, independent of the dose actually encountered.
Hemolytic uremic syndrome (HUS)
A complication of Shiga toxin–producing E. coli infection — hemolytic anemia, thrombocytopenia, and acute kidney injury — most common in children under five.
Immunosenescence
The age-related decline in immune function that contributes to higher foodborne-illness severity in older adults.
Infection (foodborne)
Illness caused by swallowing a living pathogen that multiplies in the host; onset is typically hours to days.
Intoxication (foodborne)
Illness caused by a toxin already present in the food before it was eaten; onset is typically minutes to a few hours.
Listeriosis
Infection with Listeria monocytogenes, which grows at refrigerator temperature and causes invasive disease chiefly in pregnancy, older adults, and the immunocompromised.
NOAEL
No Observed Adverse Effect Level — the highest tested dose producing no detectable harm in toxicology studies; the starting point for setting an ADI.
Pasteurization
Controlled heating that destroys pathogens without sterilizing; the high-temperature short-time standard for milk is 161°F (72°C) for 15 seconds.
Preformed toxin
A toxin produced by microbes in the food itself; staphylococcal enterotoxin is heat-stable, so reheating does not make the food safe.
Relative risk
The ratio of risk between exposed and unexposed groups; must be applied to a baseline risk to yield the absolute change that matters practically.
Risk
The probability that harm actually occurs at the exposure levels people encounter — hazard combined with dose.
Tolerance (pesticide)
The legal maximum residue of a specific pesticide permitted on a specific food, set by the EPA and enforced by the FDA and USDA.
Two-hour rule
The guidance that perishable food should spend no more than two cumulative hours in the danger zone, reduced to one hour above 90°F (32°C).
Two-stage cooling
Cooling cooked food from 135°F to 70°F within two hours, then from 70°F to 41°F within four additional hours.
Ultra-processed food
Under the NOVA classification, an industrial formulation built largely from refined substances and additives rather than recognizable whole foods.

Check yourself

1. Guests at a picnic begin vomiting about two hours after eating a potato salad that sat out in the sun. Why does the timing rule out an infection, and what agent is most likely?
No organism can multiply enough to cause illness in two hours, so this must be a preformed toxin — classically Staphylococcus aureus enterotoxin. Hand-handled foods held warm let S. aureus produce toxin in the dish. The toxin is heat-stable, so reheating the salad would not have made it safe.
2. A four-year-old develops bloody diarrhea without much fever three days after a barbecue, and then becomes pale and passes little urine. What is the pathogen and the complication?
Shiga toxin–producing E. coli (often O157:H7) complicated by hemolytic uremic syndrome. HUS is hemolytic anemia, thrombocytopenia, and acute kidney injury; it follows roughly five to ten percent of STEC infections and is most common under age five, which is why clinicians are cautious with antibiotics and antimotility drugs in suspected STEC.
3. Why is Listeria monocytogenes the one pathogen that refrigeration does not control, and which foods does that fact single out?
Listeria grows at refrigerator temperature (around 4°C) and tolerates salt, so cold storage does not stop it. That makes long-stored refrigerated ready-to-eat foods the concern: deli meats, hot dogs, patés, refrigerated smoked seafood, soft cheeses made from unpasteurized milk, precut melon, and raw sprouts.
4. State the temperature danger zone and the two-hour rule, including the modification for hot weather. Why does the word “cumulative” matter?
40–140°F (4–60°C) by USDA convention; perishable food should spend no more than two cumulative hours there, dropping to one hour above 90°F (32°C). Cumulative matters because time on the counter, in the car, and on the serving table all count together — several short exposures can exhaust the budget without any single one feeling long.
5. Give the safe minimum internal temperature for poultry, ground beef, a pork chop, and reheated leftovers. Why is ground meat higher than a whole cut?
Poultry 165°F (74°C); ground meats 160°F (71°C); whole cuts such as a pork chop 145°F (63°C) plus a 3-minute rest; leftovers reheated to 165°F (74°C). Grinding distributes surface bacteria throughout the interior, so the whole mass must reach a lethal temperature, while on a whole cut the contamination stays on the surface where searing reaches it.
6. A kitchen puts a full stockpot of chili straight into the walk-in refrigerator. What is wrong, and what is the correct cooling standard?
A deep pot cools from the outside in, so the centre lingers in the danger zone for hours — ideal conditions for Clostridium perfringens. The two-stage standard is 135°F to 70°F within two hours, then 70°F to 41°F within four more. Achieve it by dividing into shallow pans about two inches deep, using an ice bath or ice paddle, and leaving containers loosely covered until cold.
7. Name the four safe thawing methods, and explain why the kitchen counter is not among them.
In the refrigerator, in cold running or frequently changed water, in the microwave with cooking immediately after, or as part of the cooking process. On the counter the outer layer warms into the danger zone and stays there for hours while the centre is still frozen, so bacteria multiply on the surface long before the food is ready to cook.
8. Explain the mechanism of handwashing, and state two situations in which alcohol-based sanitizer is a poor substitute.
Soap is a surfactant that lifts microbes and oils off the skin so running water can carry them away — it is mechanical removal, and 20 seconds of friction reaches the thumbs, fingertips, and nail beds people skip. Sanitizer (≥60% alcohol) is unreliable against non-enveloped norovirus and against spores, and it does nothing on visibly soiled or greasy hands because it removes nothing.
9. A student rinses raw chicken in the sink “to clean it,” then washes lettuce in the same basin. Identify both errors.
Rinsing raw poultry does not remove pathogens and instead splashes them around the sink, tap, and nearby surfaces; the lettuce then passes through that contaminated basin and gets no kill step. The correct sequence is to skip rinsing the chicken entirely, cook it to 165°F, and wash and sanitize the sink and hands before any ready-to-eat food is handled.
10. Why is a pregnant person advised to heat deli meat to steaming, and roughly how much higher is their listeriosis risk?
Pregnancy shifts cell-mediated immunity, raising listeriosis risk to roughly ten times that of the general population, and Listeria grows on refrigerated deli meat and can cross the placenta. Heating to steaming (165°F/74°C) supplies the kill step the food otherwise never gets. This is a situational precaution for a window of vulnerability, not a judgment about the food.
11. A friend argues that pasteurization “destroys the nutrition” in milk. Evaluate that claim.
It is not supported. Protein, fat, and calcium are essentially unchanged — about 300 mg of calcium per cup either way, against an adult RDA of 1,000 mg/day at ages 19–50 (1,200 mg/day for women 51 and older and for everyone over 70). Losses are limited to a fraction of a few heat-sensitive vitamins for which milk is not a meaningful source. Meanwhile raw milk transmits Campylobacter, Salmonella, STEC, and Listeria, and standard HTST pasteurization is only 161°F (72°C) for 15 seconds.
12. A headline reads that a sweetener is “classified as possibly carcinogenic.” Explain how to interpret that, using the hazard-versus-risk distinction and the ADI.
IARC classifies hazard — whether a substance can cause harm under some conditions — not risk at real intakes, which is why aspartame was placed in Group 2B in 2023 while the WHO/FAO expert committee simultaneously reaffirmed its Acceptable Daily Intake of 40 mg/kg body weight per day (the FDA uses 50 mg/kg/day). An ADI is the animal NOAEL divided by a safety factor of 100, and typical consumption falls far below it. Ask also whether the number reported is relative or absolute, and whether one study is overturning or merely adding to prior evidence.
Nutrition · Chapter 12

Diet & Chronic Disease

Most of the illness that shortens life in wealthy countries is slow: arteries that stiffen over decades, blood sugar that drifts upward for years before anyone notices, bone that thins so quietly it announces itself only as a fracture.
