Diet is one of the levers on all of it — but the size and the certainty of that lever vary enormously from claim to claim, and the popular version of nutrition science routinely oversells both. This chapter walks through what the evidence actually supports for cardiovascular disease, type 2 diabetes, cancer, and bone health, and it is honest about where the evidence is strong, where it is suggestive, and where it is little more than a plausible mechanism. It ends with the single most robust finding in the whole field: that overall dietary patterns predict health far better than any individual nutrient does. Nothing here is medical or dietary advice for an individual — it is the background you need to read a headline, a label, or a research abstract without being fooled.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Explain how saturated, unsaturated, and trans fats differ in their effect on LDL cholesterol, and why what replaces a fat matters as much as the fat itself.
  • State the current sodium and potassium intake references for adults, with units, and describe where most dietary sodium actually comes from.
  • Describe the physiology linking insulin resistance, body fatness, and type 2 diabetes, and summarize what carbohydrate quality versus quantity contributes to risk.
  • Interpret the IARC classification of processed meat and alcohol, and explain why a hazard class is not the same thing as a risk magnitude.
  • Distinguish evidence that is strong (consistent trials plus mechanism) from evidence that is preliminary (single cohorts, surrogate endpoints, mechanism alone).
  • State the adult RDAs for calcium and vitamin D, note how they differ by age and sex, and name the deficiency syndromes.
  • Compare the Mediterranean, DASH, and plant-based patterns and identify the features they share.
  • Explain why single-nutrient supplement trials have so often failed to reproduce the benefits seen with whole-diet patterns.

Section 1Diet and cardiovascular disease

Lipids: what the blood is actually carrying

Fat does not dissolve in plasma, so the body packages it. Lipoproteins are shells of phospholipid and protein wrapped around a cargo of triglyceride and cholesterol, and they differ mainly in what they are carrying and where they are going. Chylomicrons haul dietary fat out of the gut. VLDL exports triglyceride made by the liver. As VLDL unloads triglyceride it shrinks into LDL, the cholesterol-rich particle that delivers cholesterol to tissues — and, when it lodges in an arterial wall and is oxidized, the particle that seeds an atherosclerotic plaque. HDL runs the other direction, collecting cholesterol for return to the liver.

The link between LDL cholesterol and atherosclerosis is about as strong as evidence in nutrition and medicine gets: it is supported by animal work, by human genetics (people who inherit lifelong low LDL have lifelong low cardiovascular risk), and by many large randomized drug trials in which lowering LDL lowered heart attacks. Note carefully what that establishes — that LDL is causal. It does not automatically mean any given food raises risk. HDL is a different story: low HDL reliably predicts risk, but drugs and genetic variants that raise HDL have generally failed to reduce events, which is a useful reminder that a marker that travels with disease is not necessarily a cause of it.

Which fats do what

Dietary fats change LDL in predictable directions. Saturated fat raises LDL cholesterol relative to unsaturated fat. Industrial trans fat from partially hydrogenated oil is worse still — it raises LDL and lowers HDL — which is why regulators in the United States withdrew its "generally recognized as safe" status in 2015 and it has since largely disappeared from the food supply, and why the World Health Organization has pushed for global elimination. Polyunsaturated and monounsaturated fats lower LDL when they take saturated fat's place.

That last clause carries most of the weight. Every food you remove is replaced by something, and the comparison is what the evidence measures. Trials and pooled cohorts agree that replacing saturated fat with polyunsaturated fat reduces cardiovascular events; replacing it with refined carbohydrate does not. A study that reports "saturated fat was not associated with heart disease" without saying what replaced it has answered a question nobody asked. Dietary cholesterol itself — the cholesterol in an egg yolk or shrimp — turns out to have a modest and highly variable effect on most people's blood LDL, which is why the old blanket 300 mg/day cap was dropped from United States dietary guidance in 2015.

Fat typeTypical sourcesEffect on LDLStrength of evidence
SaturatedFatty meat, butter, cheese, coconut and palm oilRaises LDL vs unsaturated fatStrong (many controlled feeding trials)
Industrial transPartially hydrogenated oil (now largely removed)Raises LDL and lowers HDLStrong; no safe intake identified
MonounsaturatedOlive oil, canola, avocado, most nutsLowers LDL when replacing saturated fatStrong
Polyunsaturated (n−6)Soybean, corn, sunflower oil, seedsLowers LDL; lowers events on substitutionStrong
Long-chain n−3 (EPA/DHA)Salmon, sardines, mackerel, herringLowers triglyceride; little LDL effectModerate for fish in the diet; weak for general-population supplements

Sodium, potassium, and blood pressure

High blood pressure is the largest single contributor to cardiovascular death worldwide, and sodium intake moves it. In controlled feeding trials, cutting sodium lowers blood pressure in a dose-dependent way, with larger drops in people who already have hypertension, in older adults, and in those who are constitutionally "salt sensitive." The current United States reference values for adults 19 years and older are an Adequate Intake of 1,500 mg/day of sodium and a separate Chronic Disease Risk Reduction intake of 2,300 mg/day — the level above which reducing intake is expected to lower chronic disease risk. Average intake in the United States runs near 3,400 mg/day, and roughly seven of every ten of those milligrams arrive already dissolved in packaged and restaurant food, not from the shaker at the table.

Potassium works in the other direction, promoting urinary sodium loss and vascular relaxation. The 2019 Adequate Intake for adults is 3,400 mg/day for men and 2,600 mg/day for women (these figures were revised downward from an earlier 4,700 mg value; values also differ for adolescents, pregnancy, and lactation). Most people fall well short, and the richest sources are unglamorous: potatoes, beans, leafy greens, bananas, tomatoes, yogurt, and other minimally processed plant and dairy foods. Viscous soluble fibers — the beta-glucan in oats and barley, psyllium — add a small independent LDL reduction by binding bile acids in the gut.

Substitution is the whole question. No food is eaten in a vacuum. "Is butter bad?" is unanswerable; "what happens to LDL and to events when olive oil replaces butter?" has an answer. When you read a nutrition study, find the comparison group first — if the paper does not tell you what the nutrient was replaced with, it cannot tell you what to do.
Interactive Adjust dietary factors and watch cardiovascular risk markers — LDL cholesterol, blood pressure, and triglycerides — respond with average, evidence-graded shifts. Open full ↑
Key points
  • LDL cholesterol is causally linked to atherosclerosis; HDL predicts risk but raising it has not reduced events.
  • Saturated and industrial trans fats raise LDL; unsaturated fats lower it when they replace saturated fat — refined carbohydrate replacement does not help.
  • Adult sodium references: AI 1,500 mg/day, CDRR 2,300 mg/day; average United States intake is about 3,400 mg/day, mostly from packaged and restaurant foods.
  • Potassium AI for adults is 3,400 mg/day (men) and 2,600 mg/day (women); most people do not reach it.

Section 2Diet and diabetes

What goes wrong in type 2 diabetes

Every digestible carbohydrate you eat — starch, sucrose, lactose — is dismantled to monosaccharides before it crosses the intestinal wall, and most of it arrives in the blood as glucose. Insulin is the signal that tells muscle, liver, and adipose tissue to take that glucose up and store it. In type 2 diabetes, two things go wrong together: tissues respond poorly to insulin (insulin resistance), and the pancreatic beta cells eventually cannot secrete enough extra insulin to compensate. Blood glucose then climbs. The process is gradual, which is why prediabetes — a fasting glucose of 100–125 mg/dL or an HbA1c of 5.7–6.4% — can persist silently for years before diabetes is diagnosed at a fasting glucose of 126 mg/dL or higher, or an HbA1c of 6.5% or higher.

Excess adipose tissue, particularly visceral fat around the organs, is among the strongest predictors of insulin resistance. That is a statement about physiology, not about character: adipose tissue is metabolically active, releasing free fatty acids and inflammatory signals that interfere with insulin action, and how much of it a person carries is shaped by genetics, medications, sleep, stress, food access, and income at least as much as by anything a person chooses at a meal. Risk also rises with age, with family history, and is higher in several populations at lower body weights — South Asian populations in particular develop insulin resistance at lower body mass index than European-ancestry populations.

Carbohydrate: amount versus quality

The RDA for carbohydrate is 130 g/day for adults and children, a figure set by the brain's obligate glucose demand, rising to 175 g/day in pregnancy and 210 g/day in lactation. The Acceptable Macronutrient Distribution Range is 45–65% of energy from carbohydrate. Remember the energy yields underneath all of this: carbohydrate 4 kcal/g, protein 4 kcal/g, fat 9 kcal/g, alcohol 7 kcal/g.

What the evidence supports reasonably well is that carbohydrate quality tracks with type 2 diabetes risk more consistently than carbohydrate quantity. Higher whole-grain intake is associated with lower risk across many large cohorts; sugar-sweetened beverages are associated with higher risk, and the association survives adjustment for body weight, suggesting more than a simple calorie story. The evidence for glycemic index as a standalone tool is weaker and more inconsistent than its popularity implies — the index is measured on isolated foods in fasting subjects, and mixed meals behave differently. For people who already have type 2 diabetes, low-carbohydrate patterns improve HbA1c over three to six months, but the advantage over other patterns narrows by twelve months, and adherence explains much of the difference. Several patterns work; none is uniquely correct.

FactorDirection of association with type 2 diabetesEvidence
Whole grains, legumesLower riskStrong — consistent across large cohorts, plausible mechanism
Sugar-sweetened beveragesHigher riskStrong observational; persists after weight adjustment
Body fatness (especially visceral)Higher riskStrong — cohorts plus intervention trials
Physical activityLower risk, partly independent of weight changeStrong
Glycemic index of individual foodsHigher GI, modestly higher riskPreliminary/mixed — inconsistent across studies
Specific "superfoods," cinnamon, chromiumClaimed lower riskWeak — small trials, surrogate endpoints, poor replication

The weight question, handled carefully

The strongest prevention evidence in this whole chapter comes from the Diabetes Prevention Program, a randomized trial in adults with prediabetes. An intensive lifestyle program targeting roughly 7% body weight reduction and 150 minutes per week of activity cut new diagnoses of type 2 diabetes by 58% over about three years, outperforming the drug metformin, which cut them by 31%. The Finnish Diabetes Prevention Study found nearly the same figure independently. Later work has shown that substantial weight loss can put type 2 diabetes into remission in a meaningful fraction of people.

Two honest caveats belong beside that result. First, the trial delivered an intensive, supported, expensive program — sixteen structured sessions plus coaching — not an instruction to try harder, and its results say nothing about the worth of anyone who does not reach that outcome. Second, the intervention changed activity, food quality, and weight simultaneously, and activity improves insulin sensitivity even when body weight does not change at all. Metabolic health is not a single number, and improvements in blood pressure, lipids, fitness, and glucose are all real whether or not the scale cooperates.

Read weight-adjusted associations carefully. When a cohort reports that sugar-sweetened beverages are associated with type 2 diabetes after adjusting for body mass index, the usual reading is that the association is not purely a calorie effect — the liquid sugar load and the absent fiber and satiety signals may matter on their own. Hold that reading loosely. Body weight plausibly sits on the causal path between the drink and the disease, and adjusting for a mediator can distort an estimate as easily as it can clarify one. The association itself is consistent enough across cohorts to take seriously; the mechanism proposed for it remains inference rather than settled fact.
Interactive Move body size, physical activity, and diet-pattern quality to see how they interact — activity blunting adiposity-linked risk — in a live, illustrative, weight-neutral model of type 2 diabetes relative risk. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Type 2 diabetes combines insulin resistance with failing beta-cell compensation; prediabetes is a fasting glucose of 100–125 mg/dL or HbA1c 5.7–6.4%.
  • Carbohydrate RDA is 130 g/day for adults and children (175 g/day pregnancy, 210 g/day lactation); energy yields are 4, 4, 9, and 7 kcal/g for carbohydrate, protein, fat, and alcohol.
  • Carbohydrate quality — whole grains and legumes versus sugar-sweetened beverages — predicts risk more consistently than total carbohydrate.
  • The Diabetes Prevention Program reduced incident type 2 diabetes by 58% with an intensive supported lifestyle program; activity helps insulin sensitivity independent of weight change.

Section 3Diet and cancer

How the evidence is built — and why it is softer here

Cancer takes decades to develop, which makes it nearly impossible to randomize people to a diet and wait for tumours. Almost all diet-and-cancer evidence therefore comes from observational cohorts supported by mechanism, and the field grades it explicitly: expert panels label conclusions as convincing, probable, limited-suggestive, or insufficient. Learn to look for that label, because the popular press flattens all four into "linked to cancer." The strongest dietary-lifestyle finding is not about any single food at all — excess body fatness is judged to raise the risk of at least a dozen cancers, plausibly through insulin and IGF-1 signalling, chronic inflammation, and increased oestrogen production by adipose tissue.

Fiber, processed meat, and alcohol

Fiber and whole grains. The evidence that whole grains and fiber-containing foods lower colorectal cancer risk is judged strong by expert panels, on the strength of consistent cohort data plus a believable mechanism: fiber speeds transit, dilutes carcinogens, and feeds colonic bacteria that produce butyrate, the preferred fuel of colonocytes. Be honest about the gap, though — randomized trials that added fiber supplements and looked for fewer recurrent adenomas over a few years were null. The most defensible reading is that fiber-rich foods over decades help, and that a fiber supplement bolted onto an otherwise unchanged diet for four years is not the same intervention. Adult fiber Adequate Intakes are 38 g/day for men and 25 g/day for women aged 19–50, falling to 30 g/day and 21 g/day after age 50, derived from about 14 g per 1,000 kcal.

Processed meat. In 2015 the International Agency for Research on Cancer classified processed meat as a Group 1 carcinogen and red meat as Group 2A, based mainly on colorectal cancer. This is the single most misreported result in nutrition. IARC groups describe how certain we are that something can cause cancer, not how much cancer it causes — tobacco smoking and processed meat share Group 1 while differing enormously in effect size. The underlying number: about 50 g of processed meat per day is associated with roughly an 18% higher relative risk of colorectal cancer, which against a modest baseline is a small absolute increase. Plausible mechanisms include N-nitroso compounds from nitrite curing, heterocyclic amines and polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons formed in high-heat cooking, and heme iron.

Alcohol. Ethanol is also a Group 1 carcinogen, and here the evidence is stronger and the mechanism cleaner. Alcohol dehydrogenase converts ethanol to acetaldehyde, itself a carcinogen that damages DNA; aldehyde dehydrogenase then clears it, and people carrying a low-activity ALDH2 variant — common in East Asian populations, and the cause of the flushing response — accumulate more acetaldehyde and show higher oesophageal cancer risk when they drink. Alcohol is causally linked to cancers of the mouth, pharynx, larynx, oesophagus, liver, colorectum, and female breast, and no threshold below which cancer risk is unaffected has been identified. The old "J-curve" suggesting cardiac benefit from moderate drinking has weakened considerably; much of it appears to reflect former drinkers who quit because they were ill being counted as abstainers, and genetic studies do not support cardioprotection. Ethanol also supplies 7 kcal/g with no nutrient content.

ExposureJudgementWhat it means in practice
Alcoholic drinksIARC Group 1 — carcinogenic to humansCausal for several cancer sites; no identified safe threshold for cancer
Processed meatIARC Group 1Certainty is high; effect size is small — about 18% relative risk per 50 g/day for colorectal cancer
Red meat (unprocessed)IARC Group 2A — probably carcinogenicWeaker and less consistent than processed meat
Whole grains, fiber-rich foodsProbable/convincing protectiveCohorts plus mechanism; fiber supplement trials were null
Excess body fatnessConvincing risk factor for many cancersActs through insulin/IGF-1, inflammation, oestrogen
Beta-carotene supplements (smokers)Harmful in randomized trialsTwo large trials found more lung cancer; supplements are not food
"Group 1" is about confidence, not danger. The IARC classification answers one question: is the evidence that this agent can cause cancer in humans sufficient? It says nothing about dose or magnitude. A headline that puts bacon and cigarettes in the same sentence has confused a statement about certainty with a statement about size — and you now know how to spot that.
Interactive Grade real diet–cancer claims from preliminary to strong by adjusting the four criteria scientists actually weigh — study design, consistency, dose–response, and amount of evidence — and watch a live strength meter and study-design pyramid recompute. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Diet-and-cancer evidence is mostly observational and is formally graded; look for the grade before believing the headline.
  • Fiber-rich whole foods are linked to lower colorectal cancer risk, while short fiber-supplement trials were null — food and supplement are not interchangeable.
  • IARC Group 1 = high certainty of carcinogenicity, not high magnitude; processed meat carries roughly an 18% relative risk increase per 50 g/day for colorectal cancer.
  • Alcohol is causally linked to at least seven cancer sites via acetaldehyde, with no identified safe threshold, and supplies 7 kcal/g.

Section 4Diet and bone health

Bone is a bank account you fill early

Bone is living tissue in permanent turnover: osteoclasts resorb it, osteoblasts rebuild it, and the balance between them shifts across the lifespan. Deposition outpaces resorption through childhood and adolescence, so that most adults reach peak bone mass — the maximum bone they will ever have — by their late twenties. After that the balance tips toward net loss, slowly at first and then rapidly in women for several years after menopause when oestrogen withdrawal accelerates resorption. This is why calcium requirements peak in adolescence rather than in old age, and why loading the skeleton with weight-bearing activity in youth pays dividends decades later.

Osteoporosis is the endpoint: bone mass low enough and microarchitecture degraded enough that fractures occur under trivial loads. It is defined on bone densitometry as a T-score of −2.5 or lower, with −1.0 to −2.5 called osteopenia. It is silent until it breaks something — classically hip, vertebra, or wrist — and hip fracture in an older adult carries substantial mortality.

Calcium and vitamin D: the numbers

Calcium RDAs in the United States are 1,300 mg/day for ages 9–18, 1,000 mg/day for men aged 19–70 and women aged 19–50, and 1,200 mg/day for women aged 51 and older and men aged 71 and older. Pregnancy and lactation do not raise the adult requirement — maternal absorption efficiency rises instead. The Tolerable Upper Intake Level is 2,500 mg/day for adults aged 19–50 and 2,000 mg/day from 51 on; chronically exceeding it is associated with kidney stones and hypercalcaemia.

Vitamin D is not really a vitamin — skin makes it from 7-dehydrocholesterol under UVB light — but latitude, season, sunscreen, clothing, skin pigmentation, and indoor life make dietary intake matter. Its job in bone is indirect and essential: calcitriol drives intestinal calcium absorption. The RDA is 600 IU (15 mcg)/day for ages 1–70, including pregnancy and lactation, and 800 IU (20 mcg)/day from age 71, with a Tolerable Upper Intake Level of 4,000 IU (100 mcg)/day for adults. Severe deficiency produces rickets in growing children, where unmineralized growth plates bow under load, and osteomalacia in adults, a soft-bone syndrome with bone pain and weakness — distinct from osteoporosis, in which the bone is normally mineralized but there is simply less of it.

Absorption is where dietary detail earns its keep. Roughly 30% of the calcium in milk is absorbed; low-oxalate greens such as kale and bok choy do better per unit calcium, while spinach, despite a high calcium content, is nearly useless as a source because oxalate binds the mineral. Fractional absorption falls as a single dose rises, so calcium spread across the day is absorbed more efficiently than the same amount taken at once. Adequate protein supports bone rather than harming it — the old "acid-ash" idea that dietary protein leaches calcium from the skeleton has not survived testing. Very high sodium intakes do modestly increase urinary calcium loss, one more reason the sodium discussion in Section 1 keeps reappearing.

NutrientReference intake (adults)Notes and variationDeficiency
CalciumRDA 1,000 mg/day (men 19–70; women 19–50)1,200 mg/day women 51+ and men 71+; 1,300 mg/day ages 9–18; UL 2,500 mg/day (19–50), 2,000 mg/day (51+)Low peak bone mass; contributes to osteoporosis
Vitamin DRDA 600 IU (15 mcg)/day, ages 1–70800 IU (20 mcg)/day from age 71; unchanged in pregnancy/lactation; UL 4,000 IU (100 mcg)/dayRickets (children), osteomalacia (adults)
ProteinRDA 0.8 g/kg body weight/dayHigher in pregnancy, lactation, and likely in older adultsMuscle and bone loss; poor fracture recovery
Vitamin KAI 120 mcg/day (men), 90 mcg/day (women)Needed to carboxylate osteocalcin; supplement trials for fracture are preliminaryImpaired clotting; bone role less certain
MagnesiumRDA 400–420 mg/day (men), 310–320 mg/day (women)Varies with age; structural component of bone mineralHypocalcaemia, neuromuscular irritability
Osteoporosis and osteomalacia are not the same disease. In osteoporosis there is less bone, but what remains is normally mineralized. In osteomalacia there is enough bone matrix but it is under-mineralized because calcium or vitamin D is lacking. They can coexist, they look different on biopsy, and confusing them leads to confusing the evidence about what nutrition can and cannot fix.
Interactive Follow a bone-density curve from childhood to old age, adjusting habitual calcium, vitamin D, weight-bearing activity, and midlife estrogen to see how each shifts the peak and the rate of later loss. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Peak bone mass is reached by the late twenties; adolescence is the highest-requirement window (calcium RDA 1,300 mg/day, ages 9–18).
  • Adult calcium RDA is 1,000 mg/day, rising to 1,200 mg/day for women 51+ and men 71+; UL 2,500 mg/day (19–50) and 2,000 mg/day (51+).
  • Vitamin D RDA is 600 IU (15 mcg)/day to age 70 and 800 IU (20 mcg)/day after; deficiency causes rickets in children and osteomalacia in adults.
  • Bioavailability matters — oxalate in spinach blocks calcium absorption, while low-oxalate greens and fortified foods deliver it well.

Section 5Dietary patterns

Why patterns beat single nutrients

The history of nutrition research is littered with nutrients that looked essential in observational data and then failed, or backfired, in trials. Beta-carotene tracked with lower lung cancer risk in cohorts; two large randomized trials in smokers found more lung cancer in the supplemented groups. High-dose vitamin E was expected to prevent prostate cancer and modestly increased it. Antioxidant supplements broadly have not reproduced the benefits of antioxidant-rich diets. The pattern in these failures is instructive.

There are four reasons the single-nutrient approach keeps failing. The food matrix matters — a nutrient arrives inside a structure of fiber, water, and thousands of other compounds that changes its absorption and effect. Nutrients interact — calcium needs vitamin D; iron absorption depends on vitamin C and is blocked by phytate. Diets are substitutions, not additions — eating more legumes means eating less of something else, and the study measures the swap. And nutrients travel together, so the "effect" of one is statistically tangled with everything else that keeps it company on the plate. A dietary pattern captures all four automatically. That is not a rhetorical preference; it is the best available explanation for why pattern-level trials have succeeded where nutrient-level trials have not.

Three patterns with real trial evidence

Mediterranean. Built on vegetables, fruit, legumes, whole grains, nuts, fish, and extra-virgin olive oil as the principal fat, with modest dairy and little red or processed meat. The PREDIMED trial randomized about 7,400 adults at high cardiovascular risk and found roughly a 30% relative reduction in major cardiovascular events in the Mediterranean-diet groups. Two caveats belong with that number: the trial was retracted and republished in 2018 after randomization irregularities were found at some sites, with broadly similar conclusions on reanalysis, and the participants were at high baseline risk, so the absolute benefit in a low-risk population would be smaller.

DASH. Dietary Approaches to Stop Hypertension is the cleanest feeding trial in the field: fruit, vegetables, low-fat dairy, whole grains, nuts, and reduced saturated fat, with all food provided and body weight held constant. It lowered systolic blood pressure by about 5.5 mmHg overall and by roughly 11 mmHg in participants who already had hypertension — drug-like effects from food alone, over eight weeks. The follow-on DASH-Sodium trial showed the pattern and sodium reduction were additive, with the largest reductions when both were applied.

Plant-based. A spectrum rather than a category, from fully vegan through vegetarian to mostly-plant omnivorous. Large cohorts of vegetarians report lower rates of type 2 diabetes, lower blood pressure, and lower cardiovascular mortality. This evidence is observational, and vegetarians in these cohorts also smoke less and exercise more, so residual confounding is a real concern. It also matters which plant-based diet: a pattern of whole grains, legumes, nuts, and vegetables behaves very differently from one built on refined starch and sugar, even though both are technically plant-based. Fully plant-based eating requires planned attention to vitamin B12, which occurs naturally only in animal foods, and often to iron, zinc, iodine, calcium, and long-chain omega-3s.

PatternDefining featuresBest-supported outcomeEvidence type
MediterraneanOlive oil as main fat; vegetables, legumes, nuts, fish; little processed meatFewer major cardiovascular eventsRandomized trial in high-risk adults, plus cohorts
DASHFruit, vegetables, low-fat dairy, whole grains, nuts; low saturated fat and sodiumBlood pressure reduction (about −5.5 mmHg systolic; more if hypertensive)Controlled feeding trial — strongest design available
Plant-based / vegetarianLittle or no animal food; quality varies enormously within the categoryLower type 2 diabetes and cardiovascular mortalityObservational cohorts — confounding possible
Traditional Okinawan, Nordic, and othersRegionally distinct, share the same base foodsSimilar directional benefitsMostly observational; growing

What they share — and what "healthy" cannot mean

Line the successful patterns up and the overlap is more striking than the differences: plenty of vegetables, fruit, legumes, whole grains, and nuts; unsaturated fat as the main fat; fish or plant proteins in place of much processed meat; limited added sugar, sodium, and ultra-processed food. Beyond that, the patterns diverge freely on dairy, on meat, on grain type, on cooking fat — and they succeed anyway. There is no single correct diet, and the same principles can be expressed through Mediterranean, South Asian, West African, Latin American, East Asian, or any other cuisine.

Two practical honesties finish the chapter. First, adherence beats optimization — a pattern a person can actually eat, afford, buy near home, and cook produces better outcomes than a theoretically superior pattern they abandon in a month. Second, food is not a moral test. Cost, time, transport, culture, family, disability, and food environment shape what lands on a plate at least as much as knowledge does, and framing eating as virtue and vice reliably makes both nutrition and mental health worse. The clinical and educational job is to describe physiology and evidence accurately, and to leave the moralizing out of it.

The supplement graveyard is the argument for patterns. Beta-carotene, vitamin E, high-dose antioxidants, fiber pills for adenomas — each looked excellent as a nutrient and disappointed or harmed as a pill. Whole diets built from the same foods keep working. When someone proposes to bottle the active ingredient of a dietary pattern, that history is the reason to ask for a trial before believing it.
Interactive Compare the Mediterranean, DASH, and plant-based eating patterns side by side, with a live macronutrient-to-energy calculator that turns grams into kcal. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Single-nutrient supplement trials have repeatedly failed because of the food matrix, nutrient interactions, substitution effects, and correlated intakes.
  • PREDIMED (Mediterranean) reduced major cardiovascular events by roughly 30% in high-risk adults; the trial was retracted and republished in 2018 with similar conclusions.
  • DASH lowered systolic blood pressure by about 5.5 mmHg overall and roughly 11 mmHg in hypertensive participants, with sodium reduction additive.
  • Successful patterns overlap heavily in their base foods; adherence, affordability, and cultural fit matter more than choosing between them.

The chapter in one breath

  • LDL cholesterol is causal in atherosclerosis; saturated and industrial trans fats raise it, and unsaturated fats lower it when they replace saturated fat — substitution is the question.
  • Adult sodium references are an AI of 1,500 mg/day and a CDRR of 2,300 mg/day against an average intake near 3,400 mg/day, most of it from packaged and restaurant food; potassium AI is 3,400 mg/day (men) and 2,600 mg/day (women).
  • Type 2 diabetes is insulin resistance plus beta-cell failure; carbohydrate quality tracks risk better than quantity, and the RDA for carbohydrate is 130 g/day (175 g/day pregnancy, 210 g/day lactation).
  • The Diabetes Prevention Program cut incident diabetes by 58% with an intensive supported lifestyle program — and activity improves insulin sensitivity even without weight change.
  • Diet-and-cancer evidence is mostly observational and formally graded; IARC Group 1 signals certainty, not magnitude, and processed meat carries about an 18% relative risk increase per 50 g/day for colorectal cancer.
  • Alcohol is a Group 1 carcinogen acting through acetaldehyde, with no identified safe threshold for cancer, and it supplies 7 kcal/g (carbohydrate and protein 4, fat 9).
  • Bone: peak mass by the late twenties; calcium RDA 1,000 mg/day for most adults (1,200 mg/day for women 51+ and men 71+, 1,300 mg/day ages 9–18) and vitamin D 600 IU (15 mcg)/day to age 70, 800 IU (20 mcg)/day after — deficiency causing rickets or osteomalacia.
  • Dietary patterns — Mediterranean, DASH, well-planned plant-based — outperform single nutrients because food comes as a matrix, nutrients interact, and every choice is a substitution.

ReferenceWords worth knowing

A quick reference for the terms in this chapter — skim it now, or circle back whenever a word trips you up.

Added sugars
Sugars and syrups added during processing or preparation, as opposed to those naturally present in fruit or milk; United States guidance recommends keeping them under 10% of daily energy for ages 2 and older.
Atherosclerosis
The build-up of lipid-rich plaque within artery walls, driven substantially by LDL particles that lodge and are oxidized there.
Bone mineral density
The amount of mineral per unit area of bone, measured by densitometry and reported as a T-score; the standard for diagnosing osteoporosis.
Cardiovascular disease
Umbrella term for disorders of the heart and blood vessels, including coronary artery disease, stroke, and peripheral arterial disease.
DASH diet
Dietary Approaches to Stop Hypertension — a controlled-trial pattern of fruit, vegetables, low-fat dairy, whole grains, and nuts that lowers blood pressure independent of weight change.
Dietary fiber
Carbohydrate that resists human digestive enzymes; Adequate Intakes are about 14 g per 1,000 kcal, or 38 g/day for men and 25 g/day for women aged 19–50.
Dietary pattern
The overall combination of foods and beverages habitually consumed, analysed as a whole rather than nutrient by nutrient.
Glycemic index
A ranking of how much an isolated carbohydrate food raises blood glucose relative to a reference; useful conceptually, but inconsistent as a standalone predictor in mixed meals.
HbA1c
Glycated haemoglobin, reflecting average blood glucose over roughly the previous two to three months; 5.7–6.4% indicates prediabetes and 6.5% or higher is diagnostic of diabetes.
Insulin resistance
Reduced responsiveness of muscle, liver, and adipose tissue to insulin, requiring higher insulin output to maintain normal blood glucose.
LDL cholesterol
Cholesterol carried on low-density lipoprotein particles; causally linked to atherosclerotic cardiovascular disease.
Mediterranean diet
A pattern centred on vegetables, legumes, whole grains, nuts, fish, and olive oil as the principal fat, with limited red and processed meat.
Osteomalacia
Softening of adult bone from inadequate mineralization, usually reflecting vitamin D or calcium deficiency; the adult counterpart of rickets.
Osteoporosis
Low bone mass with degraded microarchitecture and increased fracture risk, defined as a T-score of −2.5 or lower; the remaining bone is normally mineralized.
Peak bone mass
The greatest amount of bone tissue a person accrues, typically reached by the late twenties, after which net loss begins.
Prediabetes
Blood glucose above normal but below diagnostic thresholds — fasting glucose 100–125 mg/dL or HbA1c 5.7–6.4%.
Processed meat
Meat preserved by smoking, curing, salting, or the addition of chemical preservatives; classified by IARC as Group 1 based on colorectal cancer evidence.
Saturated fat
Fatty acids with no carbon–carbon double bonds; raises LDL cholesterol relative to unsaturated fat.
Sodium
The cation whose intake most strongly influences blood pressure; adult AI 1,500 mg/day, with a Chronic Disease Risk Reduction intake of 2,300 mg/day.
Trans fat
Unsaturated fat with at least one double bond in the trans configuration; the industrial form from partially hydrogenated oil raises LDL and lowers HDL and has been largely removed from the food supply.
Unsaturated fat
Fatty acids containing one (monounsaturated) or more (polyunsaturated) double bonds; lowers LDL when substituted for saturated fat.
Whole grain
A grain retaining bran, germ, and endosperm in their original proportions; consistently associated with lower type 2 diabetes and colorectal cancer risk.

Check yourself

1. A study reports "no association between saturated fat intake and coronary events." What is the first question you should ask before accepting the headline?
What replaced the saturated fat? Diet is a set of substitutions. Replacing saturated fat with polyunsaturated fat reduces events; replacing it with refined carbohydrate does not. A study without a defined comparison cannot answer a practical question.
2. LDL and HDL both predict cardiovascular risk. Why do we treat them so differently in causal terms?
LDL is causal; HDL appears to be a marker. Genetics, animal models, and many randomized trials all show that lowering LDL lowers events. Interventions and genetic variants that raise HDL have generally not reduced events — a marker that travels with disease is not necessarily a cause of it.
3. State the adult sodium Adequate Intake and the Chronic Disease Risk Reduction intake, with units, and say roughly where most dietary sodium comes from.
AI 1,500 mg/day; CDRR 2,300 mg/day for adults 19 and older. Average United States intake is near 3,400 mg/day, and roughly 70% of it is already in packaged and restaurant food before anyone reaches for a salt shaker.
4. Give the energy yield per gram of carbohydrate, protein, fat, and alcohol.
Carbohydrate 4 kcal/g, protein 4 kcal/g, fat 9 kcal/g, alcohol 7 kcal/g. Alcohol sits between protein and fat energetically while contributing no essential nutrients.
5. Describe the two physiological failures that together produce type 2 diabetes, and give the laboratory definition of prediabetes.
Insulin resistance in muscle, liver, and adipose tissue, plus beta-cell secretory failure that can no longer compensate. Prediabetes is a fasting glucose of 100–125 mg/dL or an HbA1c of 5.7–6.4%; diabetes is a fasting glucose of 126 mg/dL or an HbA1c of 6.5% or higher.
6. What is the RDA for carbohydrate in adults, why was it set at that value, and how does it change in pregnancy and lactation?
130 g/day for adults and children, based on the brain's obligate glucose requirement. It rises to 175 g/day in pregnancy and 210 g/day in lactation. The Acceptable Macronutrient Distribution Range is 45–65% of energy.
7. Summarize the Diabetes Prevention Program result, and state two caveats that keep it from being read as "just try harder."
An intensive lifestyle program targeting about 7% weight reduction and 150 minutes per week of activity reduced new type 2 diabetes by 58% over roughly three years, beating metformin's 31%. Caveats: the program was intensive, structured, and resource-heavy rather than simple advice; and it changed activity, food quality, and weight together, with activity improving insulin sensitivity even when weight is unchanged.
8. A headline says processed meat is "as carcinogenic as smoking" because both are IARC Group 1. Correct the error.
IARC groups describe the certainty that an agent can cause cancer, not the magnitude of risk. Both are Group 1 because the evidence is sufficient in each case, but the effect sizes differ enormously — roughly an 18% relative increase in colorectal cancer per 50 g/day of processed meat, against a comparatively modest baseline risk.
9. Fiber-rich diets are linked to lower colorectal cancer risk, yet fiber supplement trials were null. Give the most defensible interpretation.
Fiber-rich foods eaten over decades are not the same intervention as an isolated fiber supplement added to an unchanged diet for a few years. The cohort evidence plus the butyrate and transit-time mechanisms support the food; the null trials caution against assuming a pill reproduces it.
10. Explain the mechanism by which alcohol causes cancer, and why people with a low-activity ALDH2 variant are at particular risk.
Alcohol dehydrogenase converts ethanol to acetaldehyde, a DNA-damaging carcinogen, which aldehyde dehydrogenase then clears. A low-activity ALDH2 variant — common in East Asian populations and responsible for the flushing response — slows that clearance, so acetaldehyde accumulates and oesophageal cancer risk with drinking is higher. No safe threshold for cancer risk has been identified.
11. State the calcium and vitamin D RDAs for an adult, note how each changes with age, and name the deficiency syndrome caused by lack of vitamin D in a child versus an adult.
Calcium 1,000 mg/day for men 19–70 and women 19–50, rising to 1,200 mg/day for women 51+ and men 71+, and 1,300 mg/day for ages 9–18. Vitamin D 600 IU (15 mcg)/day for ages 1–70, rising to 800 IU (20 mcg)/day at 71. Deficiency causes rickets in growing children and osteomalacia in adults — both are under-mineralization, distinct from osteoporosis, where mineralization is normal but there is less bone.
12. Give three reasons dietary patterns outperform single nutrients in predicting health outcomes, and name a supplement trial that illustrates the point.
Any three: the food matrix alters absorption and effect; nutrients interact (calcium with vitamin D, iron with vitamin C and phytate); every dietary change is a substitution rather than an addition; and nutrients are highly correlated with one another in real diets. Illustration: beta-carotene tracked with lower lung cancer in cohorts, but two large randomized trials in smokers found more lung cancer in the supplemented arms.
Nutrition · Chapter 13

Assessment & Diet Planning

Everything in the previous twelve chapters was about what nutrients do. This chapter is about how anyone actually finds out what a real person is eating — and what to do with that information. You will learn the four-part framework professionals use to size up nutritional status, why every method of measuring food intake is wrong in a predictable direction, how to read the two panels on a package that are legally required to tell you the truth, how to build a day of eating that genuinely meets the reference intakes, and how to tell a defensible nutrition claim from a confident-sounding one. Throughout, the posture is the same one the evidence supports: describe physiology, weigh evidence, and leave moralizing about food and bodies out of it.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Describe the ABCD framework of nutrition assessment and state what each domain can and cannot establish.
  • Distinguish nutrition screening from nutrition assessment, and explain why no single measurement diagnoses nutritional status.
  • Compare the 24-hour recall, the food record, and the food frequency questionnaire, and name the characteristic error of each.
  • Explain why underreporting is systematic rather than dishonest, and how recovery biomarkers such as doubly labeled water revealed it.
  • Read a Nutrition Facts or Supplement Facts panel: serving size, %DV, the 5/20 heuristic, and the ingredient list.
  • Use the DRI family — EAR, RDA, AI, UL, AMDR, EER — correctly when planning intake for one person versus a group.
  • Build a day of eating that meets energy, macronutrient, fiber, and priority micronutrient targets, and state which values change with age, sex, or pregnancy.
  • Evaluate a nutrition claim by study design, effect size, and conflict of interest, and recognize the recurring signatures of pseudoscience.

Section 1Nutrition assessment

Screening asks a question; assessment answers it

Two different activities get called "checking someone's nutrition." Nutrition screening is a fast, low-burden filter applied to everyone in a setting — a few questions about recent unintentional weight change, appetite, and ability to eat — designed to be sensitive rather than precise. Its only job is to flag who needs a closer look. Nutrition assessment is the closer look: a structured, multi-source evaluation that combines measurements, laboratory data, physical findings, and a detailed intake history into a picture no one of those sources could produce alone.

The reason assessment is deliberately multi-source is that every individual measure is ambiguous. A low body weight can reflect genetics, disease, or an ordinary healthy build. A low serum ferritin is fairly specific for depleted iron stores, but a normal or high one proves nothing when inflammation is present. A cracked mouth corner is consistent with several B-vitamin inadequacies and also with ill-fitting dentures. Convergence across domains is what turns a suggestive finding into a defensible conclusion.

The four domains: A, B, C, D

Anthropometric measures are physical dimensions of the body: height, weight, body mass index (weight in kilograms divided by height in meters squared), waist circumference, mid-upper-arm circumference, and skinfold thickness. In children, the meaningful anthropometric output is not a single number but a trajectory plotted on a growth reference — a child tracking steadily along a low percentile is a very different situation from a child who has crossed downward through two percentile channels. BMI deserves particular care: it was designed to describe populations, it does not distinguish muscle from fat, and its relationship to health outcomes varies by age, ancestry, and body composition. It is a screening input, not a diagnosis, and it says nothing about how a person eats.

Biochemical data are laboratory measures of nutrients, their metabolites, or their functional consequences. Serum 25-hydroxyvitamin D is the accepted marker of vitamin D status because it integrates both dietary and sun-derived supply. Serum ferritin reflects iron stores, hemoglobin falls only after those stores are exhausted, and soluble transferrin receptor helps separate iron deficiency from the anemia of inflammation. Serum vitamin B12 is an imperfect first pass; methylmalonic acid and homocysteine rise before B12 falls out of range and are the more sensitive functional markers. One important correction to older teaching: albumin and prealbumin are not nutrition markers. They fall with inflammation, injury, and illness regardless of intake, and current professional guidance treats them as markers of inflammatory burden rather than of protein status — a good example of strong evidence overturning a convenient shortcut.

Clinical assessment is the physical examination and history: skin, hair, nails, eyes, mouth, and muscle and fat stores, alongside the medical history and medications. Physical signs are late and frequently non-specific — by the time they appear, depletion has usually been prolonged, and most signs are compatible with several nutrient inadequacies and with non-nutritional causes. A handful are close to diagnostic in the right context, such as Bitot spots or perifollicular hemorrhage, but most earn their value only alongside the other three domains. Dietary assessment is the intake history, which in practice means far more than a food list. It includes meal patterns, cooking facilities, food security, budget, chewing and swallowing ability, gastrointestinal symptoms, allergies, medications that affect nutrient absorption, and cultural and religious food practices, all of which determine whether any plan built later is realistic.

DomainTypical measuresAnswers wellFails at
AnthropometricHeight, weight, BMI, waist circumference, MUAC, skinfolds, growth trajectoryChange over time; population-level screeningBody composition; any individual diagnosis from one number
BiochemicalSerum 25-hydroxyvitamin D, ferritin, hemoglobin, B12 with methylmalonic acid, urinary sodiumObjective nutrient status; subclinical depletionInterpretation during inflammation; timing lag; cost
ClinicalSkin, hair, nails, eyes, oral exam, muscle and fat wasting, medical history, medicationsAdvanced deficiency; functional barriers to eatingEarly or mild inadequacy; signs overlap across nutrients
DietaryRecall, record, food frequency questionnaire, food security screenWhat is actually eaten and whyAccuracy — every method carries systematic reporting error

Physical signs worth recognizing

Classic deficiency syndromes are uncommon where the food supply is fortified and varied, but they are not extinct, and they cluster in predictable situations: restricted or highly repetitive diets, malabsorption, bariatric surgery, alcohol use disorder, and food insecurity. Recognizing them is a matter of pattern, not memorization for its own sake.

NutrientDeficiency syndromeRecognizable features
Vitamin CScurvyPerifollicular hemorrhage, corkscrew hairs, bleeding swollen gums, poor wound healing
Vitamin AXerophthalmiaNight blindness first, then conjunctival dryness and Bitot spots; a leading preventable cause of childhood blindness worldwide
Thiamin (B1)Beriberi; Wernicke encephalopathyPeripheral neuropathy and high-output heart failure; or confusion, ataxia, and eye-movement abnormalities
Niacin (B3)PellagraThe classic triad: dermatitis in sun-exposed skin, diarrhea, dementia
IronIron-deficiency anemiaFatigue, pallor, spoon-shaped nails (koilonychia); ferritin falls before hemoglobin does
IodineGoiter; congenital hypothyroidismThyroid enlargement; irreversible neurodevelopmental harm when deficiency occurs in pregnancy
Vitamin DRickets (children); osteomalacia (adults)Bowed legs and widened growth plates; bone pain and proximal weakness
Vitamin B12Megaloblastic anemia with neurologic injuryNumbness, gait change, cognitive change; the neurologic damage can become permanent
Never diagnose from one column. Anthropometry without intake history, a lab value without inflammatory context, or a physical sign without a medication list will mislead you. Assessment is a triangulation exercise, and its output is a description of nutritional status — not a verdict about a person's discipline, character, or body.
Interactive Explore the four methods of nutrition assessment — Anthropometric, Biochemical, Clinical, and Dietary — with a live BMI and waist-to-height calculator plus tappable labs, physical signs, and intake tools inside each letter. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Screening is a sensitive filter for everyone; assessment is the detailed multi-source evaluation of those flagged.
  • The four domains are Anthropometric, Biochemical, Clinical, Dietary — each is ambiguous alone and informative together.
  • BMI is a population screening tool that cannot distinguish muscle from fat and cannot diagnose an individual.
  • Albumin and prealbumin reflect inflammation, not protein intake — a widely taught claim that current evidence does not support.
  • Physical deficiency signs appear late and are often non-specific; biochemical change usually precedes them.

Section 2Measuring intake

Three ways to ask, and what each is for

The 24-hour dietary recall is a structured interview covering everything consumed in the previous day. Done properly it follows a multiple-pass method: a quick uninterrupted list, then prompts for commonly forgotten items (condiments, drinks, snacks, additions to coffee), then time and occasion, then detailed portion and preparation questions, then a final review sweep. It has one enormous practical advantage — it is retrospective, so the person cannot change their behavior in response to being measured — and one enormous limitation: a single day is not a usual diet. Day-to-day variation within one person is large, especially for nutrients concentrated in a few foods, so estimating usual intake for an individual requires several nonconsecutive days including at least one weekend day.

The food record (food diary) is prospective: the person writes down foods as they eat them, usually for three to seven days, with weights or measured portions if the study demands precision. Records avoid memory error almost entirely. Their signature problem is reactivity — recording changes eating. People simplify meals, skip the snack they would rather not write down, or eat less because logging is effortful. Burden is high and adherence decays sharply after the first two or three days.

The food frequency questionnaire (FFQ) asks how often each of roughly 100 to 150 foods was eaten over a long window, typically the past month or year, sometimes with portion-size options. An FFQ is cheap, self-administered, and captures long-run patterns and episodically eaten foods that a few recall days would miss entirely. It is poor at absolute quantities — the food list is finite and portion assumptions are coarse — so its legitimate use is ranking people relative to one another in large studies, not stating that a given person consumed 1,840 mg of sodium. A brief screener is a short FFQ aimed at one target (fruit and vegetable servings, added sugars, calcium), useful for a rough read and nothing more.

MethodTime frameBest used forCharacteristic error
24-hour recallPrevious day; repeat on several nonconsecutive daysGroup mean intakes; usual intake with enough repeatsMemory lapses, portion misestimation, underreporting; one day is not usual intake
Food record3–7 consecutive days, prospectiveDetailed, near-real-time intake; clinical follow-upReactivity — recording alters eating; high burden; adherence fades
Food frequency questionnairePast month to past yearRanking individuals in large epidemiologic studiesPoor absolute accuracy; fixed food list; portion assumptions
Brief screenerRecent weeksFast targeted estimate of one dietary componentVery coarse; not valid for full nutrient analysis
Direct observation / weighed intakeMeal or dayReference-quality data in controlled settingsExpensive, unrepresentative of free-living eating

Every method errs in the same direction

Self-reported energy intake is systematically lower than measured energy expenditure. We know this with confidence because of doubly labeled water, a reference method in which a person drinks water labeled with stable isotopes of hydrogen and oxygen; the differential disappearance of the two isotopes over one to two weeks yields total energy expenditure with high accuracy. When self-reports are compared against it in weight-stable adults, reported energy commonly falls short by roughly ten to thirty percent, and the shortfall is not evenly distributed — it is larger in people with higher body weight, and larger for foods carrying social judgment. Parallel recovery biomarkers exist for individual nutrients: 24-hour urinary nitrogen for protein, and 24-hour urinary sodium and potassium for those minerals.

The important interpretation is that this is measurement error, not deceit. Its components are ordinary human cognition: forgetting items eaten while distracted, estimating portions poorly because almost nobody has practiced it, telescoping (misplacing a food into the wrong day), and the social pressure of describing your eating to a stranger holding a clipboard. Because the error is directional rather than random, it does not average out with a bigger sample — which is why nutritional epidemiology invests so heavily in calibration substudies and biomarkers.

From foods to nutrients

Reported foods become nutrient numbers by lookup in a food composition database, and that translation adds its own uncertainty. Composition varies with cultivar, soil, season, storage, and cooking method; a database entry is a representative average, not the sandwich in front of you. Some nutrients are especially unstable — folate and vitamin C degrade with heat and time — and mixed dishes must be broken into components or matched to a generic recipe. Digital tracking apps do not escape any of this; they often add a crowd-sourced database whose entries are unverified, so the apparent precision of "312 kcal" carries far more error than three digits imply.

Treat a food record as data, not as a confession. The single fastest way to corrupt an intake history is to react to it with approval or disapproval. People stop reporting what triggers judgment, error grows, and the assessment becomes worthless. Neutral curiosity — "walk me through the rest of the afternoon" — produces better data than any refinement of the instrument.
Interactive Compare 24-hour recalls, food records, and food frequency questionnaires, using a live plot and a days-of-data slider to see the random and systematic error each method introduces. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Recalls are retrospective and non-reactive; records are prospective and reactive; FFQs capture long-run patterns but rank rather than quantify.
  • A single day cannot represent an individual's usual intake; multiple nonconsecutive days are required.
  • Underreporting of energy is systematic, commonly on the order of 10–30 percent, and was established by comparison with doubly labeled water.
  • Directional error does not shrink with sample size — only calibration and recovery biomarkers address it.
  • Food composition databases add a second layer of uncertainty; reported nutrient totals are estimates, not measurements.

Section 3Reading a food label

Serving size is a reporting unit, not a recommendation

Every number on a Nutrition Facts panel is scaled to the serving size printed at the top, so that line governs the interpretation of everything below it. Serving sizes are not nutrition advice and never were. In the United States they derive from Reference Amounts Customarily Consumed — regulatory values based on survey data describing how much people actually eat at one sitting, which is why the 2016 label overhaul raised many of them and required that certain packages between 200 and 300 percent of a reference amount carry a dual-column panel showing both "per serving" and "per package." The practical habit is simple: before reading any other line, compare the stated serving with the amount you are realistically going to eat, and scale mentally.

The same overhaul made calories larger and bolder, added a mandatory Added Sugars line in grams with a %DV, replaced the required vitamin A and vitamin C declarations with vitamin D and potassium, and required actual quantities alongside %DV for the declared micronutrients. Calcium and iron remained required. Fiber, sugars, and protein grams are also given; %DV for protein is not required unless a protein claim is made, because protein adequacy is rarely the limiting issue in the populations the label serves.

%DV and the 5/20 heuristic

The Daily Value is a single set of reference numbers — deliberately one set, not a table by age and sex — chosen so one label works for the whole general population aged four and older. The %DV tells you what fraction of that reference a serving supplies, anchored to a 2,000-calorie reference diet. That anchoring is the main caveat: your own energy needs may sit well above or below 2,000 kcal, in which case the percentages for energy-scaled nutrients drift accordingly.

NutrientDaily Value (adults and children 4+)Reading note
Total fat78 gType matters more than total; the panel breaks out saturated and trans
Saturated fat20 gDietary Guidelines advise keeping it under 10 percent of calories
Sodium2,300 mgMost sodium comes from packaged and restaurant food, not the shaker
Total carbohydrate275 gIncludes fiber, total sugars, and added sugars
Dietary fiber28 gMost people fall far short of this
Added sugars50 gExcludes sugars naturally present in fruit and dairy
Protein50 g%DV optional unless a protein claim appears
Vitamin D20 mcg (800 IU)Newly mandatory in the 2016 format
Calcium1,300 mgDeclared in milligrams as well as %DV
Iron18 mgSet near the higher requirement, so %DV overstates adequacy for some
Potassium4,700 mgNewly mandatory; a shortfall nutrient for most adults

The 5/20 rule makes %DV usable at a glance: 5 percent DV or less per serving is low in that nutrient, and 20 percent DV or more is high. It works in both directions. You can use it to find foods high in the things most people under-consume — fiber, potassium, calcium, vitamin D — and to notice when a single serving carries a large share of the sodium or added-sugars reference. Regulated nutrient content claims follow the same arithmetic: "good source of" means 10–19 percent DV per serving, "excellent source" or "high in" means 20 percent DV or more, and "low sodium" means 140 mg or less per serving.

The ingredient list, allergens, and claims

Ingredients are listed in descending order by weight, which is the single most informative structural fact on the package: whatever appears first contributes the most mass. The list also exposes what the Nutrition Facts panel aggregates. Added sugars arrive under many names — sucrose, high-fructose corn syrup, cane juice, dextrose, maltose, honey, agave, fruit juice concentrate, malt syrup — and splitting sweeteners across several names moves each one further down the list even though their combined weight is large. Reading the two panels together defeats that: the Added Sugars gram line is unaffected by how the ingredient is named.

In the United States, the nine major allergens must be declared in plain language — milk, eggs, fish, crustacean shellfish, tree nuts, peanuts, wheat, soybeans, and sesame, the last added effective January 1, 2023. Beyond that, distinguish three tiers of claim. Nutrient content claims are arithmetic and verifiable. Health claims linking a food to a disease require prior authorization based on significant scientific agreement, and qualified health claims must disclose the weakness of their evidence. Structure/function claims — "supports immune health," "promotes healthy digestion" — require no premarket authorization and carry a disclaimer stating the claim has not been evaluated by the Food and Drug Administration. That third tier is where most marketing language lives.

Dietary supplements are regulated as food, not as drugs. Under U.S. law they do not require premarket approval for safety or efficacy; enforcement is largely after the fact. A Supplement Facts panel therefore tells you what the manufacturer declares, not what an agency has verified, and third-party verification marks address identity and purity — not whether the product does anything.
Interactive Explore an interactive Nutrition Facts panel and watch every value and % Daily Value recompute as you change the serving size. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Every panel number scales to the serving size, which reflects customary consumption rather than a recommendation.
  • %DV is anchored to a 2,000-calorie reference diet; the 5/20 rule reads 5 percent DV as low and 20 percent DV as high.
  • Current DVs include sodium 2,300 mg, fiber 28 g, added sugars 50 g, potassium 4,700 mg, and vitamin D 20 mcg.
  • Ingredients run in descending order by weight; sweeteners can be split across names, so read the Added Sugars line too.
  • Structure/function claims need no premarket authorization — the disclaimer is the tell.

Section 4Planning a diet

Know which reference value you are using

The Dietary Reference Intakes are a family of values, and using the wrong member is the most common planning error. The EAR (Estimated Average Requirement) is the intake meeting the needs of half of a healthy group — it is the correct tool for assessing or planning for groups, never for judging one person. The RDA is set roughly two standard deviations above the EAR so that it covers about 97–98 percent of healthy individuals in the life-stage group; it is the goal for an individual. The AI (Adequate Intake) is used where the data are too thin to compute an EAR, so it is an observation-based estimate rather than a requirement. The UL (Tolerable Upper Intake Level) is the highest chronic daily intake unlikely to cause harm — it is a ceiling, not a target, and it matters mainly for supplements and fortified foods. The AMDR gives macronutrient ranges as a share of energy, and the EER estimates energy needs from age, sex, weight, height, and physical activity.

Energy accounting rests on the metabolizable energy yields you have used all book: carbohydrate 4 kcal/g, protein 4 kcal/g, fat 9 kcal/g, alcohol 7 kcal/g. For adults, the AMDRs are 45–65 percent of energy from carbohydrate, 20–35 percent from fat, and 10–35 percent from protein; the fat range is deliberately higher in early life, at 30–40 percent for ages 1–3 years and 25–35 percent for ages 4–18 years, because young children need energy-dense food. The carbohydrate RDA is 130 g/day for adults and children aged 1 year and older, based on the brain's glucose use, rising to 175 g/day in pregnancy and 210 g/day in lactation. The protein RDA is 0.8 g/kg body weight per day for adults aged 19 and over, rising to about 1.1 g/kg/day during pregnancy and lactation; older adults and people recovering from illness or injury are frequently studied at higher intakes, and that literature is suggestive rather than settled. Fiber has an AI of 14 g per 1,000 kcal, which lands near 25 g/day for women and 38 g/day for men aged 19–50.

Anchor the priority micronutrients

You cannot hold every DRI in mind, and you do not need to. A small number of nutrients do most of the work in planning because requirements shift sharply across life stages or because population intakes commonly fall short. Note how many of these values are not one number but several.

NutrientReference intake by populationWhy it drives planning
IronRDA 8 mg/day men 19+; 18 mg/day women 19–50; 8 mg/day women 51+; 27 mg/day in pregnancy. UL 45 mg/day adultsThe largest sex- and life-stage swing of any mineral; heme iron is better absorbed, and vitamin C improves non-heme absorption
CalciumRDA 1,300 mg/day ages 9–18; 1,000 mg/day adults 19–50; 1,200 mg/day women 51+ and all adults 71+Peak bone mass is built in adolescence; requirements rise again later in life
Vitamin DRDA 15 mcg (600 IU)/day ages 1–70; 20 mcg (800 IU)/day age 71+. UL 100 mcg (4,000 IU)/day adultsFew foods contain it; fortified foods and sun exposure dominate supply
FolateRDA 400 mcg DFE/day adults; 600 mcg DFE/day in pregnancy; 400 mcg/day of folic acid advised for anyone who could become pregnantNeural tube closure happens before most pregnancies are recognized — the evidence here is strong
Vitamin B12RDA 2.4 mcg/day adults; adults over 50 are advised to get much of it from fortified foods or supplementsFood-bound B12 malabsorption rises with age; strict plant-based diets require a reliable source
Vitamin CRDA 90 mg/day men, 75 mg/day women aged 19+; add 35 mg/day for people who smokeOne of the few RDAs adjusted for a behavior rather than a life stage
Vitamin ARDA 900 mcg RAE/day men, 700 mcg RAE/day women. UL 3,000 mcg/day preformed retinolPreformed retinol is teratogenic in excess; provitamin A carotenoids are not subject to the UL
SodiumCDRR: reduce intake if above 2,300 mg/day for adultsMost intake is already in food before it reaches the table
WaterAI about 2.7 L/day total water for women, 3.7 L/day for men aged 19+, roughly 20 percent from foodTotal water, not "eight glasses"; needs rise with heat, activity, and illness

Build the day, then check it

Planning works best as a two-pass process. The first pass builds a plausible day from food groups rather than from nutrients, because food groups are how nutrients actually travel: vegetables and fruit carry potassium, fiber, folate, vitamin C, and carotenoids; whole grains carry fiber, magnesium, and B vitamins, while enriched refined grains carry added iron and folic acid; legumes, fish, eggs, poultry, and meat carry protein, iron, and zinc, with B12 coming only from the animal-source members of that group or from fortified foods; dairy or fortified alternatives carry calcium, vitamin D, and potassium; nuts, seeds, and liquid plant oils carry unsaturated fat and vitamin E. Spreading protein across the day rather than concentrating it at dinner is a reasonable default, though the evidence that distribution matters much for healthy adults is preliminary.

The second pass checks the draft against the targets that most often fail. Run the energy arithmetic with the 4/4/9 yields and see whether the day plausibly meets estimated needs. Then check fiber, calcium, iron, vitamin D, and potassium specifically — these are the recurring shortfalls — and check the ceilings that matter for that person, usually sodium, added sugars, and any supplement approaching a UL. Finally, and most importantly, check feasibility: cost, cooking time, kitchen equipment, work schedule, cultural and religious practice, appetite, and preference. Adequacy is achieved over days and weeks, not at every meal, and a slightly imperfect plan someone will follow outperforms an optimal plan they abandon by Thursday.

Planning stepWhat you are doingCommon mistake
1. Set energyEstimate EER from age, sex, size, activityTreating an estimate as a precise prescription
2. Distribute macronutrientsApply AMDRs; convert percentages to grams with 4/4/9Forgetting the 130 g/day carbohydrate RDA floor
3. Draft with food groupsBuild meals and snacks that cover each group across the dayPlanning nutrient by nutrient and producing food nobody eats
4. Check shortfall nutrientsFiber, calcium, iron, vitamin D, potassiumAssuming a varied-looking day automatically covers them
5. Check ceilingsSodium, added sugars, supplement ULsStacking a multivitamin with fortified foods past a UL
6. Check feasibilityCost, time, equipment, culture, preference, appetiteSkipping this step, which is why most plans fail
Plan for the eater, not for the spreadsheet. A day that meets every DRI but requires four cooked meals, an unavailable ingredient, and food a person dislikes has an adherence rate of zero and therefore an effectiveness of zero. The best plan is the most nutritious one that fits the life it has to live in.
Interactive Assemble meals across breakfast, lunch, dinner, and snacks and watch energy, fiber, and macronutrient targets fill in live against reference ranges. Open full ↑
Key points
  • RDA is the target for an individual; EAR is for groups; AI is an estimate where data are thin; UL is a ceiling, not a goal.
  • Energy yields are carbohydrate 4, protein 4, fat 9, alcohol 7 kcal per gram.
  • Adult AMDRs: carbohydrate 45–65%, fat 20–35%, protein 10–35% of energy; the fat range is higher for young children.
  • Key values shift with life stage — iron 18 mg/day for women 19–50 versus 8 mg/day for men, and 27 mg/day in pregnancy.
  • Build with food groups, then verify shortfall nutrients, ceilings, and feasibility.

Section 5Evaluating nutrition information

Ask what kind of evidence this is

Nutrition claims are not all built from the same material, and the first useful question about any claim is what study design stands behind it. Mechanistic and cell-culture work explains how something could work but says nothing about whether it does in people. Animal studies add a living system but not a human one. Cross-sectional surveys describe correlation at a single moment. Case-control studies look backward and are vulnerable to recall bias. Prospective cohort studies follow people forward for years and are the workhorse of nutritional epidemiology — genuinely informative, but always shadowed by confounding, because people who eat any one thing differ systematically in dozens of other ways. Randomized controlled trials break that confounding by assigning the exposure, and systematic reviews and meta-analyses pool the whole body of trials.

Nutrition, though, imposes real limits on that ladder, and honest evaluation acknowledges them. You cannot blind people to broccoli. Outcomes like fracture or cardiovascular death take decades to appear. There is no true placebo diet, because eating more of one thing means eating less of another. Adherence to an assigned diet decays over months. Consequently much of the strongest nutrition evidence is a convergence across designs — consistent cohort findings, supportive trials on intermediate outcomes, and a plausible mechanism — rather than one decisive trial. Be equally wary of claims resting on mechanism alone and of demands that nothing count until a 20-year randomized trial exists.

Weigh the claim, not the confidence

Once you know the design, interrogate the numbers. Ask about effect size in absolute terms: "raises risk by 40 percent" is uninformative until you know whether the baseline risk is 1 in 2 or 1 in 100,000. Ask whether the outcome was a hard endpoint (heart attack, fracture, death) or a surrogate (a cholesterol value, a marker of inflammation) — surrogates move more easily and do not always carry the outcome with them. Ask about sample size and confidence intervals, about whether the finding is one result from many comparisons, and about who funded the work and who profits from the conclusion. Ask whether the dose studied is achievable through food, and whether the population studied resembles the one the claim is now being applied to.

Credentials help but do not settle anything. In many jurisdictions the title "nutritionist" is unregulated and can be used by anyone, while registered dietitian and registered dietitian nutritionist are protected credentials with defined coursework, supervised practice, and a national examination. A clinician or researcher speaking outside their field carries no special authority there, and expertise never substitutes for the evidence itself.

Red flagWhy it signals troubleBetter question to ask
"Detoxes" or "cleanses" the bodyNamed toxin is almost never specified; the liver and kidneys already do this continuouslyWhich substance, measured how, cleared by what mechanism?
One food or nutrient fixes many unrelated conditionsReal interventions have narrow, mechanism-bounded effectsWhat is the single primary outcome, in one defined population?
"Clinically proven" with no citationThe phrase is unregulated marketing languageWhich study — design, size, journal, who funded it?
Testimonials and dramatic before/after storiesSelected anecdotes cannot show effect; regression to the mean and placebo response are unmeasuredWhat happened to the people who did not improve?
"Natural," "ancient," or "chemical-free"Origin says nothing about safety or efficacy; every food is chemicalsWhat does the evidence show about this preparation, at this dose?
Proprietary blend, undisclosed amountsPrevents any comparison with a studied dose or with a ULHow many milligrams of each ingredient per serving?
The person making the claim sells the productA direct financial conflict of interestDoes independent evidence exist from people who do not profit?
"Doctors don't want you to know"Conspiracy framing pre-empts scrutiny instead of answering itWhat would it take to change your mind?
Foods sorted into "clean" and "toxic"Moral framing of food is not a physiologic category, and it is associated with disordered eating patterns rather than with better healthWhat does this pattern of eating do, measured how?

Why single-nutrient extrapolation goes wrong

A recurring failure mode deserves its own attention, because it looks like good reasoning. The chain runs: people who eat more of food X have less of disease Y; food X is rich in nutrient N; therefore supplement N to prevent Y. Each link is plausible, and the conclusion has repeatedly failed when tested. The clearest example is beta-carotene. Observational data consistently associated carotenoid-rich diets with lower lung cancer rates, but two large randomized trials of high-dose beta-carotene supplements in smokers and asbestos-exposed workers found more lung cancer in the supplemented groups, and one was stopped early. Similar reversals followed trials of high-dose vitamin E for cardiovascular prevention. The lesson is not that food does not matter — it is that a nutrient extracted from its food matrix, given at a dose no diet delivers, is a different intervention from the diet that generated the hypothesis.

The practical stance that follows is calibrated rather than cynical. Some nutrition conclusions rest on strong, convergent evidence: folic acid before and during early pregnancy reduces neural tube defects; iodine sufficiency in pregnancy protects neurodevelopment; vitamin D prevents rickets; replacing saturated with unsaturated fat lowers LDL cholesterol. Others are genuinely preliminary, including much of the work on the microbiome, on precise meal timing, and on personalized nutrition by genotype. Saying clearly which tier a claim occupies is the whole skill — and remember that general education of this kind is not individualized medical or dietary advice, which belongs with a qualified clinician who knows the person's full situation.

The fastest triage question is "compared to what?" Every meaningful nutrition claim contains a comparison — this pattern versus that one, this dose versus none, this population versus another. When a claim has no comparator, no numerator, and no denominator, there is usually nothing there to evaluate.
Interactive Examine five real-style nutrition claims and tap the persuasion tactics you spot; a hand-drawn credibility gauge swings from well-supported to many red flags, and one deliberately fair claim teaches what careful, hedged evidence sounds like. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Evidence runs from mechanism and animal work through cohorts to randomized trials and systematic reviews; nutrition constrains that ladder in real ways.
  • Judge absolute effect size, hard versus surrogate endpoints, confidence intervals, and funding — not the confidence of the speaker.
  • Recurring red flags: unspecified detox, cure-alls, uncited "clinically proven," testimonials, proprietary blends, and moralized food categories.
  • Single-nutrient extrapolation from dietary patterns has failed in randomized trials — high-dose beta-carotene increased lung cancer in smokers.
  • Distinguish strong conclusions (folic acid and neural tube defects) from preliminary ones (microbiome, meal timing, genotype-based personalization).

The chapter in one breath

  • Nutrition assessment triangulates four domains — anthropometric, biochemical, clinical, dietary — because no single measure is interpretable alone.
  • BMI screens populations and cannot diagnose an individual; albumin and prealbumin track inflammation, not protein intake.
  • Recalls, records, and FFQs answer different questions and fail differently: memory, reactivity, and coarse quantification respectively.
  • Energy underreporting is systematic — roughly 10–30 percent against doubly labeled water — and is measurement error, not dishonesty.
  • On a label, serving size governs every number, %DV assumes 2,000 kcal, the 5/20 rule reads low versus high, and ingredients run by descending weight.
  • Plan with the right DRI: RDA for an individual, EAR for a group, AI where data are thin, UL as a ceiling, AMDR for macronutrient ranges.
  • Energy yields are 4/4/9/7 kcal per gram for carbohydrate, protein, fat, and alcohol; requirements for iron, calcium, vitamin D, and folate shift with age, sex, and pregnancy.
  • Evaluate claims by design, absolute effect size, endpoint, and conflict of interest, and keep strong evidence clearly separated from preliminary evidence.

ReferenceWords worth knowing
